# Triple-Formation at a Break in the Animal Kingdom.

By

**Hans Przibram.**

From the Biological Experimental Institute of the Academy of Sciences in Vienna, [Zoological Division].¹⁾

With Plates III to XXI.

*(Received on 9 October 1919.)*

*Archiv für Entwicklungsmechanik der Organismen*, vol. 48 (1921).

> **Full translation.** A complete English rendering of Przibram's comparative monograph on triple-formation at a break in the animal kingdom — supernumerary and reduplicated limbs and the symmetry rules governing them (Bateson's reduplication) — surveyed across many genera, with the figure and plate legends.

> ¹⁾ An abstract of this work appeared under the title: Communications from the Biological Experimental Institute of the Academy of Sciences, Zoological Division; Head: H. Przibram. 44. The Triple-Formation at a Break in the Animal Kingdom, in the Akademischer Anzeiger of the Academy of Sciences in Vienna, No. 18, 1919.

## Introduction.

On the occasion of the description of objects from our developmental-mechanics Museum (Grosser and Przibram, 1906), I pointed out the significance of malformations for the analysis of the form-building capacities of the animal body, and various groups of teratological finds were discussed.

Since then I have continued to direct my attention to the acquisition of interesting museum specimens, and during a journey to England and the United States of North America I studied the objects preserved in the museums there, as well as making myself acquainted with the relevant literature.

In particular, those supernumerary appendages engaged my interest which, as paired additions to a limb, give rise to a tripling of that limb. And among these triple-formations it was once again those at the chelae of the decapod crustaceans that necessarily struck me especially, since, as a consequence of many years' occupation with the diverse formation of crustacean chelae and their variability following injury with subsequent regeneration and compensation, I had acquired a precise knowledge of the form-building capacities of just these structures.

Just as with the earlier publications, which took their point of departure from objects of our Museum (1910 *Homoeosis*, Grosser and Przibram, 1906), this time too I wished to attach considerations of general interest to the cataloguing of the objects, and for this purpose to bring in, presented at the appropriate place, other findings as well. But the scope of the treatise soon grew so much through the inclusion of the entire literature that our museum objects could claim only a very subordinate place.

For the understanding of the individual cases it became necessary to put forward an overview of the polarity- and symmetry-relations which the various animal classes presuppose, in order — as I shall attempt to show — to bring them into close connection with the form-building capacities.

On the occasion of several lectures on regeneration as a general phenomenon in the three kingdoms of Nature (1906), and on the organ-building capacities (1910, 1911), of an essay in the Roux-*Festschrift* (1910) and of some summarizing accounts (1906, *Kristallanalogien*, 1909, and 1913 *Experimentalzoologie*), I have already, in passing, made mention of those triple-formations, whose general symmetry-relations were described in detail by Bateson (1894). This researcher conceived these structures at that time as inborn mutations and rejected their regenerative origin. We read on p. 526: »A good many authors from the time of Rösel von Rosenhof onwards have said that these cases are a result of injury, or of regeneration after injury. For this belief I know no ground. It should be remembered as an additional difficulty in the way of this belief, that when the limb of a Crab or Lobster is injured it is usually thrown off bodily, while the extra parts most often spring from the periphery of the chela. But since, according to Heinecke, such mutilated parts are sometimes retained, this must not be insisted on.«

(»A whole number of authors, beginning from the time of Rösel von Rosenhof, have said that these cases are the consequence of injury or of regeneration after injury. For this belief I know no ground. It should further be remembered, as an obstacle in the way of this belief, that when a limb is thrown off entirely from the body, while the supernumerary structures for the most part spring from the periphery of the chela. But since, according to Heinecke, such injured parts are sometimes retained, no weight need be laid upon this.«)

Later, Bateson (1913) did indeed expressly endorse my interpretation of these triple-formations as consequences of wounding, yet emphasized their essential difference from simple regeneration.

He writes on p. 71: »Often classed with regenerations but in reality quite distinct from them are those special and most interesting examples where the growth of a paired structure is excited by a simple wound. Some of the best known of these instances are presented by the paired extra appendages of Insects and Crustacea. .... The more recent researches of Tornier and especially his experiments on the frog have shown that a cut into the posterior limb-bud induces the outgrowth of such a pair of limbs at the wounded place. Few observations can compare with this in novelty or significance; and tough [though] we cannot yet interpret these phenomena or place them in their proper relations with normal occurences [occurrences], we feel convinced that here is an observation which is no mere isolated curiosity but a discovery destined to throw a new light on biological mechanics. The supernumerary legs of the frog are evidently grouped in a system of symmetry similar to that which those of the Arthropods ex- 

hibit, and though in Arthropods paired repetitions have not been actually produced by injury under experimental conditions we need now have no hesitation in referring them to these causes as Przibram has done.«

(»Often classified with the regenerations, but in reality quite distinct from them, are those special and most interesting cases where the growth of a paired structure is called forth by a simple wound. Some of the best-known examples are furnished by the paired supernumerary appendages of insects and crustaceans. .... The more recent investigations of Tornier and especially his experiments on the frog have shown that a cut into the bud of the hind leg awakens the sprouting-forth of such a pair of limbs at the place of wounding. Few observations can compare with this in novelty or significance; and although we cannot yet interpret these phenomena or set them into their correct relation to normal occurrences, we nevertheless feel convinced that here is present an observation which is no mere isolated curiosity, but a discovery destined to throw new light on biological mechanics. The supernumerary legs of the frog are evidently arranged in a system of symmetry similar to that which those of the arthropods exhibit, and although among the arthropods paired repetitions have not in fact yet been produced by wounding under experimental conditions, we now need not hesitate to refer them to these causes, as Przibram has done.«)

It seems that Bateson therefore does not wish to count the triple-formations among the regenerations, because in his view it is here not a matter of the re-generation of a lost part, but rather of the appearance of a bud, which, just like the embryo of an organism, consists of an anlage of the left and an anlage of the right body-half; whereas I would see, in the mirror-image-reversed supernumerary part, merely a reversely growing anlage of the same body-side.

Bateson (1913) expresses himself on this on p. 75: »A very interesting question arises whether it is strictly correct to describe the extra pair as a right and a left, or whether they are not rather two lefts or two rights of which one is reversed. This question did not occur to me when in former years I studied these subjects. It was suggested to me by Dr. Przibram. The answer might have an important bearing on biological mechanics, but I know no evidence from which the point can be determined with certainty. In order to decide this question it would be necessary to have cases in which the paired repetition affected a limb markedly differentiated on the two sides of the body, and of course the development of the extra parts in order to be decisive must be fairly complete. One example only is known to me which at all satisfies these requirements, that of the lobster's chela figured (after Van Beneden) in Materials for the Study of Variation p. 531, fig. 184, III.«

p. 76: »If these indications are reliable*) und are established by further observation we shall be led to the conclusion that the bud which becomes an extra pair of limbs does not merely contain the parts proper to the side on which it grows, but is comparable whith [with] the original zygotic cell, and consists not simply of two halves, but of two halves differenciated [differentiated] as a right and a left like the two halves of the normal body.«

> *) Dr. Przibram, I should mention, concludes that on the whole the facts are against this interpretation, but as more evidence is certainly required, I call attention to the possibility.« 

(p. 75: »A very interesting question arises whether, strictly taken, it is correct to describe the supernumerary pair as a right one and a left one, or whether they are not rather two left ones or two right ones, of which one is reversed. This question did not occur to me when in earlier years I studied these questions. It was put to me by Dr. Przibram. Its answer could be of great importance for biological mechanics, but I know of no documentary material from which the point could be decided with certainty. In order to decide this question, it would be necessary to possess cases in which the paired repetition affected a limb strongly differently formed, and naturally the formation of the supernumerary parts, in order to become decisive, must be quite complete. One single example is known to me which fulfils these requirements at all, that of the lobster chela, figured (after Van Beneden) in Materials for the Study of Variation, p. 531, fig. 184, III.«)

(p. 76: »If these indications are reliable*) and are corroborated by further observations, then we shall come to the conclusion that the bud which develops into a pair of supernumerary limbs does not merely contain the parts of that side on which it grows, but is comparable to the original zygotic cell, and consists not merely of two halves, but of two halves which are differentiated as a right one and a left one, like the two halves of a normal body.«

> *) Dr. Przibram, I must add, concludes that in general the facts speak against this interpretation; but since more evidence is certainly required, I call attention to this possibility.«)

Less skeptical than Bateson, and convinced through his own experiments of the regenerative character of the triple-formations, Della Valle (1913, p. 130, note 2) already holds my earlier statements to be sufficient.

The present treatise is intended, on the basis of an extensive body of evidential material embracing all the main groups of the animal kingdom, and where necessary also through experiments, to furnish the incontrovertible proof that the triple-formations following Bateson's symmetry-rule are all to be traced back to regenerative processes, and indeed to the same capacity of regeneration which, after the complete severance of a part, leads to its restoration on the part of the bearer of the loss.

In particular, the proof will be furnished that the mirror-image-reversed component of the triple-formation in no case arises from an anlage of the opposite body-half. The difference between the triple-formations and typical regeneration lies, as we shall see, solely in the kind of injury, which, in contrast to the simple transverse amputation with one wound-surface, must exhibit a break with two wound-surfaces.

I have therefore chosen the short designation »Bruchdreifachbildung« [Triple-Formation at a Break] for the title of the work.

 

## I. Promorphological Survey of the Animal Kingdom.

Haeckel's complaint about the neglect of the doctrine of fundamental forms [Grundformenlehre] in the organic sciences, which he let resound in his *generelle Morphologie* [General Morphology] (1866), is even now still justified. To be sure, fragmentary indications about the axial- and symmetry-relations of the various classes are to be found in the textbooks, without, however, a complete system with uniformly carried-through nomenclature being provided. I therefore fall back on Haeckel's representation, although in the assignment of the concrete cases into the categories of his Table (pp. 556—557) I should like to permit myself some deviations¹), in that I use only persons [individuals], not colonies or parts of persons, as types, and in the selection take into account especially the knowledge of the form-building potencies. Haeckel first distinguished between irregular forms which show no fixed axis (I. Anaxonia), and forms with at least one fixed axis (II. Axonia).

(I) As anaxons he adduced the freshwater sponge *Spongilla*; yet I should not like to designate this form, which represents an animal colony, as such, since the individual animals consist of two layers standing in a definite positional relation to one another, with axial reference to the attached and free surface. By contrast, *Protomyxa* and *Myxastrum radians* may be adduced as examples, very simple rhizopods with diffusely distributed nuclear substance, of which Haeckel (1868, 1870) had found that any arbitrarily torn-off little piece grew up again into an animal. Here no direction is to be ascertained which would be tectologically or physiologically favored, or of any significance.

(II) Among the axons Haeckel again made a distinction between such forms with countless equal axes (II 1, Homaxonia) and such with one or more favored axes (II 2, Heteraxonia).

(II 1) The Homaxonia, according to their geometric determination, can consist merely of spheres, which must be concentrically nested one within another if tectological differences are to exist.

A magnificent example of these kinds is furnished by *Thalassicola nucleata*: in the center lies the spherical nucleus within the likewise spherical central capsule; this in turn is enclosed by a vacuolated spherical shell, which is still surrounded, at the spherical surface, by a thin layer of denser cytoplasm.

> ¹) I shall not enter here into the parallel drawn by Haeckel at the same place between the inorganic and organic fundamental forms; rather, their discussion shall take place on the occasion of a continuation of my »Kristallanalogien zur Entwicklungsmechanik der Organismen« [Crystal-analogies to the developmental mechanics of organisms] (whose first series appeared in 1906).

Verworn's experiments (1892) showed the necessity of nucleus and central capsule for the restoration of the whole animal. The remaining parts, i.e. those farther from the center (more distal), could be regenerated by the nucleus-bearing central capsule. The enucleated central capsule, too, began to regenerate the more distal parts, but perished before their complete formation. From the distal parts, the parts lying more proximally toward the sphere-center could not be re-formed at all.

We have here before us the simplest case of a rule we shall encounter again and again later, that more distal parts can be restored to their original form from proximal ones, but not conversely proximal ones from more distal ones.

(II 2) The Heteraxonia Haeckel divided into such with several constant axes without planes of symmetry (2 A. Polyaxonia) and such with one constant principal axis and with planes of symmetry (2 B, Protaxonia).

(2 A) As polyaxons almost all, or perhaps even all, Infusoria must be described. The absence of planes of symmetry is clearly expressed in the spiral ciliary wreaths of the Peritricha, which run either right-turning (Dexiotrichidae) or left-turning (Skaiotrichidae). In the natural division of the longitudinally dividing dexiotrichous *Carchesium polypinum*, the right-turning sense is maintained in both daughter-pieces, so that not even the plane of division is a plane of symmetry (cf. Delage and Hérouard, 1896, I. p. 486). The same holds for the transversely dividing Hypotricha, e.g. *Stylonichia* (Wallengren, 1902). Quite likewise proceeds the restoration of the form after artificial divisions, whereby the anterior end completes a posterior end and conversely, provided a nuclear fragment remained in the plasma (Prowazek, 1903).

(2 B) The Protaxonia were divided by Haeckel into such without cross-axes, i.e. with infinitely many equal planes of symmetry layable through the principal axis (B a, Monaxonia), and into such with cross-axes, which create preferred planes of symmetry (B b, Stauraxonia).

(B a) As Monaxonia most of the Flagellata and Porifera can be described. The Sporozoa, too, furnish good examples. Perhaps among these there are same-poled ones (which, with Haeckel, one might designate as a 1, Haplopola); all other examples concern forms with two differently formed poles (Haeckel's a II, Diplopola).

(a I) The spore-cysts of the Sporozoa are certainly to be designated as Haplopola; here, as a rule, symmetrical division-figures form themselves out (cf. e.g. the schema of the Myxosporidian division in Delage and Hérouard, 1896, I. p. 293), and the young Myxosporidian itself is two-nucleated [zweikernig].

(a II) The eggs of the Metazoa belong, at least from the time of their maturation onward, already to the diplopolar form insofar as the nucleus does not stand at the middle of the germ cell, but lies nearer to one — moreover often already morphologically distinguished — end of the principal axis, the "animal" pole, than to the opposite "vegetative" pole.

As the numerous developmental-mechanical experiments on the form-building capacities (potencies) of the eggs in their earliest stages have shown, this morphological and tectological differentiation of the principal-axis poles also corresponds to a physiological polarity.

Namely, if eggs — whether in the natural cleavage process or through artificial intervention — are divided into two parts, then the fate of the isolated cleavage spheres is either alike or else different. The parts undergo a like fate when the dividing furrow passes through the principal axis (meridional course), a different one when the dividing furrow cuts perpendicularly to the principal axis (equatorial course). (For a more exact account and literature compare Przibram, Exp. Zool. I. Embryogenesis, 1909; The Distribution of Form-Building Capacities in the Animal Body in the Dorsiventral Direction, Arch. f. Entw.-Mech. XXX, 410, 1910; The Distribution of Organ-Building Capacities over Body-Regions, Verhandlungen zool. bot. Gesellschaft Wien, LX, 111, 1910; Asymmetry Experiments as a Key to the Bilaterality Problem, VIII. Intern. Zoologenkongreß, 272, 1910, published 1912; the latter three writings summarized in Exp. Zool. IV. Vitality, 1913, p. 32, Chap. III.)

The two first blastomeres here, in the meridional course, are not only equipotent (to make use of the terminology employed by Driesch), but also totipotent, i.e. two whole — only diminished — animals proceed from the halved egg. Here the "prospective potency" (Driesch) of these first blastomeres is evidently greater than their "prospective significance" (Driesch), since indeed in the natural context only one half of the animal each would arise from one blastomere. In the equatorial course there arise from the halved egg two incomplete animals, whose defect does not concern the same region. Here, then, the blastomeres are neither equipotent nor totipotent, and their prospective potency reaches only little beyond the prospective significance.

Since, as was said, one and the same egg can take the one or the other alternative according to the course of cleavage, a structure must already be ascribed to the uncleaved egg which permits both, and that is a stratification of substances one above another in the direction of the principal axis in parallel circles. If one imagines a meridian plane of such a figure rotating about the principal axis, it will come successively into coincidence with nothing but identical planes; it is therefore a "rotational structure" of Roux's.

The eggs of the molluscs appear, according to the experiments hitherto, to possess no rotational structure; yet it is not excluded that the obliquely-to-the-principal-axis course of cleavage that normally takes place is to blame for this deviation, and that it will yet succeed in inducing the mollusc eggs, in early stages, likewise to form equi- and totipotent blastomeres.

(B b) The forms provided with cross-axes, or Stauraxonia, Haeckel likewise divided into homopolar (b I, Homopola) and heteropolar (b II, Heteropola).

(b I) Examples of Homopola are to be found, according to Haeckel, among the Radiolaria, e.g. Heliodiscus with three-fold, Acanthostaurus with four-fold cross-axes. Although no direct experiments have been carried out, it is to be assumed that, as in most other unicellular organisms, a physiological polarity exists only in the proximal-distal direction, while otherwise all parts are equipotent.

Among the multicellular organisms I have found no homopolar Stauraxonia which correspond to whole persons and are "harmoniously" (Driesch) composed, i.e. contain all sorts of normal parts in a proportionally correct conjunction. But certainly homopolar forms can be produced through accidents, or by experimental means, out of heteropolar Stauraxonia; these are the axial heteromorphoses of Loeb's; I shall return once more to their conditions in the discussion of the various heteropolar forms.

(b II) The Heteropola fall, according to Haeckel, into those with nothing but equal radial or semiradial cross-axes (II 1, Homostaura) and into those with unequal cross-axes (II 2, Heterostaura).

(II 1) Through the meridional cleavage the egg with rotational structure passes over into a four-fold homostaurous form; the cross-axes are all equal, also in physiological respect, since the first four blastomeres (and later harmoniously composed fragments) are equi- and totipotent. The prospective potency is here again greater than the prospective significance, for the later bilaterality seems normally — at least according to Roux's experiments on the frog — to be given by the meridian of insemination, that is, already by the course of the first furrow.

Of developed animals, many Hydrozoa belong to the Homostaura. The new cross-axes are recognizable in the tentacle ranks (and on the sexual organs), always equal and equipolar, but of very variable number. In physiological respect there exists a polarity corresponding to the morphological differentiation of the principal-axis poles. For example, in Hydra the tentacles standing at the oral pole are not able to regenerate the remaining parts.

In the double-walled tube which the Polyps represent, it is moreover well known that the one layer is not able to replace the other (the irreplaceability of the germ-leaves); yet it is still always questionable how the germ-leaves behave in budding.

(II 2) The Heterostaura split into forms with nothing but same-poled cross-axes (2 A, Autopola) and into those with at least one unequal-poled cross-axis (2 B, Allopola).

(2 A) Typical Autopola are numerously represented among the Scyphozoa and Ctenophora. In particular the gastric and tentacle planes of the rib-jellyfishes (Rippenquallen) show clearly the formation of distinct, but same-poled cross-axes. In the physiological respect too the alternating difference of the radii shows itself, in that during the embryonal (and larval) development each spherical segment develops further for itself without regeneration of the missing parts. (The literature references for these and other embryological experiments are to be found in Exp. Zool. I. Embryogenesis, 1907.)

In the transformation into the imago, however, the missing half of the half-larva that arose from a first cleavage cell is supplemented. (In the so-called "dissogony", Chun, 1892.)

(2 B) If, in addition to the principal axis, there appears a second, differently-poled axis, then the body thereby loses all other planes of symmetry except that one plane which can be laid through the principal and the second unequal-poled axis. This "median plane" then divides the body into its two halves (hence "Bilaterier", Hatschek, 1888), which we are accustomed to designate as left and right. The unequal-poled cross-axis we call the dorsiventral axis, since it connects, in our body, the back with the belly-side; that plane which separates the back- and belly-side, "frontal plane", after the direction of our forehead; the third plane, perpendicular to these two planes, which in our body cuts the principal axis perpendicularly, separating front from back, "transversal plane". The axis connecting the right with the left body-side, lying in the transversal plane, is called "lateral"; it thus stands perpendicular to the antero-posterior principal axis and to the dorsiventral axis.

The lateral axis is either same-poled (in Haeckel's Amphipleura, Eutetrapleura, and Eudipleura) or unequal-poled (in Haeckel's Dystetrapleura and Dysdipleura). In adherence to Haeckel's terminology, whose further subdivisions would be of no concern to us, I shall designate the Allopola with same-poled lateral axis as "Eupleura" (B a), those with unequal-poled lateral axis as "Dyspleura" (B β).

(B a) To the Eupleura can be reckoned the great majority of the "Bilaterier", especially if one considers only the external morphe. In the inner structure there are admittedly often to be found far-reaching asym- …symmetries, as indeed we already know from the anatomy of the human being; differences also in the formation of body-appendages on the right and left body-side are not rare: we shall return precisely to these in the course of our investigation, in particular in the case of the crustaceans. In eggs with equatorial course of the first furrow the difference shows itself very plainly, often already in the externally recognizable stratification, always in the physiological polarity, whereby the axis connecting the animal and vegetative pole turns out to be the one designated in the human being as the dorsiventral axis. Dorsal and ventral blastomere can never wholly replace one another reciprocally, as the lateral ones can. Just as little does it succeed, at later stages, in letting the dorsal and ventral side reciprocally replace one another completely; but in the case of the lateral sides this very often succeeds, as numerous natural ones traceable to injury and namely also the double-formations produced by longitudinal splitting in the median demonstrate.

The physiological polarization in the antero-posterior direction will be treated in detail in the next section; with advancing developmental height there shows itself a decrease of totipotency, which mostly belongs only to the middle body-portion and is finally restricted only to the germ cells. At the two-cell stage, however, the anterior and posterior blastomere still appear totipotent, in that through regeneration the missing half can be produced ("post-generation" of Roux's).

(B β) As Dyspleura are to be designated above all most molluscs, among which the snails carry the form differing in pole in the lateral axis strikingly on display in their shell. Here the halves of the animal also cannot be brought to coincidence as mirror-image, but certainly there are, in many species, individuals which are the mirror-images of the predominating form ("enantiomorphic").

## II. Description of the Break-Triple-Formations.

### A. At the Principal Axis.

#### a) Coelenterata.

##### α) Hydrozoa.

**No. 1.** *Tubularia mesembryanthemum.* Experiment of Hadži's, with Fig. 3, communicated in Przibram, 1906. Lecture, Stuttgart.

After an incision immediately under the little head of a Polyp, there formed two little heads surrounded by a common perisark at the wound-site, whose halves turned toward one another had fused with one another under shrinkage of the tentacles. The coenosark could be followed, separated from each little head, up to the place of origin on the old principal axis. The free margin of the component of the supernumerary forma- -tion standing nearest to the old little head passes over immediately into the zoenosark-neck leading to the old little head, the free margin of the more distant component into the more proximal zoenosark-part of the old principal axis. All three little heads have a common plane of symmetry and lie, moreover, in such relation to one another that any two adjacent little heads can be represented as mirror-images by a plane interposed between them. Since the little heads are symmetrical in themselves, these mirror-images can here, however, also be brought to coincidence through rotation.

Potencies in the Principal Axis of the Hydrozoa.

The greatest part of the principal axis is in the Hydrozoa totipotent to that extent that, upon transverse section of the Polyp, every such part-piece grows again into a whole Polyp. (Summary in Peebles, 1900, p. 444.) In many genera there can thereby, through the action of external factors, indeed even without injury, be brought to development at one pole parts which normally belong only to the other pole. Thus there behave in particular branching forms, which also resemble in this the plants, which are caused, e.g. through the moisture of the air, to drive roots even at the sprout-pole.

Yet the Hydranth-(little-head)-formation proceeds more rapidly at the original little-head-pole when little-head-formation was induced at both ends of the axis. If only the rudiment of the more distal of the two tentacle-wreaths is removed at a regenerate, then no proximal tentacle-wreath can be regenerated out of this rudiment (Driesch, 1897); but if the section is laid through the rudiment of the proximal tentacle-wreath, then its aboral end is indeed likewise not able to form the more proximal parts, but certainly a second tentacle-wreath at the aboral end together with mouth-opening (Peebles, 1900, p. 459).

In the case of very small pieces of the foremost little-head-region, there comes about, upon simple transverse section, often the formation of a double-head, whose two little heads, turned away from one another, without an intervening middle-piece, continue the principal axis in opposite directions, of which one is thus to be designated as a heteromorphosis.

Somewhat larger pieces, provided with a ligature in the middle and suspended free in the sea-water, likewise form little heads in both directions, which in this case are still connected by a middle-piece. According to Loeb it is the interruption of the streaming of specific substances, normally circling from the one to the other body-pole, which, in connection with the oxygen acting at the free surfaces, occasions the little-head-formation.

To these hypotheses I shall return once more later. I should like here only to point to the analogy in the position-relationships of the two little heads in the polar heteromorphosis and in the break-triple-formation in *Tubularia*: we need only think Hadži's …merely halfway-conducted transverse section as carried wholly through, then the proximal stem-fragment will form orally a new little head, whose neck-part hangs together immediately with the old zoenosark of the stem, while the distal — lying near the old oral part — aboral end of the cut-off little-head-bearing piece likewise forms a little head, a heteromorphosis. The difference will lie only in the complete formation of the little heads and in the straight direction of the principal axis. In other words, in the break-triple-formation the symmetry-relationships are best understood if we regard the middle component as a polar heteromorphosis formed aborally from the original little-head-part, and the third component as a normal regenerate growing orally from the proximal stem-fragment. Herewith there explain themselves at once the course of the zoenosark-connection, the situation in a common plane — that is, the one passing through the old longitudinal axis and perpendicular to the direction of the incision (wound-surface) — as well as the other symmetry-relationships.

The deviations in the directions of the principal axis and the partial fusion of the supernumerary little heads now require an elucidation, which a consideration in the sense of Tornier's (1896 ff.) Biotechnics furnishes. Through the incision a relaxation of the directly affected side was brought about, so that the distal (little-head-) end was bent toward the opposite side, and a gaping wound with two wound-surfaces arose, of which the one was open orally at the proximal stem-piece, the other aborally at the distal (little-head-) piece. The little heads sprouting out from each of these wound-surfaces now came, from lack of space, into closest contact with one another, and therefore fused in a middle-plane.

There remains only still the question open: why grew, out of the aborally turned surface of the distal (little-head-) piece, a heteromorphic little head and not a proximal stem-piece together with root-pole? It is, in my opinion, the ever-recurring case that the most distal parts can indeed be regenerated by the side of the proximal ones, but not conversely the proximal parts by the side of the more distal ones. But if the potency for a definite formation is no longer present, it by no means follows therefrom that henceforth nothing is regenerated; rather, the still-remaining potencies now come into activity. As growth-direction, that one is retained which would also have been taken for a non-heteromorphic formation. Thus the broken principal axis indicates the direction of the regeneration, which, according to Barfurth's Rule (1888, 1891, compare Prz., Exp. Zool. 2. Regeneration 1909, p. 204, § 10a and p. 232 II), grows perpendicular to the wound-surface, hence, in the case of perpendicular cross-section, in the prolongation of the old… …parts, which, of course, in consequence of the lack of space for the two supernumerary little heads experiences a bending.

Since *Tubularia* belongs to those genera in which a reorientation of the poles succeeds, at least under some circumstances, even without far-reaching injury — so that heteromorphic little-head- and root-poles appear also at other than the most distal pieces — the extinction of the potency for stem-piece-formation at the aboral surface of the distal break-off-piece does not come clearly to expression.

We turn ourselves therefore toward a genus in which a heteromorphosis is to be attained only with difficulty, and indeed only by the detour over transplantation, namely *Hydra*, in order to gain greater clarity concerning the connection between potency and heteromorphosis. In *Hydra* we can distinguish, in the aboral-oral direction, the region of the foot-disc, of the middle-piece, of the hypostome, and of the tentacles. The middle-piece, when cut in two, always lets the missing, more distal regions come forth again out of itself, under upright-maintenance of the polarity; it is therefore totipotent. The regeneration-quality toward the aboral pole is lesser: the foot-disc itself is no longer able to reproduce the whole Polyp. Toward the oral end, namely toward the mouth-opening itself, the regeneration of tentacles increases in quality. The hypostome regenerates them the most rapidly; yet in the hypostome-region there is again to be noted a decrease of the foot-disc-potency. Few hypostome-cells with a tentacle are no longer at all capable of forming a foot-disc (E. Peebles, 1897; W. Koelitz, 1911, I. p. 22). Tentacles without hypostome-cells can supplement neither hypostome nor other parts of the Polyp. Yet the bulging-out of a regeneration-bud at the proximal cut-end of an isolated tentacle has been observed, which, to judge from the case to be described directly, could be a heteromorphic tentacle-tip. Koelitz (p. 195) implanted a hypostome with tentacles into a second Polyp; here one of the tentacles had grown fast with its tip to the mouth-margin, so that it retained no free end any longer. This tentacle was then cut through in the middle, whereupon it showed itself "that it had really grown fast with its oral end to the hypostome". The Polyp became wholly regular and the tentacle thus transplanted inversely was not resorbed, so that therefore its earlier proximal end must have formed a tentacle-tip.

Just as the polarity of the tentacle can apparently be reversed through transplantation, so this succeeds in similar manner with the hypostome or the foot-disc. Namely, if of two Polyps the aboral poles are removed by section, the cut-surfaces of the Polyps so injured are healed to one another, and now through a… …section near the union-site the one Polyp is again cut off, so that of it only a narrow ring remains, then this no longer develops anew the missing parts of the Polyp or a hypostome with tentacles, but rather a foot. Conversely, if two Polyps, robbed of the head-end (hypostome with tentacles), are united by the cut-surfaces, and then the one Polyp is cut off near the union-site, there develops at this new wound-surface not the missing part of the Polyp or a foot-end, but rather a head-end.

*[End of the paragraph-ownership range. The final paragraph that begins on p.14 continues onto p.15 above per the paragraph-ownership rule; the remainder of p.15 (the H. D. King discussion and following) belongs to the next page-range and is not translated here.]* [...] a cut near the place of union, then this no longer reforms the missing parts of the polyp or a hypostome with tentacles, but rather a foot. Conversely, when two polyps are robbed of the head-end (hypostome with tentacles), united by their cut surfaces, and then one polyp is cut off near the place of union, there develops at this new wound surface not the missing part of the polyp or a foot-end, but a head-end.

H. D. King (1903, p. 229) interprets her experiments to mean that an influence of the smaller piece by the larger one took place. But this is contradicted by her own earlier experiments (1901, p. 158), in which the foot-ends of two Hydras were removed, the cut surfaces united, and each of the two Hydras was cut through near the place of union. Here one of the new cut surfaces developed a head-end, the other a foot-end, although no influence on the polarity-change could have proceeded from the different size. A more plausible explanation seems to me to be that the original section of the foot-end in the Hydras had not been made exactly in the same region: that component which had still retained more of the foot-region will, on being sectioned again near the place of union, have received only more substance of the foot-region, while the other will have received more substance of the middle region. The latter will, without change of the directions in the principal axis, regenerate what is lacking; but the former, since the foot-end lacks the head-forming potency, will exercise in the opposite direction what is still proper to the region, namely foot-formation. In an analogous way the heteromorphic formation of the head-end out of the hypostome-region, which is devoid of the foot-potency, can be explained. If this derivation is correct, then it must not be possible to obtain heteromorphoses by grafting in the manner described when both components are cut far from the place of union, where the two new amputations fall into the totipotent middle region. This is indeed also in actual agreement with the experiments of King. F. Peebles (1900, p. 478) emphasizes that Hydras grafted together with their oral cut surfaces formed an aboral head only when, after removal of a piece of the one component, the cut ran quite close to the place of union. Otherwise, neither here nor in the case of polyps grafted together aborally did it ever come to heteromorphosis, so that the case described by Wetzel (1896), in which a second cut close behind the tentacle-crown of the one Hydra is supposed to have given occasion to the formation of a foot, could not be confirmed. Yet I find in Koelitz (1911, II, p. 439) again two such cases cited, but at the same time the explanation for this exceptional behavior. In one case it came, namely, without a fresh section, to a transformation of the one component of aborally united polyps into a foot-end, and indeed under prior reduction and dwindling of all regions of this grafting-component down to a small aboral zone.

Now, in surveying the whole of the experiments on *Hydra* which have been described by various authors, I arrive at the result that heteromorphosis occurs when the totipotency is not present at the relevant site. The non-totipotent site is then able to unfold the potency still proper to it also in that direction in which, in totipotent pieces, its formation cannot come about.

Probably small, quite isolated pieces of the foot-disc would form a foot on both sides, small pieces of the hypostome-region tentacles on both sides, if they could be kept alive long enough. Through transplantation it is possible to confer viability upon them. We shall come to know quite analogous cases in the earthworm.

If an incision is made in the side of the *Hydra* transverse to the principal axis, then tentacle-formation can come about at the orally directed wound surface, but never at the aborally directed one.

Here a foot would be to be expected, were it not that, before its formation, a different regulation of the very plastic animal always came about first.

Break-triple-formations are therefore, as a consequence of the totipotency of the middle piece of the *Hydra*, in this genus not to be expected, and they have also not been described in the remaining numerous experiments.

##### Potencies in the cross-axes of the Hydrozoa.

If a hydroid polyp is bisected lengthwise wholly or partly, then the missing lateral part is reformed. The cut-plane becomes a plane of symmetry; but since all cross-axes are equal and equal-poled, and the cross-section of the animal decomposes at every bisection into equal symmetrical parts, right and left side can be distinguished only with respect to the wound surface.

##### β) Skyphozoa.

**No. 2.** *Lucernaria quadricornis.* Natural find by Carlgren, with Fig. 29, Pl. 2, communicated 1919, p. 27: »Two neomorphoses distal from the middle of the body. Between these lie several squeezed-out filaments (*fi*) all around, which is healed to the wound of the body-wall. Distal, neomorphic little head (*nh*) quite high, cup-shaped. Mouth-disc quadrangular, drawn out with its longer axis in the length of the mother-stem.

Two distal and two proximal tentacle-groups, which lay far apart and contained 5—7 tentacles. In the middle of the disc a well-differentiated mouth-tube — in the figure one sees the tip of the mouth-tube strongly curved toward the proximal side of the little head. Between the tentacle-groups lying side by side there runs a tæniole with its septal funnel invaginated from the disc. The two tæniolae of the little head fuse, at the base of the little head, with one tæniole of the mother. — Proximal, neomorphic little head: little head cylindrical, quite long. Disc insignificant, drawn out transversely to the longitudinal direction of the mother-stem, densely set with tentacles, of which the middle ones are the weakest. No mouth-tube. Only one tæniole present.«

##### Potencies in the principal axis of the Scyphozoa.

The typical formations of the oral pole, namely tentacles and mouth-tube, can in *Lucernaria* be developed on all cross-pieces, but just above the foot-plate only slowly. On the other hand, a foot-plate is reformed at the aboral end of the anterior cross-piece only when the cut was carried out in the posterior half of the stem. Otherwise, a mouth-tube and tentacles, hence a heteromorphic anterior end, mostly form also at the aboral wound surface of the anterior partial piece. Correspondingly, longitudinal pieces lying toward the oral end and provided on both sides with wound surfaces also again form oral poles on both sides. In other words, the potency for the formation of the aboral pole is, before the middle of the stem, lacking entirely (Carlgren, 1909, p. 5). From this it follows, analogously to the conceptions developed for the Hydrozoa, that a break or tear transverse to the principal axis in the upper half of the stem must develop a new oral pole at both wound surfaces, as we also see in the mentioned figure of Carlgren, where the two supernumerary heads arose, in front of and behind a healed transverse wound, from the upper half of the stem.

In passing, I should still like to mention, from Carlgren's experiments, that on the part of the mouth-tube no tentacles, but on both wound surfaces of transversely cut-off cup- or arm-pieces a mouth-tube and tentacles, could be formed. In contrast to *Hydra* (and the remaining Hydrozoa), it is therefore not the arms with the tentacles that appear here as the foremost body-part, but the mouth-tube, which has forfeited the most of its potencies. This corresponds to the structure of the Scyphozoa, in which the oral pole appears sunk in as a gullet-tube, so that the tentacle-arms are no terminal organs, if one imagines the gullet-tube everted, whereas in the Hydrozoa the mouth opening coincides with the protostoma itself (cf. Hatschek, 1888, p. 250).

Concerning the physiological polarity of the tentacles themselves, no data exist for *Lucernaria*, but indeed for many other Scyphozoa. According to the concordant statements of various investigators, the tentacles of the actinians are never able to reform the more proximal parts of the animal, not even the gullet-tube.

On the other hand, all the remaining parts (perhaps, however, in *Cerianthus*, with the exception of the foot-end — Loeb, 1891, 1892) are able to form a head-pole toward the front, a foot-pole toward the rear. Correspondingly, as in *Hydra* among the Hydrozoa, incisions into the body-wall are not able to call forth heteromorphoses, since it is always only at the orally directed wound surface that tentacles and mouth opening form. To be sure, it seems that even in the actinians the foremost region, the one surrounding the mouth opening, is not always capable of producing the foot-pole, since small pieces of the anterior region in *Cerianthus* (Child 1903) and in *Renilla* (Wilson, 1903) formed tentacles on both sides. Two experimental results of Moszkowski (1907, p. 423) on *Actinoloba dianthus* also admit this interpretation: »The cut-edges of the body-wall here had not grown together with those of the œsophagus, but were inserted laterally thereof on the œsophagus-wall. Thus exactly the same external conditions were created as at a regenerating anterior cut surface.« Just as a forward-directed free œsophagus-surface, so now in both cases the backward-directed surface too produced a tentacle-crown — hence a heteromorphosis. From this I should like to conclude — in contrast to Moszkowski — that the œsophagus-region possesses a potency restricted to tentacle-formation, which is exercised only when the body-formation on the part of the mantle-region fails. This œsophagus-region would correspond to the cup with the arms in *Lucernaria*.

##### Potencies in the cross-axes of the Scyphozoa.

Since the Scyphozoa possess different, though mostly equal-poled, cross-axes, several questions arise with respect to the potencies and the symmetry-relations of cross-pieces, which could not yet be decided in the homostaurous hydroid polyps. To begin with, a different behavior showed itself in *Lucernaria*, according as the animal had been cut lengthwise through the septa (tæniolae) or through the gastral spaces. In the former case the cut-edges healed without further formation; in the latter case the missing half was supplemented.

In transverse sections, regenerates of the tæniolae, and correspondingly also the gastral spaces, continued themselves in it into the same-named parts of the old piece. The same is stated of the heteromorphoses and Carlgren's neomorphoses.

Herewith, then, the symmetry is determined for the break-triple-formation, namely that all three components possess a common plane of symmetry, which stands perpendicular to the wound-tear. The middle, aborally directed component is a mirror-image of the original head as well as of the one growing out at the orally directed component, which is directed in agreement with the original head. This symmetry-determination is here to be given with certainty for the orientation of the components, yet, owing to the autopolar form, it is not possible to distinguish between upper and lower, right and left side. Heteromorphosis and prototype can still, after equal orientation of the tentacle-pole, be brought into coincidence in four positions (each by a rotation of 90°).

In *Cerianthus*, which is developed bilaterally, namely internally, through the formation of a dorsal directional-chamber differently shaped from the ventral directional-chamber, it could already be decided whether the dorsal directional-chamber, in all new-formation processes, passes again into a dorsal one, and the ventral one into a ventral one. Then, in polar heteromorphoses, the mirror-image position relative to the normal formation would be established also with respect to the body-sides.¹) Unfortunately, the few available cases are not investigated with respect to the septum-formation. The sea-anemone species investigated in detail by Carlgren (1904 and 1908) with regard to the behavior of the directional mesenteries after longitudinal division belong to the two-rayed species provided with two equal directional-chambers, and can therefore also not be drawn upon directly by us here.

However, bilateral forms can also proceed from the biradial form through fragmentation, in that only one pair of directional mesenteries comes to development. On such a bilateral form of *Sagartia viduata*, Carlgren (1908, p. 15, Figs. 4—6, Pl. 1) now observed an aboral heteromorphosis, and in this little head the inner septum-formation is a true mirror-image of the original little head, in that each septum passes over into the corresponding one of the other, the wound-plane functioning as a true mirror-surface.

There is here only one single position in which heteromorphosis and prototype can be brought into coincidence, namely after separation in the mirror-plane, through a rotation of 180° in the principal axis and 180° in the plane perpendicular to it.

#### b) Zygoneura.
##### α) Skolezia.

**No. 3.** *Planaria morgani.* Experiment by N. M. Stevens, 1909, p. 616 with Fig. 16, shows a somewhat unusual case of multiple tail. The worm had been split far forward on March 18, but grew together again in the anterior part and produced a pharynx in the old pharynx-pouch. On April 18, one month after the operation, a growth was observed between the two tails and was taken for a heteromorphic head;

> ¹ Since, according to Van Beneden (1867), the development of the right dorsal septa precedes that of the left, there could here be a mirror-image without the possibility of coincidence.

but it turned out to be a third tail with the main branches of the digestive tract, as indicated by the dotted lines. On April 23 the animal was fed with the eye-pigment of a fly, and the distribution of the digestive tract was thereby made clear. It seemed possible that this might be a case of regulation that would entail the degeneration or absorption of the lateral tails, but nothing of the kind could be detected up to May 7, on which day the worm was killed and dissected into serial sections in order to determine the number of nerve-cords in these tails. The number and distribution of the nerve-cords showed themselves to be equal to that of the digestive tract.«

This triple-formation, which arose after splitting of the hind end, can be traced back to a cut-direction not running exactly in the median plane of the planarian body, whose two cut-edges have brought forth regenerates. The cut had evidently attacked somewhat to the left of the tip of the tail, so that the right side of the tail comprised more than half, and had then penetrated somewhat to the right, as far as the midline of the body in the pharynx region. In this way the right tail received both branches of the digestive tract and of the nerve-cord at the tail-tip, while the left received only a part of the left branches in the vicinity of the pharynx. Now this latter regenerated the distal part of these branches into a left tail, while the right side of the tail brought forth a new tail at the cut surface lying somewhat farther back. It might be objected that the left tail should likewise form a second nerve-branch; but according to Flexner's (1898) investigations, in planarians artificially made double-headed by an anterior incision, each head too possesses only a single nerve-cord, in that no new cords regenerate in either of the grown-together inner head-halves (cf. *Exp. Zool.* II, p. 77).

##### Potencies in the principal axis of the Planarians.

The numerous experimental data on the potencies of the developed planarians agree in this, that in the middle body region, which comprises the pharynx, heads are formed toward the front, tails toward the rear, whereas in the head region only heads, in the tail region only tails are formed, without regard to the orally or aborally directed position of the cut surface. We therefore have to regard the middle region as totipotent; head and tail have an unequivocally differentiated potency.

It is here a matter of indifference whether the relevant portions are completely separated off or merely incised. In the head region there arise, in the former way, double heads, in the tail region double tails (Morgan, 1904) without intervening pharynx portions. Upon a mere incision, supernumerary heads or tails arise.

##### Potencies in the lateral axis of the Planarians.

In consequence of their dorsiventrally paper-like compressed shape, a severance, apart from perpendicular to the principal axis, succeeds only in the same. If an incision is carried out wholly or partly in the midline from the anterior body-end, then the two cut-halves supplement what is missing, so that two complete, respectively partly doubled, planarians arise.

If the cut is led from the tail-tip medially up into the head region, then there form, at the wound-angles turned away from the head-end, new heads in single (Stevens, 1909, p. 619, Figs. 22, 23) or double number (van Duyne, 1896), according as the wound has gaped more weakly or more strongly, while the two trunk- and tail-halves grow out into two complete trunks with tails. When, in *Planaria Morgani*, the heteromorphic head was cut off transversely by Stevens, then no new one formed, but the cut-off head formed at its cut surface a heteromorphic trunk and tail. — At first glance this seems to stand in contradiction to my view that polar heteromorphoses are conditioned by an extinction of the potency. In fact this would be the case if it concerned one of the double-heads mentioned above, which arises after complete section of the trunk. But in the lengthwise-split planarian the heteromorphic regenerate springs at least in part not from the head region, but from the median split-surface of the trunk, as the course of the nerve-cords in Stevens's Fig. 22 clearly shows. Here the left-median-lying regenerate of the right split-half of the trunk bends into the left half, and analogously it behaves with the left split-half of the trunk.

With respect to the two trunks, the head-regenerate growing opposite to the old head is not at all heteromorphic; this follows also from the further course of the experiments: if one of the trunk-tails was cut off together with the head-regenerate, then this at once set itself into the principal axis of the trunk-tail and a wholly normal worm came about. This behavior becomes still clearer when — probably as a consequence of a not quite median cut-direction — the head-regenerate had grown perpendicular to the longer wound-side, as Stevens depicts in Fig. 23. If the head, together with a piece of the trunk-part of the longer trunk-tail, is cut off, then it forms a new pharynx and tail at the pole turned away from the head (that the formation has proceeded out of the cut surface turned toward the old head-pole, as Stevens describes it, does not accord with her figure). There now exists the possibility that the severance of this head-regenerate takes place at that site of the trunk at which the trunk-tail-potencies are extinguished; then it should thus come about that at this cut-site the original planarian forms a heteromorphic head — and Stevens has indeed observed this twice, precisely under these experimental circumstances.

The planarians represent, with respect to their symmetry, the type of the "Eupleurae": the differently-poled principal axis and the differently-

## β) Aposcolecia

**No. 4.** *Allolobophora terrestris*, experiment described and illustrated by Korschelt (1904) (there Pl. XIII, Fig. 2), p. 270.

"This concerns a piece of a medium-sized, not fully sexually mature *A. terrestris* situated behind the genital region, which consisted of 19 body rings, of which three anterior ones and one posterior one were injured. A head-piece of 6–8 segments and the genital region had been removed, after which the worm was cut through 19 segments farther back. The piece was 11 mm long and formed a posterior regenerate of 5 mm length and 29 segments; toward the front a regeneration bud arose, which at first did not differ from such anterior regenerates, tapered at its anterior end and brought an opening to development there. This part of the anterior regenerate also later still made itself recognizable, by its darker coloration and opaque consistency, as the older [tissue]. On this anterior regenerate, thus seated on a newly formed base, two new regeneration buds later came to development, which lay laterally against the first and were shaped quite similarly to it. The three anterior regenerates consisted, apart from the likewise newly formed base, of 6–8 segments and measured about 0.8 mm."

Korschelt now describes, on the basis of several frontal sections (which are also illustrated), the structure of the three regenerates: a central nervous system was demonstrable in the two later-formed ones, but not in the first. The dissepiment relations could not be clarified. A gut was present in all three components of the triple-formation. Despite the absence of the pharyngeal ganglion in the first regenerate, Korschelt nevertheless decides in favor of the head character of this bud as well. With his argument, that the formation of a tail beside two heads would be improbable, I cannot agree (in planarians such a thing occurs). As we shall report presently, the middle body region of the earthworm possesses totipotency, so that there a head is usually formed toward the front, a tail toward the rear. It could now be that the middle regenerate arose from an originally aborally directed wound surface, the two others from orally directed ones. This is, however, contradicted by Korschelt's statement that the middle regenerate could at all times be recognized by its color as the original one (although in his illustration nothing of this is to be seen); yet if this one, in contrast to the others, was a tail, then perhaps a somewhat different coloration may be connected precisely with this. With certainty we can draw from Korschelt's triple-formation the conclusion that in the earthworm triple-formations can arise as a consequence of injury through regeneration, that all three components lie in one plane, and that the dorsal side of the old wound-piece passes over into the dorsal sides of all three components, just as the ventral sides pass over into the three ventral sides.

### Potencies in the principal axis of the remaining Zygoneura.

Quite analogous relations to those in the planarians we find again in the metamerically built aposcolecid worms. Thus, for example, in the earthworms (*Allolobophora*, *Lumbricus*, and others) the middle body region is capable of regenerating a head toward the front and a tail toward the rear, whereas the posterior body section cut off behind the clitellum regenerates toward the front merely a tail again (Morgan 1902), whose symmetry corresponds to a mirror-image of the old hind end. By rotation through 180° these mirror-images can be brought into coincidence, since they are built symmetrically within themselves. In order to obtain regenerates from pieces of the head region as well, one must proceed to the inverse-oriented transplantation of these pieces onto longer worm-parts. The formerly aboral, now orally facing, end of the little piece from the head region then produces a heteromorphic head (Hazen, 1899). It is not, say, the longer piece of the worm body that compels the small piece to develop a head-end as the harmonic completion of the worm, for small pieces from other regions, which no longer regenerate a head, are not, after analogous transplantation, transformed into head-ends (Leipoldt, 1911; the author does indeed seem to assume such an influence for totipotent little pieces from the middle worm region, but there is no compelling ground for this).

### Potencies in the lateral axis of the Zygoneura.

Analogous to the two-headed and two-tailed planarians, there are such [forms] from the other groups of the Zygoneura, e.g. again in the earthworm. After splitting of the end in question, the missing inner halves of the split pieces are produced again (Korschelt, 1904). By setting two wound surfaces at the tail end of the snail *Nassa mutabilis*, Hankó (1914) obtained a doubling of the tail appendages, so that here too the hind end, besides the posterior potency, also exhibits a lateral one. As natural finds, double heads are known in fly larvae and in tails from the molluscan crayfish [crustacean] (Packard, 1872; Smith, 1904), further lobster larvae with a doubled trunk-tail (Herrick, 1895, Pl. 48, Figs. 199–200).

### Potencies in the dorsoventral axis of the Zygoneura.

Let us stay with the example of the earthworm: after removal of ventral longitudinal pieces together with the nerve cords lying in them, a new head can be formed only at the proximal cut surface, which Morgan referred to a formative influence of the nervous system. But since, after tearing-out of the nerve cord without removal of the rest of the ventral region, a new head formed in many cases far separated from the end of the remaining part of the nerve cord (Goldfarb, 1909), I (1910, *Roux-Festschrift*) saw the cause of the absence of the head regenerate in a potency lacking to the ventral side for the formation of the dorsal parts.

According to the cases hitherto adduced concerning the heteromorphic development of the regenerates sprouting from a body part with restricted potency in the direction of restriction, it would be expected that the wound in Morgan's experiments would cover itself over with skin of dorsal character. Since Morgan mostly sutured the wound lengthwise together, a tube composed entirely of dorsal skin must necessarily have arisen at first, so that a decision about the later behavior cannot be reached.

## c) Chordonii.

**No. 5.** *Ciona intestinalis*, experiments by Jacques Loeb (1892, *Organbildung*, p. 35 with Fig. 6 and plate-figs. 14, 15, 16). When a transverse incision was made in a siphon of this tunicate, which ran wedge-shaped, thus possessed a distally and a proximally directed cut surface, there developed at both cut margins ocelli, that is, the organs lying distal to the cut site at the normal siphon end. These ocelli are, at the proximally turned cut margin, heteromorphoses; at the distally turned cut margin, orthomorphic regenerates. Later a whole new siphon forms around the cut site, in that the cut site itself develops into the siphon opening. Such a siphon thus consists, properly speaking, of one orthomorphic and one heteromorphic half-tube, which are fused with each other along their margins lengthwise, so that they form a complete accessory siphon. The old siphon and the two new half-siphons have one plane of symmetry in common, namely that plane which runs perpendicular to the cut site through the longitudinal axis of the siphon. Owing to the effaced bilaterality of the transformed tunicates, no statements can be made about the rest of the behavior of the various sides; the regenerates could be brought into coincidence with the normal siphon end in many positions by rotation.

### Potencies of the tunicates in the longitudinal axis.

According to experiments of various authors on *Ciona*, and then especially by Driesch (cf. *Exp. Zool.* II) on *Clavellina lepadiformis*, the developed tunicates are totipotent on all cross-sections upon transverse division of the whole animal. The completion takes place by various routes, and can also occur through the after-sprouting of the lost parts in orthomorphic regeneration. One might therefore have expected that, owing to the absence of regions with restricted potency, no heteromorphoses and triple-formations at a break would form. But now it turns out that cut-off siphons do not grow out again into whole animals, so that here too after all a restriction of totipotency is present. Experiments which Secerov carried out at my instigation, with inverse transplantation of siphons, unfortunately yielded no clear result. I would expect that on them too, at the old proximal, now distally directed, cut surface new ocelli would arise.

**No. 6.** *Petromyzon Planeri*; natural find of a larva with three tail-tips, described and illustrated by D. Barfurth (1899, Pl. III, Figs. 31–33), p. 27:

"About 3 cm before the tail end the bifurcation takes place. The continuous fin-seam of the larva divides at the place of bifurcation into three lamellae, which then farther peripherally each accompany and surround one of the three tail-tips. The position, length, and curvatures of the individual tips are evident without further ado from the drawing and require no further description. A difference between the three tail ends was macroscopically otherwise not present: each behaved, in regard to fin-seam, integument, and musculature, like a normal tail end." The lamprey was further investigated on a section series. "Although at the place from which the sections come, externally nothing at all of a bifurcation was yet to be seen, the first of these sections nevertheless already showed the accomplished bifurcation of the chorda dorsalis ... Of interest is the behavior of the spinal cord in this section. Its bifurcation is just caught at its beginning. Each tail end in process of formation has its own transversely striated musculature. The integument is dorsally still uniform, ventrally already in process of constriction." Barfurth himself traces, in analogy to the experimental production of multiple-formations at the tail end of amphibians (cf. farther below), the triple-formation back to an injury, perhaps the bite of a fish.

From his description and illustration it can at once be seen that the dorsal surfaces, or better edges, of the tails all proceed from the dorsal side of the animal, the ventral ones from the ventral side. The injury must have penetrated somewhat obliquely into the longitudinal axis, so that a case quite similar to No. 3 in *Planaria morgani* is present; the supernumerary tails springing from the two gaping wound surfaces must have completed lateral parts again. Even the pushing-aside of the middle component from its originally orally directed growth can be recognized, according to Barfurth's drawing, at the bending of the fin-seam. I hold the bent-off tail-tip to be the original end, the shortest end to be the regenerate from the distal break surface. On the sections the lying of the three branches in one plane can still be made out, but otherwise, owing to the little-differentiated form of the lamprey tail, nothing about the symmetry can be recognized.

**No. 7.** *Esox lucius*, natural find in the egg of a pike, described by Lereboullet (1855) and illustrated (*Annales des Sciences naturelles*, Pl. III, Figs. 24, 25), who reports about it on p. 217:

"On the 25th of April, toward the middle of the fifth day [reckoned from the artificial insemination — Ref.], I find an embryo with a double body, of which the two components diverge from each other so strongly that they stand, as it were, opposite one another.

The right part, simple in its posterior half, itself divides again into two approximately equal branches, of which the left one ends in a head with two optic vesicles, the right one in a pear-shaped process.

The left part bears a normal head (Fig. 24).

The tail is long, bent over, and folded onto the common part of the two bodies.

On the 28th of April, end of the eighth day, the bodies had become shortened (Fig. 25). The left one had normal shape; the right-lying double body had reached the same developmental stage as its left neighbor; but only one of the two heads of this double body was normal, the other, namely the right one, had only one eye, that of the right side; the left eye was lacking.

One saw two hearts beating: the one lying between the simple and the double embryo, the other between the two heads of the double embryo. These hearts beat weakly and independently of one another; there were no red blood corpuscles, neither in the heart nor in the yolk."

This is the only triple-formation which Lereboullet found beside numerous double-formations of pike embryos.

**No. 7a.** *Salmo fario*, natural find in the egg of a trout, described and illustrated by Rauber (1880) Pl. VIII, Fig. 15, but not further observed.

"16 days after fertilization. Three embryonic anlagen of unequal strength and length. The connecting angle of anlagen II and III has an anterior opening and amounts to 45°; to their axis of connection the anlage I stands at a forward-open angle of setting of about 20°. The blastoderm has not yet reached the equator of the yolk-sphere."

In contrast to the older view, which regarded eggs with several germs as the cause of most double- and triple-formations, we must now assume splitting or break of an anlage with subsequent regeneration as their cause. This standpoint is also represented by the great work on "The Teratology of Fishes" by James Gemmill, which is unfortunately at present not accessible to me.

The symmetry relations offer nothing new as against the cases discussed earlier; since each head is symmetrical in itself, the middle one of those [anlagen] — which moreover again lie in one plane (here, owing to the curvature of the egg, better a surface) — can, by rotation, also be brought into a position coinciding with one of the others. Likewise all dorsal sides and all ventral sides pass over only into the homologous side of the other two components.

**No. 8.** *Pelobates fuscus*, natural find of a garlic-toad [common spadefoot], described and illustrated Pl. XI, Figs. 1, 2, by C. Bruch (1873).

The tail-tip contains three chorda-ends, of which the middle is most weakly developed. The longest, uppermost end is at its tip curved several times over, corresponding to the action of a tearing force setting in ventrally. A part of the blood vessels between the second short chorda-end and the lowermost, middle-length one is bent back headward. All three chorda-branches lie in one and indeed the median plane. Concerning further symmetry relations nothing further can be stated, owing to the round shape of the chorda and the otherwise slight differentiation of the tail.

### Potencies of the amphibians in the principal axis.

Well known is the regenerative capacity of the transversely severed amphibian tail (for literature cf. Przibram, *Exp. Zool.* II, 1909). The regeneration of the foremost body end, of the jaw-tips, was also demonstrated (Werber, 1906).

The distally facing cut surface thereby produces the tail end again; but of what is a proximally facing cut surface, that is, the one remaining on the tail, capable? No one will expect that the wholly cut-off tail should regenerate a body for itself again; but although the tails, as Vulpian (1859, 1877) observed, still develop further for a long time, one has again resorted to transplantation in order to be able to demonstrate clearly the character of the regenerate.

When Harrison (1898) amputated the tail-tips of two tadpoles and brought the wound surfaces to healing, he could, after detachment of the one tadpole body, demonstrate with all certainty that out of the now proximally facing wound site of the tail a tail end grew. The myotomes of this regenerate are inversely directed to those of the healed-on tail-piece. The tadpole tail thus brings forth, like the remaining hitherto discussed restrictedly endowed parts, a polar heteromorphosis, when it is given opportunity for further life and proximal regeneration. With this agrees Morgan's (1900, p. 111) observation, that after a wedge-shaped excision from the middle of the tail-back an accessory, forward-directed tail-tip grows out.

**No. 8a.** Finally, Godlewski (1904) by incisions into the vertebral canal of the tail of newts directly observed the forward-growth of one tail-tip from the proximally facing, and the backward-growth of a second accessory one from the distally facing, wound surface.

The totipotency of the middle body region shows itself very beautifully in Tornier's (1907) experiments on the four-cell stage of the axolotl or frog egg. If through pressure the anterior blastomere of one body side is driven somewhat away from the posterior (text-fig. 4), then the freed aborally facing surface of the anterior blastomere is able to complete trunk and tail (text-fig. 5), the orally facing one of the posterior blastomere a trunk and head (text-fig. 6); yet it mostly comes thereby to the suppression of one of these formations. Supernumerary transversely standing trunk-tails also arose (Tornier, Figs. 7, 8) when the two anterior cleavage cells in the ventral district (namely in the white field) had been driven apart from each other by pressure; from such a median break surface the regenerate turns, when it is developed by the left blastomere, toward the right, in the opposite case toward the left. If through pressure-action an anterior blastomere had been pushed away from a posterior in the ventral district, then Tornier (Figs. 9, 10) obtained larvae with a heteromorphotically forward-directed tail. In the interpretation of this finding I should like to differ from Tornier. He himself opines (p. 77): "The reason is: the anterior cleavage cells loosen, even under all these circumstances, their normally not-into-development-coming hind-body-building materials, and form thereby over their own, now independently become, white field a blastopore with medullary folds, and thus produce a full supernumerary hind end of the organism. Its strange growth direction, however, comes from the fact that — as a genuine regenerate, which it is — it begins to appear perpendicular to the wound surface from which it stems." According to Tornier's Figs. 9 and 10, the trunk-tail regenerate stands perpendicular to the forward-facing break surface of the district of the posterior blastomere, thus seems not to stem from the anterior one. Further, the origin of the regenerates shows itself farther caudalward than in his Figs. 7 and 8, where the formations grow over the heart. It can therefore, for the solution of the contradiction contained in Tornier's statements, be assumed that in Figs. 9 and 10 a heteromorphic tail grew forward out of the district of the posterior blastomere, since at the level in question the head-building materials were already no longer present, whereas in Figs. 7 and 8 it is a matter of orthomorphic regenerates growing perpendicular to the wound surface, proceeded from a region with totipotency.

### Potencies of the amphibians in the lateral axis.

Upon divergence of the two anterior blastomeres of the four-cell stage there arise, through completion of each anterior half, double-headed forms, as Spemann (1900, 1901) demonstrated for *Triton*, Tornier (1901) for *Pelobates* (and *Rana*). These, as well as the already mentioned ortho-, neo- and heteromorphic regenerates, which owe their origin to one blastomere, that is, only one body side, prove — just as does the possibility of rearing whole larvae from one blastomere of the median-constricted *Triton* egg (Herlitzka, 1897) — that in the lateral axis there is no restriction of potency in the sense that the left side would be incapable of forming a right one anew, and vice versa. Admittedly, however, from the present experiments no conclusion can be drawn as to whether really the organs of the opposite side, or not merely a mirror-image of one's own side, are produced.

Double-headed or other double-formations are seldom found in nature among the amphibians; I find actually only Xiphopagi and Opodymi of *Salamandra maculosa* [modern *S. salamandra*], which Pontallié has described, mentioned (Yourdain, 1877), and I believe I also remember having seen double heads in salamander larvae which were prepared out of the uterus.

### Potencies of the amphibians in the dorsoventral axis.

From Spemann's experiments on the *Triton* egg it emerges that dorsal and ventral blastomeres are not in a position to substitute for each other, but produce incomplete embryos. We shall therefore also be permitted to expect that, upon sagittal division of an amphibian [embryo, the regeneration takes place not by completion of the lacking parts, but...] — *(paragraph continues on p.29, outside this assignment)*.

By making an incision in the tadpole tail, forkings of it are easily produced (Vulpian, 1862; Barfurth, 1898, 1900; Tornier, 1900, 1901; cf. Lit. Prz., *Exp. Zool.* II, p. 206), but, as already mentioned in connection with the description of a triple-formation in the garlic-toad [common spadefoot], our question cannot be solved here on account of the slight differentiation of the tadpole tail.

**No. 9.** *Calotes cristatellus* Fitz., an Indian lizard, described and illustrated in Fig. 7 by H. Brindley (1898), p. 684:

"A larger deviation from the norm is shown by a *Calotes cristatellus* Fitz. (Agamidae) [Indo-Malayan Region] in the University Museum at Cambridge. The dorsal view of the abnormal part of the tail can be seen in Fig. 7. The first forking is 18.2 cm and the second 19.0 cm from the cloaca. The three branches grow in the horizontal plane and are, from right to left, 3.0, 2.3, and 4.7 cm long respectively. The soft parts form so thin a layer that the vertebral column can easily be traced without the undesirable destruction of the specimen by sectioning. The last vertebra still present is bent outward to the right at an angle of 70°, so that the distal three-quarters of its length run into and from the base of the right-hand branch. From this point on, which lies 0.8 cm from the inner angle of the first forking, the right branch is a regenerate. The same is true also of the whole of the two remaining branches and of the short stump from which they spring.
