This statement is in agreement with the fact that only on the right branch, and on it only as far as the end of the last vertebra reaches, are the scales of the same size as on the normal part of the tail. Everywhere else the scales, though of normal type, are smaller than on that tail region which, through the presence of the vertebral column, proves itself to be normal."

Brindley then adduces cases of injuries to the lizard tail with subsequent accessory regenerations, and regards the triple-formation as a consequence of such an injury, without however becoming clear about the more precise circumstances.

**No. 9a.** *Lacerta vivipara*, experiment by Tornier (1897), provisionally communicated by him with a drawing made from a roentgenograph.

A point of bending [kinking] on the tail has produced two break surfaces, one facing distally on a vertebra, one facing proximally at the articulation of the next vertebra. From each of these a new tail-end has sprouted, as always in reptilian regenerates, without a vertebra. The two regenerates, springing up close to one another, are fused within a common cutaneous pocket, so that the tail externally appears only two-pronged. All three tails lie in one plane, namely in the median. The end of the original tail, likewise torn off at the bend, has also regenerated into a long point without a vertebra.

### Potencies in the principal axis of the reptiles.

For the understanding of cases Nos. 9 and 9a we must recall that the tail of the reptiles regenerates without vertebrae, only with a skeletal tube, and often with altered scaling (Lit. Prz., *Exp. Zool.* II, p. 188 ff.); the jaws regenerate their transversely amputated tips — Werber, 1905. To this extent, the tail-regenerates represent lengthened ends of the normal tails, for here too there is lacking the formation of vertebrae, and an aberrant scaling is often present. After the extinction of the vertebra-potency and the normal-scale-potency of the severed tail-portion, the formation of distalmost parts thus sets in. This now takes place not only at the distally facing break-points of the lizard tail, but also, upon complete severance of the tail vertebral column — where, however, the end still connected with the soft parts has remained adhering — at the proximally facing break-surface. Tornier (1902, Fig. 8) has excellently explained the mechanical processes in the origin of lizard triple-formations on the lizard tail, but has not particularly pointed out the heteromorphic character of the one, middle regenerate. If the tail is only fractured, the vertebral column however not entirely severed, then, on a proximal gaping of the wound-margins, a tail grows out from each break-surface. Both lie mostly in a common cutaneous pocket, since in their growth they come too close to one another to remain separate (Tornier, 1902, Fig. 7). Thus it must have been in the case of the lizard No. 9a, while in the case of the agamid No. 9 it is rather the basal parts of the accessory tails that have fused with one another.

### Potencies in the lateral axis of the reptiles.

Experiments are available only on the splitting of the tail (cf. Lit. Prz., *Exp. Zool.* II, p. 206), yet most of the cases belong to the incomplete break-triple-formations; whether vertebrae, when split, would be capable of completing the lacking half, is not known.

In analogy with the amphibians we may, on the other hand, refer the two-headed natural finds in reptiles to a divergence of the first blastomeres with subsequent regeneration of the facial halves (Lit. Prz., *Exp. Zool.* II, p. 207).

### Potencies in the dorsoventral axis of the reptiles.

Among the tail-double-formations there occur superimposed ones, whereby the one, ventral component receives the continuation of the ventral scale-armor, the other, dorsal one the continuation of the dorsal scale-armor. The question now is whether the underside of the dorsal component receives ventral scale-armor or dorsal? On these relations only Brindley (1898) seems to have reflected: with side-by-side components of a double-tail of *Mabuia carinata* (ibid. Figs. 3, 4) the large ventral scales are continued onto both; with superimposed ones of a double-tail of the same species, however, the large ventral shields were present indeed on the ventral component (ibid. Fig. 6), but not on the dorsal one (ibid.

Fig. 5). Unfortunately the scale-armor is here complicated, in every double-regeneration of *Mabuia*, in such a way that larger shields than normal appear dorsally as well, so that the two branches of the last-mentioned double-formation also exhibit, dorsally (the dorsal one shifted to the left), a row of larger shields. The testing of the question on more favorable objects (species with constant dorsal and ventral scale-difference also of the regenerates) would still be of interest.

**No. 10.** *Homo sapiens*, a three-headed boy, is illustrated by Galvagni from Catania (Guinard, 1893, p. 494, Fig. 272).

On the very broad trunk, which however bears two legs and two arms at normal places, there sit two necks, of which the right-standing one again divides and bears two heads. Unfortunately nothing is communicated about the skeleton, so that it cannot be decided whether the vertebral column of the middle-standing head stems from a proximally facing break-surface of the component standing farthest to the right. The possibility of fusion from three parallel-placed germs remains.

**No. 10a.** *Ovis aries*, a three-headed sheep of Ambroise Paré, illustrated by the surgeon Bellanger (Guinard, 1893, p. 493, Fig. 271), allows still less to be said about symmetry and genesis, and is cited only for the sake of the most complete possible enumeration of triple-formations.

### Potencies of the birds and mammals in the principal axis.

Postembryonic regeneration is known of the bird-beak (cf. Przibram, *Exp. Zool.* II, *Reg.*, 1909, p. 161; further Werber and Goldschmidt, 1909), and of the dormouse-tail (ibid. p. 162; further Klintz, 1914); otherwise any [eventual] regenerates must be referred to embryonic processes (bird-tail, after experiments by Lillie, 1904, Lit. Prz., p. 166).

### Potencies of the birds and mammals in the lateral axis.

Double-headed monsters and analogous double-formations of the hind body-end have been described not seldom in the warm-blooded animals. For lack of corresponding experiments, however, it cannot yet be determined whether one is dealing with splittings followed by regeneration of the split-halves in the lateral direction, or with separation of the first cleavage-cells, or with fusion of two embryos. Against the origin of such double-formations, as well as of the so-called "Siamese" twins and their transitions, out of originally separated fruits,

> ¹) To No. 10b. On p. 211 of the same book I expressed the opinion that the "cock with a triple beak mentioned by Perrault 1676" might be a regenerative break-triple-formation. According to consultation of the original (p. 148 and Fig. C on p. 146) it concerns a male specimen of a curassow species, *Mitu-paranga*, which possessed a beak as if grown together in parallel from three beaks. From the description and illustration it does not emerge whether one is dealing with a variety or a monstrosity; but, according to the parallel position of the components, it cannot be a break-triple-formation.

speaks the circumstance that the two components, in the overwhelming majority of cases, possess the same sex, and also tend to exhibit toward one another the extraordinary resemblance valid for identical twins. Thus Förster (1861) adduces, for man, no different-sexed double-formations, without however drawing attention to this point; Schwalbe (1907, p. 2) says straight out: "The double-formations are always, like identical twins, of the same sex. A certain case which shows that different sex too occurs has not become known to me."

### Potencies of the birds and mammals in the dorsoventral axis.

[These] can, just as little as with the foregoing vertebrate groups, be inferred beyond the prospective significance of the embryonic parts.

## B. Triple-formation at a break in an antimere.

### d) Ambulacralia.

**No. 11.** *Echinaster sespitosus*, experiment by Schapiro (1914), illustrated Pl. VII, Figs. 15 and 16, described p. 234:

"I began, in the angle between two arms, with a perpendicular separation-cut through the disc, and carried it to the midline, ambulacral groove, of the opposite arm, which I then halved precisely in the middle in the longitudinal direction. In this way the disc was now divided fairly precisely into two halves. From that half of the disc which contained the madreporite-plate I detached 1½ arms (one whole arm and the halved one) — one arm was left on the disc-half (Pl. VIII, Fig. 17). The re-replacement now took place ... in the form of a super-regeneration, whereby the small stump of the halved arm reproduced three arms, namely one arm as the normal re-replacement of the lacking one, while the two superfluous arms grow out from the wound-margin of the ambulacral groove divided along the midline (see also Fig. 16), that is, take it as their base."

According to the illustrations, all three components of the triple arm lie in one plane; the dorsal sides all face toward the dorsal side of the disc, the ventral sides toward the ventral side. The two supernumerary arms do not, say, radiate — as one might suppose from Schapiro's description — from one point; rather the one standing nearest the disc springs from the boundary between disc and arm-stump, while the middle one clearly takes its origin from the regenerating part of the halved arm-stump, with the formation of a mirror-image symmetry to the same; it alone deserves the designation of a heteromorphosis, which Schapiro applies to the whole super-regeneration.

**No. 11a.** *Porania pulvillus* Gray, a starfish, described by Herdman (1886) and by Bateson (1894, No. 675), with a sketch by Herdman (ibid. Fig. 134), p. 440:

"Specimen 5 cm in diameter with five short rays. The ray opposite the madreporite-plate, *viewed from the aboral side* [italics in the original — Ref.], is distinctly forked, about 1 cm from its end. The ambulacral groove of this (Fig. 134) abnormal ray divides into two branches at a distance of 2 cm from the rim of the mouth. One of these branches runs along one of the forking-prongs of the ray to its end without further complication; but the other branch, belonging to the second forking-prong, divides again 2 mm from the first forking, so that it forms two tracts, which again unite 3 mm farther on, in that they in such manner enclose a small piece of ordinary integument within an ambulacral area. Finally this ambulacral area divides once more close at the tip of the ray. There are no signs of injury or disease on the specimen."

In spite of the last remark, I allow myself to address this case as a break-triple-formation and to place it in parallel with the origin, described by Tornier, of two accessory lizard-tails in a common cutaneous pocket. The description would briefly run: "Starfish with break of one ray near the tip and regenerative tips which, grown from the two wound-surfaces, have in part, in consequence of too slight a gaping of the wound, reunited." As otherwise in break-triple-formations, the three components lie in one plane, all dorsal sides toward one direction, all ventral sides toward the opposite direction. Any two adjoining components are mirror-images, yet they can, on account of the in-itself symmetrical structure of each ray, be brought into a position of coincidence by rotation.

The middle component has sprung from a proximally facing wound-surface, and is therefore to be designated as a heteromorphosis.

### Potencies in the principal axis of the antimeres of the Asterids.

*Echinaster sespitosus* belongs to those Asterines which do not regenerate from a severed arm without a portion of the disc; Schapiro (p. 222) writes: "Among the more than 150 arms which I had in my aquaria, not a single one regenerated, and all perished, despite careful tending, after a shorter or longer time," "even though the amputation surface very often ... covers itself even with a little skin and scars over." After the failure of the disc-potency, then, in case the arm can still be nourished through connection with a disc, the formation of distal tips is also to be expected at proximal wound-surfaces, as evidently occurred in No. 11. About the regeneration of *Porania* or of the Gymnasterids in general nothing is known to me, yet quite similar conditions are likely to prevail here. True comet-forms, that is, the growing-out of whole discs with all the lacking rays from a disc-less arm, are namely reported in agreement only from the family of the Linckiidae, to which *Porania* does not belong (Lit. in Prz., *Exp. Zool.* II, p. 41, and Richters, 1912). For the rest, no certain experimental results are as yet available even for these, in particular it is not known how far the totipotency extends distally from the place of attachment of the arm at the disc.

### Potencies in the lateral axis of the antimeres of the Asterids.

By vertical splitting, double-formations (*Echinaster* — Schapiro, 1914; *Asterias* — King, 1898) have often been produced in starfish arms; there is no doubt that the lacking longitudinal half of the arm is formed anew (Lit. on natural finds in Prz., *Exp. Zool.* II, p. 49).

### Potencies in the dorsoventral axis of the Asterids.

If arms are cut through horizontally, then there regenerates, indeed, on the ventral split-part a new dorsal side, but from the dorsal split-part there forms only a tube consisting of the dorsal tissue-parts, without it coming to the formation of a ventral side (*Asterias* — King, 1898; *Echinaster* — Schapiro, 1914; Nusbaum, *Zool. Anz.* 1915). On the dorsal half, then, the ventral potency is extinguished.

### Potencies in the disc of the Asterids.

From the disc, arms can be regenerated; the dorsal side of the disc, cut out together with the madreporite-plate, is also replaced (Schapiro, 1914); the ventral half of the disc thus shows itself just as totipotent as that of the arms in the ventro-dorsal direction.

**No. 12.** *Antedon rosaceus*, a triple-formation that arose in my experiments on arm-regeneration in crinoids at Naples 1905, now preserved as No. 375 of the developmental-mechanics museum in the Biological Experimental Institute of the Academy of Sciences in Vienna; provisionally communicated with Fig. 4 and demonstrated at the Naturforschertag [Congress of Naturalists] in Stuttgart (Przibram, 1906).

The specimen has a disc-diameter of ½ cm, an arm-span of 1 dm. Three arm-branches had been cut off beyond the forking, namely, of those standing to the left and right of the anal papilla, the one nearer to the anus, and further, of the one lying opposite the anus, the (reckoned from the anus) left branch. This last one bore, at the breaking-off of the experiment, a long regenerate of normal type. The regenerates from the two remaining amputation-sites, on the other hand, were short; as closer examination showed, here too injury had again occurred, namely from the stump standing to the left of the anus further distichal joints had fallen off, while on the stump standing to the right of the anus some distichal joints were indeed about to fall off, but, in a position bent to the right, had remained connected with the base through soft parts.

This last arm still offers the picture of a typical break-triple-formation, in that from each of the cross-surfaces formed there rises a regenerate of an arm-tip.

The [regenerate] from the distally facing break-surface of the distichal piece that has remained standing is, just like that from the distal amputation-site of the bent-off distichal part, 4 mm long and rolled in toward the second [arm-tip] of the same arm — as corresponds to the norm. The regenerate growing from the proximally facing break-surface of the bent-off distichal part, on the contrary, is only 2 mm long and rolled in in the reverse direction, so that it forms a true mirror-image to each of the remaining regenerates. The pinnules of all three regenerates face, as would correspond to the normal direction, dorsalward; one can therefore say that the three regenerates lie in one plane, if one disregards their curvature.

**No. 12a.** *A. rosaceus*, the case illustrated by Bateson (1890, p. 584, Fig. 4) of a feather-star [crinoid] with a six-fold arm-ending is, according to the foregoing and following, to be regarded as a regeneration after manifold injury. (The symmetry is unclear.)

### Potencies in the principal axis of the antimeres of the Crinoids.

As I (Przibram, 1901) demonstrated, and as has since been confirmed from various sides (Minckert, 1905; Reichensperger, 1912), the single arms regenerate progressively from the disc toward the tip, always only the structures lying distal to the amputation-site. An arm-pair therefore regenerates, after removal from the disc, only heteromorphic arm-tips at the wound-surface, not a disc again. In agreement with this stands now the regeneration of an arm-tip from the proximally facing break-surface of a piece of arm that has remained hanging: the experiment has unintentionally developed into a parallel with the transplantation-experiments on polyps, earthworms, and tadpoles, where likewise little pieces incapable of life on their own could reveal the regeneration-potency dwelling within them.

### Potencies in the lateral axis of the antimeres of the Crinoids.

In the course of my experiments on *Antedon* in 1905 I obtained double-formations in regenerates as well (communicated 1906, Fig. 4), and also collected more fully developed natural finds (B. V. A. museum-catalog No. 387; not published), which prove the completion-capacity of a split-half of the single arm in the lateral direction into fully symmetrical arms.

### Potencies in the dorsoventral axis of the Crinoids.

As my experiments showed, the ventral disc can be formed wholly anew, whereas the transversely amputated calyx is not reproduced.

The severed disc cannot reproduce either the calyx or arms, although regenerative potencies are not lacking to it, for the excised anal papilla is regenerated when the disc is left on the calyx; likewise the experiments of Minckert and Reichensperger show that the tentacles seated dorsally on the calyx can be regenerated, that thus on the dorsal calyx-half regenerative potencies are present, which however are not capable of reproducing the proximal [parts].

## Potencies of the echinoderms in general and the conception of them as colonies of persons (stocks).

Intentionally I have at first subjected to our consideration the principal axis of the echinoderm-antimeres, and not an antero-posterior principal axis of the whole echinoderm [spiny-skinned animal].

In this way we have obtained a complete parallel of the break-triple-formations in starfishes and feather-stars with those at the principal It is well known that Haeckel (1866, p. 329 among others) conceived of the echinoderms as animal-stocks [Tierstöcke], arising out of five single persons which proceed by budding (similar to the synascidians) from the bilaterally-symmetrically developed larva and have more or less lost their self-dependence through the formation of a centralization. Haeckel (1878) distinguished three stages of this advancing centralization (p. 443):

«I. First main class: Protestrellae, star-animals without inner centralization, with total morphological autonomy of the astrolenes, without predominance of the central astrodiscus. The intestinal system composed of a single central gut and five (or more) developed fork-cleft special-guts.

1. Class: Asteriae.»

«II. Second main class: Anthestrellae, star-animals with partial inner centralization. The proximal part of the astrolenes, together with the entire intestinal system, has been absorbed into the formation of the central astrodiscus, while their distal part forms free arms.

2. Class: Ophiurae. 3. Class: Crinoida.»

«III. Third main class: Theeestrellae, star-animals with total inner and outer centralization of the five astrolenes and completely absorbed into the formation of a simple capsule-shaped or tube-shaped astrodiscus. The central gut simple, without special-guts.

4. Class: Blastoida. 5. Class: Echinoida. 6. Class: Holothuriae.»

With regard to the potencies, Haeckel was thinking in this connection of the capacity of sea-stars to regenerate from a single arm, to form «comet-forms», whereas the Anthestrellae, namely the snake-stars [brittle-stars] and hair-stars [feather-stars], are not able to do this, though they can still reproduce the arms that have been torn off. In the Theeestrellae such independent, detachable radii are no longer present at all.

Haeckel's scheme requires correction insofar as not all sea-stars have retained the physiological independence of regenerating from a single arm; yet it has much that is captivating in its favour through its agreement with the regeneration-potencies of the groups later established. I should like to remark further that the Ophiurae, just as little as the Crinoids, are able to form a complete star from a single arm-fragment alone. When Semon (1889) describes and depicts an *Ophiopsila aranea* (Plate XXIX, Figs. 1, 2), which is supposed to be a «case of the insertion of a regenerated disc with three tentacles into an arm that has, moreover, regenerated its tip at the proximal part», then Ludwig (1888) has rightly objected that it is a matter of a disc-fragment with two injured arms, since the position of the little feet on both old arm-fragments is directed toward the disc.

If, however, we do not accept Haeckel's view and prefer to adopt the homologizing of the developed echinoderm with a single individual, then in the sea-stars, snake-stars and hair-stars the arms become body-appendages, and we must regard our triple-formations as structures on lateral axes — whereby, however, owing to the in-itself symmetrical construction of each radius, no comparison results with the metameral appendages of the body-sides of other bilaterians. We must then take as the anterio-posteriorly running main axis that section through the disc which contains the eccentric anus or the madreporic plate. Cut in this direction, or perpendicular to it and to the plane-spread of the disc, the spiny-skinned creatures in question prove to be totipotent; we shall here not come to see any form of heteromorphosis or triple-formation at a break. A main axis is distinctly expressed in the sea-cucumbers; after transverse section both parts proved to be totipotent, in that, while maintaining the polarity, they complete what is lacking (Cucumaria — Monticelli, 1896).

In agreement with this stands the case of a *Cucumaria planci* described and depicted by Ludwig (1892), in which on the left side of the body, somewhat above the middle, a half tentacle-crown had grown out, which — just like the normal oral one — bore smaller gill-branchlets on the left, and whose radii took their origin from two radii of the left side of the animal. As Ludwig remarks, it cannot be a matter of an embryonic malformation, because the holothurian radii first come to development on the larva, but rather of the consequence of a bite into the left flank.

With regard to the potencies in the embryonic and later development of the echinoderms, I should like to repeat that, given exact homologizing of the regions, no case is known in which a later stage had greater potencies than an earlier one (I shall return later to the germ-cells).

## C. Triple-formation at a break on paired, metameral body-appendages of continuously-growing invertebrate animals without thoroughgoing metamorphosis.

### ↦ Mollusca.

(No. 12/13.) Šećerov has published «on the experimentally produced double-, triple-, and multiple-formations of the tentacles [Fühler] in snails, especially in *Limnaea stagnalis* L.», a separate work (Arch. f. Entw.-Mech., XL, 105, 1914).

The experiments to which he refers had been begun at my suggestion and under my direction. I was, however, unable to convince myself that the results would have been sufficient for an analysis of triple-formation at a break in the snail tentacle, and had therefore declined to bear the responsibility for their publication in the issues of the *Archiv* from the Biologische Versuchsanstalt in Vienna. The presentation in the publication that has now appeared outside our issues strengthens me in my rejecting standpoint. Neither the figures of the whole animals or tentacles, nor the histological pictures, permit any conclusions whatever. In particular, there is also no clarity in the conception of the direction of growth of regenerates as a consequence of the directions of cell-division; rather, only further treatment is promised. Even the schematic figures do not show the behaviour that is described in the text. Under these circumstances I may refrain from a closer description of his results.

Other regenerations or malformations clearly recognizable as triple-formations at a break are likewise not to be mentioned in the molluscs; the occurrence of double tentacles, eyes, or dorsal papillae, and furthermore lateral outgrowths on tentacles and arms, are no doubt often to be traced back to such an origin, but admit of no analysis. (Literature in Przibram, Exp. Zool., 2nd Reg., 1909, p. 130, § 10, in the cited work of Šećerov, 1914, and in Hanko, 1913, 1914.)

### a) Non-claw-bearing appendages of the Crustacea.

No. 13. *Palinurus vulgaris* Latr. [Plate XVIII, Fig. 1]. Langouste [spiny lobster] with triplication of the left (second) antenna, reported with two figures by Przibram and Matula (1913), preserved in the developmental-mechanical Museum of the B. V. A. as No. 405, obtained through the mediation of Prof. Cori, director of the zoological station at Trieste, from Comisa on 17 December 1912 for the price of 16 crowns. The animal could be kept alive for some days in a sea-water aquarium and examined as to the irritability of the antennal branches, on which an account has been given in the previously mentioned treatise.

The langouste measured 41 cm from the middle small rostral tip to the end-point of the tail-shield. The right normal (second) antenna consists of the three shaft-joints, together 6 cm long, and of the 26 cm long, uninjured flagellum. The left (second) antenna bears, upon a normally-formed and normal-sized first shaft-joint, a strongly broadened second shaft-joint, which splits at the front inner angle into three branches, each of which possesses the foot-tubercles and spines of the normal second shaft-joint; yet on the middle component these stand mirror-image-reversed, that is, just as on the normal right second antenna, namely on the right side of the shaft-joint instead of on the left. The mirror-image inversion of the middle component shows itself still more distinctly on the third shaft-joints, which spring from the branches of the second. Whereas the third shaft-joint of the first and third components bear at the right front angle two blunter, yellow-coloured spines, and at the left front angle one black and one brown, black-cored pointed spine, the arrangement in the middle component is reversed: at the right front angle stand the black, then the brown, black-cored spine; at the left, the two yellow, blunter spines. Each of the 3rd shaft-joints of the triple antenna ended in a flagellum, whose exact length, owing to repeated breakage before and after capture, cannot be given. Certainly the flagellum of the component standing most toward the left, that is quite on the outside, was the longest; it measured, including a tip broken off after death, a small part of the tip, 36½ cm. The middle component bore a flagellum, somewhat broken off at the end, of 10 cm; the last was broken off at the very capture, and after the capture it had detached itself completely [to the joint] and was sent on to us in a letter; the length of this fragment amounted to 6 cm. More important is the consideration of the breadths, which permits the conclusion that the two injured flagella cannot have been much shorter than the normal flagellum of the right (second) antenna. While the flagellar curvature of the I. and III. components corresponds to that of a normal left antenna, the curvature-arch in the II. component is opened the wrong way round, hence runs just like the curvature-arch on the flagellum of the right antenna.

The left component of the triple-formation must, according to its whole formation — to which also corresponded complete flexibility, motor as well as sensory irritability of each member — be regarded as the original left antenna. The broadening of the second shaft-joint and the smaller size of the following shaft-joints on the two remaining components make it probable that these two accessory components owe their origin to a regeneration after injury. These structures were not motorically excitable, but indeed sensorily so. On the middle component, the second and third shaft-joints were altogether grown together, and scarcely anything of musculature was present. A nerve-branch ran from the boundary of the first and second shaft-joints into the first and third components, but into the middle one there seemed merely to enter, in a backward direction, a single nerve from the boundary of the second and third shaft-joints of the left component. In any case the middle component, which throughout exhibited the reversed symmetry relative to the other components, was also the least well and independently developed. It springs, in my opinion, from a proximally-directed break-surface, whereas the right component will have sprung from the distally-directed [surface] of a tearing-wound which, penetrating at the inner front angle of the second shaft-joint, had caused this to gape, so that a narrow strip together with the old inner angle gave way to the right, but the dorsal round red marginal tubercle, together with the third shaft-joint, gave way to the side to the left, opening between them approximately a right angle.

Out of each of the two break-surfaces there now emerged once again, with maintenance of the existing dorsoventrality, all the parts lying distal to the injury-site. In so doing, the inner margin of the normal antenna continued into the inner margin, the outer margin into the outer margin of the accessory components, whereby the symmetry of the middle component had to develop as inverse. If we imagine the left antenna cut off entirely and regenerating according to the principle that at the break-site all distally-lying parts are to be re-formed — whereby the dorsal side continues into the dorsal side, the ventral into the ventral side, likewise the inner-margin side into the inner-margin side and the outer-margin side into the outer-margin side — then we should obtain a mirror-image figure whose plane of mirroring would be the break-surface. (By transplantation this ought also to be attainable in the crustaceans, only all attempts to transplant in them have hitherto failed.) This figure offers, in contrast to the mirror-images discussed hitherto, the new feature that these mirror-images cannot, by rotation, be brought into coincidence with one another in any position, and indeed for the reason that the normal antenna is not in itself symmetrical, but has formed a left and right side as inner margin and outer margin respectively. If, now, the antenna is not entirely torn off, then the heteromorphic component sprouting out of the proximal wound-surface is pushed aside by the orthomorphic component growing toward it, but is not further hindered in the formation of its sides. Incidentally, it cannot be decided with certainty whether the heteromorphic component did not perhaps arise from the base of the III. component, displaced toward the right, and not from a break-surface of the I., which would give our case a similar origin to that which *Antedon* No. 12 had shown. This interpretation appeared to me even the more probable, if the origin of the nerve of component II perhaps after all did not arise from the nerve of the I. component (which, with certainty, could not be followed gaplessly in the anatomical investigation). In any case the triple-formation exhibits all the marks of a break-injury; the three components lie, apart from insignificant twistings, all spread out in the frontal plane, just as the normal right antenna.

For the rest, the langouste is normal except for the smaller length of the left first antenna in comparison with the right; this smaller size can, just like the smaller length of the flagellum on the right second antenna in proportion to that on the I. component of the left, easily be traced back to regeneration. Finally I should like to remark further that many parts of the langouste, among others also all three components of the triple-formation, are overgrown with serpulid shells, which permits the conclusion that the last-occurring moult lies far back.

No. 13a. *Palinurus vulgaris*, described and depicted by Léger (1886) with Plate VI, recently investigated by Bateson (1894, p. 538), described p. 538 and depicted in Fig. 196. The third shaft-joint of the right second antenna bears, on its abnormally broadened end, beside the normal flagellum bent off toward the right, in the frontal plane two further flagella grown together at the base, of which the one standing nearest to the normal flagellum possesses the symmetry of a simple normal left antenna. As in our No. 13, the flagella were somewhat twisted out of one plane, which may be ascribed to the direction of the force producing the abnormality. The depiction given by Léger of the abnormality from the rear face leaves no doubt that the inverse flagellum owes its origin to the proximal break-surface of a flagellum-joint-piece that has been broken off, the distal end of which grows out into the third flagellum.

No. 13b. *Astacus fluviatilis*, after Stamati (1888 with Fig.), cited by Bateson (1894, p. 538, No. 829, with Fig. 197): «Exopodite of the left antenna bears two supernumerary tips, R′ and L′, which apparently were inserted on the inner margin of the normal exopodite.» According to the figure, the right-angled bending-off of the normal tip and the growth of one new tip out of each face of the gaping wound, all remaining in the frontal plane, is almost palpably distinct.

No. 13c. *Palinurus vulgaris*, after Léger (1886 with Plate VI), investigated anew by Bateson (1894, p. 526, No. 808, with Fig. 180): «Left penultimate walking-leg bore two supernumerary legs. Coxopodite of great breadth. The basipodite had three articular surfaces, as seen in Fig. 180, each bearing a complete leg.» The middle leg had the symmetry of a right leg. As Bateson himself states, the normal leg must in any case have been pushed out of its position, for only the middle one, with its reversed symmetry, stands in the straight continuation of the basipodite and coxopodite. In my opinion the leg designated by Bateson as L′ has been broken at a right angle in the basipodite, and has regenerated from its proximal break-surface the leg R′, and from the distal break-surface the leg that Bateson questioningly designated as the left normal leg.

On account of the equal size of all three components, it is also possible that a large piece of the normal leg had, besides, been torn off entirely and had also been re-formed by the regenerative path.

No. 13d. *Lithodes arctica*, Herklots (1852 and 1870 with Fig.) cited in Bateson (1894, p. 527, as No. 809 with Fig. 181). The second walking-leg of the right side bore, on the broadened end of the propodite, three dactylopodites, of which the middle one exhibited the symmetry of the left side. All three propodites were shorter than normal, and with regard to their position, what was said above concerning No. 13c applies.

No. 13e. *Cancer pagurus*, Richard (1893, 102, Fig. 3; cited by Bateson, 1894, p. 527, as No. 811, II). The last left leg, similar to the previous case, yet all three components are of almost normal length. Inwardly from the normal dactylopodite, and grown together with it up to half its length, lies the inverse component, to which in turn the third component attaches itself with a similar fusion. I hold it for questionable whether this latter does not rather represent the original dactylopodite.

No. 13f. *Cancer pagurus*, Richard (1893, 105, Fig. 4). Walking-leg — side and body-segment not given — bears a strongly broadened and shortened member in place of the dactylopodite. It ends in five claws, which stand out by their gleaming black colour. One of these is, in the view from above chosen for the figure, not visible, since it is concealed by the one appearing furthest to the left; Richard thinks that the other structures still visible, which however do not bear black claws, are to be regarded as rudimentary dactylus-ends. I rather believe that they are scars which stand in relation to the formation of the fivefold-formation. Without having the specimen itself in hand, however, it can only be surmised that it is a matter of two pairs of supernumerary dactylopodite-tips, which owe their origin to two gaping wounds.

No. 13g. *Carcinus maenas*, Duns (1888; cited by Bateson, 1894, p. 527, as No. 811 with Fig. 181, III). According to the figure, the second left leg, with strongly in-bent dactylopodite, out of whose base grow two accessory smaller dactylopodites soldered together but ending freely. All three tips lie in one plane. Notches make the origin from a right-angled gaping wound very probable.

### Regenerative potencies of the non-claw-bearing appendages of the Crustacea.

If we designate as the longitudinal axis of a body-appendage that direction in which the single members succeed one another, beginning from the branching-off of the appendage at the trunk, then all statements for the Crustacea agree in this, that upon amputation at any place, members lying proximal to it are never re-formed, but always those standing distal (Lit. Prz., Exp. Zool. II.). Nevertheless it is not yet thereby determined what grows again, since also distal parts of body-appendages can come to appearance which normally do not belong to the body-segment in question, but to another of the same animal-body. This phenomenon, named by Bateson (1894) Homoeosis, which he had conceived as a homoeotic variation, I have, in another place, according to its rules and causes, discussed (Przibram, 1907, further 1910, 1917 I, 1917 II, 1919), and I need not return to it here, since hitherto a complication of this homoeosis with triple-formation has not become known.

If we designate as the transverse axis of a body-appendage that direction which lies perpendicular to the longitudinal axis in that plane which separates the upper from the under side of the appendage (and of the trunk), then the plane laid through the longitudinal axis perpendicular to the transverse axis separates a left from a right half of the appendage. Carried through as a plane of cleavage, each of these halves is able to complete the other again, as an experiment on the left great swimming-antenna [oar-antenna] of *Daphnia pulex* (Przibram, 1899, p. 8, Plate 31, Fig. 15a–2), on the eye of *Palaemon serratus* (ibid., Plate III, p. 20, Fig. 35a–d), on the propodite of the 5th leg of *Carcinus maenas* (1902, 509, Plate XXI, Fig. 5) has shown me, and as Reed (1904, p. 315) reports for the leg of the hermit-crab. Concerning a separation of the ventral from the dorsal part of a claw-less appendage there are, to my knowledge, no statements.

In the claw-less appendages it is not easy, on detached pieces, to decide whether they belonged to the left or the right side of the body, because upper and under side are for the most part little different from one another. A thorough consideration of normal and supernumerary appendages, however, always furnishes points of reference which permit inverse components to be recognized as such. There is no position in which such inverse components could be brought into coincidence with their normal mirror-image. Yet here we still gain no point of reference with regard to the question whether it is a matter of an anlage [primordium] of the opposite side or of a reversal of the same side.

### b) Equal-clawed [gleichscherige] appendages on each side of the body of the Crustacea.

#### α) Chelipede.

No. 14. *Homarus americanus*, lobster with regeneration of triple claw-joints on a walking-leg, described and depicted in Figs. 10–18 by V. E. Emmel (1907).

From a female lobster 215 mm in length, both claws [chelae] and the second as well as the third right walking-leg had been removed by autotomy. On the 35th day after the amputation, there appeared on the regenerating second walking-leg a bud branching off ventrally at a right angle from the carpopodite and bending backward, which exhibited the indication of all the distal parts of an accessory leg (Emmel, Fig. 10), which later, on the 43rd day, was distinctly articulated and bore at the tip notches as if toward a doubling (Emmel, Figs. 11, 12). On the 48th day, through grazing of the accessory structure against the aquarium-wall, the same was so injured that it was now directed forward, parallel to the normal end of the walking-leg (Emmel, Figs. 13, 14). The wound that had arisen, however, healed, and after the next moult, 65 days after the amputation, the accessory structure showed itself distinctly as two walking-legs grown together along the margin of the dactylopodites and of the part following proximal to them.

At the distal end of the meropodite the joint-socket was divided into two somewhat unequal portions; indications of doubling and scars were perceptible down to the ischiopodite.

The peculiarity of this case lies in the fact that the three components do not even approximately lie in one plane, and that although the two accessory feet are arranged symmetrically to one another, their position relative to the normal foot-end is not mirror-image-related. Emmel designates one of the accessory feet as the left one, namely the one which in his Fig. 15 comes to lie below. If one regards, in his Fig. 16, the supernumerary claws from below, then the left-symmetrical one lies to the right; seen from above it would therefore lie to the left of the right-symmetrical one. Such symmetry relations occur in the longitudinal splitting of a limb (cf. further below on the potencies of the chela-bearing appendages in crustaceans).

My conjecture now goes to the effect that in this case it is not a matter of a typical triple-formation at a break, but rather of the result of a slit arising longitudinally on the ventral side of the leg through abrasion against the ground, a slit which had extended through several segments. This is indicated by the traces of injury on the ischiopodite, among them a scar running longitudinally on the right side (Emmel, Fig. 15g), the doubling of the distal joint-socket on the meropodite, the widening of the carpopodite through the ventral bulge, upon which then the fused pincers are seated, each of which will have sprung from one of the two slit-margins.

No. 14a. *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 261, Pl. II, Fig. 2; Bateson, 1894, 529, No. 814, Fig. 182 III). "This cheliped is provided with two chelae: one of them has the ordinary form and structure, but it is bent off at a large angle from the long axis of the leg. The second chela seems to have sprouted from an amputated surface of the propodite. It consists of two fingers, which have the form of the normal dactylus and index, but neither is articulated to the other at the base. The two fingers seem morphologically to be equivalent to a single segment, and represent a two-branched supernumerary dactylus." Bateson seeks, further, in a small connecting cap not described by Faxon but demarcated on the figure, the rudiment of a double supernumerary index; I do not entirely reject this interpretation — it would correspond to a triple-formation at a break at the flexure-point still recognizable in front of the articulation of the dactylopodite and the branching-off of the index on the propodite (cf. quite analogously the later-to-be-discussed case of Maggi at the chela of the crayfish).

No. 14b. *H. americanus* (Herrick, 1895, 147, Pl. 47, Fig. 195, from below, ²⁄₃ nat. size). The second or third pereiopod bears, in place of a dactylus, three, of which two arise together from a common joint, the third independently. Since Herrick ascribes to this case "exactly the same relations" as prevail at the chela to be described later under No. 93, it is to be regarded as a typical triple-formation at a break.

No. 15. *Cambarus Bartoni*, described and figured by E. Andrews (1904, p. 75, Figs. 1–2), bore on the third left walking-leg a "second claw-like end." Judging by the figure, there was situated internally¹) the

> ¹) In the designation of the sides of a crustacean limb, namely of the chela-bearing ones, great confusion prevails. Sometimes the surfaces in which the chela-movement is enclosed are designated as anterior and posterior (as in Andrews), sometimes as upper (dorsal) and lower (ventral; as in Herrick); in this way the dactylopodite is sometimes assigned a dorsal or higher (superior, for crabs — Bateson) position, sometimes an internal (internal, for the lobster — Bateson) position.

articulation-site of the dactylopodite on the widened propodite, a second articulation-site, which gives origin to a pair of dactylopodites fused with one another at the base. These accessory dactylopodites lie in one plane with the normal dactylopodite and with the normal propodite-finger of the propodite lying opposite to it. The accessory formation standing nearer to the normal dactylopodite is a mirror-image to that one and to the second accessory dactylopodite, for it bears the toothing of the cutting-edge — just as the normal right walking-leg seen from the lower surface (G-I, Fig. 2) — on the left, whereas the normal one and the outermost accessory dactylopodite bear the toothing on the right. Andrews is therefore in error when he writes: "Rather it will be seen from the above Fig. 2 that the chela-finger nearer the dactylus is not a mirror-image of this dactylus, but that it represents an index, and likewise the other prong is not a mirror-image of the index, but represents the dactylus; this is correct, since all their little teeth lie nearer the posterior surface than the anterior. There is in this way a deviation from Bateson's symmetry-rule ....." We shall more often encounter this confusion of the double dactylus with a whole chela, namely with the dactylus and the propodus-finger (index) lying opposite. In the present case the strong curvature of the mirror-image accessory formation already speaks against an index; furthermore, there is no joint present between the accessory formations. We shall be able to derive the origin from an injury, of which Andrews himself found a scar on the propodite, namely in front of its widening. His experiments on over 100 Cambarus to produce the malformation by injury failed because of the healing-over of the wounds or because of the death of the crayfish. His mode of operation, moreover, will probably not have corresponded to the natural find. I picture to myself that an enemy holding the walking-leg fast at the site of the still-visible propodite-scar had torn out a piece from the joint between the dactylopodite and propodite in a distal-internal direction, so that at the site of the joint a wound with two gaping wound-margins arose, each of which now produced a new dactylopodite. The one arising from the proximally-directed wound-surface shows the inversion already known to us and met with its inner cutting-edge the counter-growing inner cutting-edge of the dactylus regenerating from the distal surface, so that fusion at the base and pushing-together of the teeth on both sides necessarily took place, as Andrews also describes. It should be added that the inverse dactylus lacks the terminal hook, so that, like inverse formations, it often has a lesser development than the correctly symmetrical ones.

## β) Chelae (Homoiochelae).

No. 16. *Astacus fluviatilis* (Cantoni, 1883, 771, Fig.; Bateson, 1894, 537, No. 827, Fig. 195). Large ♀; from the carpopodite of the left chela there grows externally an outgrowth with a pair of symmetrical small chelae, the symmetry of the one nearer the normal chela being inverse; further, from the angle of attachment of the outgrowth on the carpopodite a still smaller third supernumerary chela, whose symmetry is doubtful. I picture to myself that, similarly as in the triple-formation at a break of *Antedon*, No. 12, a flexure took place, whereby the flexed-off piece, injured at both ends, remained still hanging by soft parts. The injured carpopodite then regenerated its distal part together with a normal chela, and the hanging piece a smaller, but normally symmetrical chela at the distal break-surface, and an inverse chela at the proximal break-surface. Finally, from the part lying in the long axis of the carpopodite of the tearing-off wound there also came about a small chela, because the tearing-off surface may have run only at the dorsal-distal angle of the carpopodite transversely, but later obliquely toward the ventral-proximal angle.

No. 17. *Astacus fluviatilis* (Maggi, 1881, 333, Fig.; Bateson, 1894, 529, No. 813, Fig. 182 IV). About three years old, right chela irregular, probably injured. Left chela bears, internal to the articulation-site of the dactylopodite on the propodite, an outgrowth with two accessory mirror-image chelae, whose propodite-prongs are fused with one another lengthwise, whose dactylopodites move freely each on a joint in the same plane as the normal dactylus. A wholly typical triple-formation at a break, the middle chela with inverse symmetry having arisen from the proximally-facing, the second accessory one from the distally-facing break-surface of a gaping transverse wound of the propodite.

No. 17a. *Astacus fluviatilis* (Roesel von Rosenhof, 1755, 345, Pl. LX, Fig. 28). From the external propodite-margin there rises, opposite the normal dactylopodite-articulation, a formation that bears two indexes and one joint-surface (for a dactylus formed from two fused ones?). The description as a "crown-shaped excrescence" permits no further interpretation.

No. 18. *Astacus fluviatilis* (Roesel v. R., 1755, 345, Pl. LXI, Fig. 32). From the outer margin of the propodite-index, whose tip appears bent off toward the dactylus, there rises a pair of supernumerary index-tips fused at the base, which turn their cutting-edges toward one another. Typical triple-formation at a break.

Roesel himself remarks, concerning this and similar cases still to be cited, p. 344: "Now there is still a particular property remaining belonging to the crayfish, which — so long as we knew nothing of the polyps discovered by Herr Trembley — was, among all animals, almost uniquely peculiar to it, and this consists in this, that when the same loses one of its feet, or also a chela, the place of the lost one is replaced by a new foot or by a new chela .... This very impulse, which is found in crayfish, and which has a resemblance to the impulse of growing things, also brings it about that we sometimes perceive, both on the nose of the crayfish and also on their chelae, a particular outgrowth, which gives them a quite monstrous appearance. For when a crayfish that still has a soft shell is pinched or injured by another whose shell is harder, or when the same otherwise suffers harm: then the flesh of the crayfish at once pushes out through the injured spot, and from this then arise the chelae, in form quite shapeless and furnished with several points, which even with the repeated molting of the crayfish suffer no alteration, and of which I have not only myself collected some, but have also received some from a distinguished patron for drawing........"

No. 18a. *A. fluviatilis* [Pl. XVIII, Fig. II], a photograph sent to me by Carlgren, clearly shows the initial stage of a double-broad regeneration from a flexure-wound at the outer index-margin of a right chela. The outgrowth bears nowhere any cutting-edge development.

No. 18b. *A. fluviatilis* (Roesel, 1755, Pl. LXI, Fig. 31) and No. 18c. *A. fluviatilis* (Roesel, 1755, Pl. LXI, Fig. 30), further, No. 18d. *A. fluviatilis* (Tiedemann, 1819, 127, Fig. 2; Bateson, 1894, 536, No. 825),

No. 18e. *A. fluviatilis* (Lucas, 1844, 41, Fig. 3; Bateson, 1894, 536, Fig. 193 III),

No. 18f. *A. fluviatilis* (Jäger, 1851, Pl. I, Fig. 7; Bateson, 1894, 536) and

No. 18g. *A. fluviatilis* (Eßer, 1863, several cases; once on both sides) are probably all analogous to No. 18a; only the distance of the outgrowths from the tip of the original index varies.

No. 19. *Astacus torrentium*¹) (own sketch from a male specimen purchased on the Vienna market through the Court Museum). The right chela has the index bent outward, and at the cutting-edge of the bending-site there springs a projection clearly consisting of two indexes fused at their outer margins. Apart from its almost doubled breadth as against the original index, this makes itself known through the bilateral toothing of the cutting-edges, the two terminal spines, and the bilateral symmetry, which has arisen through the fusion of the inverse with a correctly-standing component.

No. 19a. *A. fluviatilis* [Pl. XVIII, Fig. III], likewise photographed by Carlgren, shows an analogous initial stage as No. 18a, but at the cutting-edge margin of the index. One should note that the cutting-edges run on both sides of the outgrowth, which can therefore be thought of as composed of two indexes fused at the outer margins; whereas in the preceding case the same holds for the cutting-edge margins.

No. 20. *Astacus fluviatilis* [Pl. XVIII, Fig. IV], male crayfish from the old collection of the Natural History Court Museum in Vienna, most kindly placed at my disposal by the curator Dr. O. Pesta and photographed by Herr Dr. Rechinger, to both of whom I herewith thank for their support, shows the dactylopodite of the right chela swollen just behind its articulation and then bent off under the propodite. From the bending-angle there springs a formation that at first glance seems to be a small whole chela, but exhibits no joints. On closer inspection the two prongs of this supposed chela prove to be two mirror-image dactylopodites, for otherwise at least one would have to exhibit the tooth standing on the propodite in front of the tip at the cutting-edge, which is not the case. The symmetry is quite that of a typical triple-formation at a break. The case is of particular interest because the swelling of the break-site simulates a hand and shows how other researchers could be misled in similar cases, so that they wished to see a whole chela in the mirror-image cutting-edges.

No. 20a. *A. fluviatilis* (Roesel, 1755, Pl. LX, Fig. 29): the dactylopodite of the right chela, somewhat bent off against the index-cutting-edge, bears at the free margin two supernumerary ends turned toward one another with their cutting-edges. Typical triple-formation at a break.

No. 20b. *A. fluviatilis* (Eßer, 1863, 180), quite the same case.

No. 20c. *A. fluviatilis* (Jaeger, 1851, Pl. I, Fig. 3), quite analogous to the previous case, only the accessory formations spring nearer to the articulation-site of the dactylus.

No. 20d. *A. fluviatilis* (Jaeger, 1851, Pl. I, Fig. 2), just as No. 19, but the accessory formations are fused with one another almost to the tip.

No. 20e. *A. fluviatilis* (Jaeger, 1851, Pl. I, Fig. 1), similar to the previous cases, but the double nature of the supernumerary formations doubtful.

> ¹) It is very doubtful whether the cases cited as *A. fluviatilis* relate precisely to this species; therefore I have not held a separate treatment of this case to be advisable.

No. 21. *A. fluviatilis* (Roesel, 1755, Pl. LXI, Fig. 33), the right chela bears on the dactylopodite, right at its articulation on the free margin, two prongs standing side by side and bent away from the direction of the dactylus-tip, whose free margin exhibits no cutting-edge toothing. Can be regarded, in analogy with cases discussed earlier, as a slit-triple-formation.

No. 22. *Astacus leptodactylus* (Károlyi, 1877, 53, Fig.; Bateson, 1894, 532, No. 819, Fig. 187 II), left chela bears, on the dactylopodite twisted out of its normal position, two supernumerary ends, the middle of which exhibits the symmetry of the opposite side. According to my conception, the dactylopodite had broken somewhat above its articulation toward the right and had regenerated from the gaping wound-margins each a new tip.

No. 23. *Platycarcinus* (= *Cancer*) *pagurus*. Borradaile (1897, Pl. VIII) describes a female crab located in the Museum of the Jersey Marine Biological Station, whose carapax measures 8 × 12.5 cm: "The peculiarity lies in the fact that the large chela on the right side is lacking, and is replaced by three small limbs resembling a normal chela except in subordinate points which shall now be described. The other limbs are normal, but the second walking-leg of the right side and the movable 'finger' of the left chela are broken off. Of the three abnormal appendages, two are directed forward and the third outward. Of the forward-directed ones, again the one can be distinguished as the outer from the other inner one .... So the three have the positions described by Bateson, which he has found in such cases of supernumerary limbs or parts of limbs in secondary symmetry — that is to say, each two abutting limbs stand to one another in the relation that an object has to its image in the mirror." Whereas two of the small chelae have basipodites, which stand in connection with one another through a calcareous bridge, the most outwardly-standing chela is said to lack the basipodite altogether; the connection with the coxopodite — which is, moreover, common to all three chelae, ring-shaped, and lacking the outer joint-process — consists in all of them of a membranous membrane. Borradaile points further to the fact that the autotomy-site lies at the suture between basipodite and ischiopodite, and remarks finally: "One assumption which there is a natural tendency to make with regard to this specimen is that the right chela broke off and was abnormally replaced by three. The small size of the abnormal limbs supports this somewhat. On the other hand one would, if this were the case, expect to find that the three limbs had not only the coxopodite but also the basipodite in common, and there is, furthermore, no reason why the coxopodite would not be like that of the opposite side, for there is no trace of an injury of the same present. We have therefore probably to do with a true variation ....." As against this statement, I do nevertheless hold fast to the view that it is a matter of a typical triple-formation at a break on a regenerate. The absence of the outer process on the coxopodite speaks for an injury of the same; evidently Borradaile again does not think of the often-proven possibility of regeneration proximal to the autotomy-site. The regenerate sprouting from the coxa will now have suffered a break, which bent the basipodite back from the coxa, so that the limb remained only in membranous connection with the coxa. A remnant of the basipodite is, moreover, clearly recognizable in the figure at the outermost-standing small chela. From the proximally-facing break-surface of the torn-off limb there now sprouted the middle inverse, from the distally-facing one of the coxa a second normally-directed, but somewhat inwardly-deflected chela.

No. 24. *P. pagurus* (Le Sénéchal, 1888, Fig. 1; Bateson, 1894, 530, No. 816, without Fig.). Right chela bears at the inner margin of the propodite two index-tips standing side by side and grown with their outer margins onto the outer margin of the normal index, toward which two supernumerary dactylopodites, fastened articulately on a bulge of the propodite outer-margin, turn their cutting-edges. Probably arisen from a tear running along the outer margin of the propodite. The three dactyli do not lie in one plane, just as little do the indexes; rather, each group forms an isosceles triangle, whose third, unequal, shorter side connects the supernumerary formations, so that the height of the triangle runs from the inner to the outer margin of the chela and stands perpendicular to the tear-line.

No. 25. *P. pagurus* (Bateson, 1890, 581, Fig. 2C; 1894, 530, No. 815, Fig. 183). Right chela bears, at the base of the leftward-bent index-cutting-edge, a process toothed symmetrically on both sides, which forms with the normal index a typical triple-formation at a break. Next to the dactylopodite, which is bent off toward the right, there is inserted quite at the rounded propodite-end on an enlarged joint a double dactylopodite, which with the simple one likewise represents a typical triple-formation at a break — position in one plane, mirror-image symmetry of the components. In consequence of the opposite-sense divergence of the original dactylus and index, it is not the simple prongs that come to stand opposite one another, but the double ones place themselves crosswise opposite the simple ones. Bateson wonders greatly at this; but if we proceed from our hypothesis of origin through a break, then the case will find its resolution easily through an accident, which drove the two prongs of the chela apart and broke them off at their base in opposite directions. Through this single accident two groups of break-surfaces arose, which then, independently of one another, had to bring forth two supernumerary formations fusing with one another. The crosswise position is then a natural consequence of the opposite-direction breaking-apart of the normal prongs; because the break-surface grows partly onto the still-standing remnants of the normal extremity.

No. 26. *P. pagurus* (Pocock, 1898, 436, Fig.), right chela of a half-grown crab bears, to the right next to the articulation of the normal, non-bent-off dactylus, two dactylopodites separately articulated, making out with it a typical triple-formation. The index, which exhibits evident mutilation and is turned outward, bears at its base a process notched, according to the figure, only on its inner margin, which comes to stand opposite the normal dactylus. The case is therefore, with the exception of the not-as-double-recognizable shape of this process and the separate articulation of the supernumerary dactyli, which stand not to the left but to the right of the normal one, similar to the previous one and finds an analogous explanation of its origin, whereby the normal dactylus remained unharmed and a supernumerary index did not come to development.

No. 27. *P. pagurus* (Bateson, 1894, 535, Fig. 191, Coll. Surg. Mus.). Right chela bears externally from the branching-off of the index on the propodus a pair of mirror-image indices.

[No. 27a. *P. pagurus* (Le Sénéchal, 1888, Bateson, 1894, 535, Fig. 191 II). Left chela bears at almost the same site, only somewhat more toward the tip, analogous accessory formations; it is erroneously cited by Bateson, belongs to *Portunus puber*, see there, No. 121.] No. 28. *P. pagurus*, sketch from a specimen in the British Museum, London, from Plymouth 1906; index of the right chela bears a typical triple-formation at a break of the dactylus, which was probably bent outward.

No. 28a. *P. pagurus*, likewise in the British Museum. Index of the left chela bears at the outer margin two unequally-large supernumerary tips, which lie in one plane with the normal index, but allow no toothing of the cutting-edges to be recognized. It is probably a matter of a preliminary stage of the development of the previous case.

No. 28b. *P. pagurus* (Le Sénéchal, 1888, Fig. 3). Right chela bears at the outer margin of the index a long, but cutting-edge-less process, and

No. 28c. *P. pagurus* (Bateson, 1894, 536, Fig. 193 I; College of Surgeons, London), right chela with a short process near the tip of the index at the outer margin, represent perhaps still less far-advanced stages.

No. 29. *P. pagurus* (own sketch from below, Bateson, 1890, 581; 1894, 531, Fig. 185), a female crab presently located in the collection of the College of Surgeons in London (Cat. No. 378) bears at the free margin of the dactylopodite of the right chela two typically mirror-image accessory ends with good development of the cutting-edges turned toward one another. Very clearly there is the bending-off of the longer normal tip and the counter-directed course of the punctation on the adjoining accessory formation with the symmetry of the opposite side, as is evident from my exact reproduction of the specimen.

No. 29a and No. 29b, two quite similar cases, but apparently without such clear bending-off, are located, according to a communication from Professor Cori, in the Heligoland North Sea Museum.

No. 30. *P. pagurus* (Duns, 1888, Fig.). Right chela likewise bears two mirror-image, but for a stretch fused, supernumerary dactylus-tips at the free margin of the dactylopodite.

No. 31. *P. pagurus* (Bateson, 1890, 581, Fig.; 1894, 531, Fig. 185). Right chela bears three dactylus-tips lacking cutting-edge toothing, of which the one standing directly opposite the index is pointed and slender, the two others, fused with one another almost up to their blunt ends, form a longer, broad continuation at the free dactylus-margin. It is questionable whether here the original dactylus was broken toward the index or away from the same, that is, whether the accessory formations are the two nearer the index or the two more distant from it.

No. 31a. *P. pagurus* (Bateson, 1894, Fig. 193 II), right dactylopodite bears at the free margin a short process notch-less on both sides, probably the beginning of a formation similar to No. 31.

No. 32. *P. pagurus* (Fischer, 1888, Fig.). Left chela is said, according to the author, to bear on the dactylopodite yet a whole supplementary chela. The figure clearly shows that it is a matter of three dactylus-tips in typical triple-formation at a break. The strong development of the branch at the free margin, as well as the clearer punctation, leads one to conclude that at the break of the dactylus it was bent away from the index and the accessory formations have grown from its cutting-edge margin.

No. 33. *P. pagurus* (Bateson, 1894, 533, No. 820, Fig. 187 I; Coll. Surg. Mus.). Right chela bears, to the right in the middle of the cutting-edge margin, a pair of mirror-image dactyli, whose cutting-edges appear rotated by 90° upward as against the normal cutting-edges. The cause is probably the downward sinking of the heavier prong-blade, which took place after the almost complete tearing-off of the terminal half of the dactylopodite.

No. 34. *P. pagurus* (Patterson, 1897, Fig.), a dactylopodite, which — to which chela it belongs is not stated — bears, on one of the broader surfaces (whether on the volar or on the "contra-volar" surface is not to be gathered from the drawing), two processes which could be a supernumerary pair of dactyli, which would have sprung from a wound of the evidently hook-shapedly bent-off normal dactylus. The indistinctness of the formations forbids more exact statements. Rightly the author points to the fact that the case does not agree with any of the cases described by Bateson. Yet the validity of the symmetry-rules for it would still be possible, in case the original dactylus underwent a deviation not only in the volar surface but also, in particular, perpendicular to it, which unfortunately cannot be recognized with certainty from the figure.

**Nr. 35.** *Maja squinado* Latr., in the Museum of the B. V. A., Cat. No. 294, obtained through Cori from Trieste; ♀ of 14 cm carapace length measured from the depression between the two rostral spines to the posterior suture. The left propodite is swollen toward the index, bears wound-traces there, and at the inner margin a straight, bluntly ending process 3½ cm long, furnished on both sides with the character of the free shear-margin, while the normal index measures 4 cm. Just before the sending-out of this process — which must be conceived as a double index fused along the cutting edges — there is also a small tubercle.

**Nr. 35a.** *M. squinado*, in the Museum of the B. V. A., likewise sent from Trieste by Cori, ♀ entirely similar in dimensions and malformation to the previous one, only the long process is thinner and more pointed and the small tubercle is lacking.

**Nr. 36.** *M. squinado*, in the Museum of the B. V. A., Cat. No. 295, from Cori, Trieste, ♂, similar dimensions to the previous one. The right propodite is swollen toward the index; the latter itself is clearly recognizable as a still-short regenerate of 1 cm without differentiation. The dactylopodite, lengthened by a good 1 cm beyond the 5 cm long dactylopodite of the opposite side, is bent toward the free margin in its last third and bears, opposite the bending-site, on the cutting edge a triangular process beset on both sides with cutting-teeth, which must be regarded as a double dactylus fused with the joint-margins [Klinkenränder].

## c) Appendages with unequal shears on the two body-sides in Crustacea.

### γ) Chelae (Heterochelae, Poterochirae).

**Nr. 37.** *Homarus americanus* (Emmel, 1907, 108, Pl. II and Diagram B).

"Living lobster, caught at Cape Newagen, Maine. The left shear of this lobster was a normal pinching-shear [Zwickschere], but on the right shear there were, instead of one, three separate shears." …

"Ischiopodite. This abnormally large and twisted segment is not easy to describe; in general, however, it can be compared with a three-sided prism. One edge of the prism will then lie ventral, and the opposite prism-face will correspond to the dorso-lateral face of the segment. The dorso-lateral segment-face is also marked by a triangular scar or notch, which may possibly be causally connected with the origin of the more distal abnormalities."

"The meropodite broadens distally and finally ends in two prongs or branches. The furrow between these fork-branches stands at a small angle to the dorso-ventral plane of the normal limb."

"A consideration of the surface structure shows that the fork-branch m [i.e. the one bearing the simple carpopodites — Ref.] runs morphologically uninterrupted from the distal ending through the ischiopodite to the basal segment or basipodite, where it apparently forms the whole joint with the following segment." "The remaining distal segments … show the usual characters, and the shear opens in a horizontal plane. The shear-tips are slightly bent downward, a condition that may have been occasioned by a crowded position of these parts during the hardening after the molt. At first glance it is not quite clear whether this shear is a 'pinching-shear' or a 'snapping-shear' [Knackschere]; in its general shape it is rather elongate and slender, like a little-toothed shear [Zähnchenschere]; but on the other hand the tactile hairs are almost wholly absent, and the toothing is distinguished by the presence of 'snap'-teeth [Knackzähne]. This shear is therefore of the snapping-shear type, although in many respects it may be regarded as a transition from the little-toothed to the snapping-shear." "A phenomenon not unusual in the lobster, especially in the case of regenerated shears." "Returning to the abnormal part of the meropodite, it is evidently clear that the next segment, or the carpopodite … is morphologically double. It is very massive and more than twice as large as the corresponding normal segment; a longitudinally running furrow divides it into two equal sections; the outer face of each of these sections is beset with a group of spines, and distally each half forms a true joint with the corresponding propodites. Each half of this segment, with its joint and its spination, is therefore homologous with the normal carpopodite. This double character of the abnormal carpopodite can also be traced back into the meropodite."

"The morphological character of the two supernumerary shears can be expressed in a single word, since these shears are almost exact mirror-images of one another." "It is clear at first glance that these two shears are both of the 'snapping-shear' type. In size the two supernumerary shears are somewhat larger than the third … shear. They do not lie in exactly parallel planes, but are inclined toward one another at a small angle, and indeed in such a way that the dactyli stand nearer and the indices farther apart from one another. The common plane of movement for these two shears also stands at a right angle to that of the normal shear. These spatial relations of the shears, together with the fact that the double supernumerary carpopodite also lies at a right angle to the corresponding normal segment, are points of considerable theoretical interest … Another point of interest is the colour-relations of the most leftward-standing shear. This shear has been so completely reversed in its position during its formation that its normally lower side now lies uppermost, and vice versa."

"The lobster … was a male and measured about 10 inches [= 25.4 cm, Ref.] in length." Emmel's attempt to obtain a regeneration after autotomy of the threefold shear failed because of the early death of the specimen (various experiments with incisions on other lobsters also led only to the shedding-off or to the unaltered healing and regeneration of the simple limb). Emmel continues, p. 139: "This specimen showed certain peculiar deviations from Bateson's rules, but we will first consider the points in which agreement prevails. The pair of supernumerary shears are mirror-images of one another, and the shear nearest the normal shear is also the mirror-image of the latter and belongs to the opposite body-side, i.e. it is a left shear … But a closer consideration reveals a considerable deviation … It is a well-known fact that, both in ontogeny (Herrick, 1905) and in regeneration (Emmel, 1906) of the shear-bearing legs, 'the endsegments gradually rotate downward and inward, so that a rotation of approximately 90 degrees is brought about and the shear now opens inward in the plane characterized in the natural way as horizontal'. This means, therefore, that in our present specimen the upper side of the normal shear, as it appears in the drawing, ought to be its morphological hinder side, and that is indeed actually the case. But now it strangely seems that each component of the pair of supernumerary shears has, for its part, rotated through a further 90 degrees during its development … In a word, we have here the remarkable circumstance that, while the normal shear has turned in the usual way through an angle of 90 degrees, each of the supernumerary ones has, on the other hand, undergone a rotation of 180 degrees." Emmel now seems to mean that a first torsion of 90° took place with the position of the supernumerary limbs on the torsioned normal shear, but that this was followed by a second torsion of the later-developing supernumerary shears through a further 90°, because torsion also occurs in regeneration. As Cole has already remarked (1910, 264, note 1), this furnishes no explanation. I should like to point out more particularly that the origin of the triplication reaches deep into the meropodite, which itself undergoes no torsion of 90°, neither in ontogeny nor in regeneration, so that a participation of the accessory formations in the torsion of the normal shear appears unwarranted. They ought therefore to rotate only once through 90° and thereby, just like the normal shear, come to lie spread out side by side in the horizontal plane, as Bateson's rules require. A plausible interpretation of the case is first furnished by the biotechnical analysis. If we consider the three shears with respect to size and development of the K-character, then the most leftward-standing one shows itself to be the largest and best-developed K-shear; then comes the middle one, only slightly smaller, with a somewhat less distinct K-type, especially at the dactylus cutting-edge; finally the most rightward-standing one, which is much smaller than the others and still distinctly shows the transition from Z to K. This one, interpreted by Emmel as the normal shear, is so indeed according to its position, but certainly not according to its originality. Rather I must perceive in it a typical regenerate. Its similarity to a regenerating K-shear has indeed also struck Emmel in the words cited above. As a regenerate still in the course of formation it is, however, evidently the youngest of the three shears; conversely I conceive the largest, completely developed shear as the oldest shear. If we keep this in mind, and further keep before our eyes our consistently applied principles concerning the origin of inverse formations, then the following picture of the origin of our threefold shear can be sketched: a force holding the ischiopodite fast, whose impress is still visible at the triangular scar, prevented the K-shear then standing in the place of the shear designated by Emmel as normal from escaping a second force, which turned it up and forward, called forth a tear through the meropodite, so that, after the constraint was released, the injured shear — still hanging together by the base — fell over not to the right but to the left and thus came to lie upon the surface that normally faces upward. This original K-shear is our largest, most leftward-standing shear. On the meropodite it exhibited a proximally, rightward-upward directed break-surface, which brought forth a mirror-image shear, our middle shear. Finally, there also regenerated, from the remnant of the original meropodite that had remained standing in the old position, a correctly oriented shear, namely the one designated by Emmel as the normal, most rightward-standing shear. It is now clear that we obtain a complete agreement with Bateson's rules as soon as we think the rotation through 90° caused by the injury turned back again.

**Nr. 38.** *H. americanus* (Cole, 1910, 252, Figs. 1–4). Caught in the harbor of Gloucester, Mass., there preserved in the United States Fish Hatchery. The right shear was lost at the time of capture — to judge from the appearance of the autotomy-site and the absence of a regenerate, only recently shed. The left K-shear bore, beginning from the ischiopodite, the character of a triplication, which, at first connected with an almost complete fusion of the three components, leads to the development of three distinctly separable shears. "The somewhat larger, innermost-lying component, rightly addressed by Cole as the normal shear, is displaced from the normal position only slightly, and indeed in such a way as if it had been somewhat hindered in its normal rotation by the supernumerary shear-pair adjoining at the lower half of its outward face. The supernumerary shear-pair is fused with the free margins of the dactylopodites and along the whole adjoining propodus-parts, whereby likewise the complete rotation of these shears into their horizontal position appears to be prevented. For the rest, the symmetry-relations are those of a typical break-triplication." As is to be expected, "the teeth on the supernumerary shears are distinct, like those of the normal shear, of the 'snap'-type" (Cole, p. 256).
