The rather equal state of development of the three shears permits the inference of an approximately simultaneous origin of the three components; it would presumably be a matter of the break, occurring in the ischiopodite, of a regenerate, whereby — in contrast to the previous case — only an insignificant twisting played a part. When Cole says that a scar noticeable on the carpopodite cannot come into consideration for the genesis of the triplication, because the traces of the triplication are also distinctly demonstrable proximal to the carpopodite, this is no certain argument, since it might be a matter of a trace of that incisive force which had prevented an evasion of the extremity in the face of the breaking force. Yet the position of the scar on the carpopodite-part belonging to a supernumerary propodus is, to be sure, not favorable to such an interpretation. That it is in general a matter of regenerates is probable, because the state of development of the K-shear, for the size of the lobster — a male of 237 mm length from the tip of the rostrum to the end of the telson — had, to judge from the figures, not yet reached the normal degree of development.

**Nr. 39.** *H. americanus* (Emmel, 1907, 107, Pl. I, Fig. 4). The right Z-shear bears at the outer margin of the propodite a pair of supernumerary indices joined almost up to their tips, and the remainder of the broken-off dactyli. A break-triplication similar to the previous one, typical, which, however, took its origin at the propodus itself, as is proved — besides the absence of the triplication at the carpopodite — by the still distinctly visible scarring-furrow between the normal shear and the supernumerary shear standing next to it. All cutting edges with Z-toothing.

**Nr. 40.** *H. americanus* (own sketch after a dry-preserved right shear from the Museum of the Zoological Society Boston, Acc. No. 2825, Mass. Bay? B. H. van Stick, catalogued). The shear shows in its irregular shape distinctly the traces of an accident undergone. The index is bent outward from the propodite, and proximal to the bending-site lies a small circular scar. In the break-angle of the propodite there grows out of each break-face one small index each, with the well-known mirror-image symmetry. But the dactylus too is bent, and indeed out of its joint, and there stands opposite it a pair of supernumerary dactyli, joined almost up to their tips, likewise bent over, which have almost attained the size and quite the development of the normal dactylus. The cutting edges of all the dactyli and propodites show quite typical Z-shear characters. The origin of this malformation is to be traced back to a force which tore the shear apart from within, so that both index and dactylus were bent off and broken at the base. Such a tearing-apart we can imagine in case a shear of another crustacean, gripped by our lobster, opened itself by force, or also when the lobster with such force itself attacked too large a hard object, so that its shear cutting-edges could no longer withstand the pressure.

**Nr. 41.** *H. americanus* (Emmel, 1907, 105, Pl. I, Fig. 4, left shear. ½ nat. size). "This singular specimen is so sadly disfigured and misshapen that one can scarcely recognize it as a shear of a lobster. As is apparent from the figure, the normal dactylopodite and index have been nipped off just at the joint, doubtless as a consequence of some submarine combat. The exposed ends of the stumps are covered with a dark-brown blood-crust. Although not enough characters have remained to determine with full certainty the shape of the original parts, it nevertheless seems clear, to judge from the generally slender form of the stumps and the tufts of hair at the base of the dactylopodite, that the original shear was of the 'Z'-type. That these two stumps represent the normal index and dactylus is concluded not [merely] from the fact that they are larger than the other similar formations on this shear, but also because their morphological surfaces run continuously, according to their structure, through the whole limb. With respect to position, the index and dactylus are thrown horizontally outward at an angle of about 45° from their normal relations to the shear. On the inner or morphologically dorsal side of the second segment [Emmel reckons here from the tip — Ref.] there is a broad expansion of the propodite, so that this segment appears practically double, and is almost twice the usual size. At the periphery of this supernumerary propodite-formation stand two supernumerary segments … They are practically equal in size, and each of these segments forms a true joint with the propodite … The general form and relation of these two supernumerary segments show clearly that they are both dactyli; the character of the toothing, together with scattered tufts of tactile hairs, also brings these two dactyli under the type of the Z-shears. Between these supernumerary dactyli there projects from the propodite a small spur … This supernumerary formation is really morphologically a double index."

"The third segment or carpopodite is unusually broad and massive. The carpopodite is also marked, on its dorsal side, with a transverse furrow or notch (not to be seen in the figure), which looks as if the limb had been pinched, or strongly bent back upon itself, at a time when the shear was still in a more plastic condition." This scar, the outward rotation of the normal shear, and the symmetry of the dactyli agree splendidly with a typical break-triplication. The slight development of the supernumerary indices is to be traced back to the position of the break, which occurred at the inner margin, that is, at the propodus-side at which normally only the dactylus came to development. The indices began to grow at all only because the break still reached into the mid-line of the normal propodus, as the deep scar still shows, so that the region of the normal index too was drawn into sympathy. The following cases make this still clearer.

**Nr. 42.** *H. americanus* (Herrick, 1895, 146, Pl. 48, Fig. 197, ⅔ nat. size). The right Z-shear bears, at the inner margin of the propodite near its origin, a process with an articulated segment. Herrick's description of the same as a simple dactylus is incorrect. From the bilateral basal spine and also from the absence of any toothing it emerges that it is a matter of two supernumerary dactyli fused with their cutting-edges. Likewise the symmetrical spination of the propodite-process shows that it is a matter of a mirror-image doubling of the leftward-lying propodite longitudinal-half, to be designated morphologically as dorsal. Typical break-triplication with fusion of the regenerates coming forth from the break-surfaces. According to the outline, the supernumerary dactyli would, just like the original shear, take on Z-character.

**Nr. 43.** *H. americanus* (Herrick, 1895, 146, Pl. 47, Fig. 192, ⅔ nat. size). Left K-shear, similar to the previous case, yet here the bending-off of the original shear is more distinctly recognizable and the fused dactyli are longer, while the supernumerary propodus-parts are more strongly fused with one another.

**Nr. 44.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 260, Pl. I, Fig. 16, ½ nat. size). Left Z-shear. "From the inner and deeper part of the propodus there rises a process, which is not articulately joined with the main part of the propodus. On its upper face (in the figure turned away from the observer) there is a projecting spine, like those which are developed along the inner margin of the normal propodite. At the distal end of this process there is articulated a long, bent, pointed, toothless segment, which is an incomplete doubling of the dactylus … The secondary dactylus comes almost equal in length to the primary one and … swings in a plane at a right angle to the plane of movement of the normal dactylus." The cause of these movement-relations escaped Faxon for the reason that he had not recognized the double nature of the supernumerary dactylus and of the propodite-addition. There stand, both on the inner and on the outer side of the dactylus-base of the supernumerary formation, the holding-spine normally belonging only to the inner side of the dactylus, which is meant to check an over-large excursion of the dactylus.

**Nr. 45.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 259, Pl. I, Fig. 12, ½ nat. size). Right Z-shear. "The inner margin of this propodite is disfigured by a wound, which has occasioned an outgrowth of a simple, blunt, movably-articulated segment, which evidently represents an abnormal supernumerary dactylus. On the upper side (the figure shows the lower side) [i.e. the side directed downward — Ref.], near the articulation on the propodite, there is the small spine characteristic of the normal dactylus. The abnormal finger moves in a plane at a right angle to the plane of movement of the normal dactylus." Here the same remark holds as for the previous case.

(**Nr. 45a.** *H. americanus*, Faxon, 1881, 259, without fig. Just such a case, but the normal dactylus has been lost. Like the two earlier abnormalities, preserved in the Museum of Comparative Zoology of Harvard College, Cambridge, Mass.)

**Nr. 46.** *H. americanus* (Herrick, 1895, 145, Pl. 46, Fig. 187 from below, Fig. 188 seen from above, nat. size). The left shear bears three indices, of which the longest, seen from above, stands opposite the dactylus, while the two others come forth distinctly with their cutting-edges and tips only when seen from below, since all fuse with one another at the morphological hinder side. The figure shows that it is a matter of a break-triplication, in which the index had been broken obliquely upward, so that two long break-surfaces, almost parallel to the normal dactylus cutting-edge, arose. The upward-turning of the index beyond the norm is distinctly recognizable in the view from below, since of the toothing — in contrast to that of the dactylus — nothing is to be recognized. For the rest, all the characters are given which permit the inference of a regenerative origin of the whole shear. The whole shear-hand is too small in relation to the other shear-leg segments, and the cutting edges of the dactylus as well as of the two index-toothings visible on the under side point to a K-type not yet fully developed. The third index is, as the view from above shows, likewise of this character.

**Nr. 47.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 260, Pl. I, Fig. 14). "Right shear. A severe injury, which had befallen the propodite, resulted in the growth of a projection from near the origin of the index, which doubles the index. It is curved downward and inward, beneath the under side of the primary index, and provided with sharp teeth on its inner side. At the base of the toothed margin of the secondary index there springs a very small projection, which shows a row of very small teeth on its inner margin, and seems to be a rudimentary third index. The dactylus does not meet the primary index when the shear is closed." This remarkable specimen I have examined and sketched anew in the Peabody Museum, Cambridge, Mass. To Faxon's good account it is still to be added that the original shear is a pronounced K-shear, whereas each of the accessory indices has a distinct Z-character, which has come to expression in the slender shape, the fine toothing, and the beset with tactile hairs. In contrast to the previous case, to which this shear will otherwise have been similar with respect to the place and direction of the break, it is evidently not a matter of a regenerate of the whole propodite, but of the appearance of a break-triplication on an original K-shear. On this there grew two regenerates, one of which, growing inverse out of the proximally-turned break-surface, is still very small, while the other, with correct orientation and better nutritional conditions, was able to develop more strongly at the distally-facing break-surface. The still very slight age of the accessory indices may also be inferred from the toothing, which has not even yet taken on the transitional character toward the K-shear (cf. on this Nr. 51), yet it is to be taken into consideration that the basal portions of the cutting-edges, which first take on the K-character, have here not come to development (cf. on this Nr. 85, 86, 94, 95, 96, 101, 103, 106, 106a).

**Nr. 47a.** *H. americanus* (own sketch after a right shear in the Agassiz Museum, Cambr., Mass.). A little-toothed shear in transition to the K-shear, bears multiple traces of injury and a supernumerary short index, which has the same type and, seen from above, runs out from the middle of the propodus in the direction of the normal propodus. A deep pit between the two indices points to a break-wound, whose proximally-facing wound-surface has, for unknown reasons, brought forth no regenerate — perhaps still no regenerate? — or [the inference] that one has been lost.

**Nr. 47b.** *H. americanus* (own sketch after a right shear in the Agassiz Museum). A transitional type has deformed the propodite, broken off the index to half its length; out of a wound at its base there grows, in distal direction, visible from above, a short index provided with teeth of the transitional type, over a deep pit. The case is to be judged analogously to the previous one.

**Nr. 48.** *H. europaeus* (Lucas, 1844, fig., Bateson, 1894, 534, Fig. 189 III) [the entry continues on the following page].

**Nr. 49.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 261, Pl. I, Fig. 17; Bateson, 1894, 534, Fig. 190 II). The left chela, whose dactylus, of unusual slenderness, does not close onto the index, bears a pair of supernumerary indices fused at their outer margins halfway to their tips, which arise above, beside the base of the normal index, and whose common axis of symmetry forms an angle of about 60° with the main axis of the normal index. A triple-formation at a break, arising in a manner analogous to the previous case. All cutting edges with typical Z-dentition. The slenderness of the dactylus and also of the two indices points to regeneration.

**Nr. 50.** *H. europaeus* [Pl. XVIII, Fig. V]. (After a photograph by Prof. O. Carlgren from the Museum of the Zootomical Institute of the University of Stockholm.) The left chela, whose dactylus is lost, bears a pair of indices fused at their outer margins to two thirds of their length, which arise from the base of the normal index (more from the upper side of the horizontally lying chela?), and whose common axis of symmetry forms an angle of about 45° with the propodus. A triple-formation at a break, arising from a wound lying almost exactly perpendicular to the horizontal. All cutting edges with typical Z-dentition.

**Nr. 51.** *H. americanus* (own sketch after a specimen in the Agassiz Museum, Cambridge, Mass., seen from below). The left chela, with a greatly broadened, massive index, which runs out into three lobes lying in one plane. The outermost looks like a pushed-back and bent-over index end; the two others are straight short points on a triangular process projecting from the middle of the index cutting edge. The index cutting edge shows K-character from the base to the beginning of this triangle; the dentition of the two mutually averted cutting edges of the two-lobed triangle, to be interpreted as fused indices, shows transitional character, while the cutting edge running out to the outermost lobe and the dactylus still display a pronounced Z-character. The interpretation of this monstrosity, which undoubtedly shows marks of injury, presents itself, in the sense of our Nr. 47, as follows: by an act of force the dactylus of a K-chela was completely torn off, and the index, with loss of its tip, was bent outward to half its length and probably also crushed. As original parts of the index we accordingly have to regard the first half provided with K-teeth and the broadening lying before the Z-toothing of the outermost tip. All the other formations, as well as the dactylus — which perhaps derives from a later regeneration (Z-type!) — are regenerates (transitional types!).

**Nr. 52.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Boston Museum Soc. nat. hist., Acc. Nr. 3630, Port au Port/Newfoundland Expedition 1885; from below). The right chela bears, on the excessively broad propodus, in the middle of the cutting edge, a triangular process toothed on both sides and forked at the end, which can be construed as two supernumerary indices fused at their outer margins. At the point of origin of this formation, and opposite on the free index margin, there are strong scars; the tip of the index itself is set off, as if a regenerate.

All cutting edges have Z-dentition. A triple-formation at a break not unlike the previous one, but on a Z-chela and probably without loss of the dactylus; yet here too the set-off tip is perhaps a regenerate.

**Nr. 53.** *H. europaeus* [Pl. XVIII, Fig. VIII] (after photograph by Carlgren, Zootom. Inst. Univ. Stockholm). The index of the left chela is bent outward a short distance distal to the base. From the break, still recognizable, two supernumerary small indices have sprouted, fused on their outer sides halfway to their tips. All cutting edges with Z-dentition.

**Nr. 54.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, British Museum, 47, 53. Seaton, Devon, 1847, W. C. Travelyan don.; Bateson, 1894, 534, Fig. 189 II). The index of the right chela is bent outward from the middle. Opposite the point of bending, two supernumerary index tips, fused to half their length at their outer margins, grow out of the cutting edge. All cutting edges with Z-dentition. Typical triple-formation at a break.

**Nr. 55.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum). The index of the right chela with two thrust-folds on the outer margin, opposite which lies a break with two supernumerary index tips. All cutting edges have irregular K-teeth.

**Nr. 56.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 222, Pl. I, Fig. 9, ½ n. s.; Bateson, 1894, 534, Fig. 189 I). The broadened, outwardly bent index of the right chela bears, from the base to the middle of its cutting edge, a triangular plate, which, judging from the toothing on both sides, is to be regarded as a pair of supernumerary indices united at their outer margins. At the end, two tips still project, likewise injured, as is the end of the simple index. Typical triple-formation at a break with strong fusion. All cutting edges of typical Z-character.

**Nr. 57.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum). The index of the right chela with a thrust-fold on the outer margin and bending of the tip outward. Opposite the portion thus delimited there projects from the cutting-edge margin a half-elliptical formation, toothed on both sides, with a simple ending. By analogy with the preceding case, it is a pair of indices fused right to the end. The regenerative triple-formation at a break emerges here very clearly. All cutting edges have typical Z-dentition, which, however, on the supernumerary indices looks as if it had been formed in over-haste, or as if it had not yet had time to unfold completely.

**Nr. 58.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum). The left chela, whose dactylus is too slender and too short, bears from the base to the middle of the index cutting edge a triangular plate, to be regarded, from its toothing on both sides, as fused indices. All cutting edges Z-dentition, but the teeth on the plate are much shorter and reveal the periodic arrangement less well than elsewhere (regeneration).

**Nr. 59.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Peabody Museum, Cambridge, Mass., from below). The left chela, whose dactylus shows an irregular margin, bears in the middle of the cutting edge of the index — which is truncated at the end — a triangular, bilaterally toothed, short process: two supernumerary fused index tips. All cutting edges Z-dentition.

**Nr. 60.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum). The index of the left chela, whose dactylus is moreover too short, truncated, shows near the base on the outer margin a saddle-shaped depression, whose saddle-flanks are provided with molar teeth. Since the remaining stumps of the index cutting edge facing the dactylus also display the K-character, we evidently have before us a rudimentary triple-formation at a break. The dactylus teeth too are of K-character, perhaps likewise still with traces of the transition, as are the saddle-teeth.

**Nr. 61.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Boston Museum of Zool. Soc., Old Coll.). The index of the left chela shows clearly, on the outer margin opposite the base, the apex of the break, from whose sides supernumerary indices grew out, which are only slightly fused. The middle index, with inverse symmetry, is broken off to half its length and still covered with a blood scab; the other supernumerary one shows in its set-off tip a clearly regenerative origin; the original index still has on the outer margin a notch attributable to wounding. The cutting edges of all the indices and of the dactylus have K-character. This case can be construed as a better-developed stage of the previous one. If one views the depression between the supernumerary indices from the outer margin (Fig. 61, "seen from outside"), one sees a deep saddle-shaped pit, which corresponds to the original wound and to the depression still lying open in the previous case. (Figs. 61 and 61* are, to save space, linearly reduced to half the natural size.)

**Nr. 62.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Boston Mus. Soc. nat. Hist., Acc. Nr. 2904, G. of St. Lawrence). The index of the left chela bent in the middle toward the dactylus; at the apex of the bend two supernumerary indices, turned toward each other with their cutting edges. The end-tips are broken off. All cutting edges Z-type. Typical triple-formation at a break.

**Nr. 63.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Peabody Mus., Cambridge, Mass.; from below). The index of the left chela with two supernumerary tips, turning their cutting edges toward each other, on the outer margin near the normal tip. Dactylus and all index cutting edges of K-character. One must not be misled by the absence of true rounded or flat molar teeth on the supernumerary indices, since such teeth are always lacking on the corresponding end-section of the normal index too. The outwardly gaping wound, with the formation of its two fracture surfaces, still comes clearly to light here.

**Nr. 64.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Peabody Mus.). On the outer margin of a right Z-chela there is, opposite the largest tooth, a deep notch, and farther distally, halfway to the tip, again a smaller depression. Between them rise two rudimentary index tips, fused halfway with the cutting edges. A few small teeth and tufts of hair characterize them as Z-type.

**Nr. 65.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Peabody Mus.). The right-angled bent tip of a left Z-chela lets two accessory index tips, fused almost to the end by the cutting edges, emerge from the apex of the bend of the outer margin; these bear clear Z-teeth. On both sides of the added member, notches in the outer margin likewise betray the origin as a triple-formation at a break.

**Nr. 66.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Mus.). The index of a right Z-chela, notched in the middle of its outer margin, bears near the tip, outward, a fish-tail-shaped formation, to be interpreted, from the dentition of the end-edges, as partly fused index tips. Further development of the earlier cases would lead to this consequence. All cutting edges of Z-type.

**Nr. 67.** *H. europaeus* (own sketch, British Museum London, presented by the Directors of the Harrods Stores 1906, 7, 3, 2; ½ n. s.). A male of 112 mm carapace length possesses on the left a normal K-chela, on the right a triple-formation of the propodite on the Z-chela.

The form similar to the previous case, but no piece had been torn out of the original index; rather, the break had occurred inward, past the dactylus. All cutting edges typical ZZ.

**Nr. 68.** *H. americanus* (Herrick, 1895, 145, Pl. 47, Fig. 190, from below, ⅔ n. s.). The left K-chela, whose dactylus is lost, bears on the outer margin of the index's beginning a pair of supernumerary indices fused with their cutting edges almost to the tip. As a result of the fusion, of the dentition only a row of fused teeth near the tips can be seen, which can be construed as transitional character. The supernumerary formations are set off from the proximal part of the propodus' outer margin by a shallow indentation, and from the distal part by a fine furrow, so that the position of the wounding as the cause of the triple-formation at a break is still demonstrable.

**Nr. 69.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 259, Pl. I, Fig. 11, ½ n. s., Museum comp. Zool., Harvard Coll., Cambridge, Mass.). "Left chela. A long, triangular comb, directed outward and forward from the middle of the outer margin of the penultimate segment. The comb-like process has a strong downward curvature. There are several chelae similar to this one in the collection." According to the preceding cases, this is undoubtedly a pair of accessory indices, whose cutting edges, through complete fusion right to the tip, no longer reveal any dentition. Dactylus and simple index have K-type with traces of transition.

**Nr. 70.** *H. europaeus* [Pl. XIX, Fig. IX–XI], (orig. photograph after a specimen in the Court Museum, Vienna, from Lesina, presented by G. Bucchich, 1907; from above, below, and right; O. Pesta, 1912, 28, Fig., only an incidental outline drawing). A male, 20 cm total length, 5 cm broad; each chela-leg 18 cm long. On the left, typical Z-chela. The dactylus of the right K-chela is bent upward and outward at a right angle just above its origin. From the bend of the dactylus there arise two almost equally long, supernumerary, mirror-image dactyli, which together with the bent-over one make up a typical triple-formation at a break and, like it, bear K-cutting-edges with transitional character, all of which, however, turn toward the index under different angles — the index having developed its K-teeth well. Most similar to the Z-chela is still the dactylopodite lying in the prolongation of the inner propodus margin, that is, the one situated most to the left, correctly symmetrical, among the supernumerary dactylopodites. The molar teeth of the middle component are clearly visible on view from below, those of the original, bent-over dactylus on view from the right.

**Nr. 71.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, Pl. I, Fig. 15, ½ n. s.). "Left chela. The dactylus is here bent upward and outward at a right angle, at a point halfway between base and tip. Two finger-like processes arise close together out of the bend of the dactylus. Of these the proximal is a little longer than the distal. Both lie in the normal course of the dactylus and show a row of teeth directed against the teeth of the distal end of the index. When the dactylus is closed, however, the teeth of neither of these two processes meet the teeth of the index, but fall on both sides of them." Typical triple-formation at a break following an act of force breaking the dactylus upward and outward. All cutting edges K (transition?).

**Nr. 72.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Mus., view onto the cutting edges of the dactylus). The dactylus of the left chela, bent outward under more than a right angle, bears at the apex of the bend two mirror-image, diverging supernumerary dactylus tips, which at the base are separated by two deep, fine furrows. In the middle of the cutting edge of the bent dactylus the teeth are lacking, but here there is a distinct scar. The teeth of all cutting edges of the dactylus are transitional-K; the end-tip of the bent-over dactylus is freshly broken off, still covered with a blood scab. The index is, from that point on where the bent-over dactylus has crossed it, obliquely truncated and scantily regenerated, the Z-type still predominating. In this case, on following the continuity of the dentition from the bent-over, as the original, dactylus to the smaller, supernumerary ones, the inverse growth of the dactylus sprouting from the proximally-facing fracture surface is to be noted. The absence of the teeth at the normal middle of the cutting edge, and the furrows that separate the supernumerary dactyli from one another, together with the still visible scar-trace and the truncation of the index, complete the picture that we can form of the break of the dactylopodite.

**Nr. 73.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Mus., from below; Faxon, 1881, 261, Pl. II, Fig. 1). Right chela, propodus lost. — Dactylus bent down and inward at almost a right angle in the middle. At the apex of the bend there lie, in the same plane as the whole dactylus, two pairs of laterally fused supernumerary dactylus-ends with free tips. All cutting edges K-type. Arisen from a double break, probably brought about by the resistance of the powerfully developed middle molar teeth, which for this same reason did not break through the cutting edge itself, as had, however, happened in the previous case with weaker dentition.

According to my interpretation, then, the more distally situated pair of ends, together with the normal, bent-over end, corresponds to a typical triple-formation at a break. The middle component of it has its origin, just like the adjoining one of the pair, from a break-angle whose apex lies between a rectangular and a distally adjoining kidney-shaped molar tooth. The supernumerary ends both begin with one, even if still smaller, yet distinctly kidney-shaped molar tooth, and farther on toward the tip also repeat, in reduced form, the teeth adjoining this molar tooth distally. The proximal pair of ends has its break-apex between a proximally lying, large square molar tooth and a narrower rectangular one; here too both components of the pair first repeat the rectangular teeth lying distal to the presumptive break-apex, and then also, farther on, the teeth adjoining distally on the normal dactylopodite. In each of the supernumerary pairs of ends, then, in a fine illustration of the principles applied hitherto, only those parts are always developed which lie distal to the point of origin on the normal limb. This law thus holds not only for the regeneration of successive limbs, but also within a single limb. On closer examination of other cases, further evidence can easily be adduced by drawing on the differences among the teeth.

**Nr. 74.** *H. europaeus* [Pl. XIII, Fig. VI–VII], (after phot. Carlgren, Zootom. Inst. Univ. Stockholm, from above and below). The left Z-chela shows, behind the middle of the dactylus, an offset, as if the distal part were set on. The normal dactylus cutting edge, equal in length to the index, is from this point on shifted somewhat outward and upward, the continuity of its dentition interrupted. Fused with it almost to the end is a second, mirror-image cutting edge; a third, somewhat freer one joins, with a curved course, onto the proximal part of the main cutting edge. Besides this supernumerary pair of dactyli, whose cutting edges all turn the same Z-dentition as the normal dactylus cutting edge toward the index, there is also a long, roll-shaped outgrowth grown out of the free margin of the dactylus at the mentioned point of attachment, which, according to our previous expositions, is to be regarded as a pair of supernumerary dactyli fused with their cutting edges. As in the previous case, we thus have to do with two pairs of supernumerary dactylus-ends, only that there both gaping wounds lay side by side in one plane, whereas here the one lay transversely to the free margin, the other under an angle to this on a flat side. The case is therefore especially instructive for the connection between the position of the point of origin and the development of the formations sprouting from it, as Bateson set forth in 1894 for the arthropods, p. 481.

**Nr. 75.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum). The right Z-chela has crushed the index broadwise and bears on the dactylus-side margin two diverging short cones. The dentition on these and on the rest of the index is, owing to the crowded position, only indistinctly Z-like, since shifts and fusions of the periodic humps into larger masses occur in many places.

**Nr. 76.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum, from below). The dactylopodite of the right Z-chela, bent toward the free margin and somewhat downward, kinked just behind the articulation, bears opposite this point a bilaterally toothed, lancet-shaped process, a pair of laterally fused supernumerary dactyli. The periodic character of the dentition is somewhat indistinct.

**Nr. 77.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum, from below). Right Z-chela, developed quite similarly to the previous one, only the process arises somewhat farther distally and the original dactylopodite is less bent.

**Nr. 78.** *H. americanus* (Emmel, 1907, 104, Pl. I, Fig. 2, ⅔ n. s.). Left dactylopodite, which appears drawn too far toward the free margin, bears just behind the articulation a bilaterally toothed process, reaching almost to the index tip, which represents two dactyli fused with their free margins. Triple-formation at a break from a slightly gaping wound. All cutting edges of typical Z-character.

**Nr. 79.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 259, Pl. I, Fig. 10, ½ n. s.). "Right chela. The dactylus is abnormally short and curved, and its proximal half is transformed into a large rounded plate, toothed at the margin, of which only the basal part closes against the index."

**Nr. 80.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum). Right chela similar to the previous case, but the margins of the plate and of the shortened dactylus more rectilinear, the dentition of all cutting edges more irregular.

**Nr. 81.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 261, Pl. II, Fig. 4). "Dactylus of a right [left? — Ref.] chela. Near the base the dactylus divides into two branches, a long one, which seems to be the distal part of the original dactylus, bent so that it forms almost a right angle with the proximal part, and a shorter one with a forking at the end..., and a row of teeth on the inner as well as on the outer margins. This shorter branch has the normal direction of the dactylus and is probably a secondary outgrowth from the primary dactylus." The kink which the original dactylus has had to undergo toward its free margin is distinctly recognizable by a deep, fine furrow which bisects the kink-angle. The tip of the kinked dactylus is broken off, its distal part narrow; the dentition, unmistakably of Z-type on all cutting edges, is disturbed in its periods on the supernumerary parts.

**Nr. 82.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 259, Pl. I, Fig. 8, ½ n. s.). "Right chela." "The dactylus is strongly bent away from the index and elongated." Opposite the largest index tooth, on the free margin of the dactylus, there is the scar, the result of that wounding which probably caused the curvature of the dactylus. A toothed outgrowth, meeting the index tip when the chela is closed, functionally replaces the dislocated dactylus end. Besides this, a second process projects at a right angle to the bent-over part of the dactylus. This process shows a row of teeth opposed to those found on the remaining cutting edges of the tripled dactylopodite, but of the same Z-character. This distinct triple-formation at a break — the newer parts are recognizable, according to Faxon, by the stippling — also furnishes us with the interpretation for the three preceding cases. If we imagine the original dactylus bent over somewhat less strongly, then the supernumerary dactylus-ends growing forth from the fracture surfaces will, with their pawl-edges [Klinkenränder], abut one another and fuse, so that either only the outermost tips remain free, as in Nr. 81, or nothing at all of the pawl-edge remains free, as in Nr. 79 and Nr. 80 — the smaller the bending, the stronger the fusion.

**Nr. 83.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 258, Pl. I, Fig. 7, ½ n. s.). Left chela. Similar to the previous case, but all cutting edges K-type and the additional tips of equal breadth, whereas in the previous case the inverse one is by far the weakest.

**Nr. 83a.** *H. americanus* (Faxon, 1881, 259, without fig.). Quite analogous case. Perhaps identical with one of the two following specimens.

**Nr. 84.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum; from below). Left dactylopodite similar to Nr. 83, but somewhat less bent, the accessory ends therefore adjoin one another immediately at the base. All cutting edges K-type, on the accessory ones less clumsy.

**Nr. 85.** *H. americanus* (own sketch, Agassiz Museum; from below). Left K-dactylopodite similar to the previous one, but since the point of bending lies farther distally, only the small teeth normally lying before the dactylus tip are reproduced on the supernumerary ends, so that their K-character cannot be distinguished with certainty.

**Nr. 86.** *H. americanus* (Herrick, 1895, 145, Pl. 47, Fig. 191). Left dactylopodite similar to the previous one, also as regards its dentition, only that, as a result of the excessive shortening of the dactylus-ends, the toothing lets the K-type emerge still less. The case, however, presents a further uncertainty owing to the approximately equal length and development of the three dactylus-endings, which do not without further ado allow one of the branches to be recognized as the more original. I have decided in favor of the bending of the original dactylus having occurred toward the free margin, and thereby in favor of an origin of the supernumerary formations at its cutting-edge margin, especially because of the analogy in the excessive shortening of the original dactylus with unambiguous cases, e.g. Nr. 81 and 82, and then because the rounding in the connection with the middle component corresponds better to the inverse growth of the latter from the bent-over dactylus than the sharper angle to the third component, which continues the proximal part of the dactylus in normal direction.

**Nr. 87.** *H. europaeus* (van Beneden, 1864, 371, fig.; Bateson, 1894, 531, Fig. 184 III; Bateson, 1913, 76, Fig. 12) p. 372: "Among... lobster consignments from Norway there was found one of medium size, whose large chela or foreleg of the right side, in place of a single dactylopodite, bears three, and these three dactylopodites, of approximately the same size and of the same form, move like a single piece. They all three have one of their margins toothed like the normal limb, with a slightly hook-shaped bent end. Two among them have the toothing on the same side and in the normal position, while the middle piece turns its little teeth outward. If the three dactylopodites were movable, there would be two complete chelae, as one can see in the accompanying figure. The lobster showed nothing irregular in its remaining structure, neither externally nor internally."

From both the figure and the description it follows with certainty that this is a K-claw. The type is most clearly expressed in that component which stands nearest to the index and which, by virtue of its upward and outward bending, is to be addressed as an original dactylopodite broken at the base. Not much less well developed is the somewhat shorter inverse component half-fused with it; it represents the regenerate sprouting from the proximally-turned fracture surface. Still clearly a transitional form — indeed, in the view given by van Beneden, appearing almost entirely as a Z-type — is the third component, which stands farthest from the index. I regard it entirely as a regenerate from the distally-facing fracture surface of the break that occurred at the joint. The resemblance to a Z-dactylopodite is, however, not so great as one might conclude from the figure. Van Beneden indeed expressly remarks that all the dactyli had approximately the same form. That in saying this he did not, say, disregard the difference between the claws is evident from a later passage, p. 376: "In the lobster, for example, the two claws are always different from one another; that of the right side is usually¹ shorter and stronger than the other. But if the claws of the right side have been injured, it is quite possible that the left one becomes the stronger; this we hope soon to be able to set out more fully on the basis of direct observations.²"

> ¹ Probably van Beneden was led astray either by too small a number of specimens, or by the formerly widespread belief that in the lobster, just as in man, the right hand is as a rule the stronger. Against this, Roesel had already pronounced himself on the basis of his own experience (1755, 345). In the lobster the large claw is distributed by halves to the left side as well (Herrick, 1895, 143); in other crustaceans there is, to be sure, a predominance of one side (cf. Przibram, Heterochelie 1905).

> ² A further publication on van Beneden's part, however, does not appear to have followed, perhaps because experiments on lobsters with the claw differentiation into K- and Z-claw already accomplished were bound to yield negative results (cf. Przibram, 1902). In other crustaceans, to be sure, such a thing could be achieved [Przibram, 1901, 1907 Scherenumkehr (claw reversal)], and in the lobster before the differentiation of the claw-difference (Emmel, 1907).

Is there, then, a gross carelessness on the draughtsman's part? I believe that this too is not the case, but rather that it is a matter of the position of the cutting edge relative to the plane of the paper. Since the dactylus crossing the index is twisted somewhat upward, then — if this is to lie in the plane of the paper — the whole propodus must be tilted somewhat to the right. Then the teeth of the index will be turned somewhat more toward the viewer, but those of the dactylus growing in the normal direction will be correspondingly turned away, so that a perspectival foreshortening of its teeth results. Now it does in fact emerge from van Beneden's figure that the draughtsman strove to set the two components lying farther to the right into the plane of the paper, for the spines projecting strongly at the inner margin of the propodus also appear foreshortened, and the spine standing at the base of the dactylus is likewise not seen in profile. Had the draughtsman turned still farther, the propodus-spines would have appeared to be pushed entirely into the propodus, so that no toothing at all would any longer have been perceptible. With a still more strongly upward-twisted dactylus this already holds true when the latter is set into the plane of the paper, as is visible in our photograph of Nr. 70 (from above).

Nr. 88. H. americanus (Faxon, 1881, 257, Taf. I, Abb. 2, from below, ½ n. Gr.; Bateson, 1894, 532, II). Left claw quite similar to the previous case. The depiction from below in Faxon's figure lets the K-transitional character of the component of the triple-break-formation farthest from the index be better recognized, whereas the toothing of the component standing nearest to the index has become less visible owing to its partial turning-away from the viewer. According to Faxon this toothing corresponded to that of the component farthest from the index; from the figure, however, it ought to have typical K-toothing. His homologizing of the toothing of the middle component with that of an immovably attached index rests, however, on the repeatedly occurring error that a claw could arise distal to the articulation-place on the dactylopodite, and does not accord with his own figure. (The similar cases summarily mentioned by Faxon are probably identical with those reaching description later from my own sketches.)

Nr. 89. H. americanus (Faxon, 1881, 258, Taf. I, Abb. 4, from below, ½ n. Gr.). "Right claw. Here the dactylus is bent, at a short distance from its articulation with the propodus, at a right angle to its normal direction, and thrown out of the plane of the hand in such a way that, closed, it crosses the index. The tip is broken off. From the untoothed margin of the dactylus, near its proximal end, two outgrowths project . . . at an angle of about 45° to one another, the distal one taking the normal direction of the index. Both of these outgrowths are toothed on the margins facing each other, but it is noteworthy that the teeth of the two outgrowths are not directed exactly toward one another, but rather inclined somewhat downward, as if they were drawn toward the teeth of the thumb. It is of interest to note that the toothed margins of index and thumb are beset with an unusually large quantity of stiff bristles, and that this character is repeated on the toothed margins of the monstrous outgrowths." A triple-break-formation similar to the two preceding cases, but which corresponds to a much more widely gaping wound and which — as is clear from the figure, still more from the description of the cutting-edge teeth and bristles — exhibits the pure Z-type in all components.

Nr. 90. H. americanus (Faxon, 1881, 258, Taf. I, Abb. 5, ½ n. Gr.). Left Z-claw, similar to the preceding, but the less strongly bent-away dactylus bears two supernumerary terminations crossing each other at the tips, whose tips are essentially weaker than that of the normal dactylus.

Nr. 91. H. europaeus [Taf. XIX, Abb. XII–XIII], (photographs after a specimen sent by Cori from Trieste to the Museum of the B. V. A., now Kat.-Nr. 406). Carapace length 16 cm; on the right a normal Z-claw. The left K-claw has crossed the dactylopodite — provided with typical K-toothing — somewhat over the index. At the base of its free margin this same dactylopodite bears an outgrowth consisting of two initially fused supernumerary dactyli turned toward each other with their cutting edges, whose slenderness, straightness, and toothing, viewed from above, recall Z-claws rather than K-claws. Seen from below, however, the K-character emerges distinctly in the coarseness and unevenness of the toothing. A deep scar at the base of the outgrowth, before its branches separate, likewise attests, in this same view, to the regenerative origin of the accessory dactyli, of which the proximal one has, incidentally, lost its tip through a later injury. The lobster also shows further traces of injury elsewhere and is overgrown with all sorts of attached animals; it had therefore evidently not molted for a long time.

Nr. 92. H. europaeus (own sketch from the British Museum, London; Blasket Isd. 1907, 6.5.I. pres. by George Coverly Esq.). Carapace length 13.5 cm; on the left a normal K-claw, but with an injury at an analogous place as on the left Z-claw. This latter shows a shattering of the dactylus-joint. From the wound there grows, with a distal direction, a considerable regeneration-bud of a supernumerary Z-dactylus; just distal to its origin-angle, but separated by a deep furrow, there grows a similar but still much smaller regenerate with inverse symmetry. The toothing is unmistakably of the Z-type on all cutting edges of the claw. This case is conclusive for the regenerative origin of a triple-break-formation without strong bending of the normal dactylus, which fits almost exactly onto the index.

Nr. 93. H. americanus (Herrick, 1895, 145, Taf. 47, Abb. 193; from below, ⅔ n. Gr.). The right Z-claw bears three dactyli, of which the two nearest the index sit on the propodus as mirror-images of each other with a common joint, whereas the third sits with its own joint. The tip of the third dactylus is torn off. Herrick regards the dactylus slightly crossing the index-tip as the original one, in which I may concur with him. All cutting edges of typical Z-character.

Nr. 94. H. americanus (Emmel, 1907, Taf. I, Abb. 1, ½ n. Gr.; from the collection of Cartland, Pemaquid Beach, Maine). "Left claw. The propodite or index-portion of this claw appears to be normal in every respect, except that two scars . . . are present along its smooth outer margin; these scars may perhaps be connected with the origin of the abnormalities in the rest of the claw. The toothing of the index shows clearly that it belongs to the 'K'-type of the claw. The dactylus is strongly bent and bent upward out of its normal plane. Therefore, with the claw closed, the dactylus is not in normal opposition to the index, but crosses it over its upper side. It too is toothed with the K-type of teeth. From the smooth or morphologically dorsal margin of the dactylus there rise two supernumerary spurs or outgrowths . . ., which lie practically in the dorso-ventral longitudinal plane of the claw. One of the margins of each of these two abnormal outgrowths is toothed, and the spatial relations are such that the toothed margins are turned toward each other . . . The character of the toothing is such that traces of 'periodic sequence' can be recognized, i.e. teeth are arranged in groups, marked by higher projecting teeth with smaller teeth inserted between . . . Along these toothed margins stand tufts of tactile hairs. With respect to toothing and the presence of tactile hairs, these outgrowths therefore seem to resemble a 'pinch'- (Zwick-) rather than a 'snap'- (Knack-) claw type . . ."

If one examines Emmel's figure more closely, one sees that the repeated cutting-edge pieces — corresponding to the origin of the accessory dactyli from the middle of the dactylus and to the fusion extending beyond it — correspond merely to the ends of the dactylus, and that at the end of the original dactylus, as already noted earlier for the normal claw, small teeth recalling the Z-type tend to stand, provided the claw is not too old. But neither can any conclusion be drawn from the presence of the tactile hairs, since such hairs occur on the transitional claws toward the K-type also on the regenerates. It thus cannot be decided here whether the supernumerary structures form Z-claws or transitions to the K-type.

Nr. 95. H. americanus (Bateson, 1894, 532, Abb. 186 III, from below; allegedly after Faxon, but not there, 1881). Left K-claw similar to the previous case, for which, regarding the toothing, the same holds. Differences lie in the lesser curvature of the original dactylus and in the more proximal origin of the accessory structures, which, however, in compensation, are fused farther up with the cutting edges.

Nr. 96. H. europaeus (sketch by Cori after a specimen in the Hamburg Museum). On the left a Z-claw; on the right a K-claw with shortened index and dactylus. On the free margin of the latter, two supernumerary dactyli with fine teeth, turned toward each other with their cutting edges. To these the remark to Nr. 94 is likewise applicable. The regenerative origin is recognizable already from the shortening of the normally-positioned dactylus.

Nr. 97. H. americanus (own sketch after Agassiz Museum). The right K-claw has the end of the index set off and shortened, and has bent the dactylus over the index along the set-off place. Right at the articulation of the dactylus there rises a pair of supernumerary dactyli, of which the more distal one has, however, secondarily lost its tip. The teeth of these dactyli, fused up to about the middle of their cutting edges, still show, in the K-character, traces of periodicity, as it also persists for a long time at the distal end of the normal dactylus; also the large tooth characteristic in the middle of the cutting edge is still to be seen: it bears the K-character; of the more proximal molar-teeth, on the other hand, nothing more is to be recognized.

Nr. 98. H. americanus (own sketch from Agassiz Museum, from below). On the left K-claw the dactylus is broken in a semicircular form past the index below toward the outside and bears a fish-tail-like outgrowth at the break-place, which consists of two dactyli fused up to the middle tooth of the cutting edges. The toothing repeats the corresponding portion of the normal dactylus.

Nr. 99. H. europaeus [Taf. XIX, Abb. XIV], (after a photograph by Carlgren from the Zoological Institute of the University of Stockholm). Left claw similar to the previous, but the dactylus only slightly bent away, the supernumerary dactyli fused up to beyond the middle tooth, and the whole outgrowth marked off from the normal dactylus by furrows. The middle dactylus is broken off in its non-fused part, so that the remark as for the previous cases can be applied only to the toothing of the second accessory one.

Nr. 100. H. americanus (own sketch after Boston Museum Soc. Nat. Hist., Acc. Nr. 3630, Port au Port, Newfoundland, Exp. of 1885). The left Z-claw bears, on the dactylus crossing the index almost at a right angle — whose outermost tip is broken off — two accessory Z-dactyli fused up to the half. The course of the break-wound is still recognizable in the sculpture of the cuticle, as the sketch reproduces it.

Nr. 101. H. americanus (own sketch after Agassiz Museum, from below). The left K-claw bears, on the circularly-bent dactylus crossing below the index at its tip, a trapezoidal outgrowth consisting of two dactyli fused up to two-thirds with their cutting edges, to whose periodic toothing the remark made at Nr. 94 applies.

Nr. 102. H. americanus (own sketch after Boston Museum Soc. Nat. Hist., Acc. Nr. 3630, Port au Port, Newfoundland, Exp. of 1885, from below). The right Z-claw bears, on the free margin of the [dactylus bent] semicircularly against the index cutting edge, a cap-shaped outgrowth, consisting of two Z-dactyli fused almost up to the ends with the cutting edges.

Nr. 103. H. americanus (own sketch after Agassiz Museum). The right K-claw has the index-tip and dactylus-tip strongly set off and tapered, as though regenerated; it bears, on the free margin of the dactylus, two further dactylus-tips standing on an elevation, which appear to represent dactyli fused up to the middle tooth, but for this are only long enough on the assumption that they are regenerates. Apart from the claw-tips, traces of injury are also still to be noted on the outgrowth. Regarding the toothing, the remark to Nr. 94 holds.

Nr. 104. H. americanus (own sketch after Agassiz Museum, from below). The right Z-claw has the index broken off to half; the dactylus crosses the index at a right angle above along this break-place, has a short regenerated tip, and bears at its bending-place two further regeneration-buds turned toward each other with the Z-cutting-edges. The regenerating portion is bounded on both sides by deep furrows.

Nr. 105. H. americanus (own sketch after Agassiz Museum). The left Z-claw bears, at the base of the free margin, a sugar-loaf-shaped outgrowth consisting of two supernumerary dactyli fused almost up to the tips with their Z-cutting edges; here too furrows and traces of injury are visible on both sides of the outgrowth.

Nr. 106. H. americanus (Faxon, 1881, 257, Taf. I, Abb. 1, ½ n. Gr.; Bateson, 1894, 532, Abb. III). "Right claw. In this claw the dactylus is . . . bent strongly outward against the index and thrown upward out of its normal plane, so that, closed, it does not meet the index but crosses it. The grasping-ability of the claw is thereby destroyed. From the inner margin [= free margin — Ref.] of the dactylus there is developed an enormous flattened outgrowth, which at the tip divides into two prongs . . ., which are toothed on the margins facing each other. Near the middle of the outgrowth there is a deep scar, visible on both sides." Index and original dactylus of the K-type. To the added structures the remark to Nr. 94 applies.

Nr. 106a. "There is a specimen quite similar to this one . . . in the Museum of Yale College, New Haven, Conn."

Regarding the toothing of the dactyli of such specimens, what was said at Nr. 84 holds in still higher degree, since only the portions of the cutting edges corresponding to the outermost dactylus-end still appear exempted from complete fusion.

Nr. 107. H. americanus (Faxon, 1881, 258, Taf. I, Abb. 3, ½ n. Gr., from below). The right Z-claw has the dactylus, sharply bent against the index at the end, broken off shortly before the tip; on the free margin before the bending-place there is on the dactylus a pointed protrusion. Distal to it are developed two supernumerary claw-tips turned toward each other with the cutting edges. According to Faxon they are supposed to repeat, in their form, index and dactylus; from everything brought forward so far it is clear that this will be a case of a pair of mirror-image dactyli.

Nr. 108. H. americanus (own sketch, Peabody Museum). The right Z-claw has regenerated the index-tip and has probably bulged out the middle of the index cutting edge into a doubled abnormal index-end fused along the whole free margin. The strongly curved dactylus bears, in the middle of the free margin, first a notch and farther distally two supernumerary dactylus-ends fused almost to the tip, of whose cutting edges no tooth at all is any longer visible.

Nr. 109. H. americanus (Faxon, 1881, 258, Taf. I, Abb. 6, ½ n. Gr., from below). Right Z-claw. "Just behind the middle of the dactylus there arises a simple branch, directed forward at an angle of 45° to the longitudinal axis of the dactylus. This branch shows no tendency to form teeth." We are most probably dealing with two supernumerary dactyli completely fused with their cutting edges right up to the outermost end.

Nr. 110. H. americanus (Herrick, 1895, 145, Taf. 47, Abb. 189, ⅔ n. Gr.). "Right K-claw bears, at the tip of the dactylopodite on the outside, a triangular outgrowth, which can be regarded as a pair of supernumerary ends, wholly fused with the tooth-cutting-edges, in the very first beginning of sprouting ('budding of dactyl'), but which could also represent a simple structure, so that the case would then be regarded as a double-formation."

Nr. 111. H. americanus (Herrick, 1895, 145, Taf. 47, Abb. 196, seen from the free margin; ⅔ n. Gr.). Dactylus of a right K-claw with three tips, whose cutting edges are all turned away from the viewer, hence turned toward the index cutting edge. To this deviation from Bateson's rule Herrick draws special attention. This arrangement of the cutting edges does not preclude the middle tip from being able to appear as the mirror-image of the others, if other characters were taken into account. It may be a matter of a triple-break-formation in which the tip of the dactylus was so far displaced outward that its parallel direction to the index cutting edge underwent no alteration.

Nr. 112. Nephrops norvegicus (Arthur A. Patterson, 1905, 350, Abb.; Ibis House, Great Yarmouth). "As may be seen from the photograph, the formation is of a very extraordinary kind, as though a supernumerary claw were growing separately out of the claw-hand between the fixed and the freely-movable tip, running out into a V-shaped end . . . A grown specimen which had not molted recently, as may be concluded from the presence of numerous acorn-barnacles which had made themselves comfortably fastened on the claw." On examination of the figure it emerges that on a right Z-claw the dactylopodite is opened upward and bears, beside its base toward the index, two dactylopodites fused almost up to the tip with the latch-margins. The two cutting edges are, just like those of the original dactylus, of the finely-toothed Z-type.

Nr. 112a. N. norvegicus (Gray, 1898, 561, without figure; caught at Cumbrae, presented through Liddle to the Robertson Museum of the Millport Marine Biological Station). "... Bearing three fingers on the right claw or large pincer. The supernumerary finger, which is fully two-thirds the size of the normal one, arises from the inner side of the base of the inner movable finger and is sharply toothed on both sides and opposable to the outer finger. The normal finger is fully developed and bends outward from the supernumerary one at a large angle, their distance at the ends amounting to a good ¾ inch [= 1.9 cm, Ref.]. They move together and permit an opening of about ½ inch [= 1.27 cm, Ref.] between the supernumerary and the normal outer finger, so that little or no inconvenience may have been caused to the animal during life." This description is just as little correct as that of the previous case. Here too it is evidently a matter of a supernumerary dactylopodite-pair of the Z-type, fused with the latch-margins, but which has not yet attained normal length. Whether the claw itself and the original dactylopodite were of the Z- or K-type cannot be gathered with certainty from the description, since the designation "large pincer" is probably used as a synonym for "claw" and not as the designation of the larger K-claw, which incidentally in Nephrops often falls short in length behind the Z-claw; the contrast, too, of the "full development" of the normal finger and the "sharp" toothing of the added structure need not refer to the stronger development toward the K-type, but may go only to the more considerable size of the former.

Nr. 113. Uca una (Jaeger, 1851, 35, Taf. I, Abb. 12, from above; Abb. 13, from below) = Ucides (Rathb.) una (M. E.), a land-crab from the family Gecarcinidae, which often have one claw very strongly enlarged, without this affecting a definite side (cf. Przibram, 1905, Heterochelie, p. 220). The right claw has a tripling of the index and dactylus. The supernumerary dactyli turn their cutting edges away from each other and come somewhat out of the plane of the claw-closure; they are fused with the latch-margin only up to the half and are jointed together beside the normal dactylus. Of the indices, two are fused with the latch-margins up to the end and turn their cutting edges, diverging only slightly, away from the normal dactylus; the third end is grown onto the others up to the half along the analogous margin and turns its cutting edge, as is best seen from below, toward the cutting edge of the normal dactylus, and is thus probably to be regarded as the normal index. This triple-break-formation concerning the propodus-dactylus-joint stands midway between the cases that became known in Platycarcinus pagurus, cited by me as Nr. 23 and Nr. 24. It is a matter of a tearing-apart of the claw through a pressure from within, which broke both the base of the dactylopodite and the index-angle. Regarding the character of the claws, it is to be noted that all dactyli exhibit quite similar toothing among themselves, likewise all indices. In general the claw bears a transitional character from a small to a large claw, yet, given the slight constancy of the heterochely in the land-crabs, no certainty is afforded. Jaeger himself seems to have assumed that the other claw was a small left one and the monstrous one corresponded to a large right one — perhaps, however, only because he held these crabs to be right-handed.

## d) Chelae (Heterochelae, Dexiochirae).

Nr. 114a. Carcinus maenas (Rayer, 1851, 11, without figure; transmitted by M. Valenciennes). "... The claw-leg of the right side normal. The corresponding claw-leg of the left side, weaker than the right one, ends in two claws, of which the one is directed in the direction of the claw of the opposite side and toward the body-axis, and the other in the opposite sense, so that they form a kind of T with the other segments that bear them. A third supernumerary claw arises from a segment common to the two earlier named. To sum up: the crab has a normal claw on the right side and three small claw-legs on the left." From the incomplete description of this case, which unfortunately also has no figure attached, one may indeed conclude that it is a typical triple-break-formation on a Z-claw, but one can scarcely sketch a more exact picture of the phenomenon.

Nr. 114b. C. maenas (Bateson, 1894, 534, Abb. 190 I; Coll. Surg. Mus. London). "After removal of the dactylopodite of a left claw, one perceives, viewing from the dorsal side — which in the crab-claw lies above — three indices projecting side by side in front of the joint-surface of the dactylus. They have the typical symmetry of a Batesonian triple-formation; none of them would, with the claw closed, strike upon the dactylus. The outermost-standing index corresponds most closely to a normal one and is also designated as such by Bateson. Then, in the case of the innermost-standing one, it is a matter of an index broken inward from the base onward — for which the somewhat inclined direction of the cutting edges also speaks — in the case of the middle one, of the inverse component grown out of the latter's proximal fracture surface, while the outermost-standing one represents the regeneration of the stump that remained standing and therefore corresponds most closely to the normal index direction. All three indices have the fine toothing and the elongated form of the Z-claw, which in Carcinus maenas normally stands on the left.

Nr. 115. *C. maenas* (Taf. XX, Abb. XV), (after Phot. from Carlgren, Zool. Inst. Univ. Stockholm). The left chela of one of the specimens has the dactylopodite bent upward at more than a right angle and bears, on the fracture surfaces, a supernumerary dactylopodite of somewhat smaller size. Very clearly the course of the punctation of the middle component is to be recognized in inverse direction from that of the original dactylus. The cutting-edges of the index and of the original dactylus bear large teeth, differing little from one another and otherwise just like those of the left Z-chela of this crab-species, which corresponds to such a size. The supernumerary formations [Zusatzgebilde] have much smaller and more numerous teeth, as is typical for smaller specimens or for regenerates of Z-chelae.

Nr. 116. *C. maenas* (own sketch after College of Surgeons Museum, Cat.-No. 377; Descriptive Catalogue of the Teratological Series by B. Thompson, Taylor & Francis, London, 1893, p. 97: »a male common Shore-crab ... purchased«). Similar to the previous specimen, corresponding to the smaller animal-size all cutting-edges are developed as finely-toothed Z-chelae; the lowermost component is not clearly recognizable as a regenerate. Right typical K-chela.

Nr. 117. *C. maenas* (own sketch after British Museum, London; probably identical with Bateson, 1894, 531, Abb. 184 I). Still finer than the previous specimen, hence all cutting-edges still more finely toothed, otherwise quite analogous. Both supernumerary formations are clearly regenerates.

Nr. 118. *C. maenas* (Duns, 1888, 75, Taf. II, Abb. 1; seen from in front). Likewise a triple-formation at a break of the dactylopodite on a left Z-chela of one of the smaller specimens, all cutting-edges finely toothed. It might be questionable which dactylus is to be regarded as the original one, yet the comparison with the foregoing cases speaks for the one standing farthest from the index; its somewhat coarser teeth and the interruption of the furrow-punctation proximally, on the other hand its continuation into the inverse component, give almost full certainty.

Nr. 119. *C. maenas* (Lucas, 1844, 41, Taf. I, Abb. 2, 2a; Bateson, 1894, 531, Abb. 184 II). In this triple-formation at a break on a left dactylopodite it is a matter, in contrast to the foregoing cases and to the figure-designation chosen by Bateson, of an inverse position toward a break to the index. The original dactylus was, under a right angle, broken off at the index, so that it sank, bending forward and downward, on the inner or inside of the same. From its proximal-turned fracture surface there arose the inverse component, in which each of the longitudinal furrows continues from the longitudinal furrow of the broken dactylus. From the old fracture surface of the stump the third component regenerates, whose furrows, completely separated from one another, derive from the old furrows of the stump. As a consequence of the space narrowed by the inverse component, this third component has been pressed into the position assumed at the side of the dactylus when the chela is fully opened. The dentition of the original, longest dactylus corresponds to that of the index and to the Z-type normal for a crab of this size-grade of the left chela. Somewhat shorter and more uniformly toothed is the inverse component, while the third, slightly longer than the inverse, exhibits a very fine dentition strongly reminiscent of regeneration.

Nr. 120. *Portunus puber* (Goltz de Carvalho, 1894, 125, 2 figs.). Right chela with typical triple-formation at a break of the right index. Carvalho raises the question whether it arose regeneratively or as a malformation; this could only be decided by experiments in the aquarium. In the present case it is, if the drawings are exactly correct, a matter of a regenerated chela: the right chela in *Portunus puber* should namely be larger than the left, and at the base of the dactylopodite-cutting-edge the so-called »clapper-teeth« [»Klöppelzähne«] should sit, as is to be seen in the following case Nr. 121 on the figure (cf. Przibram, 1905, *Heterochelie*, p. 209). In regeneration the clapper-tooth at first does not appear (cf. Przibram, 1902, p. 523), and on Carvalho's figures nothing of such a tooth is to be seen, while index and dactylus of the left chelae appear larger than those of the right. The dentition of all cutting-edges seems to be the same and might correspond to the Z-type; yet in the absence of a clear difference at the index of the normal K- and Z-chelae no more certain conclusion as to the type can be drawn here.

Nr. 121. *P. puber* (Le Sénéchal, 1888, 123, Abb. 4; Bateson, 1894, 535, Abb. 191 II, erroneously designated as *Carcinus*). The right K-chela bears, at the outer edge of the propodite from the base of the index, two supernumerary ones springing out, the homologous joints fused together along their front surfaces. The denticulation of all three indices is the same, but difficult to distinguish as K-type, since the clapper-tooth characteristic for the right K-chela is normally located here only on the dactylopodite. The case is in any event proof that in analogous cases it is not always a matter of wholly fused entire chelae, but of two mirror-image indices.

Nr. 122. *P. puber* (Le Sénéchal, 1888, 123, Abb. 5; Bateson, 1894, 535, Abb. 192). The left Z-chela bears, at the outer edge of the propodite from the base of the index, a cone-shaped process springing out, which according to the course of the punctation must be judged to consist of two indices fused with one another at the tip.

Nr. 123. *P. (Callinectes) sapidus* (Rathbun, 1896, 374, Taf. XXIII, Abb.; ⅓ reduced). The right chela bears, beside the diverging chela-fingers, two fingers fused laterally almost to the tip, so that in all three indices and above them three dactyli stand. With Rathbun's chosen position of recording, the cutting-edges of the dactyli cannot be seen; those of the indices allow the mirror-image symmetry of the supernumerary formations to be recognized, but, on the basis already mentioned previously, no determination of the K-type at the teeth. Yet in the picture the propodus-angle seems to project strongly forward over against all three dactylus-bases, as is normally the case with the K-chela in connection with the stronger excision of the index-base for the clapper-tooth of the dactylus.

Nr. 124. *P. (Callinectes) sapidus* (Rathbun, 1896, 374, Taf. XXIII, Abb.; ⅓ reduced). The left Z-chela bears, at the base of the index-cutting-edge, a process toothed on both sides, which is to be regarded as a pair of supernumerary indices fused with one another on the outer surfaces. The index allows a blunt-angled bending to be recognized, the angle of which corresponds to the abnormal formation.

Nr. 125. *P. (Callinectes) hastatus* (Faxon, 1881, 262, Taf. II, Abb. 5, seen from in front). On the left chela there are three dactylopodites, each of which is too short in relation to the index. All three lie approximately with it in one plane, although the tip of the nearest-lying dactylus crosses the index somewhat behind. It cannot be established with certainty whether this branch was the original dactylus — as Faxon assumes — that is, whether the break occurred downward, or whether the uppermost-lying dactylus was the original one, which was broken upward. The dentition is the same on all branches, but allows, as remarked above, no compelling conclusion as to belonging to the Z-type.

Nr. 126. *P. (Callinectes) sp.?* (Leavitt, 1909, 89, Abb.; Cole, 1910, 266, Abb. 9). The right chela bears, at the dactylus-tip, which—bent upward—was broken off at the clapper-edges, the side of the supernumerary dactylopodite. The denticulation is everywhere the same; on account of the absence of proximal parts it already allows no conclusion as to the type of the supernumerary dactyli. Yet the chela lacks, according to the drawing, the clapper-tooth completely, and it is overall probably a matter of a Z-regenerate in place of an earlier K-chela. The whole repeated analogy of the supernumerary formation of Leavitt and similar misinterpretations have already been refuted by Cole.

Nr. 127. *P. (Callinectes) sapidus* (Rathbun, 1896, 374, Taf. XXIII, Abb.; ⅓ reduced). The left Z-chela bears, at the dactylopodite in the middle, angle-shaped, bent upward against the index, a process raised, which is to be regarded as a pair of supernumerary dactyli completely fused with the cutting-edges.

Nr. 128. *Eriphia spinifrons* (Herklots, 1871, 69, Abb. 5—8; Bateson, 1894, 528, Nr. 812, Abb. 182 I, II). Very large specimen, 34 mm carapace-breadth, 25 mm carapace-length, right the K-chela usually standing there. On the left, on the Z-chela standing there as usual, index and dactylus are bent downward at almost a right angle to the limb-longitudinal-axis, and from the break a supernumerary pair has grown in the typical manner. The dactyli have unfolded themselves freely, whereas the indices are completely fused at the clapper-edges, so that there stands only a both-sided toothed process over against the corresponding dactyli. All cutting-edges have the typical K-formation; likewise the slender shape of all three dactyli also betrays the Z-type. Bateson establishes here, in a footnote on p. 528: »In relation to this case Herklots establishes that the right chela in the norm is larger and otherwise deviates from the left (1 out of 8 behaving the reverse in this relation). It seems from the figure indeed not that such a differentiation was present in the supernumerary pair, but in future cases it should be sought after.«

Nr. 129. *E. spinifrons* (Taf. XX, Abb. XVI—XVII), (after Phot. from Prof.

Curt Herbst, Heidelberg; seen from above and from in front; somewhat reduced). The left normal small K-chela of Z-form. The right K-chela bears, at the basal-angle of the upward-bent dactylus, two supernumerary dactyli, which exhibit the typical relations of the triple-formations at a break and whose length stands back somewhat behind that of the original dactylus. All cutting-edges have the same K-type and clearly allow the clapper-tooth at the base to be recognized.

Nr. 130. *E. spinifrons* (Taf. XX, Abb. XVIII), (orig. phot., ♂ received from Cori at Trieste, Cat.-No. 333 of the Museum B. V. A.; somewhat reduced). Carapace-breadth 8, carapace-length 6 cm; left (compensatorily) enlarged Z-chela, right smaller Z-chela, whose dactylopodite is bent upward by more than a right angle and from whose fracture-angle a pair of supernumerary dactyli sprouts, which are somewhat, but only a little, shorter than the bent-off one. Very clearly there can be recognized, by the continuity of the dark-brown, sharply set-off coloring of the dorsal dactylus-base with the same coloring of the bent-off dactylus, the origin of this latter; likewise, through the interruption of this coloring between the two supernumerary ones, that here no common base is present. All cutting-edges bear Z-type, the clapper-tooth does not stand out. The right chela itself also appears, according to size, shape and sculpture, as a Z-chela which has grown in place of an originally K-chela.

Nr. 130a. *E. spinifrons* (♂, Museum B. V. A., Cat.-No. 322b). Small specimen, which has on its left, enlarged chela a regenerating index, which is bent upward toward the dactylus and possibly shows further rudiments toward a triple-formation at a break.

The triple-formations at a break in *Eriphia spinifrons* are therefore of particular interest, because it can be demonstrated with the greatest probability that these malformations are in nature not of native, but of regenerative origin. As I have already shown earlier (Przibram, Exp. Stud. üb. Reg. II, 1902), this species belongs to those heterochelous crab-species which, on loss of the K-chela, undertake a »reversal of the chelae« [»Scherenumkehr«]. In a later communication (»Heterochelie«, 1905, p. 214) the predominant right-handedness, in the meantime observed also by Duncker (1904), was confirmed; at the same time, however, I traced the left-handed specimens back to regeneration. This tracing-back furnished the explanation of the fact mentioned by Duncker, that the left-handed specimens are on average larger than the right-handed ones. I could not at that time yet determine »whether the difference of the chelae in the right- and left-handed ones is equally pronounced«, since »sufficient material for my own investigation« was not at that time at my disposal. Since then I have, in the years 1910 and 1911, thanks to the kindness of Herr Prof. Cori from the zoological station at Trieste, received a larger number of Eriphiae, which with full certainty proves the stronger development of the right K-chela over against that of the left K-chela. In the adjoining little table the numerical data are given. While the great majority of right-handed Eriphiae exhibit a typically developed K-chela, in the left-handed ones the K-chela is only quite exceptionally fully typical. With regard to the typical or atypical development of the K-chela it must be kept in mind that the well-developed K-chela (Taf. XX, Abb. XIX and XX) exhibits a complete smoothing of the outer surface of the propodite, whereas the Z-chela bears several rows of cone-shaped knobs. These knobs now remain visible also on the left chela of the left-handers (Taf. XX, Abb. XXII and XXIII) when the toothing has already come close to the typical character of the K-chela. The same holds for those cases in which both chelae, by a smaller difference in size, allow one to conclude a both-sided but almost completed regeneration (Taf. XX, Abb. XXI). The two triple-formations at a break (Nr. 129 and 130) of the right chela now still show the raised knobs on both chelae. In Nr. 129 (Taf. XX, Abb. XVI and XVII) the right chela is much larger than the left; it is probably a matter of both-sided regeneration, and on the right-sided regenerate the break with subsequent triple-formation then arose. On account of the simultaneous loss, the original right-handedness should be re-established. In the other specimen, Nr. 130 (Taf. XX, Abb. XVIII), the right chela is already recognizable as a regenerate by its small size and is a Z-chela which is typically developed except for the triple-formation at a break. The non-deformed

*K* = Knoten- oder Knackschere [knot- or cracking-chela].
*Z* = Zähnchen- oder Zangenschere [little-tooth- or pincer-chela].

(*Z l.* reg.) | **Linkshänder** [Left-handers] — *K l.* typisch (*Z r.*) | *K l.* typisch fehlt | *K* atypisch zu groß [too large] | *K* atypisch Übergang [transition] | fehlt (*Z r.*) | *Z l.* (*Z r.* reg.) | **Rechtshänder** typisch % | atypisch % | **Linkshänder** typisch % | atypisch % |
