| » h | *Broscus* | *cephalotes* | 8 | 545 | 24 | » [left] | 10. | 10./11. | hintereinander [behind one another] | hinterer Ast kürzer, nicht dünner [posterior branch shorter, not thinner] |

## b) Claw-bearing appendages of the Hexapoda.

### α) Coleoptera.

**Nr. 151.** *Cerambyx scopolii*, Bateson (1894, S. 491, Nr. 753, Abb. 160), after a specimen of Gadeau de Kerville. Out of the forward-widened hip-recess [Hüftausnehmung] of the right fore-breast [Vorderbrust] there arise two fused, and in front of them yet one single, hip-members [Hüftglieder], each of which bears a complete leg. The middle leg has the symmetry of the opposite side and is — just like the one arising on a single coxa further forward — more slender than the one lying farthest rearward. This last one represents, according to Bateson, with whom I here can concur, the normal leg, which, in my view, bent backward in the hip [Hüfte], let the two supernumerary, more slender legs arise out of the break-wound that had formed. The whole presents a typical break-triple-formation lying in one plane.

**Nr. 151a.** *Pyrodes speciosus*, Bateson (1894, S. 496, Nr. 759, without Abb.), from the Hope-Collection in Oxford. This longhorn-beetle [Bockkäfer] has a quite similar triple-formation to the previous one, only it is the left middle leg [Mittelbein] which is represented by three legs, and the normal component lies farthest forward, while the two others insert behind it on a fused coxa (and somewhat dorsally, as in Nr. 151).

**Nr. 152.** *Prionus coriarius*, Krause (1888, S. 125, Abb.), Bateson (1894, S. 484, Nr. 739). The right hind-breast [Hinterbrust] of this Leather-longhorn [Lederbock] bears a strongly broadened hip-member, on which sit two legs fused up to the end of the femur [Schenkelende], and outside of these yet a small hip-member with a single leg. This one, and the leg neighboring it and mirror-image symmetric, are shorter and weaker than the innermost leg, which I regard as the original right hind-leg, pushed away against the midline of the body by a break of the coxa. Three tarsal members of the middle, and one of the outer, component seem to have been torn off subsequently — perhaps during the imaginal molt [Imaginalhäutung], so dangerous for such abnormalities.

**Nr. 153.** *Melolontha vulgaris*, Wiedemann (1819, S. 125, Taf. II, Abb. 1), Bateson (1894, S. 512). In place of the right hind-leg there are three legs, of which two represent a supernumerary »pair«. The cockchafer could use all of them for walking; they therefore probably had their own hip-members. Which one represents the original leg is not to be discerned, since all components are developed somewhat weaker than the normal left hind-leg.

**Nr. 154.** *Rhizotrogus castaneus*, Bassi (1834, S. 373, Taf. VIIa), Asmuss (1835), Bateson (1894, S. 512). Right foreleg, including the trochanters, a typical break-triple-formation; original leg indistinguishable, since all components full and scarcely weaker developed than the leg of the opposite side. The three branches lie behind one another.

**Nr. 155.** *Aromia moschata*, Kolbe (1889, S. 169, Abb. 1–2), Bateson (1894, S. 503, Nr. 775). Right fore-coxa bears, behind one another, two trochanters, of which the anterior goes out into a simple right leg, the posterior into two legs separated only from the middle of the femur onward; the middle component of the break-triple-formation has, as usual, the symmetry of the opposite side. The right mandible was strongly shortened and toothless.

**Nr. 155a.** *Agestrata dehaanii*, Bateson (1894, S. 491, Nr. 752, without Abb.). »The coxa of the right foreleg [of this leaf-horned beetle, Blatthornkäfer] has two articulations, an anterior and a posterior. With the anterior there articulates a single trochanter, bearing a normal right leg. The posterior articulation bears a large formation, which consists of trochanters united with one another. This double trochanter bears two legs and is so positioned that these two do not lie in the same horizontal plane; rather, the more posterior supernumerary leg lies in the same plane with the normal one, while the more anterior supernumerary leg is pushed out toward the ventral side, between the normal leg and the more posterior supernumerary leg.« Apart from this twisting [Verdrehung], the typical symmetry-relations of the break-triple-formation are found; all components were equal to one another in size, development, and color.

No. 156. *Calosoma inquisitor*, Bickhardt (1908, p. 1, fig.). Collected by F. Schwager near Erfurt, but no longer in his collection. The strongly broadened coxal segment of the left hind leg bears three legs, of which at least two still have their own trochanters. The position of the legs given in the figure does not correspond — as Bickhardt too considers possible — to that in the life of this pupa-robber, for the leg marked *c*, which has the symmetry of the opposite side, evidently belongs pushed forward between the two other components, and the rounded trochanter belongs to the leg marked *a*. From the figure it cannot be decided which was the original leg, since all three components are complete and, but for slight cripplings, of the dimensions of the normal right hind leg.

No. 157. *Scarites pyracmon*, Lefebvre (1831, p. 40, Pl. XL, figs. 1–2), Asmuss (1835), Bateson (1894, p. 500, No. 765): "At the base of the posterior trochanteral surface of the normal left foreleg, immediately above the cotyloid articulation, an elongate lanceolate segment was implanted. This segment was directed rearward and represented a pair of trochanters fused along their anterior surfaces. At each of the two tips of the doubled trochanter a complete leg was articulated, in every respect formed as a foreleg. The two moved as a complementary pair." Typical symmetry of the triple-formation at a break.

No. 157a. *Agra catenulata*, Stannius (1835, p. 306, fig. 13), Bateson (1894, p. 512). Not completely preserved triple-formation, similar to No. 157, but concerning the left hind leg of a ground beetle.

No. 158. *Carabus graecus*, v. Heyden (1881, p. 105, fig. 25), Bateson (1894, p. 486, No. 744): "The trochanter of the right middle leg bears a supernumerary pair of legs with common trochanter, femur, tibia, and 1st tarsal segment. The coxa of the normal leg is enlarged and the trochanter has two heads, of which the anterior belongs to the supernumerary pair of legs. The femur of the extra pair is a single piece, but morphologically double, representing two structurally anterior and two structurally ventral surfaces, the latter being inclined to one another at an angle of about 120°." The double nature of the tibia and of the 1st tarsal segment is, moreover, recognizable from the spination. "The 1st tarsal segment ends in two articular facets; but while the posterior one bears a complete four-segmented continuation, the [other] bears only a single rudimentary segment, of which possibly something has been broken off, but this is not certain." Apart from bendings of the femora and the mentioned fusions, typical triple-formation at a break.

No. 159. *Carabus Scheidleri*, f., Kraatz (1873, p. 432, fig. 9), Bateson (1894, p. 483, No. 736, fig. 155): "A pair of supernumerary legs with common femoral portion springing from the trochanter of the right foreleg . . . The trochanter bears a leg articulating as usual. Immediately ventral to this articulation is a second one upon a small elevation. This bears a doubled femur, which is composed of parts of a pair of femora fused along their dorsal margins." Apart from the absence of the claws of the middle component, typical triple-formation at a break.

No. 160. *Carabus nemoralis*, Tornier (1900, p. 556, figs. 31, 31a). In this ground beetle the left middle-leg trochanter ends in two branches standing one above the other, separated by a scar, the upper of which runs out into a single femur together with tibia and a tarsus broken off at the 1st segment, while the lower bears a pair of femora fused along their upper sides, as is evident from the indentation-grooves laid down on the upper and lower sides for the two separate tibiae, so that the doubled femur is bounded by two undersides. As usual, the middle component of the triple-formation at a break has inverse symmetry, as follows from the reversed sequence of the tibial end-spurs. The tarsus of this inverse tibia is broken off, that of the third component fully developed.

Besides the tearing-wound at the trochanter, there is also a scar between the points of articulation of the two tibiae on the doubled femur, and furthermore a compression of the left wing-cover over the monstrous leg can be recognized.

Tornier traces the latter crippling, no doubt rightly, just as he does the breaking-off of foot-segments, back to the difficulty of the imaginal molt. On the other hand, I cannot agree with his account of the larval injury that led to the tripling of the leg. He has the bending force act upon the upper side of the femur and there at first produce a gaping wound, which is still visible between the tibial articulations and from which the inverse tibia took its regenerative origin. It then reads, further on (p. 557): "In correlation with its malformation this femur has, in its anterior part, formed two undersides out of itself — that is, without external compulsion . . . . This is easy to recognize, for whereas on every truly normal beetle femur the upper side is smooth and rounded, while the underside bears a longitudinal groove into which the lower leg can be folded in jackknife fashion, the present malformed beetle femur bears two such indentation-grooves, one on its upper, the other on its underside.

What is very important here is that the upper one of these indentation-grooves is not even usable at all, for the additional femur has grown out of the parent femur upward, yet so obliquely rearward, that the tibia attached to it cannot be folded into its groove at all. Proof that this groove, too, did not arise out of considerations of usefulness, but solely out of the need of the organism, wherever a tibia is produced, also to produce an indentation-groove for it."

From a second wound, which this middle leg had suffered at the trochanter through tearing on its upper side as a consequence of the downward pressing of the femur, the upper additional leg is then said to have grown out.

That the inversely regenerating component should exert such an influence upon the femur in a proximal direction, so as to produce an indentation-groove for its tibia, stands in such contradiction to everything we know about the decrease of regenerative potencies in a distal direction, that only the most compelling arguments could force me to this assumption. One cannot, indeed, in view of Tornier's emphasis on the non-foldability of the inverse tibia, even think of any functional form-production. But it is by no means necessary to make a second wound at the femoral end responsible for the inverse tibia. Rather, a gaping wound at the trochanter suffices to give rise, just as in the preceding and following cases, to two additional formations that fuse with the first segments as a consequence of colliding in a confined space. Probably, too, it is not the branch designated by Tornier that is the original tibia, but the one ending in the first tarsal segment. On this, a force pushed the tibial end upward and produced a tear at the ventral tension-apex of the trochanter. Out of this — where a scar is still clearly to be seen — there grew from the original trochanter, proximally, a new foot, as usual with inverse symmetry, and soon, owing to its position perpendicular to the plane of injury, it collided with a correctly growing regenerate of the trochanter from the second, distally facing wound-surface, and fused with it as far as the diverging segments of the symmetrical pair were not able to overcome the confinement of space.

In Bateson's terminology, the supernumerary pair would be situated in position V (ventral); the monstrosity, apart from the slight, probably secondary, curvature of the branches, follows entirely from this plane, in accordance with Bateson's rule.

No. 160a. *Clythra quadripunctata*, *Mocquerys (1880, p. 42, fig.), Bateson (1894, p. 508, No. 781, without fig.). From the anterior surface of the left front trochanter springs a pair of supernumerary complete legs, both of which turn the dorsal side of the anterior surface toward the normal leg, and indeed the one furnished with the same symmetry stands below, the one with the opposite [symmetry] above the horizontal plane of the normal leg. The causative wound must therefore have had a proximally directed wound-margin dorsal to the normal femoral articulation and a distally directed one ventrally. It is, however, impossible to form a precise idea without a renewed examination of the object.

No. 161. *Eurycephalus maxillosus*, *Mocquerys (1880, p. 54, fig.), after Bateson (1894, p. 487, No. 745, fig. 157): "The right anterior femur [of this longhorn beetle] divides at the base into two parts, of which the posterior bears a normal leg. The other part of the femur is bilaterally symmetrical, composed of the anterior surfaces of two femora, for both sides show the same convexity, in that neither of them is flattened like the posterior surface of a normal anterior femur. With the tip of this segment articulates a bilaterally symmetrical tibia of special breadth, bearing a 1st and 2nd tarsal segment, each almost of double breadth. The 2nd tarsal segment bears two 3rd tarsal segments." Of the claw-segments not all are any longer preserved on the specimen kept in the Museum at Rouen.

Typical triple-formation at a break after tearing of the femur near the base at the anterior margin (Bateson's position A = anterior).

No. 161a. *Acinopus lepelletieri*, *Mocquerys (1880, p. 41, fig.), after Bateson (1894, p. 498, No. 763, without fig.): "Two supernumerary legs [of a ground beetle] springing from the posterior surface of the base of the femur on the left middle leg. By its position the most anterior seems to be the normal one, but this is doubtful. The arrangement is almost that of position DP [dorsal-posterior], but since one of the femora is constricted and bent, the relations are rather irregular."

No. 162. *Geotrupes mutator*, Frivaldsky (1886, p. 79, Tab. IV, fig. 10), Bateson (1894, p. 504, No. 766). The right foreleg of this dung beetle is tripled from the half of the broadened femur onward, all three components equally well developed and arranged exactly according to the rules of the triple-formation at a break. In contrast to Bateson, who takes the most anterior branch for the normal one and therefore assumes position P (= posterior), I would take the rearmost for the original femur displaced rearward by a bending force, from whose anterior tension-apex wound the remaining femora have completed themselves; that would then be, according to Bateson's terminology, A.

No. 162a. *Melolontha vulgaris*, Doumerc (1834, p. 171, fig. 1). Femur of the right foreleg with cuff-like broadening, from which a typical triple-formation at a break springs. The one component has, instead of five, only two tarsal segments, but still claws.

No. 163. *Alaus sordidus*, Westwood (1847, Tab. XXV, fig. 9), Bateson (1894, p. 508, No. 780); click beetle from Ceylon. From the middle of the right middle femur there rises, postero-dorsally, an irregular swelling with a pair of supernumerary legs. Other cripplings of the legs make exact statements impossible, but the middle component of the triple-formation has the symmetry of the opposite side, the two others are proper right middle legs.

No. 164. *Silpha nigrita*, *Mocquerys (1880, p. 43, fig. 3), after Bateson (1894, p. 501, fig. 157). "From the right middle femur springs a pair of legs, which are completely united up to the tips of the last tarsal segment. The point of origin of the supernumerary limbs is at the anterior and ventral margin of the femur . . . The adjacent surfaces in this pair of legs, which are therefore obliterated, are chiefly the morphologically anterior and, to a certain degree, the dorsal surfaces. The plantar or ventral surfaces of the last tarsal segments are inclined to one another even more obliquely than those of the tibiae, so that the curvatures of the two pairs of claws are turned almost entirely forward as well as away from one another. This is not quite brought out in the figure. The position of origin is approximately VP, but the claws are in the position VPP."

No. 165. *Strataegus antaeus*, Jayne (1880, p. 159, fig. 10), Bateson (1894, p. 512). The left (according to the figure, right) middle leg has the femur tripled at least in the last third, but probably still further basalward. All three femora bear complete tibiae with tarsi belonging — and what is most remarkable — to the same symmetry. It thus appears to be one of those rare cases of triple-formations which I have above, in other animal groups, designated as "slit triple-formations" and which we can conceive of as having arisen through regeneration from the two wound-margins, running parallel to the original limb, of an elongate wounding lying in the longitudinal axis of the affected body part.

No. 166. *Sillis ruficollis*, Bateson (1894, p. 498, No. 764, fig. 165): "Soft-skinned [beetle], right foreleg bears a supernumerary appendage. The coxa and the trochanter are normal. The femur is about twice as thick from front to back as a normal femur and shows at its tip two articulations in the same plane. Of these the anterior bears normal tibia and tarsus, but the posterior bears a supernumerary tibia, which at first glance seems to be a simple structure. This tibia is more slender than the normal one and is provided with four tarsal segments, in that the end segment is shriveled and without claws. On closer examination it was found that this supernumerary tibia is in reality composed of the posterior surfaces of a pair of tibiae not separated from one another . . . . The normal tibia is not bilaterally symmetrical about its midline. On the contrary, the anterior surface is distinguished from the posterior by several points . . . The anterior spur is long, but the posterior short. The hairs on the surface of the tibia are also asymmetrically arranged, and the parting or division between them is not median, but nearer to the anterior margin. But in the supernumerary part there is no such differentiation . . . The hairs part in the middle and the spurs . . . are formed as posterior ones. This supernumerary structure is therefore put together from the two posterior margins of a right and a left tibia in position P."

Typical symmetry of a triple-formation at a break, which will owe its origin to a wound that arose at the tip of the femur from the posterior margin.

No. 167. *Metrius contractus*, Jayne (1880, p. 156, fig. 3a), Bateson (1894, p. 503, No. 770). The femur of the left middle leg of this ground beetle bears, ventrally and somewhat posteriorly, near its normal termination — which leads to a somewhat too short and bent tibia — a pair of fused tibiae with free tarsi. Typical symmetry of the triple-formation at a break, as follows from the spurs of the tibial ends.

No. 167a. *Chrysomela graminis*, Bateson (1894, p. 498, No. 761, without fig.): "The femur of the right middle leg [of this leaf beetle] bears a supernumerary pair of legs attached to the posterior and upper side of its tip. At this place an articulation is present, with which the simple proximal part of the supernumerary pair of tibiae articulates. This piece, which belongs in common to the two supernumerary tibiae, is a rounded, formless mass from which the two tibiae diverge. Each of the two tibiae bears a complete foot, except that the more rearward one has only one claw. In color the two supernumerary tarsi differ from the normal ones, in that they are brown instead of metallic green, but the tibiae are of normal color. From the form of the joints and the arrangement of the hairing it is clear that the surfaces of the legs which are by nature adjacent were formed as posterior sides, and the forms of the two are complementary to one another, in that the rearmost of the supernumerary legs is formed as a left one, the foremost as a right one." A secondary bending forward changes nothing in the typical symmetry for the triple-formation at a break.

Interesting in this case is the clear indication of regeneration, which lies, besides in the position-rules, also in the amorphous point of origin and in the deviating coloration of the supernumerary tarsi, for regenerates are indeed often deviatingly colored.

No. 168. *Cetonia floricola*, Tornier (1900, p. 548, fig. 26). The left middle leg of this rose-chafer has, from the end of the femur onward, a typical triple-formation at a break, whose supernumerary components, fused up to the claw-base, stand behind the original leg and also still reach down into the broadened femoral end, as the indentation-groove for the supernumerary tibiae proves.

No. 169. *Odontaeus armiger*, Tornier (1900, p. 550, fig. 27). The right middle leg of this dung beetle is tripled almost exactly corresponding to the previous case, except that the wounding or wound occurred ventral to the normal leg, hence the mirror-image additional legs fused with the dorsal sides.

(No. 169a.) *Odontaeus mobilicornis*, Gerstaecker (1866–1879, p. 201, fig. 41h), identical with the previous case; the nomenclature changed in the meantime.

No. 170. *Melolontha vulgaris*, Kolbe (1889, p. 169, fig.), Bateson (1894, p. 484, No. 740). The right hind leg of this cockchafer corresponds completely to the previous cases, only the tarsi are unfused throughout their whole extent. The inverse component has a much more weakly developed foot.

No. 170a. *Meloe proscarabaeus*, Heyden (1836, p. 761), *Mocquerys (1880, p. 52, fig.), Bateson (1894, p. 488, No. 748, without fig.). The right hind leg of this oil beetle bears at the anterior side of the femoral end a second termination, with which a common joint of two otherwise free supernumerary tibiae and tarsi, mirror-image symmetrical as usual, articulates. — In the collection of L. von Heyden.

No. 170b. *Pimelia bipunctata*, *Mocquerys (1880, p. 44, fig.), Bateson (1894, p. 498, No. 762, without fig.). The left hind femur of this darkling beetle gives origin, postero-dorsally to its tip, to two typically mirror-image supernumerary tibiae. Tarsi broken off.

No. 170c. *Julodis aequinoctialis*, *Mocquerys (1880, p. 47, fig.), after Bateson (1894, p. 503, No. 773, without fig.). "The supernumerary legs [of this jewel beetle] spring from the posterior and ventral side of the tibial base of the left middle leg . . . . The two supernumerary legs are fused with the tibiae and the first four tarsal segments; the 5th tarsal segments are free, but only one is left over; the earlier presence of the other shows itself only at the joint-facet. The normal tibia is constricted and bent at one place, so that it does not stand normally. The femoro-tibial joint is immovable." Typical triple-formation at a break.

No. 170d. *Hylotrupes bajulus*, *Mocquerys (1880, p. 53, fig.), after Bateson (1894, p. 494, No. 756, without fig.): "The right middle tibia [of this longhorn beetle] bears a pair of supernumerary legs with common proximal parts. From the antero-dorsal side of the base of the normal tibia there rises a slender tibial piece, which is not so long as the normal tibia and bears no spurs. At the tip of this supernumerary tibia, which is undoubtedly a double formation, there articulate a pair of tarsi fused at the 1st and 2nd segments." The lesser development of the additional structures permits the conclusion of a later wounding as the cause of the triple-formation at a break, in comparison with No. 170e.

No. 171. *Carabus graecus*, Tornier (1900, p. 534, fig. 30): "This malformed [ground] beetle is, for three reasons, of the highest importance. First, it proves, better than all previously examined ones, that in beetles . . . it is external forces which clear the way for the development of super-regeneration; then it lets us recognize, secondly, down to the last detail, the causes of origin of that polymely in which the doubled limb-section is a double structure; and thirdly, one can perceive on it just as clearly how a trimely is laid down." A "bending force gripped with the blunt edge of the [knife the] tibia on the eye-side quite far down so violently that the tibial skin at the point of attack was dented inward, whereby at the two end-points of the denting a tearing-wound arose in each case. Then the force pushed the tibia, by bending it inward, before itself, until it was fixed in the knee-joint, and now it also bent the beetle femur downward and kinked it at its pressure-side at the bending-apex. In doing so, at this place the little-foldable femoral skin loosened itself from the underlying tissue and laid itself together in pressure-folds, so irrefutably proving that here a strong pressure-force acted upon the femur. The dent, which was thus produced by the attacking force in the outer side of the tibia, is very deep, for the tibia was in doing so almost entirely compressed."

From the wound-surface lying proximal and distal to the denting there has grown, in each case, a tibial end — recognizable from the spination — and a rudimentary leg. The more proximal one consists of four, the more distal one of only a single segment; claws are not present. Neither from the description nor from the figure of Tornier can it be recognized with certainty whether the latter rudimentary leg had the symmetry of the opposite side — which would have been to determine from the spination of the tibial end — yet this is most highly probable.

No. 172. *Rhizotrogus solstitialis*, Tornier (1900, p. 534, fig. 29). June beetle with completely regular triple-formation at a break from the middle of the anterior margin on the left middle leg. The supernumerary pair of legs fused up to the tibial end, whose free spines [are] just as long as at the normal tibial end, but the tarsi more slender.

No. 173. *Eutrachelus temminckii*, Tornier (1900, p. 552, fig. 28). Weevil whose right hind leg is tripled, similarly to the previous case, from the middle of the tibia onward; yet it seems to me doubtful whether the rearmost component, despite the weakest tarsal size, as Tornier assumes, is the original one, or rather not the most anterior component of the double branch, which lacks the claws, represents the original leg. On the latter interpretation, the super-regeneration would spring from a posterior wound, on the former — as in No. 172 — from an anterior one.

No. 173a. *Blaps mortisaga*, Tornier (1900, p. 553, without fig.): "In this [darkling] beetle a pressing force entered into the tibia [of the right hind leg] on the outer side, quite shortly before the tip, from below, and produced there a deep wound with two wound-surfaces. Each of these then formed, to the ordinary leg-section lying peripheral to the wound, a counterpart. Both additional structures were thereby laid down with independent tips, but fused with one another in their lower parts and thereby became an incomplete double structure. Their tibial sections are fused right up to the tips, while these themselves, as well as their foot-joint plates, have remained entirely free. The foot-joint plates, like the feet attached to them, thereby turn their sole-surfaces toward one another." It is therefore, according to Tornier's description, a matter of a typical triple-formation at a break in Bateson's position A (= anterior).

(No. 173b.) *Blaps mortisaga*, Gerstaecker (1866–1879, p. 201, fig. 41c) is identical with the previous specimen.

No. 174. *Carabus convexus*, Tornier (1900, p. 558, fig. 32). The left foreleg of this ground beetle has a tibia abnormally broadened toward the end, with a supernumerary spine, on the outer side. From a bulge-shaped protrusion of its widening there rises a first tarsal segment drawn out in breadth and divided by a scar into two unequally broad parts: the innermost branch bears the four further tarsal segments with claws, the outer broader one a simple 2nd, then a once-more-forked 3rd tarsal segment, which on the inner narrower branch bears the two further tarsal segments with claws, but of lesser size, and on the outer stronger branch [bears] such [tarsal segments] of more considerable size.

According to Tornier, "the front tibia on the left shows, at its lower end, the beginning of a leg-malformation, whose chief seat lies in the foot, for it consists here of the inseparably fused outer sides of a right and a left tibia." "A normal tibia of this kind bears, namely, on its upper side, only parallel to the inner margin, a longitudinal row of little pits, which are connected by a flat groove, while, on the other hand, the outer margin is without pits. Besides this, it has on the foot-joint plate a spur at each edge. In the present malformed animal, on the contrary, the tibia, which below is strongly broadened, has on its upper side, parallel to each of its side-margins, a row of little pits — thus two instead of one; and besides this it has on the foot-joint plate not two but three spines, two at its inner margin and one at the outer." Together with a reference to the scar-formations coming to light at the two forkings, it reads further: "Incidentally, it cannot be determined whether this additional foot is the section of a right or a left leg, since foot-segments are normally laid down strictly bisymmetrically, the right and left side thus forming alike, and therefore being equivalent to those of the other body-side. But it is certain that the parent-foot of this malformation displays a fixed bending outward, which is especially to be recognized at its second member, on which the outer side is shorter than the inner side." "The mechanical cause of origin of this malformation was," now according to Tornier, "the following: a bending force gripped the foot at its outer side; its point of attack was foot-segment 3, into which it penetrated so deeply that on it a projecting bulge was notched out, which gave occasion to the formation of the upper supernumerary foot-tip. Then the force bent the foot so strongly toward the outer side that it received, at the tension-apex of the bend — that is, in the first segment, at the outer side — a second tearing-wound, whereby the place for the formation of the second supernumerary foot-section was given. The tibial bending observed on this leg, on the other hand, is not a consequence of an injury to the organism through an external force, but a correlation-phenomenon standing in relation to its foot-injury. Backward investigators . . . might say: this tibia required an enlargement of cross-section in order to carry the foot . . . . In fact, however, the origin of this tibial enlargement is to be traced back solely to the fact that the regenerative forces awakened in the organism strove to exert themselves as amply as possible; the possession of a tripled foot is certainly no use to the animal."

The purposelessness of the tripled foot being granted, I am nevertheless unable to concur with Tornier's interpretation. Regenerative forces nowhere appear, in the limbs of the arthropods, in such a manner that they would repeat structures situated proximal to the level of injury. Tornier, however, assumes precisely this for the terminal portion of the tibiae [shanks], which, without being directly affected by the wounding, are supposed to have newly formed a supernumerary spur together with the punctate groove leading to it. If we consider, first of all, a normal foreleg of *Carabus convexus*, which incidentally is built similarly to other species of the genus *Carabus*, viewed from above, where according to Tornier's statement only a row of pits runs along the inner margin, we notice nothing whatever of such a row, but what does strike us is the asymmetrical shape of the foretibia and of the tarsus, which according to Tornier are supposed to exhibit strict bilateral symmetry. This asymmetry consists, in the case of the tibia, in the stronger arching of the inner margin and of the surface at the tibial end, further in the concealment of the spur belonging to the outer edge by the tarsus curving outward, so that only the one tibial-end spur on the inner edge remains visible in the horizontal plane.

If we now turn the leg in such a way that we pass from the strictly dorsal view to a view directed more onto the inner surface, then we now see the row of pits leading to this inner spur, and at the same time there appears, proximal and ventral to it, the hitherto concealed second tibial spur. If we turn it still further, up to the ventral view, then we see, leading to this second spur as well — which now projects into the 1st tarsal segment — a row of pits running. In the fourth position, finally, this row of pits running to the second spur remains visible, while the one running to the inner spur disappears. According to the homology with the truly bilaterally symmetrical foretibiae of other beetles, we have to regard the second spur as the outer spur, and the asymmetry turns out to be a proximal-ventral displacement of this terminal thorn taking place. Still more extremely than in *Carabus*, this displacement is carried out in the genus *Calathus*, concerning which the cases next to be described inform us, whose significance had already been recognized by Bateson. The occurrence of a second row of pits leading to a spur at the eye-margin, on the terminal portion of the abnormal tibia, therefore need not be regarded as the inner margin of a second counter-symmetrical tibia, but can be traced back to the failure of the normally proximal-ventrally occurring displacement of the outer angle with its thorn. But since, besides this, a second thorn appears in its normal position below the inner-margin thorn, the cause of this supernumerary formation, together with the omitted rotation, must be seen in a force already attacking the tibia of the larval leg. I picture it thus: that through a dorso-ventral crushing of the tibial end the tibia received a tear [Einriß] of such a kind that the eye-margin, otherwise connected with the inner margin through the ventral portion, lost this connection and was driven upward over the outer surface. Since this wound penetrated to below the outer-margin spur, the latter was regenerated at the original site during the further normal development of the original leg, which proceeds with rotation. As the original branch I regard the innermost, whose broad-pressed segments as well as bent position still allow the traces of the original pressure to be recognized. From the bending-point there grew up perpendicularly onto the adjacent torn margin of the pressure-wound, which reaches into the middle of the tibia, the inverse and, as usual, more weakly developed middle component of the break-triple-formation. Already in the tibia, and then on up to the 3rd tarsal segment, this collided with the third, outermost component growing from the opposite torn limb [Rißschenkel]. The cause of the fusion, which had to suppress the development of both thorns of the middle component and of the inner thorn of the outermost component, lies firstly in the spatial proximity at the apex of the tear-angle [Rißwinkel], but then also in the inverse curvature of the middle foot-branch, which had to crowd the inner margins of the second and third components still closer together. To elucidate this behavior we must turn back once more to the symmetry of the normal foreleg in ground-beetles [Laufkäfer] and now consider the tarsus. As a consequence of the asymmetrical termination of the tibia, the tarsus too does not stand in the continuation of a median line, but joins in a twisting outward and downward, so that already under normal conditions the inner contours of the first foot-segments are longer than the outer ones. The segments describe, as it were, an arc outward. If one now imagines two mirror-image-wise growing feet approaching one another, then the convex middle portion of the two partners will coincide, while the terminations again diverge. This renewed divergence has now occurred in the present abnormality, whereby the separation, through the growing-apart, simulated a second wound, but perhaps even actually led to a secondary tear [Riß]. The greater length of the inner margin, namely of the 2nd tarsal segment in the outermost component, does not indicate a fixed bending, but characterizes this foot as a left one, and to this, again, the assumption would not fit that the second row of pits running along the outer margin should represent such a row of an inner margin of a right tibia fused with the original left one. Rather, this outer margin confirms itself to us as a ventral edge that has remained dorsal through the failure of rotation. The cause of the failed rotation is presumably to be found in the not fully sufficient developmental time of the regenerate, which is also expressed in the lesser size and perfection of form. We shall see in the following cases how, with full development, owing to the rotations, the otherwise valid rule — which still holds even in Tornier's *Carabus convexus* — of the lying of all three components of the break-triple-formation in one plane is modified.

No. 175. *Calathus fulvipes*, ♂, Perry (1866, p. 307, Fig. 5), Bateson (1894, p. 507, No. 777a). The right foreleg shows a splitting of the tibial end, from which on the outer branch there grows a broad-pressed foot equipped with the characteristic tarsus-form of the male ground-beetles, while on the inner one a more slender foot takes its origin, which forks again at the end of the 1st tarsal segment. Its branch continuing the inner margin of the foretibia has retained the remaining tarsal segments together with the claws, while the symmetrically branching one bears only two roundish segments. This case is, in my opinion, similar to the previous one and supplements it in a desirable manner, because here we still see before us the course of the original tear [Riß] that penetrated into the tibia. I regard the outermost branch of the tibia, with the masculinely differentiated foot, as the original one; the more slender inner one as a regenerate that has not yet advanced to full sexual differentiation, from the distally-facing tear-surface [Rißfläche]; on the other hand, the still less differentiated two-segmented additional piece as the beginning of the component growing inversely from the proximally-facing tear-surface, which, owing to the counter-curvature, had fused with the inner foot up to this branching. With the not-yet-completed development of the regenerate there occurred no essential rotation of the tarsi.

No. 176. *Calathus graecus*, ♂, Kraatz (1877, p. 62, Fig. 23), Bateson (1894, p. 505, No. 776, Fig. 168). The left foreleg shows a threefold development of the tibial end with all 3 pairs of spurs and the appurtenant feet, of which the two outer ones are fused with one another up to the base of the last tarsal segment. The component continuing the inner margin of the tibia [Schiene] is the strongest and, just like both others, completely masculinely differentiated. Its outer-margin spur has reached the normal ventral position and stands close beside the outer-margin spur of the middle component (which lags behind only through weaker development of the last tarsal segment), which has carried out the rotation in the inverse direction. Again mirror-image-wise to the spurring of the middle component stand those of the third; but owing to the rotations the mirror-images do not lie in the same plane as those of the first with the second component, and the feet fused at the inner margins turn their sole-surfaces toward one another, just as toward the non-fused foot. Evidently this latter, as Bateson too assumes, represents the original one. From a break arising in the tibial tip at the eye-margin there then grew the two additional feet, fused as they grow toward one another. The case thus represents a typical break-triple-formation, in which the stepping-out of the components from one plane is to be traced back only to the processes normally taking place in the course of development, as we have come to know something similar in the case of the lobster claw [Hummerschere] through Emmel's investigations.

No. 176a. *Calathus cisteloides*, ♂, Mocquerys (1880, p. 65, Fig.), after Bateson (1894, p. 506, No. 777, Diagram 169 II). Similar to the previous case, only it concerns a right foreleg and indeed the inner margin of the tibial tip; the fused foot-pairs have only the 1st tarsal segment in common, and the mirror-planes are more strongly inclined toward one another.

No. 177. *Ceroglossus valdiviae*, Bateson (1894, p. 509, No. 708, Fig. 166). The left foretibia of this ground-beetle, at its end of threefold breadth, the inner third separated off by a deep groove and bent inward. From the articular facet of this thus-separated tibial end rises the normal foot, flanked on the outside by the front thorn normally curved toward the 1st tarsal segment, on the inside by the straight rear thorn. The rear, double-broad end-branch of the tibia bears in the middle of its articular surface a double-broad 1st tarsal segment, which at the end has two diverging articular surfaces each with a single-broad 2nd tarsal segment. The manner of fusion of these two feet is, moreover, very clearly recognizable at the tibial spurs, whose posterior ones, owing to the mutual curvature, after initial median fusion diverge as bifid at the tip, while the anterior ones go straight apart singly. On the 1st tarsal segment too the spurring agrees exactly with the demanded scheme. The break-triple-formation will have been brought about by a force attacking shortly before the tibial margin, which bent it forward and would have produced an acute-angled tear [Riß].

No. 178. *Eros minutus*, Bateson (1894, p. 487, No. 746, Fig. 158). «Right foretibia somewhat divided at the end, forming two points. The rear point bears a normal tarsus. The front one bears a double tarsus with simple three 1st segments (the 3rd enlarged). The 4th segment is almost double-broad and bears distally two claw-segments, each with a pair of claws. From the development of this it was clear that they are a pair. Stretched out, the three plantar surfaces are not exactly in one horizontal plane, as they should be in position A, but they nearly are. This deviation is exaggerated in the figure... This figure was drawn under the microscope and is reversed.» Distinct break-triple-formation of a soft-bug [Weichhäuter] leg; the deflection of the original leg through the break still clearly recognizable. — Mason Collection.

No. 178a. *Cryptohypnus riparius*, Bateson (1894, p. 509, without Fig.). «The tibia of the right foreleg is widened at the end and bears a very large tarsal segment; this segment has two apical articulations, of which the rear one bears the remaining four segments of what is probably the normal tarsus. The other articulation [bears] a large segment common to a pair of complete supernumerary feet. This pair of tarsi stands close together by its lateral margins and with its sole-surfaces downward, but the other tarsus, which lies behind them and is probably the normal one, stands with its plantar surface turned backward.» This deviation from Bateson's scheme is undoubtedly to be traced back to the deflection of the original foot through the break. — Mason Collection.

No. 179. *Clytus liciatus*, Kraatz (1873, p. 233, Fig. 171). «Right tibia [of the leg of a longhorn beetle] reduced and thickened, formless and curved. The end shows two joints, a front and a rear one, the latter bearing a normal four-segmented tarsus. The front articulation bears a slender double tarsus, whose components are fused in the 1st, 2nd, and 3rd segment, but separate in the 4th or end-segment. The supernumerary tarsi are very slender, and the thickness of their common proximal segment is even less than that of the normal tarsus. The end-segments of the supernumerary feet are well formed, but each bears only one fully developed claw, the claw of the sides facing each other presenting itself only as a rudimentary knob. At first glance it seems as if these supernumerary tarsi, at their origin from the tibia, were only a single appendage, and that their double nature began only from their 3rd segment. This is, however, not the case, for there are five spurs on the tibia together with a small brown knob which perhaps represents the sixth spur.» The malformation of the tibia I trace back to a force attacking from outside at the tarsus and tibial end, which, with fixation of the upper femur [Oberschenkel], led to a compression of the tibia and a tear [Riß] at the tibial end, out of whose wound-limbs the super-regenerates, owing to the crowded position, grew out symmetrically, fusing at once.

No. 179a. *Aromia moschata*, Bateson (1894, p. 485, No. 743, without Fig.). «Right foretibia [of this musk-beetle] widened at the end, bears in front a supernumerary pair of tarsi. The widened end bears three supernumerary spurs, of which the middle one is thicker than a normal rear spur. This is undoubtedly a double spur representing the two rear spurs of the supernumerary tibiae.» Almost typical break-triple-formation. — British Museum.

No. 180. *Peronia Mühlfeldii*, ♂, Kraatz (1877, p. 56, Fig. 21), Bateson (1894, p. 485, No. 742, Fig. 156). This ground-beetle shows on the left middle leg a break-triple-formation corresponding exactly to the scheme, from the cuff-like widened tibial end equipped with threefold spurring. The supernumerary components, fused only in the first tarsal segment, spring from the wound-limbs of a break arising somewhat ventrally at the front margin.

No. 181. *Telephorus rusticus*, Kraatz (1880, p. 344, Fig. 33), Bateson (1894, p. 502, No. 771). This snail-beetle [Schneckäfer] bears, at the two-pronged tibial end of the left middle leg, three feet, of which the middle one is weaker and articulated inverse-symmetrically with the one lying rearmost on the same tibial branch, but on the opposite surface. Without examination of the object it cannot be decided with certainty whether this latter is the normal foot — that is, the wounding occurred anteriorly — or whether the foremost-lying component represents it and the wounding ran posteriorly. Kraatz draws on all feet fewer than the normal fivefold number of the tarsi, which is probably only an oversight.

No. 181a. *Aleochara maesta*, Bateson (1894, p. 488, No. 747, without Fig.). «Middle left tibia has at the tip two articular surfaces. The rear one bears a foot normal as to form, but without claws. The front one bears a supernumerary row of tarsi with a pair of rudimentary end-segments, each with a pair of claws. On this double foot the 3rd and 4th segments are not distinctly separated. The supernumerary parts in position A.» Typical break-triple-formation from the tibial end on, with subsequent basal fusion of the super-regenerates. — Mason Collection.

No. 182. *Aphodius contaminatus*, ♀, Kraatz (1876, p. 378, Fig. 13), Bateson (1894, p. 494, No. 757, Fig. 162). The end of the left middle-leg tibia is, in this dung-beetle [Mistkäfer], bent dorsally and a trace backward to a second termination. At this there stands terminally a double-strong spur, whose median plane serves as the mirror-plane for the feet articulated on its two sides, above and below. The dorsal tarsus is, in the 1st segment, bent further out of the normal position in the same sense as the tibial branch; its mirror-image is straight, but somewhat weaker. On the shorter branch of the tibial end stands a single spur, such as is characteristic of the leg of the ♂ *Aphodius*, and before it the third, normally-directed component of the triple-formation. All tarsal segments and claws are well developed. Bateson regards the third-named component as the original foot. I interpret the most dorsal one as the original, which, in the 1st tarsal segment and at the tibial end, bent upward and somewhat backward by a ventrally-attacking force, received basally to the point of attack a gaping break-wound and from it produced the typical break-triple-formation. The position of the super-regenerates would then be a ventral one.

No. 183. *Taurhina nireus*, L. v. Heyden and Kraatz (1889, p. 509, Fig. 18), Bateson (1894, p. 509, No. 784). The right middle tibia of this lamellicorn beetle [Blatthornkäfer] bears, one behind the other, at the widened end two feet, of which the front one is thinner. From the presence of five instead of the normal two end-spurs of the tibia (not visible in the figure), Bateson probably infers, with right, that the one foot is to be regarded as fused from two tarsal rows; that it is therefore a matter of a typical triple-formation, only with very far-advanced fusion. According to the regeneration hypothesis this rests on the slight gaping of the wound-margins.

No. 184. *Carabus granulatus*, ♂, L. v. Heyden (1881, p. 110, Fig. 26), Bateson (1894, p. 484, No. 738). The left hind tibia of this ground-beetle, bent dorsally off at the end; at the ventral bending-apex stand four supernumerary spurs, between which stand three double-broad tarsal segments. Typical break-triple-formations with complete fusion of the super-regenerated tarsi.

No. 184a. *Harpalus rubripes*, Fauvel (1889, p. 531, without Fig.), after Bateson (1894, p. 493, No. 754, without Fig.). «Left hind tibia [of this ground-beetle] bears a supernumerary pair of feet. The end of the tibia is broadened and shows two articular surfaces, of which the rear one bears a normal tarsus. The front articular surface bears a pair of complete feet with fused proximal segments. The two supernumerary tarsi are a complementary pair, the rear one developed as a right one.» According to Bateson's description, the causative wound would be to be sought at the front margin, somewhat dorsally.

No. 184b. *Anthia* sp., Bateson (1894, p. 502, No. 772, without Fig.). «Left hind tibia bearing two supernumerary feet. The postero-ventral side of the tibial end is broadened, so that it forms a triangular projection, and the point of origin of the articulation of the normal tarsus is consequently lifted upward. The projection bears two tarsi, of which the rear one, curved down and backward, is formed as a left one, while the front one curves as a right one, forward and somewhat upward.» The tarsi were apparently subsequently mutilated. The break is to be sought at the rear margin, somewhat ventrally.

No. 185. *Agabus uliginosus*, Perry (1866, p. 307, Fig. 6), Bateson (1894, p. 548). The tibia of the right hind leg of this water-beetle [Schwimmkäfer] broad-crushed, the end bent out forward, original tarsus lacking. From the bending-apex at the rear margin grow two small unfused tarsi.

No. 186. *Chrysomela banksii*, Sharp (1890, p. 583, Fig.), Bateson (1890, p. 583, Fig.; 1894, p. 494, No. 755, Fig. 161). The right hind leg of this leaf-beetle [Blattkäfer] bears, on the cuff-like broadened tibia, above and somewhat in front of the normal tarsus and indeed distinctly in an indentation, a pair of supernumerary, somewhat smaller, from the outset free tarsi. The hairing of the tibial end and the direction of mobility of these additional structures also characterize them as mirror-images, which have proceeded from the two break-margins of a dorsal, somewhat anterior wound.

No. 187. *Prionus coriarius*, ♀, L. v. Heyden (1881, p. 110, Fig. 27–28), Bateson (1894, p. 488, No. 750, Fig. 159). Both the right middle leg and also the hind leg of the same side possess, on the tibia inflated toward the end, three front and one rear thorn. Dorsal to these the tibia continues into a forked 1st tarsal segment, whose front, weaker branch is of simple nature and probably belongs to the original foot bent off by a tear [Riß]; upon this there follows still a normal 2nd segment, the further ones are broken off. The rearward branch of the 1st tarsal segment is, besides by somewhat more considerable breadth, also marked as double by a median furrow. It is a matter, as Bateson has already correctly set out, of a complete median joining-together of a right half of a right tarsus with a left half of a left tarsus, so that their front margins are fused. The connecting line is precisely that median furrow, and this is flanked, more ventrally, by two of the mentioned three front spurs. Supernumerary rear spurs have not come to development, be it that the break did not touch the rear spur, or that they were suppressed through the fusion of the supernumerary 1st tarsal segments. The wound we must in any case picture to ourselves as an acute-angled tear [Riß] in the frontal plane of the tibial end. The quite uniform malformation of the two legs standing one behind the other makes it natural to think, in this connection, of a wedging-in of the two and their liberation under tearing-out [Ausriß] of a median lobe of the tibia.

No. 188. *Carabus irregularis*, ♀, Kraatz (1877, p. 57, Fig. 27), Bateson (1894, p. 497, No. 760, Fig. 164). Both the left middle leg and also the right hind leg have a partial tripling of the tarsus distal to the 2nd segment. In the left middle leg there stand, on the thickened 2nd tarsal segment, two 3rd tarsal segments side by side; the antero-ventral one passes into the normal, but reduced foot-termination; the postero-dorsal one ends in two feet fused at the ventral surfaces, whose claws, however, are all free and distinctly allow the symmetry of the break-triple-formation to be recognized. The wound must have reached down into the 2nd segment.

The right hind leg bears, on a normal 2nd segment, the further tarsal segments, without these being set off from one another by joints; at the front margin there are, before the normal claws, still two last segment-ends each with a pair of claws; these two structures representing supernumerary 5th tarsal segments have, so far as their merely rudimentary development allows one to suppose, the symmetry of the break-triple-formation. Here the break is likely to have reached at least into the 3rd segment.

No. 189. *Philonthus ventralis*, Fauvel (1883, p. 93, Tab. II, Fig. 2), Bateson (1894, p. 507, No. 779). The 2nd tarsal segment of the right hind leg has, in this rove-beetle [Kurzflügler], two articular surfaces, with each of which a 3rd tarsal segment articulates. While the further tarsal segments of the posterior 3rd tarsal segment are simple, the anterior and more dorsal one passes into a pair of tarsal segments obliquely fused at their rear surfaces, whose last, 2nd pair bears claws arranged in mirror-image fashion.

No. 190. *Leptura testacea*, Kraatz (1876, p. 378, Fig. 14), Bateson (1894, p. 545, No. 847, Fig. 204). The left middle leg of this longhorn beetle has, at the end of the 2nd tarsal segment, on the front and on the rear side each an articular facet, at which there stands a 3rd together with the appurtenant 4th tarsal segment. Of these, the rear end-segment has two normal claws, the front one however only one median-standing claw. It can scarcely be doubtful that it is here a matter of a pair of end-segments so completely fused at their rear surfaces that the inner claws are quite suppressed, the outer ones however are fused along their median.

No. 191. *Dyschirius globulosus*, Jayne (1880, p. 157, Fig. 6), Bateson (1894, p. 512). The 3rd tarsal segment of the right foreleg has, in this ground-beetle, two articular surfaces, with each of which a 4th tarsal segment articulates. While the 4th tarsal segment of the posterior articular surface bears a normal simple 5th tarsal segment together with claws, that of the anterior articular surface ends in a double 5th tarsal segment, fused at the rear margins, on which two claw-pairs stand in mirror-image symmetrical arrangement. Typical break-triple-formation after wounding at the 3rd segment, with almost complete fusion of two components.

No. 191a. *Pterostichus lucublandus*, Harrington (1890, p. 127, without Fig.), Bateson (1894, p. 500, No. 767). The 3rd tarsal segment of the left middle leg bears, on a broadened articular surface, a tripled 4th segment. The anterior portion No. 192. *Colymbetes sturmii*, Stannius (1835, p. 512, Fig. 9), Bateson (1894, p. 512). On the widened 3rd tarsal segment of the left foreleg, this water-beetle [Schwimmkäfer] bears, one behind the other, three fold-elongations [Faltenlängungen], of which the hindmost is the largest, hence presumably the original one. All three have the correct number of segments and terminal claws and lie in one plane. From the very small figure it unfortunately cannot be gathered whether the middle component, as is to be expected, exhibited the symmetry of the opposite side.

No. 193. *Platycerus caraboides*, Mocquerys (1880, p. 67, Fig.), Bateson (1894, p. 507, No. 778, Fig. 170). The left hind leg of this stag-beetle [Hirschkäfer] bears ventrally on the 3rd tarsal segment a pair of supernumerary appendages with the typical symmetries of the break-triple-formation. The third, stronger branch, regarded by Bateson as normal, has only one claw (in consequence of subsequent loss, or of failure during the regeneration process, after the tearing-off by the wounding causing the tripling?).

No. 194. *Carabus marginalis*, Kraatz (1880, p. 344, Tab. II, Fig. 29), Bateson (1894, p. 484, No. 737). The left hind leg of this ground-beetle [Laufkäfer] has, at the break-apex of the dorsally up-bent 4th tarsal segment, a pair of supernumerary 5th tarsal segments completely fused at the dorsal surfaces except for the freely projecting claws.

No. 194a. *Cetonia opaca*, Mocquerys (1880, p. 61, Fig.), after Bateson (1894, p. 488, No. 749, without Fig.). «The right foreleg [of this rose-beetle] bears a pair of supernumerary end-tarsal-segments very near position *A*, springing from the 4th tarsal segment. All claws are directed ventralward, but those of the supernumerary segments are turned away from one another, also ventralward.»

No. 195. *Carabus perforatus*, ♂, Asmus (1835, p. 54, Tab. IX), Bateson (1894, p. 484, No. 741). From the break-apex of the upward-bent 5th or end-segment of the left hind leg there have grown, in this ground-beetle, two repetitions of the peripheral termination fused at the dorsal surfaces, so that their claws face apart.

No. 196. *Galerita africana*, Mocquerys (1880, p. 64, Fig.), after Bateson (1894, p. 495, Fig. 163). «The right middle leg [of this ground-beetle is] normal except for the last segment, which bears three supernumerary claws springing dorsally from the normal pair . . . two of them small and standing at the front margin of the segment, while at the rear margin there stands only one claw, but of considerable size. This claw is in fact a double-formation, as is clearly shown by the presence of two grooves in its concave surface.» The fusion is certainly to be traced back to the turning-toward [Zuwendung] of the middle, inverse component of the triple-formation. The next case offers further information thereon.

No. 197. *Rhizotrogus aestivalis*, ♀, Bateson (1894, p. 510, No. 786, Fig. 172). The right hind leg of this June-beetle [Junikäfer] bears, at its end-segment, a threefold armature, namely, instead of two claws together with a pair of small bristles [Borsten] standing as a ventrally-projecting "pulvillus" before the claws, three pairs of claws and three pairs of bristles. These three repetitions lie one above another and are partly fused with one another. The manner of fusion seems at first glance to deviate from the usual scheme, so that Bateson lists this case among the deviations from his rule. If we start from the most dorsal end-structure, then we meet the quite free, downwardcurved claw-pair, between which, however, there rises an enlarged pulvillus fused out of two pulvilli, with two free [bristles] but, between them, merely a bifid bristle. Further ventrally there stands on either side a fused claw-pair, and most ventrally a simple pulvillus with two bristles as normal. The fused claws are both almost straight, only somewhat bent upward, that is, curved opposite to the most dorsal ones; the tip of the more ventral fusion-partner, however, bends ventralward again, that is, faces in the same direction as the dorsal simple claws. Herein we have now to seek the key for the unlocking of the symmetry relations. To the simple, most-ventral pulvillus there belongs, of both fused claw-pairs, in each case the more ventral partner, which shows itself, by the tip-curvature, to be a correctly oriented tarsal end. Only in consequence of the basal fusion with the claws of the next component is the straightening of the claws of the next component, of the basal portion, brought about. This next component is namely, as usual, an inversely oriented one; its claws would therefore tend to project downward, which is clearly expressed by the tip-curvature, but in so doing they would have to collide, at their convex upper surface, with the upper surface of the most-ventral partner, and therefore fuse with it under equalization of the curvatures. Exactly the reverse must hold for the pulvillus; this, with the inverse component, would be displaced dorsally, did not meet here the ventral partner, but rather the most-dorsal one, which is characterized by its simple claws, and fused with the pulvillus of the same.

It is thus a matter of a typical break-triple-formation, in which the causative wound had extended parallel to the ventral surface into the end of the 5th segment as far as below the pulvillus, forming a dorsally-standing distally-directed and a ventrally-standing proximally-directed wound-edge.

No. 197a. *Ranopsis bertolinii*, Gadeau de Kerville (1886, p. CLXXX), Bateson (1894, p. 510, No. 785). On the end-segment of the outwardly-situated tarsus of the right hind leg there stand, in this lamellicorn beetle [Blatthornkäfer], six claws, three and three "onychia" instead of one. «The arrangement of the parts is,» according to Bateson, «quite complex and could not well be made clear without detailed drawings.» The examination of the original would probably be in a position to furnish a similar elucidation as, in the previous case, the figures already were, if one takes the same viewpoints into account.

### β) Orthoptera.

No. 198. *Mantis religiosa*, ♂, Przibram (1919, Fig. 43). The right foreleg of this European praying mantis [Gottesanbeterin] has the tibia [Schiene] bent downward and outward. The dorsal margin bears, before the middle, a hump, on which two rudimentary tarsus-anlagen stand; the apical tarsus too is rudimentary. The one more outwardly-standing tarsus-anlage shows a direct continuation of the chitin-structure from the base of the tibia onward, and curves in the same direction as the apical tarsus. The second, more inwardly-standing tarsus-anlage is straight-directed and is connected with the apical tarsus by retrograde branches of the chitin-structure of the distal portion of the tibia.

Although not of complete development, this case is nevertheless of particular interest, because the *Mantis* could already be observed as a nymph with a wound corresponding to the multiple formation. On the nymphal skin, after the hatching of the imago, the bending of the tibia and the blood-scab at the kinking-point are still clearly to be seen (ibid., Fig. 42).

No. 198a. *Sphodromantis bioculata*, ♀, Przibram (1906, *Stuttgart* 1919, Figs. 28—41). The right foreleg of this Egyptian praying mantis showed, on the larva standing between the 6th and 7th moult, the end of the tibia bent upward and ventrally widened. On this widening there is inserted a supernumerary tarsus, which has strictly equal dorsal and ventral margins and not the normal curvature of the apically-standing one. The structure is therefore, to judge by all the experiences hitherto adduced in insects, to be regarded as a pair of mirror-image super-regenerated tarsi fused at the dorsal margins. It consists of only two segments without terminal claws; the apical, otherwise normally-formed, narrower tarsus consists of three segments with claws. Already from this number of segments, too small in comparison with the normal number of 5, the regenerative origin of the triple-formation could have been concluded. The chief interest of the case lies, however, in the certainty of the cause of origin. Since the specimen, as No. 4f, had been observed from the beginning of an exactly controlled rearing of B., I know that it hatched with a quite normal, not previously damaged foreleg; this can even be established subsequently from the cast-off skin of the 2nd stage (ibid., Fig. 28). Before the 3rd moult the leg had been lost, as is likewise evident from the skins, and the regeneration then set in strikingly slowly, whereby a blood-scab indicated a former wounding. Thus in this case the regenerative origin is completely assured. The cause of injury was presumably, the first time, a moulting difficulty; the second time, an upward bending of the still tender regenerate in the attempt to tread upon the sharp miller's-gauze of the cage-screening.

After the 7th moult there occurred again a falling-off of parts of the multiple formation, so that on the imago only a cuff-like widened tibia without any tarsi was to be seen.

Attempts to bring about break-triple-formations deliberately by appropriate wounding had so far success, as it proved possible to obtain initial stages¹⁾ of such, which agreed entirely as regards the symmetry relations and their connection with break-edges. The clearest may be mentioned in the following. They refer to operations on the right

> ¹⁾ In beetles, Kraatz (1877, Figs. 26, 31—32) found similar structures.

foreleg of larvae of the Egyptian praying mantis operated on before the 7th moult.

No. 199b. *Sph. bioculata* (P., 1918, Figs. 25—27). Coxa of the trochanter-articulation bent dorsally as a consequence of an artificial trochanter-break and femur-amputation. Besides the rudimentary regenerate of the following limb-segments, there rises, on the ventral margin of the coxa at the traction-apex of the bending, a triangular projection, which, by its hairing, can be regarded as a mirror-image pair, fused with the dorsal portions, of repetitions of the coxal end situated distal to the break-site. Whereas, namely, on the normal ventral margin of the coxa there stands a row of teeth, which only at the end of the coxa pass over into fine hairs, on the abnormal coxa there is found an interruption of the teeth, of which only two stand distal to the projection. From these, hairs are set toward the tip of the coxa as well as toward the tip of the projection, and finally also such on the second arm of the projection toward its tip. A plant-thorn-like armature, such as the dorsal margin of the coxa shows, is wholly lacking in the projection.

No. 199c. *Sph. bioculata* (P., 1918, Figs. 9—13). Femur broken downward at the patellar joint and torn off. Dorsal before the tear-site there projects obliquely, out of the resulting break, a cone, whose margins show the characteristic plant-thorn-like armature of the dorsal side of the femur. The spines directed, on both arms of the cone, toward its tip

> ¹⁾ In beetles, Kraatz (1877, Figs. 26, 31—32) found similar structures.

show that it is here a matter of two repetitions of the distal femur-portion fused at the ventral surfaces. The case is therefore a counterpart to the previous one, only it concerns a different limb-segment. Patella, tibia and four tarsal segments with claws are, on the imago, moreover regenerated from the normal site of the femur-end.

No. 199d. *Sph. bioculata* (P., 1918, Figs. 18—24). Femur broken at the patellar joint in such a way that it has suffered a permanent inward setting of the joint, and a gaping wound arose at the break-apex. This re-forms itself into a plate-shaped articular surface, without, however, having regenerated more distal parts. The original articular facet, on which the original further limb-segments still stand, is not circular, but has a more strongly curved margin toward the outside, a flatter one toward the inside. If one imagines the supernumerary facet divided by a vertical median line into a right and a left semicircular surface, then the structure can be conceived as a mirror-image pair of oppositely-oriented facets, which are fused at their flatter margins. Just as in the earlier cases, we then have before us the typical tripling of the portion situated distal to the break.

### γ) Diptera.

No. 199. *Drosophila ampelophila*, Hoge (1915, pp. 241—285, Tab. I—V) observed, in the rearing of small fruit-flies, numerous cases of malformations of the legs, of which most were triple-formations and exhibited the typical symmetry relations. To avoid long-windedness, I have brought together in the following table all the cases figured by him, which begins with the complete tripling of the leg from the coxa onward and closes with those cases in which only the tarsus was affected by the splitting, although even in these an excessive thickness of the more proximal segments sometimes allowed a fusion of these out of several components to be inferred. For the most part I have been able to leave the interpretation given by Hoge unchanged; in single multiple formations the figures seem to me to allow another interpretation, which I have then added.

If the multiple formations in *Drosophila* furnish us a complete confirmation of the hitherto-valid rules for the supernumerary legs of varied position and depth of splitting, yet the chief interest of Hoge's cultures lies not herein, but in the testing of heredity.

Of the abnormalities found now and then in his stocks, none, with the exception of a single culture, transmitted the multiple legs to their offspring (Hoge, p. 261). The one culture, in which multiple formations appeared through several generations, showed the tendency to abnormality as a sex-limited property, which fell above all to the males and was transmitted by these, by way of their non-afflicted daughters (p. 252), to the grandsons (pp. 245; 250). A further complication consisted in the necessity of a low temperature for the becoming-visible of the property, since in summer and then also in stocks kept artificially warm in winter the abnormalities did not appear frequently even in the offspring of the mentioned culture, in order to come to the fore again upon renewed cooling (to about 10°) (p. 255). The cold was, however, not the cause of the tendency to monstrosities, since in other cultures no abnormalities could be obtained by cooling (p. 260). Since the affected culture stemmed from a particular artificial selection, Hoge repeated this breeding-selection for the same character, but likewise without success (p. 261). This character was the greatest possible number of teeth of the "sexual comb," which distinguishes the male of *Drosophila* on the inner side of the 1st tarsal segment of the forelegs (p. 242).

The closer analysis of the temperature-influence showed a number of small fruit-flies with multiple formations rising up to 100% when cooling had already been carried out from the egg-laying until the hatching-out of the imagos, and then a decreasing percentage the more days had elapsed before the bringing of the larvae into the cold, until from 6 days on the percentage of 10 was reached and maintained with slight fluctuations, which prevailed in this culture even without cooling (p. 257).

Hoge concludes from this that «the low temperature must in some manner act not only upon the cells of the anlage, but also upon the cells which are to give origin to the leg-anlage, for at the very early stages the imaginal discs are not yet formed» (p. 281). «It appears» to him «probable that the cells of the leg-anlage, in those flies which carry the a[bnormality]-factor, are extremely plastic, divide easily and just as easily fuse, when contact takes place» (p. 276). Such fusions occur not only on the legs furnished with multiple formations of the abnormal stock, but also between single legs of opposite sides (e.g. H., Fig. 25). The particular forms of the abnormalities or their localization on definite leg-pairs or legs are not transmitted (p. 246). «Rather, the character is inherited in the sense that the capacity for leg-splitting is present in every fly of the abnormal stock, whether it be realized or not . . . . The splitting of the anlage-cells . . . . must be more easily brought about when a low temperature is maintained.» «Many of the repeated formations found in *Drosophila* might, had they been discovered as isolated cases, easily have been explained as regenerations in consequence of injuries, as Tornier has proposed for similar structures in beetles. It is quite striking that the symmetry of the regenerated parts of insects which Tornier has observed is the same as that of the supernumerary appendages of *Drosophila*. There is, however, one chief point of difference. In *Drosophila* the supernumerary legs are not to be traced back to injuries, and they are fully formed when the flies hatch out» (H., p. 281).

> *Archiv für Entwicklungsmechanik Bd. 48.* — 21

## Tabelle Cb.

### Doppelbildungen? [Double-formations?]

| Nr. | Hoge Abb. | Seite | Bein-paar | Geschlecht | Bruchdreifach-bildung einschließlich | Stellung (Bateson) | Symmetrie der Komponenten nach Hoge | Verschmelzungsart | Zähne 1. | Zähne 2. | Zähne 3. |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| » y | 30 | *l* | II. | ♂ | Trochanter | V | *R, L* | true mirror-images with their claws turned toward one another (but the inner branch nonetheless curved and thickened) | — | — | — |
| » z | 38 | *l* | III. | ♂ | » | V | *R, L* | true mirror-images with their claws turned toward one another | — | — | — | Hoge thus sets his hereditary abnormalities in sharp opposition to the somatic variations, among which the consequences of injuries would also be counted. Nevertheless, a unified conception of all these superregenerates seems to me not only possible, but, according to Hoge's experiments, even more correct.

I regard the tendency of the abnormal stock toward the formation of multiple legs in the flies as a kind of bleeder's disease. As is well known, among humans there exist so-called "bleeders," who have the peculiarity of bleeding for a long time even from slight injuries, which seems to rest upon a lacking clotting capacity of the blood. The disease can lead to the death of its bearer, but, if injuries are avoided, it need not come to the fore. It is therefore not an unconditional obstacle to breeding. It is, just like the *Drosophila* affliction, sex-limited, in that it chiefly affects males and passes over their non-afflicted daughters to the grandsons (cf. Bateson, Mendel's Principles of Heredity, 1909, p. 222). The tendency toward abnormal legs is in *Drosophila* likewise a "semi-lethal" factor; this is shown by the too-small number of abnormal animals in proportion to the expected probability from known crosses, and in the frequent failure of the hatching-out of the flies which, when subsequently drawn from the pupal case, prove to be abnormal (H., p. 267).

In the regeneration-capable stages of the "bleeder"-*Drosophila*, long-remaining open wounds will lead to supernumerary regenerates, which in normal stocks do not come to formation owing to rapid healing, except when the wound-edges are prevented from re-fusing by particular bendings. Since cold delays the healing-over in and of itself, it too will favor superregeneration. Effusions of blood in the interior of injured limb-anlagen will bring forth those swollen rudimentary legs which Hoge had often observed on single legs (H., Figs. 26–28, 32, 36) or on multiple formations, and cold will also favor the fusion of two legs with their effusions-of-blood turned toward one another, as something similar is also known in other kinds of protoplasm (*Ascaris*-embryos of Skwala and zur Strassen, cf. Przibram, Exp. Zool., I., 1907). As regards the time of the causation of the multiple formations, the first embryonic stages do not come into consideration, for Hoge gives (p. 289) a table of the percentages of abnormal flies which was obtained by the action of cold upon the abnormal stock, when the cooling had been discontinued at various times before the hatching-out of the flies. In this table the eggs cooled for 1–5 days yielded merely 7%, that is, in no way more than the percentage of abnormal flies arising even without the action of cold; the longer the action of cold lasted, the higher the percentage of abnormals rose, so that after 32–35 days of cooling it reached almost 82. This behavior can be explained only by a somatic acquisition in each individual case, namely by a superregeneration following a chance, long-remaining-open wounding. The longer the cold has to act, the more probable is the occurrence of such a small wounding, and the higher rises the percentage of the abnormals. This, and not the action upon the embryonic stages, must be the cause of the high percentage of abnormal flies, reaching 100%, in the case of keeping in the cold from the egg until hatching-out; for otherwise one would have had to expect a high percentage of abnormals upon interruption of the cold-keeping after completion of the embryonic development. Unfortunately Hoge has given, regarding the speed of transformation, only the single statement that the flies hatch out at room temperature in 12 days (p. 257). Of this time we have (according to Castle, in Morgan, Exp. Zool., 1907, p. 190) to reckon one third upon the development in the egg, one third upon the larva, one third upon the pupa. Since the last larval period and the pupa are not in a position to bring forth regenerates, the action of cold can therefore also yield no increase of the supernumerary formations upon its action during these two periods, which together may make up about half of the development time. With this now agrees the absence of an enhancement of abnormality when the cold had acted only from the half of the time, i.e. 6 days, onward (H., p. 258 and Diagram 2). As regards the kind of injury, we shall have to think of breaks that arise at the sites of the future legs. That the injury and the regenerate of a larval leg also passes over to the imago has hitherto always been observed even in the insects with complete metamorphosis (cf. Przibram, Exp. Zool., 2nd ed., p. 95); the so-called imaginal discs have nothing to do with this; their role urgently requires new investigations.

## d) Hymenoptera.

No. 200a. *Tenthredo solitaria*, Bateson (1894, p. 502, No. 770, without fig.). "The tibia of the right middle leg [of this sawfly] divides in the peripheral third into two branches, of which the anterior bears the normal foot. The posterior branch springs from the postero-ventral surface of the normal one and bears a double foot, consisting of the posterior parts of a pair fused in almost exactly the position V F." Cambridge University Museum, England.

No. 200b. *Foenus tarsatorius*, Harrington (1890, p. 124, without fig.), Bateson (1894, p. 491, No. 751). This ichneumon-wasp has on the tibia a triple-formation similar to the previous hymenopteran. The spurs of the tibial end show, as also in the previous case, the middle component as mutually symmetrical. The foremost component is narrower than the other two, equally formed ones.
