Position perhaps almost exactly P, if, as Bateson is inclined to assume, the deviatingly narrower branch is to be regarded as the original tibia.

## e) Lepidoptera.

No. 200c. *Capnia atra*, ♀, from the collection of J. Morton, described and figured by Chapman (1907) (Tab. XII, Figs. 8, 9), shows the right hind leg with a very strongly broadened 1st tarsal segment, into which three 2nd tarsal segments are articulated, each of which bears a corresponding 3rd tarsal segment together with claws. The claws of the middle component are directed opposite to those of the two others; the last tarsal segment has the symmetry of the opposite side. The 2nd tarsal segments have, however, according to the figure, a position twisted with respect to one another, so that a more exact analysis of the specimen now located in the Museum at South Kensington would still be desirable for the sake of secure recognition as a typical break-triple-formation.

No. 200d. *Amerintha occellata*, O. Schultz (1897, p. 531, without fig.). Behind the 3rd leg of the left side of this eyed-hawkmoth there was a supernumerary leg with an especially thick thigh. Since the shin had unfortunately been accidentally broken off and no closer details given, one can only surmise that it is a matter of a leg fused from two legs, and that here too, once, in a butterfly, a break-triple-formation has occurred.

## Regenerative potencies of the claw-bearing appendages of the Hexapoda.

The legs are in all insect orders capable of regeneration, insofar as they have been lost at not-too-far-advanced larval stages (Przibram, Exp. Zool., 2nd ed., 1909, p. 92). The following case of double formation, which concerns the median splitting of a leg, is certainly a consequence of an operation; others are probably likewise traceable to injuries.

No. 200e. *Hydrophilus piceus*, ♀, Megušar (1907, p. 198, Pl. VII, Fig. 63); In the course of regeneration experiments on the larvae of water-beetles, a double formation of the left foreleg came to light on the imago. The investigation of the cast-off larval skin showed that the wound-scab exhibits in the longitudinal direction a small groove, by which it is divided into roughly two equally large sections. This shape of the wound-scab was probably brought about in such a way that ". . . at the deep incision the whole leg was not severed at one stroke, but first about the one half and then the second half, whereby . . . in the middle of the leg-root a gaping wound" was produced. "From the rounded articular hollow there rises in a perpendicular direction toward the prothorax a mighty, chitinous, and leftward-twisted trunk, which represents an intimate fusion of the coxae of the two twin-legs. At the distal end the trunk shows a small saddle-shaped depression, from which on each side, left and right, the remaining parts of the twin-legs proceed." "The right little leg is strongly malformed and wound ram's-horn-like. Evidently, as a result of the lack of space — since the right, non-operated leg reaches with its coxa toward the middle of the thorax — it has been hindered in its unfolding." "The left twin-sibling-leg, turned toward the outer world, is somewhat stronger and well unfolded up to the distal end of the tibia." The tarsus is lacking, perhaps subsequently broken off. "The right foreleg of the animal, lying opposite the double formation, has turned out disproportionately small in comparison to the body-size (compensatory reduction)." It is located in the developmental-mechanics Museum of the Institute.

No. 200f. *Agriotes obscurus* = *Elater variabilis*, Germar and Zincken, (p. 335, Tab. I, Fig. 12), after Asmuss (1835), Bateson (1894, p. 547, No. 852). The prothorax of this click-beetle bears on the right, one behind the other, two coxae with all following leg-segments in entirely identical symmetry. I have elsewhere (Przibram, 1910, p. 614, Fig. 3a–c) regarded this case as evidence for the fact that, although the front and rear, just like the two lateral sides, can be regenerated apart from one another, the dorsal and ventral halves cannot, so that two legs standing one above the other with identical symmetry have not come to observation.

No. 200g. *Strangalia maculata*, Tornier (1900, p. 547, without fig.). The left foreleg of this longhorn-beetle bears on its front side, somewhat behind the middle, a more weakly developed second thigh-piece with shin and a foot not differentiated into segments. Symmetry therefore undetermined, perhaps a triple-formation after all?

No. 200h. *Carabus clathratus*, Tornier (1900, p. 547, Fig. 25). Similar to the previous, only it concerns a left middle leg of a ground-beetle and the smaller branch lies toward the rear. It lacks the tarsus.

No. 200i. *Pasimachus punctulatus*, Jayne (1880, p. 156, Fig. 4), Bateson (1896, p. 567, No. 853). From the geometrical underside, corresponding to the morphological hind side, of the trochanter there springs at the left middle leg of this ground-beetle a second, less chitinized trochanter with all following segments of a leg of the same symmetry.

No. 200j. *Prionus coriarius*, ♂, Perry (1866, p. 308, Fig. 11), Bateson (1894, p. 512). The right hind leg of this tanner-longhorn-beetle is replaced by two small legs standing side by side, whose symmetry is undetermined owing to deficient terminal development.

No. 200k. *Prionus californicus*, Jayne (1880, p. 159, Fig. 12), Bateson (1894, p. 544, No. 843, Fig. 203). All legs of this longhorn-beetle have double shins; also both maxillary palps and the left labial palp show splitting. The possible explanation of this uniquely standing case I have attempted to give (Przibram, 1910, p. 610, Fig. 9/3) through pressure-action upon the embryo. The two tibiae of each femur stand everywhere one behind the other and are not distinguished in their symmetry.

[No. 200l. *Cerambyx dux*, Weber (1889, without fig.). Right middle tibia strongly thickened at the tip, with double tarsus; the last segments with claws are lacking.]

No. 200m. *Catocala nupta*, a left foreleg, described and figured (Tab. XII, Figs. 6, 7) by Chapman (1907) from the Besot collection, of a red-underwing-moth, showed itself doubled from the thickened tibia onward; two tarsal segments of the one more slender tarsus were broken off and have been completed by Chapman in the figure, and can therefore not be used for the evaluation of the case as a double- or break-triple-formation. Specimen now in the South Kensington Museum.

## E. Break-triple-formations in paired body-appendages of vertebrates that do not conclude their growth with metamorphosis and that still regenerate the appendages in question post-embryonically.

### a) Animals without metamorphosis (Pisces).

No. 201. *Protopterus annectens*, Hopley (1881, p. 487, Fig. a) and Boulenger (1891, p. 147, without fig.). Foreleg-fin with triple tip. Concerning its origin Hopley reports, translated from the English: "The keeper reported to me that some weeks previously this fin had been bitten through in the middle, but not entirely severed, an irregular wound with a hanging tip-portion remaining. It had not only healed without a trace of the wound to be left, but the almost-severed portion had also become firm and solid, forming a direct meandering line from the body. The two lateral appendages were, when at rest, at a right angle to the middle fin (or branch?); but with the movements of the fish they collapsed or spread out, just as it happened, and as far as one could decide, each seemed to possess independent movement."

The triple tip was later cut off, and a single tip grew back (Fig. f). The cut-off piece was investigated by Boulenger and preserved in the British Museum. This researcher speaks, however, not of two lateral appendages, but of "two preaxial or dorsal branches; the supernumerary rays are fused at their base by means of their proximal segments (Howes), . . . likewise as the normal, axial part is divided into cartilaginous segments."

The figure of Hopley, too, on which the ventral fringe-fringe is clearly to be recognized, leaves no doubt that it is a matter of two dorsal accessory appendages, and that the ventralmost is to be regarded as the original axial part, torn downward from its natural position by the wounding, which indeed also agrees with the keeper's report. Hopley's erroneous conception evidently rests upon the interpretation — one that at first does of course force itself upon one — of a restoration of the original growth-direction through unaltered resetting of the old tip-portion. According to all that we have already remarked above concerning the behavior of similar woundings, and shall in particular have occasion to observe in other vertebrate limbs, it has been a matter of an entirely typical break-triple-formation: the almost-torn-off tip has formed a second one proximally, and the distally-facing wound-surface of the more proximal fin-portion a third. Unfortunately the symmetry of the individual tips cannot be recognized on the fugitive tip. That the fringe-fringe does not extend further onto any of the tips cannot be made use of, since it also no longer appeared on other regenerates of the *Protopterus*-fin.

Besides the certainty of accidental origin, the case is still especially interesting because, after removal of the triple-formation by a simple transverse section, a simple straight out-growing tip arose; thus certainly not a congenital tendency toward multiple formation, but rather the kind of wounding had given the decisive impetus to its first-time appearance.

## Regenerative potencies of the fish-fins.

No. 201a. *P. annectens*, Hopley (1891, Fig. b), also furnished a case of double formation of a breast-fin, if it is not, as so often, a matter of two fused accessory fins.

Regeneration of fish-fins has been experimentally established in many other fish species (cf. Przibram, Exp. Zool., II., 1909, p. 158), so that it seems almost surprising that I found no natural finds of tripled fish-fins recorded. The doublings of the tail- and anal-fins in goldfish (figured e.g. in Ercolani, 1881, Pl. IV, Fig. 12) do not belong here; it is traceable to an embryonic divergence of the first cleavage-cells, which arises under unfavorable conditions and represents a hereditary protoplasmic weakness (cf. *Carassius* — Milewski, 1918).

### b) Animals with metamorphosis (Amphibia).

#### α) Urodela.

##### I. Anterior limb:

No. 202. *Triton taeniatus*, Barfurth (1899, fig.; 1901, p. 116, Figs. 68–69). A natural find bears, articulated behind the normal right foreleg, an accessory limb; it possesses, in contrast to the normal four-toed hand, five fingers, of which one is moreover forked. It would probably be a matter of two mirror-images completely fused with the two toes turned toward one another, hence this case would belong among the break-triple-formations. Yet nothing certain can be concluded from the communications and figures.

No. 202a. *T. cristatus*, Jäckel (1881, without fig.). A natural find of a 16 cm long female, from Windsheim in Middle Franconia, that has on the shoulder-blade of the right foreleg an upward-standing fifth limb. This same one "has the half length of the normal foot, is movable in the elbow-joint, but not in the four toes, of which the two middle ones are grown together at the base, but separated in the terminal half, and stand upward like two oath-fingers, while the two lateral ones, somewhat obliquely crossed, are directed horizontally outward." I hold, according to the somewhat unclear description, the supernumerary arm to be one [composed] of two fused [hands], in which fusion four mutually approached fingers have been suppressed. Unfortunately figure or osteological data are lacking.

No. 203. *T. cristatus*, [Pl. XXI, Fig. XXIV], experiments by Della Valle (1911–13, Figs. 1–6). In an adult newt from S. Angelo in Formis, the upper arm had been almost entirely severed through a dorsal cut and then the forearm amputated. Repeated reiteration of the section at the same upper-arm site and finally the application of a silk ligature were necessary in order to prevent the growing-together of the two cut-edges. The expended effort was rewarded by the appearance of a completely typical break-triple-formation. There grew out of the two distally-facing wound-surfaces eupolar hands, and out of the proximally-facing one an oppositely-faced hand mirror-imaged to the others, together with the corresponding forearms. Most slowly grew this heteropolar hand, and it also showed only very restricted motility at a time when the eupolar ones had attained full mobility. The sensibility, however, was present in all three hands. Each of the three hands possessed the normal number of four fingers without deviations in their development.

Since, through the continuous observation of the course of regeneration and the site of the break, long marked by the silk thread, the position of the regenerates relative to the original arm stands fully unimpeachably secure, this case forms one of the most instructive ones for the documentation of the processes that play out in break-triple-formations. To the views of Della Valle I shall have to return later in the general concluding discussion.

No. 204. *T. alpestris*, experiment by Fritsch (1911, p. 465, Fig. T1). After amputation of the right forehand and longitudinal splitting of the forearm belonging to it (Barfurth's method), there appeared, outward from the regenerated normal hand, a five-toed limb, which I regard as a fusion-product of two four-toed hands sprung from the split-wound-surfaces. Hence this case too should probably be reckoned among the break-triple-formations. Fritsch attempted to apply other points of view for the assessment of the result of Barfurth's method; he writes (p. 463): "There arose thus two regeneration-centers, from which now the regeneration of one hand each . . . was to take place." (p. 466): "If one considers the skeletal relations of this excessively formed five-fingered hand, . . . then one recognizes that the carpus agrees in a striking manner with the tarsus of a normal hind limb, since the intermedium and ulnare are separated. One could thus designate this formation of the five-fingered hand as an atavistic one, were it not that furthermore a 6th finger (VI) already appears in the form of a small cartilage-piece. At any rate the agreement of this carpus with the tarsus of a normal foot is very striking, especially as the same also appeared in two further cases yet to be described. In the one . . . case it is a matter of the regeneration of a normal four-fingered hand and of a rudimentary one-fingered one." (p. 467): "Of particular interest is here, that the carpus of the regenerated hand corresponds in its skeletal relations to those of a normal foot, although only four fingers are clearly stamped out." (p. 468): "Most striking of all, however, this agreement appears in an otherwise quite normally regenerated anterior limb, . . . why it here comes to a separation of intermedium and ulnare cannot be explained, for as an atavistic formation one cannot well after all regard the case, since the 5th finger is completely lacking and one also cannot assume that it still appears, since the regenerate was already over 8 months old." (p. 469): "These cases show that one must after all stand quite skeptically opposite the assumption which wishes to trace the true polydactyly in the majority of cases back to atavism, and that one can furnish a proof for it only after exact investigation of the inner skeletal relations. But it should be especially emphasized, that on no account may such conclusions be drawn on the ground of external morphological formations."

Since the atavistic explanation has failed in the skeletal investigation, we shall now test the other one, as break-triple-formation, on the skeletal pieces. Fritsch gives (p. 465, Fig. U1) an outline-drawing of the excessively regenerated, externally five-fingered hand, into which, after plate-reconstruction of histological sections, the skeletal pieces are drawn in, which let the hand be recognized as six-fingered. This sixth finger is the ulnarmost one and would, according to Fritsch's designation, sit directly upon an ulnare I, a skeletal piece that normally never bears a finger. If we conceive the finger-rudiment as a remnant of a normal 4th finger, then we must conceive the skeletal piece as a fusion of an ulnare I with an ulnare II. But what then is the distally-following ulnare II of Fritsch? Upon this piece there would normally have to sit a finger (IV.); instead we find, however, two fingers articulated upon it; I therefore conceive the piece again as a fusion of two carpalia, u III and r(m)III, and designate the fingers, proceeding radialward, as III. and II. finger. Now we arrive, in the last carpalia-row, at the segment designated by Fritsch as ulnare III, upon which a finger sits, which would be correct. We postpone the determination of the segment until we come back to it from the radial edge. On the side of the supernumerary hand on which the radius should be located, and on which, according to the reconstruction too, a radius-like bone is found, there join onto the head of this latter the carpalia designated by Fritsch as radiale I, rII, and r(m)III. Upon the so-called radiale II and radiomediale III jointly, however, there sits a finger, after Fritsch I., while upon the rm III and yet the rIII of Fritsch there sits a further finger, which would thus have to be II. Now there exists, however, normally no finger that would sit upon a radiale II. If we regard the front edge of the supernumerary limb not as the radial edge, but as an ulnar edge symmetrical to the four-fingered, more anteriorly lying component, then we can designate the first carpal-segment of this edge as uI, the second as uII, and upon this sits an ulnar-edge-finger IV, still supporting itself upon the following uIII, to which also the next finger is articulated as a III. Now we encounter again, proceeding toward the hindmost ulnar edge, the piece designated by Fritsch as rIII. It now shows itself, consistently, to be an r(m)III with the upon-sitting finger II. Now there is still lacking the homologization of the middle carpal bones: ⊙ is certainly centrale. On the other hand, one can regard the mI, instead of as an intermedium separated from the ulnare I, as a fusion-piece out of two symmetrically collided radiale I, and correspondingly the head-end, designated by Fritsch as r, of a tubular bone, which is moreover much narrower than a normal radius, as a longitudinal-fusion out of the reversedly-symmetrical ulna with two symmetrically almost entirely confluent radii.

We must remember here that we are dealing with reconstructions, and that in doing so many difficulties prevail, which perhaps make it advisable to draw the form of a radius where the plate series has failed. Fritsch himself admits (p. 434): "The final difficulty was now the reconstruction, which proved quite difficult, partly owing to the lack of precise orientation marks. Nevertheless, I have succeeded in being able to give a whole series of these reconstructed images, which are completely beyond reproach."

Our analysis of the case would now harmonize as follows with the genesis: through a longitudinal cut into the forearm after severance of the hand, the ulnar half was bent ulnar-ward, and because it was less well supplied with blood from the body than the radial half, which remained fixed in flexed position, and was also more exposed to abrasion, it atrophied until only a transversely-standing wound surface remained, which now formed a gaping angle with the ulnar wound surface of the radial part. Out of this there grew, from the ulnar part radial-ward, a correctly-oriented hand, and from the radial part ulnar-ward a reverse-oriented hand, which now met one another with their newly-forming radial margins and, owing to lack of space, fused, without it yet coming to the formation of the radial margin itself with the finger I seated upon it. To the right and left of the fusion line, which separates the two groups of three fingers each, we are therefore dealing with the ulnar parts of two oppositely-symmetrical hands. The well-supplied radial half of the operated forearm has, besides the reverse-growing hand, also regenerated from the ulnar wound surface yet another hand in normal direction, which did not suffer from lack of space and has therefore been formed with all four fingers.

(No. 204a.) *F. a.* If the longitudinal splitting in the operation does not strike the middle of the arm, but only the radial part, then we shall expect conditions such as those present in Fritsch's Fig. V¹ (p. 466). Increased relative to the norm are only radial parts; perhaps here too the apparent separation of an intermedium from the ulnare is to be traced back to the addition of a radiale at the radial-ward wound surface? Through the forward-growth of the supernumerary finger (I), which according to the figure is seated directly upon a widening of the radius, the formation of a radiale II and a finger with opposite symmetry would have been prevented. (Yet, given the still-undivided state of the radius-head, it could be a matter of the fusion of a reverse-directed finger with a correctly-growing skeletal anlage.)

(No. 204b.) *F. a.* The third still-possible case of the operative execution, namely that the cut running strongly ulnar of the extremity axis permits only the formation of ulnar parts, seems to be realized in Fritsch's Fig. IV¹ (p. 467). Here the head-ends of the two tubular bones can both well be interpreted as ulnar; the seating of finger I upon a bone of the second carpal row suggests interpreting it not as rII but as a second uII, and the complete symmetry of the two middle fingers accords better with their interpretation as two III. fingers than as II. and III. fingers. Normally the III. finger should be considerably longer and divided off earlier than the II. finger. Again the apparently divided-off intermedium could be regarded as a rudiment of two completely fused radialia I not reaching distally to the attachment of further elements.

(No. 204c.) *F. a.* Very clearly the symmetrical formation from the hand-margins shows itself after another operative method carried out by Fritsch, namely the wedge-shaped excision of the two middle fingers together with the appertaining carpal pieces. One sees on his Fig. II¹ (p. 449)

from the basal phalanx of finger IV a second finger growing forth symmetrically; likewise two equally supernumerary fingers on the radial half are to be regarded as a repetition of a II. and I. finger with their own r(m)III. Finger III sits upon two carpalia and probably represents a fusion of two fingers.

The remaining cases of Fritsch show in several instances doublings of single fingers, but are less important for us.

No. 205. *Siredon pisciforme*, observation by Duméril (1867, p. 130, Fig. 32), after accidental wounding by bite of conspecifics: "Right forelimb. On the anterior side of the arm, somewhat before the elbow joint, an appendage a has developed, directed outward; it ends with four fingers, of which the outermost and innermost are the shorter ones; the others, more elongated, are of almost equal length. The hand seems to have undergone no other change than the replacement of its fourth finger by a finger of new formation, which is more slender and removes itself more than normal from the third. On the posterior surface one sees, at the height of the wrist, an appendage b, similar in its form to a second hand; it has set its palm opposite that of the real one, but it ends with three fingers." If one considers the position of the shortest finger of the middle and of the posterior hand, one recognizes that these too stand mirror-image to one another; we therefore surely have before us a triple-formation at a break, of which two branches have fused up to the finger-root.

No. 205a. *Siredon pisciforme*, likewise by Duméril (1867, p. 129, Fig. 31), after accidental wounding by bite of the conspecifics; translation from the French: "Right forelimb. At the height of the elbow there grows a well-formed hand directed backward. It is somewhat smaller than an ordinary limb; the four fingers have not yet attained their full development, but they have the ordinary proportions. The right forelimb had not only received the wound whose scarring furnished the origin for the monstrosity just described, for it bore, on the inner side of the thumb, a supernumerary finger of equal length to the latter."

The stylization of the last sentence allows one to recognize that Duméril did not actually observe this second wounding, but merely inferred it from the result. It is therefore permissible to give another interpretation: I conceive the hand described by him as supernumerary to be the original hand, displaced out of its direction but regenerating according to Bell's law, and the five-membered one — as in several of the foregoing cases — to be a fused accessory double-hand; whereby the case fits into the laws of triple-formation at a break, though without offering that clarity which the experimental findings do.

##### II. Hind Extremity.

No. 206. *T. (Molge) vulgaris*, Camerano (1896, Fig.) received through M. G. Perecca from Chivasso a 5 cm long newt which bore on the left a tripled hindleg. The pubic bone had two symphyses: two femur-heads and two ossa ilia were present; the one more deeply lying shank forked into two branches, each with a supernumerary foot. Although nothing further is mentioned about symmetry in the work, it nevertheless emerges from the figure that we are dealing with a typical triple-formation at a break, inasmuch as the middle component possesses inverse orientation. The left main leg was 10, the outer supernumerary leg 12, the inner likewise 12 mm, the normal right hindleg 13 mm long.

No. 206a. *T. cristatus*, Simmermacher (1885, without Fig.) described a six-footed newt from Gießen: "On the left hindleg, with a normal five toes, there sprouts in the region of the upper thigh a second downward-directed thigh, which at its end bears twice four lateral toes, between which a still-middle toe is situated. The whole supernumerary structure thus possesses nine toes, and the animal is to be designated as six-footed." Without doubt the middle-most toe forms the fusion line of the two accessory legs, and a typical triple-formation at a break is present.

No. 206b. *T. taeniatus*, Landois (1884, without Fig.) had received from Dickburg near Münster in Westphalia a six-footed newt, whose right hindleg was tripled, whereby the upper thighs were stuck in a common skin. About symmetry nothing given; according to Simmermacher (1885) the Triton was similar to the foregoing No. 206a.

No. 206c. *Pleurodeles Waltlii*, Héron-Royer (1884) mentioned, without closer detail or figure, a rib-newt observed by Blanchard with a tripled leg.

## Regenerative potencies of the urodele legs.

The following part-experimentally produced double-formations of urodele legs show the possibility of regenerative completion of split limb-parts.

No. 206d. *Amblystoma punctatum*, five-legged larva found by Winslow (1904, p. 389, Figs. 9–22), of 27 mm length. The fifth leg "stood out forward and outward from the side of the body, somewhat in front of and below the proximal end of the normal right leg." It began with a pubic bone symmetrical to the normal one, but only half as large, which took no contact with the vertebral column. The many other details given by Winslow have no essential importance for us.

No. 206e. *Triton* (sp.?) with five legs was shown by Reuter (1875) at the meeting of the natural-science society at Chemnitz on 3 November 1873. The proceedings contain neither textual details nor figure of the case.

No. 206f. *T. taeniatus* was furnished by Tornier (1901, without Fig.) as "a pelvis-derived supernumerary right hindlimb," in that, in a larva about one month old, "as its hindleg began to sprout forth, the pelvis — and naturally also the soft parts lying above — were cut through with a fine scissors hard above the sprouting limb." About the symmetry, indications are lacking.

No. 206g. *T. taeniatus*, by Camerano (1882, with 3 Figs.). Specimen with splitting of the left femur into two complete legs observed.

No. 206h. *T. cristatus* was forced by Tornier (1897, Fig.), through over-stretching of the leg-regeneration cone by means of a thread, to regenerate this leg forked from the mid-femur on, whereby the sole-surfaces were turned toward one another.

No. 206i. *Triton* (sp.?) after the metamorphosis was likewise forced by Lissitzky (1910, Table XXIV, Figs. 27A, B) by incision into the pelvis to double-leg regeneration; hereby the left hindleg was doubled from the thigh on, but fused up to the toe-beginning.

No. 206j. *Triton*- or axolotl-larvae were brought by Tornier (1901) to the production of two shanks and feet on one femur, in that he injured the right-angle-flexed leg by a horizontal cut crosswise through the shank and along the underside of the thigh.

No. 206k. *Amblystoma punctatum*; on an axolotl, Barfurth (1895, Table V, Fig. 21) had already observed the regeneration of a right forearm with a rudimentary accessory hand, which however possessed the correct number of four fingers. There are also mentioned:

No. 206l. *A. p.* (Table V, Fig. 1) regenerated left forearm with accessory three-fingered hand,

No. 206m. *A. p.* (Table V, Fig. 16) likewise, but only two-fingered hand,

No. 206n–p. *A. p.* (Table V, Figs. 17–19) three cases with only a one-fingered addition, which stands at various places of the forearm, but not as a fifth finger.

Barfurth (1895, p. 112) distinguished between a true hyperdactyly, in which the number of fingers or toes is increased by insertion onto the same wrist, and a false one, in which it is a matter of rudimentary formation of a supernumerary hand (or foot, respectively); but he himself admits that the distinction in the individual case is practically difficult to carry out. For this reason I leave the whole field of hyperdactyly outside the framework of the present work, although a far-reaching analysis — but one performable probably only on the original specimens — would presumably permit the tracing-back of polydactyly to split-formations almost or even quite without exception (cf. the analyses carried out by Tornier on mammals). In such a manner, too, the interpretation of the occasionally observed five-fingered forelegs of urodeles, which otherwise show four fingers on the anterior extremity, would presumably prove to be not atavism, but false polydactyly.

No. 206q. *A. p.* Thus the five-fingered axolotl hand depicted by Barfurth (1895, Table V, Fig. 2) can, owing to the somewhat deeper indentation between the 2nd and 3rd finger, be conceived as a doubling of the 1st finger. The regenerative origin stands firm. That parts of fingers too can fork regeneratively is shown by

No. 206r. *A. p.*, Barfurth (1895, Table V, Fig. 20) with two forked fingers.

No. 206s. *Amblystoma punctatum*, natural find by Winslow (1904, p. 387, Table V, Fig. 1) is instructive on account of the completely symmetrical division of the penultimate phalanx-bone (ibid. Fig. 2).

That the legs are able to regenerate feet not only in distal but also in proximal direction has, apart from the triple-formation at a break experimentally produced above by della Valle, No. 203, on an anterior triton-extremity, been demonstrated through experiments on reverse transplantation of the femur into the hip in *Spelerpes ruber* by Morgan (1908, without Fig.), in *Triton cristatus* by Kurz (1912, p. 606, Figs. 2–3). The latter author obtained here double-formations, probably because, after the falling-off of the femur-stump from the inversely-inserted knee-part, each of the two grown-on lower-leg bones regenerates a whole foot; for an exarticulated lower-leg bone is indeed not able to regenerate the other one laterally (Wendelstadt, 1901), but the tibia can, after exarticulation of the radius, produce a whole foot (Reed, 1903, cf. Prz., Reg., p. 168).

The cases described and depicted by Kurz (1912) are:

No. 206t. *T. cristatus* (p. 606, Fig. 2) with six-toed left, and

No. 206u. *T. cristatus* (p. 606, Fig. 3) with nine-toed right hindleg.

If it is directly proven through the cited experiments that leg-pieces, in their regeneration at inverse wounds, always produce the distal portions — namely feet — then, with respect to the triple-formations from pieces of the extremity girdle, there is still need of an analysis as to what here grows from proximal-turned wound surfaces and what is to be regarded at all as more proximal, e.g. whether scapula or coracoid is to be regarded as more proximal relative to the humerus-socket?

Pertinent experiments for the fore-extremity lie before us only from Tornier (1906, Part III, p. 60, Fig. 11):

A. "The shoulder-girdle of the ordinary *Triton cristatus* was, for the purpose of carrying out regeneration experiments, theoretically divided into three zones, a, b and c, and a group of these animals was then operated upon in such a way that only the piece a of the shoulder-blade remained preserved in the body; this piece a then regenerated the missing pieces (b and c) of the shoulder-girdle, as well as the appertaining limb removed simultaneously with b."

B. "In a second group of experimental animals only the piece c was left in the body from the shoulder-girdle, i.e. one piece of the coracoid and precoracoid; the other (b and a, together with the appertaining limb) was removed. This piece c then regenerated the lost portions of the shoulder-girdle and at the same time the appertaining limb which had been lost along with it."

C. "Thirdly, the piece a and b of the shoulder-girdle, with the appertaining limb, were then left in the body (or the latter cut back to the upper-arm head) and the c removed in such a way that a margin-portion touching b, which in other animals had regenerated b and a, here remained adhering to the piece b; although therefore in these experimental animals there was tissue at the produced wound surface which possessed regeneration-capacity, here every regeneration failed to appear, the wound simply healed."

D. "Quite the same was the behavior of the wound when the shoulder-girdle pieces c and b remained in the body, a, on the contrary, was so removed that its margin-portion abutting on b, which in other experimental animals had regenerated, remained preserved on b; here too, despite this, not the trace of a regeneration appeared." Tornier concludes from the experiments merely: "From these experiments it thus emerges that the simple setting of a wound surface does not suffice to release the regeneration-forces contained in a part of the body," and "the setting of the wound thus appeals to the regeneration-forces, prepares the ground for them and sets them free; the nourishment and feeding of the cells organizes them and releases them. Because they are, however, at the same time embryonal anlagen hitherto inhibited in their development, they are thereby simultaneously reorganized and reactivated." I am unable to find in these sentences any explanation for the failure of the regeneration experiments. Rather, Tornier has in these experiments once again unconsciously confirmed the proposition that the proximal part is indeed able to regenerate the distal, but the distal not the proximal, where it is not a matter of totipotent forms. As more proximal are to be regarded the shoulder-blade and coracoid relative to the limb-socket, since this latter gives the origin to the distally growing-on extremities. Each of these more-proximal parts is able to produce the more-distal limb and also the lost other proximal part from a wound surface which is turned toward the portions to be produced (experiments A and B). If, on the contrary, one wishes to obtain from the identical but proximally-turned wound surface of the socket-portion the completion of the scapula or of the coracoid, then regeneration does not succeed (experiments C and D), at least not without further ado, since here from the more-distal the more-proximal should be accomplished. After all the foregoing expositions I need not first explain at length that one, in case it does succeed in forcing regeneration, would have to expect mirror-image repetition of the distal parts, not completion of the more-proximal.

With this there now agree again completely the triple-formations at a break appearing at the shoulder- or pelvic-girdle of the urodeles, and, as we shall see, also of the anurans; for here not only the proximal break-line, but also the distal, inversely-looking one produces a new limb.

We are also able to a certain degree to make clear why Tornier obtained no regeneration in experiments C and D. It has namely shown itself, in transplantation of leg-pieces to various body-sites, that the entirely cut-off knee-parts at their new location mostly formed a foot only in the old distal direction (Kurz, 1912, p. 603). As in analogous cases, e.g. the hydranths of *Tubularia* (cf. Prz., Reg., p. 24), it seems to be the greater speed of "eupolar" growth which retards, or even itself suppresses, the inverse growth. We have indeed seen Nos. 206t and u regenerate inversely once, through binding of the inverse leg with the pelvis, the eupolar regeneration had been suppressed.

In the triple-formation at a break it seems to be the blood-supply made difficult by the bending-off to the old apex, often also the complete tearing-away of the same, which permit a relatively higher speed of the inverse component. This expresses itself too, often, in the lesser size of the extremity representing the original apex relative to the accessory ones — which one might otherwise perhaps have been inclined to interpret precisely as a counter-argument against their regenerative origin.

The question posed above, whether scapula or coracoid is to be regarded as more proximal, cannot for the present be answered, since both prove themselves equivalent.

A second question runs thus: how far toward the body-main-axis we can pursue the rule of the proximo-distal regeneration. Here, in contrast to older indications (cf. Prz., Reg., p. 168), Kurz (1912, p. 593) has with certainty established that, even after complete removal of the shoulder- or pelvic-girdle, regeneration of the whole extremity nevertheless ensued; if, on the contrary, the part of the vertebral column passing through the pelvis was removed along with it, then regeneration no longer ensued. The nerve-severing unavoidable in this is, according to many experiments of other authors (cf. Prz., Reg., p. 170), not to be regarded as the cause of the non-appearance, although a retarding influence on regeneration would thereby presumably be exerted. Since, then, after removal of the pelvis the vertebral portion adjacent to it is to be regarded as the more proximal site, we can, in the case of a break of the vertebral column at this place, expect the formation of a triple pelvis with appertaining limbs. To this are probably to be referred the tritons with six fully-developed legs; sure osteological findings of such a kind we shall find in the further vertebrates.

#### β) Anura.

##### I. Fore Extremity.

No. 207a. *Pelobates fuscus*, Otto (1841, p. 276, Table XXVII, Fig. 1) depicts a garlic-toad [spadefoot] with "two right forelegs," which were both fastened from the right shoulder-blade. While the more posteriorly-standing extremity seems, apart from the too-great length of the second finger, to be a normal right one, the one standing before it shows four short fingers standing in two groups, which can be interpreted as two 1. and 2. fingers, so that the two 3. and 4. of two mirror-image-fused hands were suppressed in the fusion-process. The interpretation gains in probability through the following cases, which partly bring before the eyes various transitions from typical triple-formations to this almost-complete fusion of two components, partly show still-further-going suppressions.

No. 207b. *P.* (sp.?), Gervais (1864, without Fig.) describes a garlic-toad which bore, immediately behind the left foreleg, a second one. "He could, however, see only three fingers, the two lateral ones rudimentary and, with a single stump-shaped phalanx-member, overtopping the corresponding metacarpalia, and the middle one, somewhat longer, provided with two phalanx-members." The supernumerary limb articulated on the normal coracoid by means of a supernumerary coracoid (+ "omoplate"). The symmetry of the three-fingered arm suggests the interpretation of a fusion going beyond that of the previous case, whereby the lateral toes would be homologized with fourth ones. Yet I do not hold it to be excluded that they were first ones, and that the position of the accessory arm, as standing behind the normal one, did not correspond to the anatomical state of facts.

**Nr. 207c.** *Bufo viridis*, Tornier (1901, p. 498, no figure) received from Dr. Apstein, Kiel, a toad in which a shoulder-blade … had been broken through, in which … the shoulder-blade body was not only erected vertically but, in addition, was so displaced that its wound-surface came to lie quite tightly above the neck of the shoulder-blade. As a consequence, this wound of the shoulder-blade body could not exert any superregenerative activity; it simply scarred over, and the severed shoulder-blade body thus remained forever without any connection to the shoulder girdle. By contrast, the exposed wound-surface of the shoulder-blade stump superregeneratively produced a supernumerary shoulder-blade neck up to the socket and the corresponding supernumerary limb. Here, then, according to Tornier, the one component of the triple-formation to be expected has been entirely suppressed. Unfortunately, information about the symmetry of the supernumerary limb is lacking, as well as figures, so that the cogency of the arguments cannot be checked. I cite the case nonetheless, because it is able to offer us, for the occurrence of isolated supplementary extremities, a plausible explanation in agreement with the general rules.

**Nr. 207d.** *Bufo columbiensis*, after Washburn (1899, Figs. 1–2), a five-legged toad from Oregon; correctly interpreted as six-legged by O'Donoghue (1910). In front of the normal left foreleg there projects forward and sideways a seven-fingered, in itself bilaterally symmetrical limb. In the skeleton a single shoulder-blade is recognizable, whereas a combination of triple coracoids and clavicles can be made out, so that the tripling takes its origin at the ventral part of the shoulder girdle. The thighs of the surplus pair of limbs are fused along their entire length, whereas the shanks are free. To these — which Washburn erroneously addressed as the tibia and fibula of a foreleg, since normally in the Batrachia complete fusion of these bones prevails — there attaches the seven-fingered hand formed out of two hands fused by their outer margins. The arrangement of the fingers into two groups of three fingers each, curved away from one another and separated by a forward-pointing finger, yields the typical mirror-image arrangements of the break-triple-formations, since the finger group nearer to the normal left forehand is turned toward the normal fingers, with opposite finger-curvature.

**Nr. 208.** *Rana halecina*, Johnson (1901, No. III, Figs. 2, 10–12) described a similar case from the Chicago University collection, but without giving clear information about the skeleton. Here, below the right foreleg, there arose a pair of limbs in the typical position, but the components are already entirely free from the thigh onward. The original extremity projecting beyond them is smaller than the two others, which have attained the size of the normal left one.

**Nr. 208a.** *Rana temporaria*, Sutton (1889, Case 1, Fig. 10) found, fastened by means of a supernumerary coracoid to the left half of the shoulder girdle, a supernumerary limb with three toes standing outward and in front of the normal left foreleg. The complete bilateral symmetry of the whole accessory arm is clear enough in the figure; the middle finger projects well beyond the very short lateral ones. Here it is surely a matter of two hands fused by their outer-sides [Außenseiten], and both lateral fingers are to be regarded as first ones.

**Nr. 208b.** *Rana pipiens*, Eigenmann and Cox (1901, Fig. 1) gave a description and figure of a frog in which a third foreleg, coming from the left side, ran forward to the right by means of a skin loop beneath the lower jaw. Since it appears to have possessed two thumbs, it is probably the fusion product of a pair of legs. Unfortunately the text and figures are unclear. (I have therefore not reproduced them.)

**Nr. 209.** *Rana esculenta*, Cavanna (1879, Plate I), as a continuation of his earlier publication on polymely in frogs (1877, which unfortunately remained inaccessible to me), figures a pond-frog behind whose left fore-extremity a pair of legs, fused up to the finger-base, stretches out sideways. The skeleton shows the typical mirror-image arrangement of the two supernumerary four-fingered hands. The articulation of the double leg occurred at an irregular bone that arose from a break in the forward-displaced original coracoid. The upper thigh-bone appears single; the lower leg diverges distinctly toward the wrist into two mutually mirror-image bones; the carpal bones are fused only innermost.

**Nr. 209a.** *R. esculenta*, *Bassi (1874) described, according to Taruffi's (1880, p. 116) information, a pond-frog with a "supernumerary limb on the right of the thorax, atrophic, with five fingers, and the middle finger rudimentary." It was probably a matter of two fused hands; unfortunately the original work remained inaccessible to me.

**Nr. 210.** *R. esculenta*, Ercolani (1882, p. 817, Plate IV, Figs. 7–9) lists, under No. 2537 of the Bologna Museum, a pond-frog that carries three forelegs on the left: "it cannot be said which of the three limbs represents the normal one; one can only conjecture that it is the middle one, Plate IV, Fig. 7, on account of the stronger development, in which this one possesses all the muscles of the forearm." Were this conjecture correct, we should have to regard the supernumerary forelegs as lying in front of the normal one, for what Ercolani designates as the middle one is, to judge by his osteological sketch, the hindmost component, beneath which the actually middle, mirror-image one crosses backward.

The development of the three components is, except for the mentioned crossing-over of the two accessory shanks, an entirely typical one. In the ventral shoulder-girdle region there are, instead of the one clavicular-coracoid hole, three present, which are formed by the clavicle and three coracoids. The shoulder-blade is single but bears two articulations.

**Nr. 211.** *R. esculenta*, Ercolani (1882, p. 816, Plate IV, Figs. 1–3) describes, as No. 3248 of the Bologna Museum [Alessandrini], a frog at whose left foreleg two accessory legs attach with fused shoulder-blades in such a way that they lie horizontally behind the normal limb with correctly oriented upper and under side. To the tripled shoulder-blade, which according to the figures unfortunately cannot be clearly analyzed, there attaches a bony mass which Ercolani probably correctly interprets as two fused coracoids, as is inferred in particular from the ventrally attaching muscles. To these coracoids fused along their length the accessory thighs insert, with the further mirror-image paired leg segments. The clavicles seem to be represented by the bony loop projecting between the heads of the thighs, unless they have been co-suppressed in the fusion of the coracoids. To the episternum the mentioned bony part seems not to be homologous, precisely because of the loop-form to be referred to two separate bones.

**Nr. 212.** *R. esculenta* = *viridis*, Lunel (1876, p. 309, Table I, Figs. 1–6) gives the outline and osteology of a frog whose left foreleg, tripled regularly according to the everrepeated rules, arose from a defective scapula. From the preceding case Nr. 211 it differs notably by the absence of the coracoid-elongation; the accessory coracoids are, on the contrary, very short, but here too there is found, between two forelegs — and indeed the foremost and the next-to-hindmost — a bone loop. Perhaps we may conclude from the analogy with the previous case that the hindmost-lying one is the normal one.

**Nr. 212a.** *R. esculenta*, Sordelli (1878, no figure) calls an edible frog with two supplementary stumps on the otherwise normal left foreleg similar to the previous case described by Lunel; owing to the lack of more precise information as well as the absence of fingers, nothing can be ascertained about the symmetry.

**Nr. 213.** *R. esculenta*, Tornier (1898, Figs. 1–5) received a frog from Professor Braun of Königsberg with a break-triple-formation of the right foreleg. The supernumerary mirror-image limbs stood dorsal to the somewhat stunted original foreleg. From a still recognizable break of the forward-displaced shoulder-blade arose the two accessory thighs; between them, in the continuation of the broken shoulder-blade, two coracoids uniting in a V-shape, whose plane of symmetry is bridged over by a stirrup-like bone. Tornier interprets the latter as a sternum and expresses himself with regard to the origin of the symmetry of the supernumerary regenerates: "and have these two formations then so influenced one another reciprocally that out of one of them a left, out of the other a right half of the false breast-girdle [Stiefbrustgürtel] arose? This question I cannot yet answer," Tornier continues. According to the generally valid rules of break-triple-formation developed by me, the desired answer follows of itself; only we must regard the stirrup-like bone not as a sternum but as the clavicles belonging to the accessory legs, fused into a single bone. The case is a downright diagrammatic example of the arrangement of the three components. The interpretation of the stirrup as a clavicle corresponds entirely to the reciprocal arrangement of the bones of each component, and the fusion at this place corresponds to the lack of space of the mirror-images bordering on one another along this line. The interpretation as a sternum is impossible, for the latter could not extend toward the shoulder-blade neck; moreover, a regeneration of the sternum from the latter is unknown and also improbable, since it probably represents a more proximal part. On the other hand, the regeneration of coracoid and clavicle has been demonstrated according to the experiments carried out by Tornier himself on urodeles (cf. above, after Nr. 206u, α–ζ). As we shall see later in the cases of break-triple-formation at the hindleg, Tornier proceeds precisely from the conception that the accessory limbs united into a bilateral symmetry represent a doubling of the normal girdle, hence the endeavor also to find the sternum as the connecting intermediate part; on this, see below Nr. 216a and following).

**Nr. 214.** *R. esculenta*, Ercolani (1882, p. 819, Plate IV, Figs. 7–9) reports in detail, under No. 2381 of the Bologna Museum, with the adducing of illustrated muscle- and bone-findings, on a quite similar case of a typically tripled right fore-extremity. Somewhat divergent is the more anterior than dorsal position of the supernumerary formations. Interesting is the finding of the muscles, which on the middle component exhibit, in correspondence with the skeletal basis, the course of a left side. The hindmost component, which probably corresponds to the original one, is not only the weakest but also merely three-fingered.

**Nr. 215.** *R. esculenta*, Mazza (1888, Plate Ia, Figs. 1–6) received from the surroundings of Novara a frog behind whose left fore-extremity two further forelegs, fused by their thighs, extended backward in such a way that their shanks lay parallel to the main body axis and the fingers pointed tailward. The osteological examination showed that, at the sternum — to which the episternum was lacking — the hyposternum deviated somewhat to the right. To it there inserted three bones parallel to the normal coracoid and clavicle inserting above at the sternum. According to Mazza, the thickest, foremost of these three bones would be a clavicle, the thinnest middle one a coracoid fused with the clavicle of the hindmost component, the third the coracoid precisely of the hindmost component. The latter is certainly correct, but the relative breadth of the two other bones accords far better with the interpretation of a symmetry-inversion of the middle component: then the foremost, thickest bone is the coracoid of the middle, the slender middle one is the clavicle of the hindmost component. There remains doubtful only whether this clavicle also includes the inverse clavicle, or whether the latter was fused with its coracoid lying in front of it. The three bones are connected at their ends turned away from the sternum by a triangular shoulder-blade bone. This unusual form, too, is explained by the fusion of two inversely-against-one-another growing shoulder-blades, when the growing-through across the fusion-surface has not been achieved, as one can easily convince oneself schematically by a corresponding drawing.

The inversion of the middle component follows also immediately from the position and the toe-finding of this limb, which represents a mirror-image to the normal left one standing in front of it. Less clear is the symmetry-relationship of the hindmost component, which apparently had been forced by the skin-fusion of the thigh to participate in the position of the middle one, and which moreover has only one developed finger, so that the finger-position cannot be drawn upon. But for this case it is here certain that its coracoid lies behind the clavicle, that it is therefore, as regards the shoulder part, a matter of a left extremity. The special interest of the case lies in the fact that both the shoulder-blade and the coracoid-clavicle part appear tripled from the sternum onward, whereas in the previous cases the tripling tended to proceed only from one of these two bone-parts alone. Is it here perhaps a matter of a break of the sternum, and is this capable of regenerating all the other girdle parts? As the part most proximal to the midline of the animal, this would not be improbable; yet no experiments exist that would authenticate it. They would presumably be more easily carried out on urodeles, because here one can still bring developed animals to regeneration, whereas, as is still to be discussed, the anurans acquire break-triple-formations regeneratively only at larval stages.

## B. Hindlimb.

**Nr. 216.** *Alytes obstetricans*, Cisternas (1865, no figure) wrote a letter about an obstetric toad [midwife toad] in which a third leg had grown out of the rear pelvic region to the left of the median. It had only four instead of the five toes characteristic of the hindlimb, and of these the middle ones were apparently still fused, while the two outermost were the shortest. If we keep before our eyes the cases observed in the two forelimbs of anurans of apparently simple supplements, then we can in this case too admit the interpretation that it is a matter of two by their inner margins intimately fused mirror-images. The sequence of the toes from left to right would, at the left body-half, be I, II, III, IV, V — V, IV, IV, V, and the scheme, according to the designation-mode cited further below (Nr. 216a) from Tornier: *l, r′, l′, r*.

[*Lataste (1879) and *Duméril (1865), who treat a case perhaps belonging here or even referring to the same specimen, I could unfortunately not examine.]

Tornier has further succeeded (1905 and 1906) in fully demonstrating, by experimental means, the regenerative origin of supernumerary limbs in the garlic-toad [Knoblauchkröte]. The brilliant success of his experimental skill must not, however, mislead us into accepting the analyses of the regeneration process given by him as convincing. On the contrary, it is very necessary to undertake a strict separation of the actual findings and the Tornierian derivations. Also there are found in Tornier's descriptions printing errors and probably sometimes errors of reasoning, which come to light on comparison of the text with the figures and demand a critical correction. Of the figures Tornier writes in a note at the beginning of his work (1905, p. 76):

"The figures and other drawings appended to this work were prepared by the painter Herr Paul Flanderky (Berlin); it may be expressly pointed out, in addition, that they are of great fidelity to nature."

It is therefore certainly permissible to hold directly to these; I have found no reason to doubt their fidelity to nature, since in all cases — sometimes in contrast to Tornier's own statements — their agreement with the general rules of break-triple-formation has emerged, as the following lines will show.

The work (1905) "contains the description of the supernumerary hindlimbs of twelve garlic-toads (*Pelobates fuscus* Laur.), all of which have been experimentally produced according to one and the same method. The operation method was as follows: when in each of these animals, in the early larval stage, the two hindlimb anlagen — independently of one another and each at one of the body sides, as little cones with circular outline (as in Fig. 1 at b the right one) — had become clearly recognizable, the attempt was made, on each animal with only a single use of the scissors, and indeed by a single longitudinal cut, to cut through both limb anlagen in their upper part simultaneously and in like manner."

The keeping-open of the wound margins occurs in this operation method automatically:

"If, in a frog-larva, by an oblique cut that splits the hind end of its body wall, the lower tail muscles of the animal are also injured, then the tail is curved strongly upward by the muscle-tonus of its upper side, and the upper pelvic-region section moves off from the lower one rearward; and when then later the severed hind body-wall of the animal again approaches the tail, in order finally to fuse with it, the rearward displacement of the upper pelvic-region section shapes itself into the extreme" (1905, p. 88).

So far, and in this — that henceforth each of the two wound-surfaces leads to a regeneration process — I agree with Tornier. Only the manner and way in which the experimenter has the supernumerary parts proceed from the incised ones I can, as little as other authors (e.g. Della Valle, 1913, p. 122), agree with. Proceeding from the view that, in his first operation method, the anlage of the iliac wing [Darmbeinflügel] is separated from the rest of the pelvic section, Tornier has the latter of each side develop the original stem-limb and, as regenerate, a new iliac wing, while the severed iliac-wing anlage of each side is supposed to give origin to a supernumerary pelvis together with two corresponding accessory legs; and indeed the iliac-wing anlage would at the operation stage still be composed of an ischial and an iliac cartilage-nucleus, of which the one would thus produce the one accessory pelvic half together with hindleg, the other the second half with mirror-image extremity (text-fig. 7).

Tornier (p. 81) expresses himself with regard to the symmetry as follows: "The lower section (g) of each pelvic half thus preserved strictly, in its regenerate-course, its relations to the body-polarity, i.e. it produced exactly as new what had previously been lost to it. The regenerate-behavior of the upper section of the pelvic half in question (h), on the other hand, in no way corresponds to the body-polarity of the animal." From his schematic Figs. 7 to 8, however, it emerges, on comparison with the documentations [Belegungen], that this whole derivation already for this reason does not hold, because the spreading-surface of the accessory pelvises and legs would then have to stand perpendicular to the normal pelvis-leg plane, whereas in fact it always lies almost exactly in the same horizontal with it, as follows at once from the fact that the hindlegs could be represented on the figures in the plane of the paper without perspective distortion. Incidentally, this is also the case with the schematic figures given by Tornier for the osteology of the supernumerary pelvises (p. 91, Figs. 17, 18, 20), with one exception (Fig. 19), and in the text it is said about this (p. 90):

"The fifth and sixth findings of the previous chapter were that the supernumerary pelvis, with its transverse axis, either lies like the genuine one, i.e. horizontally, or is placed with it more or less vertically, which in other words says that, in order to attain these last-named positions, it has undergone a rotation about its longitudinal axis outward by an angle far over 90°."

"The cause of origin of these characters is the following: As already said earlier, the supernumerary pelvis has, normally, the endeavor to take up a position on the body of the animal that corresponds to that of the genuine pelvis, and it further sends its two iliac wings, which are always fused at the tip, forward against the sacral wing [Kreuzbeinflügel] of the body-side in question; but toward this same sacral wing the genuine iliac wing of the body-side in question now also strives, and so this latter very soon comes into a struggle for space with the like-striving iliac wings of the supernumerary pelvis. If it then (Fig. 17) succeeds in the genuine iliac wing (cl) thrusting the whole supernumerary pelvis (a′–l′) away from its place, then the supernumerary pelvis retains its hitherto, i.e. normal, orientation to the body axis also henceforth; if on the other hand (Fig. 18) the genuine iliac wing (cl) is not in a position to remove the supernumerary pelvis (a′–l′) in this way, then it first presses together both its iliac wings (cr′ and cl′) at the expense of the clearance [Lichtung] (l′) that lies between them, until under circumstances they touch, and rotates the false pelvis itself about its longitudinal axis more or less around (Fig. 19 by 90°, Fig. 20 by 180°), in that it attacks the neighboring supernumerary iliac wing (cr′) laterally from above and presses it outward; in extreme cases the genuine iliac wing (cl) fuses, thirdly, then more or less firmly with the iliac wing (cr′) of the supernumerary pelvis attacked by it."

Tornier thus himself regards, in contrast to his schemes Figs. 7–8 and the legend belonging thereto, the horizontal plane as the original direction for the accessory parts. Before I now go on to give another presentation of the developmental course of his break-triple-formations, the documentations given by Tornier shall be critically examined in the sequence he gave, and described in the sense of the symmetry-rules hitherto observed. Also only afterward shall the general rules be entered into for the symmetry-formation in regenerates, which Tornier seeks to discern in all his relevant works and which amounts to a subsequent assumption of a neighboring symmetry — "contact-symmetry" — of the regeneration buds.

**Nr. 216a.** *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 93, Figs. 22 and 23): Documentation-specimen 1. At each of the two body-halves, by a single scissor-cut, an accessory pair of legs with mirror-image symmetry has arisen. But it must first be decided by critical consideration in what relationship this symmetry stands to that of the normal hind-extremities of the frog-larva. Tornier asserts that the original arrangement of the two accessory pairs of legs was parallel to the normal ones, and "had the animal then, in the normal position — i.e. with the belly downward — counted from left to right, the following limb-arrangement at the beginning of its malformation: genuine left hindlimb, left supernumerary, right supernumerary; tail; left supernumerary, right supernumerary, right genuine, or in letters: *l, l′, r′*¹⁾; *l′, r′, r*." Later, owing to the abutting of the right hindlimb in swimming movements against its accessory right partner, the accessory pair belonging to it was rotated onto the back. If, however, one considers Flanderky's figures, one sees on the dorsal view Fig. 22 the accessory pair of the right side from the dorsal side; it is thus not turned onto the belly. That it is the dorsal side one recognizes unmistakably on comparison with the normal right one, which exhibits the same contouring and speckled marking, whereas the underside would have to be almost spotlessly pale. By contrast, the accessory legs of the left side are seen from behind, since the whole volar surface of the foot faces the beholder and the legs are drawn almost spotlessly. Quite in agreement with this interpretation stands also Fig. 23, which shows the toad lying on its back: here the accessory legs of the right side turn toward the beholder the same side as the right normal one, thus the underside; on the other hand, the accessory left-standing pair reveals itself, by the full view of the foot-dorsum and the stronger drawing as compared with the normal one, as turned forward. While Tornier thus has, from an original position *l, l′, r′; l′, r′, r*, the position *l, l′, r′; r′, l′, r* arise, it emerges from the figures that this latter arrangement has come into being by rotation of the legs *l′, r′* following upon *g* downward, hence out of *l, r′, l′; r′, l′, r*, and could at any time be restored by rotation of this pair of legs forward, whereby *r′* would come to stand, analogous to the *l′* of the right side, between the two left components. With regard to the symmetry it now shows itself that on each side the middle of the three components is inverse, and since all limbs spread out in one horizontal plane, all rules of the Batesonian scheme are fulfilled. (Osteology is not given.)

> ¹⁾ In Tornier, incidentally, a printing error: *r* instead of *r′*.

[The paragraph at the top of this page is the conclusion of Nr. 216b, which began on the previous page; it belongs to the preceding chunk and is translated there.]

Nr. 216c. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 98, Figs. 26 and 27): Specimen 3. On the left, three hind legs, of which the two hindmost are fused with each other at their outer margins. Arrangement scheme according to Tornier *l′, r′, l; r*. Thus the middle inverse component would be fused with the normal left one. According to the osteology depicted in Fig. 27, however, I consider it more probable that the foremost component is the continuation of the original leg anlage that was severed off by the scissor-cut, and that the two accessory ones are the fused ones, hence to be expressed schematically as *l, r′, l′; r*.

Nr. 216d. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 101, Fig. 28): Specimen 4. On the right, three hind limbs, quite similar to the left-side ones of Specimen 3; on the left, two left legs. According to Tornier: *r′, l; r, l′, r′*. Anatomy not given; not dissected, for the sake of undamaged preservation. (The malformation of the left¹⁾ side of the body Tornier reserves for a later discussion, which, however, I miss in the paper.)

> ¹⁾ In Tornier's text a slip of the pen: right, instead of left.

Nr. 216e. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 102, Figs. 29–31): Specimen 5. On the left, similar to the preceding ones, but the two foremost components are the fused ones; on the right normal. According to Tornier²⁾: *l, r′, l′; r*. From the osteological Figs. 30 and 31 it is supposed to emerge (p. 104): "in what manner the step-pelvis [Stiefbecken] is still otherwise related to the genuine one; and it is shown from the figure [30] that it no longer lies, as those described hitherto did, on the outer side of the genuine pelvis, but in the opening of its iliac wings (*l*); that it is, furthermore, fused with the genuine left iliac wing and forms with the same an approximately right angle — which proves, and is also shown especially clearly, secondly, from the position of the limbs attached to it — that it is directed with its underside turned upward …" To which it must be remarked that, according to Fig. 29, the accessory limbs decidedly display the same dorsoventrality as the original ones, hence a turning-over of the step-pelvis belonging to them is in the highest degree improbable. Tornier continues (p. 105): "The pelvis itself, however, has attained this position by being rotated, by means of the right [recte: left] iliac wing (*l*), so far in the clockwise sense about its long axis, until it came to lie on its back, whereupon only then did the fusion of the two take place. Thus too is it alone explained that, in the step-pelvis, the left limb (*l′*) is directed toward the right side of the animal's body, the right one (*r′*) toward the left, whereas in Specimen 2 of this paper (Fig. 25) the position of the pelvis and of the limbs attached to it is just the reverse, that is, of the step-limbs, which lie side by side in the horizontal, it is the left one that is directed outward."

> ²⁾ If one reads the text attentively and takes the figure into account; in the scheme there is the printing error in Tornier: *l, r, l, l* instead of *l, r′, l′*.

Nr. 216f. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 106, Figs. 32 and 33): Specimen 6 "seemingly has only four hind limbs, but in reality six, namely, besides the normal ones, two supernumerary ones on each side of the body. The proof lies in the following: Of the four apparent hind limbs of the animal, two (*r′, l′*) are quite uninjured, of whose full formation there will therefore be no doubt. The two others, seemingly simple hind limbs of the animal (*r, l′* and *l, r′*), are, however, strongly malformed double-limbs, in which two hind limbs, alike only in mirror-image fashion, are united in each — that is, a right and a left one — and this is, moreover, already evident externally from the fact that each of these limbs bears two shovels [Schaufeln] (*s*), which, furthermore, sit on the outer margins of the double-foot and thereby prove that the foot bearing them is really a double-foot composed of two components — a right and a left one; for the animal there results from this, however, the following limb-arrangement from left to right: *l′, r′, l; r, l′, r′*. But still better and more securely is this proved by the pelvic structure of the animal, which is represented in Fig. 33 from the upper side." On this it is to be noted that it remains questionable which are the original legs — whether, as Tornier assumes, the foremost, rudimentary-fused component, or the hindmost on each side of the body, which he designates as fully formed?

Nr. 216g. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 109, Figs. 34 and 35): Specimen 7. Behind the left, defective hind leg, which lacks the first toe and the shovel, hangs a double-leg; the right hind leg is normal. Tornier gives a circumstantial description of the manner of origin, from which, just as from the labellings of the figures, it emerges that he holds the scheme *l, l′, r′; r* to be correct, although he does not give the schematic designation for this specimen. But if one keeps to the figures themselves and takes into consideration that the 4th toe of frogs is distinguished by its most considerable length, the 1st by its least development and its nearness to the shovel, and furthermore that the upper side of the limb is more strongly pigmented while the volar side of the foot is marked by a hollowing-out, then there can be no doubt that the extremity designated by Tornier in Fig. 34 as *r′* represents a left one, and the one designated *l′* a right one. Hence the correct scheme reads: *l, r′, l′; r*. Osteologically [the noteworthy feature] is the complete fusion of the thighs of our double-leg, which also extends to the bones still adjoining the normal left iliac wing. The tibiae are represented almost only by the spurs [Sporen].

Nr. 216h. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 112, Figs. 36 and 37): Specimen 8. "This animal has, as its full image shows, four hind limbs, hence two supernumerary ones. These lie, furthermore, on the left side of the body behind the left stem-limb (*l*) and are, in their thigh region, enclosed together with the latter in a common skin-sheath. In doing so, these supernumerary limbs form, from the toes onward, a double structure (*r′, l′*), whose feet are so strongly fused with their inner margins that the two foot-shovels are fused into a double-shovel (*2 s*), which can be securely established from the characteristic heart-shape of this double-shovel. Furthermore, of the two components of this double-foot, only one, namely the right (*r′*), has the normal number of toes, the left (*l′*), on the other hand, only three toes, for it lacks the fifth and the first." According to Tornier, then, the scheme to be set up would be: *l, l′, r′; r*. If, however, one considers Fig. 36, in which the double-foot is in exact dorsal view while the normal hind limbs, owing to the half-pronated position of the feet, present their volar surface to the half [i.e. partly], then the foot designated by Tornier as *r′* reveals itself as a left one (*l′*), the one he designated *l′* as a right one (*r′*). Hence the scheme reads correctly: *l, r′, l′; r*. As regards the osteology, Tornier states (p. 113 and Fig. 37): "On the left genuine iliac wing of the animal (*l*) sits, inclined quite outward, the body of a supernumerary left pelvic half (*a′*). It is provided with a good articular surface on the outer side, in which a left step-thigh (*l′*) articulates. This passes into the supernumerary double-limb …" In the figure, there is designated by *l′* a femur weaker than the normal right thigh and standing farther forward; whereas, by *l*, a thigh standing farther back and stronger than the normal right one is designated; if the anterior one is supposed to lead to the supernumerary components, this does not agree with Tornier's earlier statement that these lie behind the left stem-limb. It would probably be a matter of a confusion of the two left-standing thighs, in that the posterior, stronger one would presumably have arisen from the fusion of the two femora of the double-leg. On the right¹⁾ side of the body, too, there are found indications of a break of the ilium with multiple-formation, namely a lumen in the iliac wing and a skin-pouch on the thigh.

> ¹⁾ In Tornier, p. 114, there is incidentally a slip of the pen, line 11 of p. 114 from the top: left side of body, instead of right.

Nr. 216i. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 114, Fig. 38): Specimen 9. "The present frog is very important, because on its right side of the body it bears a malformation which can almost be designated as the mirror-image of that malformation which, in the animal just described, belonged to the left side of the body; for this frog, too, has four hind limbs, among them therefore two supernumerary ones, which on its right side of the body form a double-limb that lies behind the right stem-limb (*r*) of the animal and, in the thigh region, shares with it a common sheath. Accordingly, the animal has the following limb-arrangement from left to right: *l; l′, r′, r*." Here something quite analogous to what was said for Specimen 8 is to be said, namely that Tornier's *l′* and *r′* indeed make up the left- and right-standing components of the double-leg, but, according to their symmetry, are to be designated the reverse, namely *r′* and *l′* respectively; hence the scheme should correctly read: *l; r′, l′, r*. Internally, all parts of a supernumerary pelvis were lacking, as were the thighs of the additional structures down to the lower heads, which were fused into one, and on which the two accessory lower legs are articulated. The suppression of these bones, as well as the other stuntings of the additional structures — e.g. the lack of a toe on the middle component — Tornier traces back, as also otherwise in similar cases, to tail-blows [Schwanzschläge], which, in the larval stage, must have struck the multiple-formation wedged between the normal limb and the tail. This possibility is not to be disputed — since, in the higher vertebrates too, atrophies of unused extremities occur, as we shall yet hear — but probably not established. In the homologization of the vanished toe, Tornier designates it as the fifth: this is evidently incorrect, for in Fig. 38 there is clearly to be recognized on *r′* (which, as set forth, should correctly be called *l′*) the very long 4th toe, onto which, forward, the 5th toe attaches in accordance with its inverse symmetry, while backward the 3rd toe is still visible. One sees, furthermore, that "toe 1, though it did indeed come to development, [did so] so low down on the component that one might almost suppose it to belong to the other double-foot component." The lacking toe is accordingly, since the 5th, 4th, 3rd, and 1st are demonstrable, the second.

Nr. 216j. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 117, Figs. 39–41): Specimen 10. "In the full image, the animal now before us shows three hind limbs, hence one of them is supernumerary, and indeed it (*l′*) lies on the left side of the body, between the left stem-hind-limb of the animal (*l*) and its coccyx (*a*), then, however, also above the left stem-limb and immediately above the anus of the frog. It is, furthermore, half flexed at the knee-joint and looks with the sole of its foot upward … Then, furthermore, as the figure also shows, the animal's vertebral column, or, more precisely speaking, its coccyx (*a*), is somewhat bent toward the right. The limb-sequence of the animal is thereby: *l′, l, r*. Very interesting then are the pelvic relations of the animal, and Fig. 40 shows its genuine and supernumerary pelvis seen from the left side. From this it follows, firstly, that the step-pelvis (*a′, c′*) is altogether independent of the genuine one (*a, c*). It lies, furthermore, high above the latter and somewhat more toward the tail than the genuine one … Then Fig. 41 furthermore shows that this step-pelvis is not, as it appears, a single pelvic half, for it bears on its inner side, which is depicted in this figure, still the remnant of a thigh-bone, *r′*, and indeed dislocated in such a manner that its lower end looks toward the snout of the animal and not tailward, as the norm would be." We thus have to supplement the scheme to: *l′, r′, l; r*. The skin-fold *b* in Fig. 39, interpreted otherwise by Tornier on p. 119, I would, incidentally, in analogy to the thigh-pouch of Specimen 8, likewise regard as a rudiment of the middle component of the triple-formation.

Nr. 216k. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 120, Figs. 42–44): Specimen 11. Very similar to the preceding one, only the supernumerary ilium attaches with its wing-tip to the normal left one, and the inverse component is entirely lacking, unless the bulge designated by *x*, opposite the femoral socket of the accessory leg, were to be interpreted as the last remnant? The scheme we can write: *l, r′?, l′; r*.

Nr. 216l. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1905, p. 122, Figs. 45 and 46): Specimen 12. "Three hind limbs present; one of them, then, is supernumerary, and lies (*l′*), as is easy to recognize, on the left side of the body of the animal. It is, furthermore, in its form a left one, which springs from a skin-pouch in such a manner that it dangles to and fro on a thin skin-stalk on the body of the animal.
