It is, furthermore, not essentially shorter than the corresponding left stem-hind-limb of the frog, behind which it lies, but much more slender … The stem-pelvis of the animal (*a, c*) appears completely normal … The skeleton of the supernumerary limb, from the thigh down to the toe-tips, is normal, but the thigh (*l′*) bears as an attachment a small tongs-like cartilage-plate (*d*). This cartilage-plate undoubtedly represents a piece of pelvis …" I would regard it as a rudiment of pelvis plus right femur, just as Tornier too believes that it is a remnant of a whole pelvis that was laid down. We thus obtain as the scheme: *l, r′?, l′; r*.

Nr. 216m. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1906, p. 268, without figures of the specimens) applied, in a second series of experiments, other modes of operation; he "cut through the left pelvic half of a series of *Pelobates fuscus* larvae in such a way that the cut (Fig. 9) either passed perpendicularly through the iliac anlage (*2*) alone, or in the same manner (at *z*) traversed the ischium (*1*). There arose in this way (example in Fig. 10), in each of the experimental animals, on the operated pelvic half a wound with two wound-surfaces that lay far apart from each other. From these wound-surfaces, each then produced a pelvic section with socket and corresponding limb, so that each of these operated pelvic halves received, in addition to its own germ-true limb, 2 supernumerary ones."

"But these limbs behaved very peculiarly" — Tornier continues (p. 269) — "of which Figs. 10 and 11 give an example. The supernumerary limb that arose on the main section of the pelvic half — that is, on that piece of pelvis which (at *b*) bears the germ-true limb — became the mirror-image of the germ-true one (*l*), hence a right one (Fig. 11: *r′*). The cut-off pelvic cap (*h*), on the other hand, completed itself regeneratively into a whole pelvic half, which is equivalent to the one from which it stems, and the limb that arose with it became for that reason a left one (*l′*). Indeed, the ilium of this pelvic half arisen from the cap (*h*) even sent out, in the present example, later, an iliac wing (*c′*) to the sacrum of the animal, which then fused with the germ-true iliac wing (*c*). The two wound-surfaces that arose in one pelvic half in this operation were thus, by the cut, removed so far from each other that their regenerates could not form symmetry for one another, but entered into symmetry with that body-part from which they proceeded."

Nr. 216n. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1906, p. 270, without figure). "As in the frog-pelvic-half just described, so now also in all the others that were operated by the same method — hence also in those in which the cut traversed the ilium perpendicularly — the two supernumerary limbs did not enter into symmetry with one another, but with the body-piece from which they stemmed."

Nr. 216o. *P. fuscus*, Tornier (1901, p. 498, without figure) had received, through Wolterstorff, a spadefoot toad [Knoblauchkröte] with two supernumerary hind limbs on the left, and addressed it as a break-triple-formation: "These have arisen from the fact that the corresponding ilium of the animal broke in the middle, whereupon out of its two wound-surfaces there grew a supernumerary pelvic half each, with corresponding hind limb. The two supernumerary pelvic halves laid themselves against each other and thus formed a supernumerary pelvis of approximately normal shape, while the lowermost of the corresponding supernumerary limbs fused, from the thigh down to the tip, with the still more deeply lying left stem-limb of the animal, whereby in this animal a double-limb arose, whose foot consists of two halves fused at the outer side, which turn their soles toward each other." This turning of the soles toward each other is a consequence of the torsion of the feet that is normal in the Batrachia, which brings it about that, on view from above, only the 5th and the margin of the 4th toe are seen dorsally, while the remaining toes, displaced toward the median plane, turn their plantar side toward the beholder, so that the soles of the normal legs too are directed toward each other.

I had already remarked, on an earlier occasion (Exp. Zool., 2nd ser., 1909, p. 210, note), on Tornier's cases published in 1906:

"Tornier contrasts, in this treatise, the new experiments with his older ones, in that in the latter the accessory legs formed symmetry with each other, in the former the nearer accessory one with the normal leg. I am unable to attach any importance to this distinction, because 1. in the latter case too, naturally, the accessory ones are likewise symmetrical with each other; 2. the scheme given by Tornier in the second treatise for the experiments of the first treatise does not fit at all for the overwhelming majority of his specimens there, but would have to be replaced by one essentially similar to the one depicted for his new experiments."

Of this one can easily convince oneself if one sets up the schematic sequences of the legs for the experiments Nr. 216m and n and compares them with those for the previously discussed Nr. 216a–l. The scheme for the second experimental method yields, according to Tornier's Fig. 11 (1906, p. 269): *l, r′, l′; r*. For the first experimental method of the 1905 work, the scheme *l, l′, r′; r* would, according to Tornier (1906, p. 268, Fig. 8), have to be set up. After the thorough critique of his 12 specimens, however, it emerges — as can be seen quite especially sharply from the appended Table C — that this gives the symmetry correctly in not a single case; rather, the middle component always represents the inversely directed one, hence the scheme for the case *l, r′, l′; r* adduced by Tornier by way of comparison

> *) It may also be pointed out that the symmetry-type of the extremities listed from left to right could indeed be assessed with certainty correctly, but that it cannot be established with certainty which component of a multiple-formation is to be regarded as the "normal" one, which here is to be designated without the addition of a little prime-mark [Strichelchen].

would have to read *l, r′, l′; r* and agrees with the scheme for the second experimental series.

A difference in the result of the two operation-methods consists solely in this, that in the horizontal severing of the iliac anlage each of the accessory legs develops its iliac wing, which unite at the tip, whereas in the vertical severing — at least according to Tornier's description and schematic picture (1906, p. 269, Fig. 10) — only the cut-off iliac cap sends out an iliac wing of its own, while the inverse limb, regenerated from the wound of the main part of the iliac anlage and remaining in any case in close connexion with the normal iliac wing, forms no wing of its own.

Herewith we now come to speak of how I conceive the Tornierian triple-formations on *Pelobates* larvae to have arisen at all. By each of his incisions into the still undifferentiated pelvic anlage of the hind legs, a more proximal part of this anlage is severed from a more distal part. Since the distal part is destined for the formation of the distally adjoining extremity, after the laying-down of the latter it is no longer able to form the more proximal parts, and produces, on its proximally turned wound-surface, an inverse supernumerary extremity, while the distally turned original end-part of the anlage forms a correctly oriented limb.

**No. 217.** *Bufo vulgaris,* Sutton (1889, Fig. 11) describes from University College London a five-toed toad whose supernumerary seventeen-toed leg projected laterally from the right pelvis. According to the figure, this formation is undoubtedly a double-leg whose components are united in mirror-image fashion, with the disappearance of the two first toes and the fusion of the two second toes. In the absence of osteological data, nothing can be said about the components of the outwardly simple-appearing thigh and lower leg. The articulation of the accessory formation seems, like the partial reduction of toes, to be similar to *Brutling 9* (Tornier, 1906; our No. 216i); the limb is accordingly to be thought of as traceable back to the normal one, whereby the regular arrangement of the triple-formation-at-a-break — *r, l, r* — appears for the right side of the body.

**No. 218.** *Rana halecina,* Johnson (1901, Figs. 3, 4, 8, 9) reports a frog collected by C. E. Cummings in Buffalo, which still bore a fused pair of legs above the left hindleg; "this formation surely confirms Bateson's findings." In the figures the symmetry is unfortunately not recognizable.

**No. 218a.** *R. palustris,* Ryder (1878, without fig.) obtained from Jacob Stauffer data and a sketch (which, however, he did not reproduce) of a marsh frog with a supernumerary six-toed hind limb colored below as on the normal upper surface. Side and symmetry are not stated more closely. Since, however, the hind body is designated as asymmetrical, the six toes as 5., 4., 3. — 3., 4., 5., and the feet, consistently, as fused along their inner margins, we may with the greatest probability assume this double limb to be articulated behind the normal one and therefore subject to the same scheme as the preceding ones.

**No. 218b.** *R. palustris,* Tuckermann (1886, pl. XVI) explains a three-toed hind limb arising on the left at the hind end of a marsh frog as a right one; but since the lower leg had no sulcus and of the toes only the middle one, with three phalanges, can be diagnosed with certainty, and indeed as the fourth, a fusion similar to the following case No. 220a is probable.

**No. 219.** *R. temporaria,* Strobel (1876, Pl. VII, Fig. 3) reports from the Museum at Reggio a frog whose left hindleg bore a second thigh, regarded by the describer as composed of two fused ones, which in turn served as the starting point for two lower legs and feet. From the figures, especially 3 and 3c, the regular symmetry of the triple-formation-at-a-break emerges; the normal limb cannot, however, be recognized among the two left hindlegs; the middle component has, as usual, the inverse symmetry, and therefore, being right-symmetrical, does not come into consideration as the normal left one.

**No. 220.** *R. temporaria,* Duméril (1865, p. 314, No. VIII, Fig. 2) cites a frog from which, from the lumbar region¹, behind the normal right hindleg, an accessory hind limb hung down. From the figure it is clearly to be gathered that this limb, although bearing only five toes, must nevertheless be regarded as a double one, fused along the inner margins. For the accessory foot consists of two groups of toes, in that the middlemost toe is by far shorter than the two standing nearest to it,

> ¹ By this is probably meant nothing other than that, in overall view, the accessory leg appears to come from this region — not, say, that it arises from the lumbar vertebrae, as Bender (1906, p. 131) believes.

but not shorter than the two outermost ones. On comparison with the normal feet we have to regard the two longest toes of the accessory one as the 4., hence those closing onto its free margin as the 5., and the one standing in the middle as the fusion product of the two 3., while the two 2. and 1. have been completely suppressed in the fusion. The arrangement of the accessory toes would thus be: 5., 4., 3̄., 4., 5., and onto these attach the toes of the normal right one with 5., 4., 3., 2., 1., if we think of the foot — shown in palmar view in the figure owing to the normal rotation of the frog's foot — as turned back into the dorsal position. The scheme for the present case thus reads, similarly to the earlier ones: *l; r', l", r.*

**No. 220a.** *R. (fusca =) temporaria,* Bender (1906, p. 122, Tab. X) described in detail an *epipygus* of the grass frog kept at the Pathological Institute in Strasbourg (Syst. Cat. A. IV. 52), of which a brief habitus note is also contained in Paulicki in the German Journal for Veterinary Medicine (XII) and in Schwalbe's Morphology of Malformations, I (1906). "The frog, otherwise of ordinary appearance, possesses on the left side, beside the normal extremity, a supernumerary one, which is attached caudally between the former and the coccyx (Steißbein) of the left pelvic half (Fig. 1). Superficially regarded, the extremity consists of thigh and lower leg, tarsus, and three toes. The thigh appears strongly shortened and bent, and is connected with the pelvis by a joint which permits free movements in all directions. The vertex of the knee joint is directed to the right and dorsally, the joint only passively movable to a limited degree. There follows the lower leg of approximately normal length and configuration; the tip of the crurotarsal joint points to the left, metatarsus and toes assume a purely caudally directed position parallel to the median line of the animal. From the behavior of the joints it is evident that the supernumerary leg stands similarly to the right one, thus representing a mirror image of the left one, a position which has indeed already been observed many times." The now-following more exact investigation of the skeleton shows noteworthy deviations from the normal behavior of a simple extremity: although the pelvis appears unchanged according to the X-ray picture, on palpation the left ilium is recognizable as thickened, but a notch or split is not perceptible. "The lower-leg bone almost reaches the usual length, yet is thin, slightly bent, and shows no composition out of tibia and fibula, since the sulcus intermedius is lacking. The proximal joint end stands in the hollow of the knee, and is thus shifted so far past the flexor side of the femoral condyles that the two bones now touch only along one edge (cf. röntgenogram)." "Instead of the two proximal tarsal bones there is found only one strong skeletal part, whose distal epiphysis is laid down very broadly and bears three well-developed metatarsals. Of the distal row there exists only a small wedge-shaped cartilage (Fig. 3) on the fibular side between tarsus and metatarsus V. . . . According to its behavior . . . the small cartilage can only be conceived as a displaced tarsale II + III." It will be shown that the interpretation of this cartilage, just like that of many muscle individuations, causes great difficulties, indeed can be impossible, as also emerges from the few analogous cases in the literature. — "The middle of the three metatarsal bones possesses four, the lateral ones three phalanges each, hence toes III, IV, V. Completely lacking are: Centrale, Tarsale I, Praehallux, the first and second ray." The interpretive difficulties seem to me to become much smaller if we regard this allegedly right-symmetrical limb as one fused out of two mirror-image ones. The toes standing on either side of toe IV can equally well be two V., the III. and V. In the fusion all toes up to IV have then disappeared, and likewise the mentioned small wedge-shaped cartilage is interpretable as the fusion product of all the distal metatarsals without displacement. The simple lower-leg bone, after complete swallowing-up of both fibulae, consists of the outer margins of the two tibiae, which join one another, equally broad and without a sulcus. That Bender misses, at the pelvis, persisting remains of wounding and on this account rejects a regenerative origin (p. 130), rests in my view on the slight divergence of the wound margins, which precisely for that reason had led to so far-reaching a fusion of the mirror-image regenerates. The muscle findings confirm the conception of the case as a triple, not a double, formation, for Bender himself found, besides the mirror-image muscle slips, yet another which ran toward the accessory leg and which he was unable to interpret (p. 125). The latter was supplied with nerves through splitting of the normal IX and X, while the VIII sent off no branch. The two other branches united in the accessory limb into one nervus ischiadicus, which runs medial and dorsal to the broadened Processus sup[erior] il[ei] and to the proximal femur end, to the hollow of the knee (Fig. 5), covered by the weak strands of the M[usculus] gracilis major and Sartorius. This nerve disposition too accords with a bilateral symmetry of a fused limb pair. According to the scheme the sequence of toes for the left body half would read: I, II, III, IV, V — V, IV = IV, V, and hence, of all legs, *l, r', l'; r.*

**No. 221.** *R. esculenta,* Duméril (1865, p. 313, No. VII, Fig. 1) further depicts a similar malformation in a pond frog, in which, however, the supernumerary double-leg arose at the posterior pelvic region, to the left of the median, but evidently in front of the articulation of the normal left hindleg. The double character of the foot is here clearer, because the two 3. toes are developed separately from one another, yet stand against each other without an intervening web. Since the accessory double-foot stands in front of the normal one, one must think of it as lifted outward and forward in order to obtain the correct arrangement of toes; it reads, if we begin with the numbering of the normal right hindleg of the frog lying on its back in Fig. 221, 5., 4., 3., 2., 1. — 1., 2., 3., 4., 5. — 5., 4., 3. — 3., 4., 5., and the scheme of the whole legs yields again: *r; l, r', l'.*

**No. 222.** *R. esculenta,* Lissitzky (1910, Tab. XXII and XXIII) and

**No. 222a.** *R. temporaria* (ibid., p. 596, Tab. XXII, Fig. 1) served, as tadpoles in which the hind limbs were at various, not fully developed stages, for experiments on the origin of multiple limbs. Although Della Valle (1913, p. 121, note 1) lists nine cases of Lissitzky's experimental frogs as typical triple-formations-at-a-break according to the scheme *r, a, n* (in which *r* represents a leg equisymmetrical with *n*, *a* an inversely symmetrical, *n* the normal leg), I have been unable to convince myself, with the original work in hand, that the symmetry in question really emerges from Lissitzky's text or figures. This author made (p. 592) "longitudinal sections parallel to the longitudinal axis of the body, whereby the deep section penetrating into the body cavity ran through the upper part of the acetabulum and the greater part of the pelvis, namely the whole pars ilei and a part of the pars ischio-pubis, remained connected with the upper end of the cut-off part. . . . For each extremity a separate section was necessary." According to all the foregoing expositions we ought now to expect three hind limbs on each side; but of these Lissitzky brings forward nothing, but describes in detail, and by means of drawings — partly obtained by plate reconstruction from histological sections — the basal parts of the accessory legs, whereby the symmetry or form of the feet is, however, not gone into. Nor can any picture be gained from the overall views, because the accessory limbs for the most part attained only the bud stage. In a single case, that of *R. temporaria* — Case I (Fig. 1) — it can be concluded with certainty from the figures that three hindlegs had developed on one side, and indeed on the right (Della Valle, however, cites it for the left side). Lissitzky gave for this, however, a quite different interpretation, namely that the innermost component corresponded to a detached piece of the opposite hindleg and had only secondarily fused with the others. Since not even in this case can the symmetry of the components be established with full reliability, this interpretation cannot be directly refuted, but in any case our interpretation as a triple-formation-at-a-break can be maintained equally well and has the advantage of fitting into the phenomena generally observed again and again. The plate model (Fig. 2) speaks decidedly in favor of our conception, since the middle component of the triple leg seems to have counter-symmetry to the innermost and the same symmetry as the opposite side, as emerges from the triangular form of the bone establishing itself between pelvis and lower legs.

**No. 223.** *R. esculenta,* Reichenow (1908, p. 680, Figs. 3, 4) gives, as an example of the deviation in the number of hind limbs, a water frog which possessed, articulated on the left, a complete accessory pelvis and leg pair, above and in front of the normal one. He writes thereto: "Against an originally mirror-image symmetry, however, speaks the behavior of the ossa ilium, of which each pelvis possesses quite normally two. Those of the posterior pelvis show the regular disposition and join on each side with the transverse processes of the sacral vertebra. The ossa ilium of the anterior pelvis, on the contrary, both rise upward on the left body side. Just below the vertebral column they fuse with one another and immediately thereafter also with the left ilium of the posterior pelvis. This fusion piece attaches to the sacral vertebra. There is accordingly only one sacral vertebra present." Despite this quite correct exposition, Reichenow then assumes as the cause a splitting of the body axis with subsequent double-formation. According to Tornier's *Pelobates* experiments, which show exactly the same pelvic conditions — which were then also imitated, though with little striking success, in the frog species drawn upon by Reichenow, *R. esculenta* of Lissitzky — there is no doubt that a break of the pelvis, without injury of the vertebral column lying in the body axis, formed the cause of the accessory leg pair. The scheme would have to read: *l', r', l; r,* corresponding to the position of the accessory pair in front of the normal left hindleg; with this the outline drawing (Fig. 3) and osteology (Fig. 4) agree.

**No. 224.** *R. (viridis =) esculenta,* Lunel (1867, p. 310, Figs. 7–10) likewise describes a water frog, onto whose left pelvic half there attaches forward a rudimentary pelvis with a fully developed leg pair, whereby express attention is drawn to the fact that the normal hindleg has been pushed somewhat backward and "the inner surface of the latter applies itself to the same surface of the supernumerary right leg"; we thus have the scheme: *l', r', l; r.*

**No. 225.** *R. esculenta,* *Van Deen (1838, Fig.), cited after Sutton (1889, Fig.) and Bender (1906, p. 221), since the rare original was not accessible to me, "described, with the aid of good figures, a *Rana esculenta* with two supernumerary hind extremities and a partial doubling of the pelvis (Ossa ilei). The mutually fused ilia of the monstrous extremity are attached to the ventral pelvic region of the frog, its anterior margin is connected by a band with the lower outer margin of the left M[usculus] rectus abdominis. Van Deen too was struck by the mirror-image position of the supernumerary parts relative to the normal ones; he says that natural and unnatural limbs relate to one another 'like two bodies which stand opposite one another with their foremost part, so that the right and left legs of both bodies always correspond.' On the left accessory leg three phalanges of the fourth and two of the middle toe were lacking; van Deen believes that these missing phalanges were probably present but had been torn off." According to the figure reproduced by Sutton, the accessory legs lie on the left in front of the normal one, and the scheme would read: *l', r', l; r.* If one thinks of the accessory ones placed opposite the normal ones, one obtains:

```
l', r',
 r; l,
```

as van Deen's joining-together with the forehalves — and indeed without alteration of the dorsoventrality — requires.

**No. 225a.** *R. esculenta,* Parona (1883, p. 259, No. 26, Figs. 5 & 7) described in detail, from the Anatomical Museum at Pavia, an edible frog similar to the preceding one, which bore, articulated on the left side beside the normal hindleg-thigh-head, a fused leg pair. Despite the long description and the figure, the symmetry cannot be gathered.

**No. 225b.** *R. (viridis =) esculenta,* *Van der Hoeven (1840) has, according to Parona (1882, p. 248, No. 5), described a green frog with two supernumerary legs, attached at the pubis. Original inaccessible to me; in Parona no data on symmetry.

**No. 225c.** *R. esculenta,* Balsamo-Crivelli (1865, No. II, without fig.) cites a frog, behind whose left hindleg a limb fused out of two legs is articulated. On the double-foot the outermost toes are shorter than the middle ones; I should therefore like to refer these to the 4., the others to the 5., while the inner 1.–3. are likely to have disappeared in fusion. We would then have the scheme *l, r', l'; r.*

**No. 226.** *R. esculenta,* Ercolani (1882, p. 822, Pl. IV, Fig. 10) preserved in the Museum at Bologna, as No. 878 (or No. 4116 of the catalogue of the Alessandrini Museum), a frog whose left hindleg bore a mightily reinforced thigh and two tibiae, of which again the weaker inner one bears two feet. According to the figure the middle component is right-symmetrical, so that the scheme *l, r', l'; r* should be applicable. Unfortunately neither a more exact description nor a figure of the bones is given; judging by all the preceding cases, it is likely to be a matter of fusion of all three components of a triple-formation-at-a-break, whose break-vertex must have lain near the femur head at the pelvis, perhaps had affected the head itself.

**No. 226a.** *R. esculenta,* Ercolani (1882, p. 812, Pl. IV, Fig. 11) gives, further, without closer description, the figure of his frog preserved under No. 2643 of the same museum, whose left hindleg was supposed to be double and indeed provided with ten toes. In the figure only nine toes are visible, and since the extremity is depicted skinned, the 10th would have to appear clearly in the skeleton if it had been present. But another reason too exists for doubting the correctness of the "doubling" of the foot. The left foot lying toward the outside possesses the five toes in normal development, so that only the longest toe IV has one phalanx more than the III. and V. attaching to it; the four-toed foot lying toward the median, however, has two equally long toes and — what is more important — both are equipped with the phalanx number of toe IV, flanked by two corresponding to a III. or V. The sequence of toes is, taking into account the characteristic rotation of the normal planta, which turns the first finger inward (cf. fig.), I, II, III, IV, V — V, IV, IV, V, and thus corresponds to a triple-formation-at-a-break of the scheme *l, r', l'* (the normal right leg is lacking from the preparation). The muscles seem, according to the figure, to run abnormally up to the pelvis, so that we should probably assume a fusion of three components far upward. Were this not the case, it would be the only case in which a tripling distal to the femoral articulation at the pelvis would be known in batrachians.

**No. 227.** *R. clamata,* Duméril (1863, p. 315, Figs. 3, 3a) obtained from North America a frog which bore "a second pair of abdominal legs not . . . at the posterior pelvic region, but at its anterior surface." According to the figures it is a matter of a pair articulated to the left of the median, lying in front of the normal one, whose posterior leg is a right, whose anterior one is a left. The scheme would accordingly have to read: *l', r', l; r.*

## Regenerative powers of the Anuran legs.

In contrast to the tailed Amphibia, the tailless ones are no longer able, after metamorphosis, to replace lost limbs or members of them.

We must therefore set the generation time of the triple-formations-at-a-break back so far into the development of the affected batrachian until we strike upon regeneration-capable stages.

This stage is governed by the general developmental level of the batrachian groups, in that the most primitive ones still supplement removed extremities until shortly before the transformation (*Alytes*), the next-higher ones still supplement well-developed ones with an acute knee-angle (*Bombinator*, *Pelobates*), all others replace legs only when these have been removed while still at the stage with an obtuse knee-angle (*Bufo*, *Rana*, *Hyla*; lit. in Exp. Zool., 2nd Reg., 1909, p. 164).

Since our most frequent batrachians belong to the latter group, we have to set the time of origin of almost all known natural finds back to those stages which extend up to the formation of the knee. Lissitzky's experiments have in fact shown that on such early stages triple-formations can arise in *Rana* (and this also agrees with the results obtained by Tornier on *Pelobates*).

But if such early stages of leg formation, at which the extremity rudiment appears either merely as a roundish blastema (as in Tornier) or has a tiny leg-bud protruding from it, are concerned, it is very improbable that an injury would bring merely a distal leg part to a break; rather, a narrow member would either be torn off altogether or the more proximal pelvic rudiment torn along with it as well. This now explains to us the otherwise striking phenomenon that all triple-formations-at-a-break on frog legs can be traced back to a break of a pelvic part, while certainly interpretable triplings that would affect merely distal members have not come our way. In a still more pronounced degree this holds for the frog arms, which are only released from their breast-pouch after the formation of the legs, and therefore can hardly be broken correspondingly at a regeneration-capable age.

Interesting experiments by Braus (1905, 1906, 1908) on the fore limbs of the fire-bellied toad, *Bombinator*, prove at once the regeneration capacity of these on stages that must be artificially released, and the capacity to supplement the distal mirror-image-wise not merely distally, but also proximally.

**No. 228.** *Bombinator igneus,* Braus (1905, p. 434, Fig. 1; p. 453, Fig. 11; p. 461, Fig. 14; p. 462, Fig. 15 [our figure]; 1906, p. 528, Tab. VI, Figs. 8, 9): "In Fig. 11 there lies, dorsal to the normal hind extremity, the implanted fore one, which, through a rotation of the bud during the operation, has developed in such a way that the dorsal side is turned toward the belly side of the main animal, the ventral side toward its back side." (1906, p. 461): "It frequently happens that the implanted limb does not remain the only supernumerary extremity, but that, through doubling, yet a second supernumerary extremity arises. If the transplanted limb was a fore one, then the one arising through doubling is also such a one, and indeed it always behaves like the mirror image to the implanted one. To a right implanted extremity, therefore, a left one joins itself; to a left one, a right one. The appearance of the doubling is independent of the place onto which inoculation was made. It occurs in transplantation onto the head just as in such onto the trunk. Indeed, the formation of these is so frequent that in seven identically treated compositions reared up to metamorphosis according to the type of Fig. 1, in all four pieces showed a doubling of the implanted limb, and only three remained permanently free of such." "The external development is illustrated by Figs. 11, 14, and 15 in the most important stages." The at first considerable difference in the development of the original and the accessory limb of the double-extremity recedes entirely later on. (p. 464): "But, as against that common ground-plan, something is . . . extraordinarily striking: the supplementary member possesses, in none of the cases obtained, a nervous system."

Braus (p. 464) "was able to follow the development of the skeleton in the accessory extremities and to establish that it proceeds, as in normal extremities, in a proximo-distal direction and brings forth the typical elements of this system. The shoulder girdle is therein in manifold connection with that of the transplanted limb. The coracoids and procoracoids of both extremities fill out the most prominent part of the cone (as was also established in the Tornier case, see above); the scapulae project into the main animal. The musculature behaves in the accessory members, in its development, quite as in the implanted extremities; here, therefore, is a sure proof of the potency of the muscle tissue to lay itself down and develop without nerve influence. The same is the case with the vascular system."

If we imagine the foreleg primordium taken by Braus as not being completely removed from its original location, we would obtain exactly the symmetry relations of break-triple-formation: namely, the proximally remaining, that is distally directed, break-surface would regenerate a limb that would be innervated by the normal nerve; furthermore the distal part of the excised primordium would produce the same, and, inverse to this, from its proximally facing wound-surface a mirror-image one — which in my view lacks the nerve precisely because in vertebrates nerves are as a rule capable of regenerating at all only peripherally, but not centripetally (as I have already once mentioned, in Grosser and Przibram, 1906, 33). In this way the presence of nerves in regenerative double-formations is explained without strain — a point to which Braus (1905, p. 463) calls attention and which he interprets as a consequence of the split parts remaining in their original connection with the central nervous system, whereas in cases of total detachment and foreign-directed transplantation no nervous tissue is allotted to the accessory limb (p. 465). I therefore regard not the location, but the direction of growth, as decisive for whether or not nerves develop. The latter occurs in cases of inversion, as in Braus' fire-bellied toads [Unken]; the former in splittings along the longitudinal axis, where no inversion of polarity takes place.

When Braus further (p. 463) says, "that in numerous cases in which, unfortunately, a more exact investigation was not undertaken or not published (as, for example, also in the important cases of Tornier), one may nevertheless with probability infer, from the good developmental condition of the doubled parts in the adult animal, a preceding function and hence also the presence of a nervous system," then this is contradicted by his own nerve-free accessory limbs which persist even after metamorphosis. Compare also the much weaker innervation of the middle inverse component in the break-triple-formation of a spiny-lobster antenna (above, No. 13).

No. 228a. *Bufo lentiginosus*, Harrison (1907, p. 265, Fig. 6) obtained just such mirror-image hind legs from right hind-leg primordia which had been taken from "de-nerved" embryos and transplanted in front of the normal right hind extremity of another toad.

No. 228b. *Rana sylvatica*, Harrison (1907, p. 255, Fig. 1) further operated on wood-frog tadpoles in the same manner and with the same success.

No. 228c. *R. esculenta*, Lissitzky (1910, Case XI, Table XXIII, Fig. 15) figures, as one result of his above-mentioned experiments (cf. No. 222), a double-formation on a right hind leg, in which, according to the reconstruction of the skeleton (Fig. 16), the one component has arisen from a proximally directed wound-surface. This case confirms the possibility of distal regenerates at proximally directed sites, as in the preceding double-formations that arose after complete section, and at the same time confirms the correctness of the interpretation given for Lissitzky's Case I (No. 222a). On the numerous individual supernumerary legs of *Rana temporaria* and *esculenta* additionally obtained experimentally by the same investigator I need not go into further, since, as mentioned, the symmetry relations were not clarified.

In the literature the following further cases are found of natural findings of single accessory limbs in anurans, of which two, marked with an asterisk, I was unfortunately unable to consult in the original, while the data on the others are too scanty to exclude doubt about the simplicity of the addition:

No. 228d. *R. (esculenta?)*, *De Supperville (1744) described, according to Taruffi (1880, p. 114), a frog which bore on the right side on its back a supernumerary limb (cf. herewith the cases Nos. 218–221).

No. 228e. *R. (esculenta?)*, *Bergendal (1889) found, according to Pol (1913, p. 84), in a frog beneath the skin of the floor of the mouth an accessory anterior extremity (cf. herewith No. 208b).

No. 228f. *R. temporaria*, Giebel (1867) demonstrated a grass frog with a simple four-toed foreleg in front of the normal left one; symmetry not mentioned, so it might therefore also have been a fused pair.

No. 228g. *R. palmipes*, Johnson (1901, No. 1, Fig. 1, 5, 6, 7) claims to have found, on a still-tailed little frog, forelegs sitting on both sides on the shoulder-blade (parallel-standing, judging by the figures, to the normal ones). Whether it was not rather a matter of an additional pair meeting lower down, springing from a shoulder-blade or from a vertebra? Neither text nor figures are clear enough for a decision.

Break-triple-formation of reptiles I have not found mentioned — with the exception of the (heads and) tails treated further above — nor have I myself set eyes on it. Let it be recalled that this vertebrate class postembryonically no longer regenerates legs, but does regenerate the tail (cf. Prz., Exp. Zool., 2nd ed., 1909, p. 160). The formation designated in various handbooks as a postembryonic regenerate of a hind leg in *Lacerta vivipara* (after Egger, 1888) was, according to the author's own statements, noticed on a young lizard that had already been brought in in a mutilated state, and is therefore not established as such. Direct experiments on regeneration of toes turned out negative (Werber, 1906).

## F. On the paired appendages of vertebrates which no longer regenerate these after birth.

### a) Birds, Aves.

#### I. Anterior extremity (wing).

No. 229a. *Columba livia*, Vrolik (1840, also d'Alton, 1853) described, without illustration (the statement of Ercolani, 1882, p. 809 about an illustration being erroneous), from the present-day Veterinary College at Utrecht a pigeon, "in which a double anterior extremity is attached to the right wing. Behind the ordinary wing there is, namely, a second one, which consists of a collar-bone without a shoulder-blade and of an upper-arm bone that widens downward and connects with a double forearm. Each of these arms consists merely of an ulna without a radius and unites with a weakly developed forehand. Since now the flight-feathers attach to the forearms, it is thereby brought about that on the right side there are really three wings. These hang together in part, in view of the union of the supernumerary forearms with the forearm of the first wing. The double-formation takes its origin at the breastbone, on which a comb-shaped outgrowth is attached sideways, which extends forward as far as the great breast-comb [keel], and which implants itself by a separate piece into the upper-arm bone. Each wing has its own tendons, blood-vessels and nerves, which are all present with the exception of the extensors of the three, which belong only to the foremost wing. In consideration of the development of the wings I should like to decide thus, that the pigeon was indeed able to raise itself into the air, but, on account of the preponderance of the right-sided motive power, was not able to maintain itself therein. Apparently the opposite direction of the supernumerary wings must also have contributed to this, one of them, namely, being directed forward, the other backward."

For lack of an illustration, no full-certainty biotechnical analysis of this case can be given, which unfortunately also applies to the other wing-multiplications of birds.

Yet the essential conditions for tracing the abnormal pigeon-wing back to a break-triple-formation are given: the double nature of the additional structure, the opposite direction of its two components, and the suppression of the corresponding radii all speak for the applicability of our scheme.

The non-development of accessory radii is of particular importance because, with reference to the anterior position of the normal right wing, in a break of the anterior extremity the more caudal ulna, given mirror-image formation of the middle component, ought again to border on an ulna, which is now the case. The significance of this arrangement is confirmed by the following case, in which the matter concerns an analogous anomaly, but with **posterior** position of the normal wing.

No. 229b. *C. livia*, Otto (1841, p. 275) described, as No. CCCCLXXII, a pigeon which bore two wings on the right: "superior duos radios continet." The clavicle was connected on the outer side of the normal one with the sternum. The scapula and furcula of the supernumerary structure were not connected with the corresponding normal pieces. Apart from the radii mentioned, all parts of the additional structure are said to have been single; thus a single ulna is probably also to have been present between the two radii.

In any case there attached, to the cephalad-lying radius of the normal posterior wing, again a radius, which formed the hind margin of the additional structure, and at the front margin of the latter there stood, just as at the front margin of a normal wing, again a radius. We should thus have here likewise a reversal of the middle component.

If it is confirmed that, contrary to the absence of accessory radii in the previous case, here a single accessory ulna — probably fused from two — was present, then this would be of interest with regard to the phenomenon appearing later in the mammals, namely the mole [Maulwurf], that the front margin of the extremities is indeed still able to reproduce the hind margin, but the hind margin is no longer able to reproduce the front margin with its characteristic bones (cf. below).

No. 229c. *Anser cinereus*, goose, and

No. 229d. *Anas boschas*, duck, each with four wings, are mentioned by Ercolani (1882, p. 809) from Haller and d'Alton, but without sufficient description; illustrations are lacking.

No. 229e. *A. boschas*, Büchner cites (1909, p. 21) the following story: "a case communicated to me by Mr. building-contractor K. in Westphalia, of a duck purchased by the same in the autumn of 1873, whose right wing-bone had been broken and had healed crookedly again, and which in the spring of 1874 hatched eight young, of which two gradually got feathered outgrowths on the right wing and two on both wings, which stood off at an angle of 45° and formed, as it were, a second pair of wings. The animals thus doubly winged in this manner present a singular, not unhandsome appearance; the abnormal wings are 4–5 inches long and stand upright above the lower normal ones, immovably and for themselves, and are also set with flight-feathers."

On first reading, this case gives the impression of an inheritance of a wing-break, which now in the descendants leads to multiple-formation. Since, however, the break itself was not observed, but merely inferred from the crooked healing, it is probably more likely that in the angular bending of the affected wing the first sign of a hyperphalangy made itself noticeable, which then came to development more distinctly in the descendants. In this it is admittedly to be assumed that the hyperdactylies — as is set forth in connection with their discussion in the case of the fowls (cf. further below) — arise from a tendency to breaks of the embryonic primordium, so that in the old duck there may in fact have been present a scarring of an old break without hyperdactyly. That in the inheritance the matter does not concern a transmission of the local break is evident already from the wing-additions making themselves noticeable in some of the young also on the left side.

An anatomical investigation of these very remarkable structures appears, moreover, not to have been undertaken, at least none such is mentioned in Büchner.

It cannot therefore be excluded with full certainty whether the additional wings, apparently each belonging to one wing, did not rather belong, as a mirror-image symmetrical additional pair, merely to the wing of one side, and their position was feigned by the different direction of their components.

No. 229f. *Gallus domesticus*, Gurlt (1877, p. 51) cites, without closer description, a little chick with seven extremities, of which four were wings.

#### II. Posterior extremity.

No. 229g. *Anas boschas*, Tornier (1901, p. 500, Fig. 4a, b, d) received from Prof. Braun a duck for investigation, which (on the left) — beside the strongly (rightward) bent-off sacrum — had inserted two supernumerary, mutually fusing pelvic halves into the normal (left) pelvic half in such a way that the medially lying half gave origin to a second left-, and the half lying between this and the normal left pelvis to a right-symmetrical leg. The scheme would thus be: l, r', l'; r.

Tornier gives schematic illustrations for the course of the displacement of the original pelvis (l) and the growing-forth of the regenerates from the wound-surfaces, of which he takes four into consideration, in that he regards the wound-margins growing forth on both sides of the great pelvic foramen as separate regenerates. Since, however — as Tornier himself also does — we must refer the bursting-apart to a very early stage of the pelvic primordium, there is no ground to assume the presence of the pelvic foramen at the time of the regenerate-primordium, and it suffices to consider the two wound-margins arising through the penetration of a single rent. Through this simplification we arrive at the applicability of the ordinary rules for break-triple-formation. Worth mentioning is the presence of a forking of the intestine, leading to two anuses, and of several caeca. These soft parts are thus either co-affected by the injury or have directed themselves after the multiplied skeletal parts.

No. 229h. *A. boschas*, Tornier (1901, p. 500, Fig. 4a, b, c) further received from Prof. Schiemenz another duck, which showed an analogous pelvic bursting, but of lesser extent. Here the regeneration process succeeded in producing only one supernumerary articular socket [acetabulum] with limb; Tornier thinks that the one of the laid-down accessory pelvic halves remained behind in its growth and could therefore produce no limb; I rather believe that the limb coming forth, according to the illustration, in the middle of the regeneration zone is a fusion product of two accessory ones; unfortunately Tornier gives neither the symmetry nor any other form of the supernumerary leg, so that I was able to ascertain nothing certain. The soft parts were doubled in a similar manner as in the previous case. (On further differences of interpretation cf. further below, No. 236.)

No. 229i. *Anser domesticus*, *Alessandrini (1854, p. 533, No. 1129 Museum [Bologna]) described — as I take from Parona (1883, No. 11) — a goose which bore a supernumerary, fused leg-pair fastened by a ligament to the coccyx; symmetry not given. It must of course remain questionable whether we here have to do with a break-triple-formation similar to some of the cases described by Tornier in garlic-toads [Knoblauchkröten] (cf. above No. 216a and following), or with a parasitic remnant of a second animal.

No. 230. *Fulica atra*, Otto (1841, p. 262, No. CCCCXXXIII, Plate XXVIII, Fig. 1) points to the malformation — rare in wild birds — of a coot, which bears by means of a ligament at its hind end an accessory leg-pair. Two anuses were present, of which the left was much smaller. The symmetry of the additional leg-pair can be described — just as in the case of the frog of van Deens (cf. No. 225) — thus, that the natural and the unnatural limbs relate to one another "like two bodies which stand opposite one another with their foremost part, so that the right and left leg of both bodies always correspond." Since the smaller left anus probably arose secondarily, the case can be interpreted as a break-triple-formation at the left rearmost pelvic wall, and schematically designated as l, r', l'; r. However, the alternative cited in the previous case cannot be excluded here either.

No. 231a and b. *Gallus domesticus*, *Alessandrini (1854, p. 528) cited, according to Parona (1883, No. 31, without ill.), as Numbers 2325 and 2326 of the Bologna Museum, two fowls from which, with merely ligamentous connection, a supernumerary leg-pair each hung down from the tail ("cocygopygie").

No. 231c. *G. domesticus*, Parona (1883, p. 277, No. 36, Fig. 14a) adds just such a case from the Museum of Pavia, Cat.-No. 6.

No. 232. *G. domesticus*, Braune (1862, p. 130, Plate XVIIIA and B) figures the skeleton of a cock, in which at the hind end of the body there is a pair of legs hanging down "directed opposite to the normal ones." The illustrations show the tail-vertebrae bent rightward and, at the right pubic bone on the inner side, a pelvis-like addition with hind-limbs articulated symmetrically left and right on it. The scheme for the components is, taking into account the statement made by Braune and the illustration: l, r', l'; r.

No. 232a–u. Braune further makes (p. 132) the statement that in the Meckel collection at Halle there were 21 bird-tripodies, of which only one doubtful case shows a simple leg-addition, all the others showing distinctly double additions. Unfortunately nothing further is stated about the symmetries; also there are probably among them cases which concern double-formations of the posterior body end. A critical working-through of this collection would surely be rewarding.

No. 233. *G. domesticus* (= *Phasianus gallus*), Ercolani (1882, p. 809, Plate III, Fig. 6) figures, as No. 2595 of the Bologna Museum, a little chick which bore on the right three pelvic halves lying one behind the other with legs of almost equal strength. The symmetry seems, according to the illustration, to be l, r, l', r'.

No. 234. *G. domesticus*, Parona (1883, p. 285, No. 41, Fig. 18a) describes and figures a fowl whose right hind-limb exhibits a typical break-triple-formation. The tail-vertebrae are bent off to the left, and between them and the right-sided ischium with the normal right hind leg there is intercalated a pelvis composed of a right and a left ischium, which bears small but thoroughly free legs, distinctly developed according to their symmetry. The scheme has to read: l; r', l', r.

No. 234a. *G. domesticus*, *Duplay (1865, ill.) is said, according to Parona (1883, p. 236 and 273, No. 32), to have figured a similar case, but Parona mentions just as little — as with the ones figured and described by himself — anything of the symmetry in the case not accessible to me in the original work.

No. 235. *G. domesticus*, Tornier (1902, p. 18, Fig. 18; 1906, p. 266, Fig. 1 and 3) analyzed, similarly to the four-legged ducks cited above, in analogous fowls the processes of origin by means of his biontotechnical method: "On the left pelvic half, which was longitudinally torn open from behind, through the great pelvic foramen (m), four wound-surfaces (r₁–r₄) had arisen, of which the two inner ones (r₁ and r₂) lay close against one another, while the two posterior ones (r₃ and r₄) stood very far apart from one another. Each of these wound-surfaces now first behaved entirely independently super-regenerally, and so out of each of them there arose an independent regenerate-cone (the one kept dark in the illustration). Of these, those of the wound-surface r₁ and r₂ soon abutted against one another and formed by fusion a supernumerary part of the injured pelvic half, whose inner rent-site was thereby simultaneously closed. The two regenerate-cones arising from the wound-surfaces r₃ and r₄, on the other hand, grew out quite independently of one another, and each became for itself the posterior section of a pelvic half with the corresponding hind-limb… In this the two supernumerary pelvic halves turn their pubis toward one another, i.e. the pelvic half arising from the wound r₄ is equal to a normal right-sided one, and the one arising from the wound r₃ equal to a normal left-sided one; they have therefore in their development striven for no symmetry-relation to one another, but have entered into symmetry to the body-part out of which they arose, and the formula for the hind-limbs of this animal is therefore: l; r', l'; r (which is also what the scheme in Fig. 3 yields, where the four limbs are so arranged that, if Fig. 3 is transferred onto tracing paper and then set in the correct way upon Fig. 1, the various limbs fit into the corresponding articular sockets)."

With regard to the manner of the wounding I agree with Tornier, but with respect to the symmetry I must remark that both supernumerary legs arose from the left normal pelvic half, and hence, since one of them is likewise left-symmetrical, both cannot have entered into symmetry to the body-part out of which they arose. Moreover the scheme has been reshaped, by the insertion of the first semicolon, into what Tornier needs; without interpretation it reads: l, r', l'; r, and corresponds therefore completely to our general regularity for the arrangement and symmetry of the components of a break-triple-formation. On further differences between my conception and his I shall enter only at the next case (No. 236).

No. 236. *G. domesticus*, Tornier (1906, p. 267, Fig. 2 and 4) now sets in contrast to the previous one another fowl, whose discovery he says he had predicted in the year 1901 on the basis of his regenerate-symmetry law, namely that in this one the matter concerns the development of a symmetry-relation between two regenerate-cones which exactly correspond to those which in the pelvis just discussed arose from the wound r₃ and r₄. For in this fowl, namely, there was split off from the just-laid-down left pelvic half, by a bending force, the posterior outer corner, and indeed in such a way that here too four wound-surfaces (r₁–r₄) arose, of which r₃ and r₄ have nearly the same position as the like-named ones of the previous pelvis, but are substantially shorter and lie substantially nearer to one another. The two wound-surfaces r₁ and r₂, which lie quite close against one another, then very soon fuse regenerally with one another; the wound-surfaces r₃ and r₄, on the other hand, which had a greater distance from one another, each sent out a regenerate-cone, to which fell the task of growing out into the hind end of a pelvic half with socket (pf'), great pelvic foramen (m') and supernumerary limb. These two regenerate-cones, however, then likewise soon abutted against one another and thereupon developed in symmetry to one another in such a way that they took their line of contact (x–x) for a kind of imaginary sacrum, onto which they attach themselves and to which they had to remodel themselves as the corresponding right and left pelvic half with the corresponding limbs. There thus became their sections, with which they abutted against one another, their inner side, and their limbs became two mirror-image-equal ones, namely to the right and left of the tiny little pelvis, into which the two regenerates besides also began to develop themselves. Thus in this animal there is built into the now fully developed germ-true pelvic half a small supernumerary pelvis, which has almost parallel position with the pelvis to which it belongs, and which — so far as it is present (it lacks the outer-margin parts of a germ-true pelvis and the pubica) — is equal down to the finest small details to the stock-pelvis. The hind-limb formula of the animal thus malformed, however, is (as Fig. 4 schematically shows): l; l', r'; r."

The development of a secondary "contact symmetry" would stand in contradiction to all the evidence hitherto brought forward for the symmetry relations in break-triple-formations. If, however, we consider Tornier's schematic illustrations, in particular Figs. 3 and 4, which are intended to represent the two different kinds of symmetry, "free symmetry" and "contact symmetry," then it appears that in both illustrations the upper-thigh articular socket of the normal left leg lies otherwise [the German is here defective: "liegt als," evidently for "anders liegt als" — lies differently than] than that of the left accessory one.

This case too thus corresponds completely to the symmetry law for break-triple-formations, that the accessory component nearer to the normal one — or, in other words, the middle of the three components — exhibits inverse symmetry. The scheme for the left side of our fowl would therefore better read l, r', l'; for both sides therefore l, r', l'; r.

Now Tornier admittedly arrives, consistently, at his formula l, l', r'; r — I again leave out the first semicolon as unmotivated —, when he considers in turn, from left to right, the projecting legs themselves. We can, however, very simply bring before our minds, by means of the scheme, the correctness of our symmetry formula, if we The whole difference between the two cases lies merely in the fact that in No. 235 the left accessory member, but in our No. 236 the right accessory member, lies nearer to the median plane. This in turn is explained by the different nature of the injury, in that here the posterior outer angle of the pelvis, but there the posterior inner angle, had been ruptured. If we take to hand the schematic figure given by Tornier himself (1901, p. 501, Fig. 4*f*) for the penetration of the injuring force in No. 236, we find, by means of an arrow, a fissure-line marked rising obliquely from the posterior corner of the pelvic rudiment toward the median plane. From its upper wound-margin, which faces distally, there will, in my opinion, develop a left, normally symmetrical extremity; whereas the ruptured-off inner-margin tip — still connected only by a slight bridge to the proximal pelvic rudiment — will indeed likewise continue its segmentation toward a left extremity, but at the proximal-facing, originally lower margin of the wound will regenerate all distal parts inversely, that is, will likewise regenerate a right hind limb.

According to the same rules the regeneration in No. 237 will proceed, except that here it must have been a matter of the penetration of a force from the inner posterior pelvic angle toward the outer side, ascending forward, so that the outer angle is ruptured off. Here the distally-turned upper wound-surface produces a left extremity, and likewise the ruptured-off outer tip, according to its original determination, produces such a one [a left extremity], but at the same time, at its proximal-facing wound-surface, again the inverse right component. With this analysis, carried out completely in the manner hitherto followed by me, it can also be explained why in the latter case no pudica are reproduced, which happens in the former case. The ruptured-off outer angle, namely, is not to be thought of as completely severed, but is still connected, by means of the portion that later becomes the pudendum, with the rest of the pelvis. Since the pudendum has remained uninjured and only distal parts are reproduced, it does not come to a new formation. With penetration of a force from the outer angle, on the other hand, the pudendum itself is cut through and is now tripled in the same manner as all the other parts lying no further proximally.

The essential difference between Tornier's and my derivation consists in this, that he regards the normal component as the original one and views the accessory ones as regenerates from the wound-margins, whereas I regard the normal one as the distally regenerated proximal part of the pelvic rudiment, the middle component as that which regenerates the distal part inversely toward the proximal, and the second accessory one as the originally distal pelvic part which redevelops distally, either growing further or perhaps re-forming after the tearing-off of a small tip-piece.

**No. 237.** *G. d.* (= *Phasianus gallus*), Ercolani (1882, p. 808, Pl. III, Fig. 7) treats as No. 905 of the Bologna Museum a small hen whose right pelvic half, fused into an inextricable mass, sends out three equally strong legs. Of these, the middle one is, according to the figure, unquestionably inverse; unfortunately the feet are not drawn, so that the position of the toes, and hence the character of the side, cannot be recognized from them as well. Yet the oppositely-directed bending of the knee is clearly visible.

**No. 238.** *G. domesticus*, Parona (1883, p. 280, No. 37, Fig. 15a) reports a hen, No. 7 of the Museum at Pavia, whose left ischium was tripled and which, between the tail-tip pushed down to the right and the normal left leg, bore two supernumerary legs fused with one another along the thighs. The symmetry is, according to the figure, the one typical for break-triple-formation: *l, r′, l′; r*. (Note that the femur of the normal left leg is concealed in the figure by the pelvis, and that the parallel-fused bones are the femora of the accessory pair.)

**No. 238a.** *G. domesticus*, Parona (1883, p. 282, No. 38, Fig. 16a) also adduces a second, quite similar hen; but about the symmetry I could make out nothing. There were three caeca and two ani present.

**No. 238b.** *G. domesticus*, Tornier (1901, p. 500, no fig.) mentions a similar case from the Leipzig Zoological Institute, in which the regenerated pelvic halves and the lower halves of the thighs were inseparably fused with one another. The hen fell under the scheme of our No. 235.

**No. 239.** *G. domesticus* (= *Ph. g.*), Ercolani (1882, p. 806, Pl. III, Fig. 5) shows as No. 958 of the Bologna Museum a small hen, in front of and above whose right hind leg, hard at the normal femoral joint, a supernumerary pair of legs, fused as far as the heels, rises up. The feet, diverging in mirror-image at the heels, have only two toes each, standing opposite one another. The foot nearest to the normal right hind leg is also a mirror-image to this one, as is evident from the oppositely-directed heel-bend and toe-position; it is therefore a matter of a typical break-triple-formation with fusion of supernumerary femora and tibiae.

**No. 240.** *G. domesticus*, Parona (1883, p. 287, No. 42, Figs. 19a and 20f) found in the Museum at Pavia also a hen whose left tripled ischium gave origin, on the inner side from the normal one, to a supernumerary leg fused into a pair as far as the toe-roots. The symmetry, which cannot be derived with full certainty from the figure, seems to have been like that of the previous ones; in any case the toes of the accessory legs were arranged turned away from one another in mirror-image. In several further cases mentioned by Parona the symmetry cannot even be guessed.

**No. 241.** *G. domesticus* (Pl. XXI, Figs. XXIV–XXVI), Cat.-No. 24 of the developmental-mechanics Museum of the Biological Experimental Institute in Vienna. A small hen bears, on the right side of the strongly leftward-displaced tail, a pair of supernumerary hind legs fused as far as the toes. Palpation, dissection, and radiography showed that it is a matter of a multiple-formation arising opposite the normal right femoral head. Whether still further pelvic parts are affected was not investigated, but it is probable, since two ani were visible. (I have also seen still other hens and ducks in which typical break-triple-formations appeared to be present, but since I have no exact records at my disposal, I will not dwell further on them.) The position of the toes and the scalation of the shanks leave no doubt that the accessory leg lying nearest to the right normal leg is a left one, the more distant one a right one, and that hence a typical break-triple-formation is present.

**No. 242.** *Passer* (*italicus*), Parona (1883, p. 268, No. 17, Fig. 13a) describes and figures a sparrow from Pavia which bears, between the left ilium and ischium, a supernumerary extremity that, being provided with five toes, is in any case to be regarded as fused from two. The symmetry is not quite clearly to be made out, but probably comes down to the scheme *l, r′, l′; r*. Certainly incorrect — be it noted in passing — is Parona's interpretation of the supernumerary bone adjoining the normal coxa as a tibia, and of the disintegration of the tarsus crosswise into two bones. To these errors he was misled by the more slender form of the femur and by the symmetry-relations of the additional structures, which remained foreign to him — e.g., the abnormal bend of the knee.

**No. 242a.** *Passer* (= *Fringilla*) *domesticus*, Ercolani (1883, p. 806, No. 932, no fig.) describes a sparrow with two legs expressly described as right legs, which stood one behind the other. A renewed investigation with regard to a possible double rudiment of one of these legs would be desirable.

**No. 242b.** *Fringilla cisalpina*, *Canestrini (1870) described, according to Parona (1883, pp. 227 and 267, No. 16), a finch on whose hind end two accessory limbs hung without bony connection; symmetry not given; questionable whether a parasitic double-formation.

[**No. 242c.** *Carduelis* (*elegans*), Aldrovandi (1642, p. 169, fig.) figured a goldfinch with two legs attached to the sides of the one hind leg; symmetry not recognizable; the figure, like all such old figures, is no longer usable.]

## Regenerative potencies of the bird extremities and polydactyly.

By the experimental route, no regeneration of any other kind than the simple physiological replacement of the claw-tips and feather-vanes has hitherto been observed in bird extremities, which is connected, on the one hand, with the hopelessness of such experiments on the animals already hatched, and, on the other, with the difficulty of the operation in the egg. Yet egg-operations are certainly promising, as the results on lens- and tail-regeneration obtained by this route (cf. Lit. in Prz., Exp. Zool., 2. Reg., 1909, p. 166) have proved. Without doubt we must trace back the regenerative multiple-formations to injuries of early embryonic stages of the birds, for we know that in those once hatched from the egg no re-production of legs lost through accidents takes place. But I can draw yet one further probability-inference to the effect that the time of origin of the break-triple-formation on bird legs is to be assigned to the stage of the pelvic rudiment before the growing-out of the leg. For there are found, namely, in the entire literature reviewed by me, no cases of break-triple-formations which in hen extremities had not already drawn parts of the pelvis into sympathetic involvement. I was further unable to find any tripling stated to proceed from a part of the femur or of the knee or of the tibia; the few cases of the anterior extremity, too, refer to the whole wing including parts of the shoulder-girdle. Presumably the regenerative capacity of the extremities is already extinguished after the first formation of the cartilage centres, hence very soon after the sprouting-out of the extremity from the pelvic rudiment, and with this the production of triple-formations by the regenerative route from the femur or more distal parts becomes impossible.

Seemingly, the occurrence of supernumerary toes in certain breeds of hens, as well as now and then in other birds (Lit. in Bateson, 1894, p. 390), formed an exception to this, if one wished to trace this polydactyly back to regenerative processes. But the careful investigations carried out, at Braus' (1908) instigation, on the Houdan hen by Marie Kaufmann-Wolf (1909, with 3 plates and 42 text-figures) have shown that the supernumerary toes are not laid down with separated cartilage-nuclei, but owe their origin to a continuous proliferation of the cartilage-centres forming for the 1st toe, and hence probably could no longer be formed after complete differentiation of the cartilage-nuclei. Of great interest for our conception of organic symmetry is the demonstration — already initiated by Bateson, further developed by the authors just cited — that the accessories adjoining the 1st toe strive to repeat the toe-row-sequence in mirror-image, as can mostly be established with certainty from their position and the number of their phalanges. For, namely, beside the 1st toe with its 2 phalanges there mostly stands an accessory toe (*a*), which turns its claw away from it and possesses 3 phalanges, which number also belongs to the normal 2nd [II] toe; scheme: II — I — II — III — IV. If 2 accessory toes have appeared, then the one nearest to the normal 1st [I] toe (*a*) reveals itself as a 2-jointed I, mostly partially fused with it, and the more distant one as a 3-jointed II; we then have the scheme II — I = I — II — III — IV. But the higher [more distal] accessory toe can also, in other cases, be partially fused with the second accessory one (*a′*) and, like it, be 3-jointed, which schematically would yield II = II — I — II — III — IV. Whereas the first two cases each possess one mirror-plane — and indeed the first running through I, the second running between I and I — the third case has two mirror-planes, the one running through I, the second, however, between the two accessory toes II and II, for these have their claw-tips turned toward one another. Here, then, the inversion-process seems to have repeated itself once more; these are the rarest cases.

As regards the cause of origin of the toe-doubling or -multiplication, Kaufmann-Wolf could not observe the penetration of amnion-threads, and also, in a case of a narrow amnion, could establish no supernumerariness. That it is, however, a matter of rupturings-off within the region of the 1st toe, the hallux, I believe I may infer from the fact, repeatedly observed by the same author (p. 247, Figs. 13–15), that the metatarsi of the normal and accessory toes, quite against the otherwise prevailing rule of their parallel attachment to the "shank-bone" [Laufknochen] of the hens, push themselves crosswise over the latter. The metatarsus-rudiment of the hallux has evidently, even before its chondrification, lost its connection with the "shank-bone" and gained free surfaces for the exercise of regeneration. In this connection it is to be assumed that at the time of injury the hallux-rudiment was not yet so laid against that of the next toe that it had lost the potency for its formation, for otherwise the regenerations could have produced only two-phalanged toes. We must conceive the regeneration-processes as being like those in longitudinal-splitting, but not — as in break-triple-formation — as a proximally growing inversion; for the latter could likewise yield only the same quality again, and is, moreover, also for that reason improbable, because in the crosswise position of the hallux-metatarsi an unjointed proximal growing-out of them occurs.

The heritability of hyperdactyly in many hen breeds, Dorking, Houdan, etc., does not speak against the origin of the supernumerary toes by the regenerative route after injury, since the disposition to such injuries, not the supernumerariness itself, would need to be inherited. For this interpretation — which conforms to that given in the Diptera (*Drosophila*, No. 199) and Fishes (*Carassius*, No. 201a) — speaks the irregularity in the appearance of the supernumerariness among the descendants of pure-bred hen breeds with supernumerary toes. Not only do these often distribute themselves variously over the two body-sides or fall away entirely on one side; there occur, moreover, among them hens without supernumerary toes which cannot exactly be regarded as recessive, since they in turn produce among themselves more-toed [hens] again. My interpretation, that here the tendency to rudiment-multiplication might have passed to other body-parts than the legs (Przibram, Exp. Zool., 3. Phylogenese, p. 148 and 1910, p. 276), is confirmed in the most satisfactory manner by Barfurth's discovery of the supernumerary wing-tubercles (1911) and by the demonstration of the agreement of the more-toed individuals, increased by these cases, with the expectation computed for chance-breeding [Ramschzucht] according to my formula (Przibram, Anwend. elem. Math., 1908, p. 53 and 1912).

It is also plausible that precisely the hen-thumb, in tame breeds which no longer are accustomed to roost up in trees, is subject to a weakening which leads to a loosening of the connection and thereby to hyper-regeneration or also to rudimentation (cf. Kaufmann-Wolf, p. 250, figs.). The great variability of the more-toed feet, too, agrees with what has been observed in other rudimenting organs. In particular there prevails great diversity in the starting-bone serving the toe-multiplication, in that the branching-points may lie in the 1st phalangeal joint, in metatarsus I, and especially, in other birds, even still more proximally in the tarsus. The latter cases then look as though the supernumerary toes came forth out of the lower leg, because in the birds the proximal tarsal-row is inseparably fused with the tibia. The agreement of the symmetry with the multiple-formations arising from more distal hallux-bones, however, vouches for the fact that there too the rupturing-off of a part of the rudiment of the hallux-margin (that is, originally the pre-axial part of the leg) furnished the cause. Besides in the gallinaceous birds (hen, pheasant, turkey) and the diurnal birds of prey (eagle, buzzard), such a case is also found in a gull (*Larus leucopterus* — Bateson, p. 393, Fig. 122), which is of interest because in many gulls (*Rissa* — ibid., p. 396) rudimentary halluces are no rare appearance.

## b) Mammals, Mammalia.

### I. Anterior extremity.

**No. 243.** *Bos taurus*, ♂; [Pl. XXI, Fig. XXVII], Przibram (1906; 1909, p. 211, Pl. XII, Fig. 23a; 1912, p. 271, Pl. I, Fig. 1).

In Vienna a bull was exhibited, from whose right shoulder an accessory pair of legs hung down. The trunk of the otherwise well-formed but small specimen was bulged out on the left side; in the opinion of the veterinarians who inspected the bull, it was supposed to be a matter of a parasitic twin. The palpation-finding on the good-natured animal, which I undertook myself, however, yielded the result that in the trunk, apart from vertebral column and ribs, there were no bony remains whatever of a second embryo. The left-sided bulging-out was without doubt a subsequent consequence of the necessary counter-balance for the right-sided overweight, as one could recognize from the exertion of the bull on rising. The accessory pair of legs itself could be lifted up so far that it stood erect in one plane with the normal right foreleg. It possessed no connection whatever with the normal vertebral column, but articulated on a normal shoulder-blade in a joint of its own. This head, common to the two accessory humeri, excludes the possibility that between these forelegs a piece of a second vertebral column could have been situated. It can therefore not be a matter of a parasitic twin, but only of a break-triple-formation. With this the symmetry-relations agree exactly. In the hanging-down position both additional legs turn their posterior side toward the observer; thereby the one standing further forward reveals itself as a right one, the one standing further backward as a left one. In the raised position these legs, just like the normal left one viewed from the left, turn their outer and anterior side toward the observer. Since the articulation of the supernumerary forelegs takes place at the anterior margin of the shoulder-blade, now, with the position of all three forelegs in one plane, the one lying further forward is to be regarded as the one further [from the normal], the other as the one nearer to the normal. It thus possesses, as always in the break-triple-formations, the middle component, inverse symmetry to the two others. Our present case is distinguished by the extraordinarily regular formation of all three components, of which the normal one is admittedly more strongly developed — perhaps partly in consequence of use — whereas in the abnormal ones the hoofs, in consequence of non-wearing, have grown out more pointed, so-called "stall-hoofs." Of interest, too, is the formation of a dewlap of the same white colour as the normal one between the accessory thighs, and the continuation of the same colour on the inner side of the lower legs, again corresponding exactly to the colouration of the normal forelegs. My intention of arranging a radiography of the bull foundered on the sudden departure of the owner from Vienna, and my inquiries after the further whereabouts of the animal remained without result.

An investigation of the skeleton would certainly be able to furnish still more exact points of reference as to how we should conceive the break of the shoulder-girdle.

**No. 244a.** *B. taurus*, ♂; [Pl. XXI, Fig. XXVIII]; from the photographer Th. Weinstabl, Schultrage 15, Amstetten, in Lower Austria, I received the photograph of a cow raised in the locality there, which on the left side possessed a monstrosity apparently quite corresponding to the previous one, only somewhat less regularly formed. But since I did not see the animal myself, I am unable to give more exact information.

**No. 244b.** *B. taurus*, Guinard (1893, p. 362, Fig. 175) figures a calf, published by Baumeister, with three left-standing forelegs. The accessory pair standing on the outer side is fused as far as the hoofs, but each component is just as strong as the normal right leg; on the other hand the innermost one, corresponding to the normal left foreleg, is quite free, but much more slender and only half as long. The symmetry seems to be the one typical for break-triple-formations. The starting-point of the monstrosity is likely to have been the outer margin of the scapula.

**No. 244c.** *B. taurus*, Guinard (1893, p. 483, Fig. 269) figures, further, the skeleton of a "notomelia" in cattle. To judge by the figure, it would be a matter of a tripling of a shoulder-blade (the side is not given), in which the two accessory scapulae — fused with one another lengthwise, which also send out a bony brace to the shoulder-blade of the opposite side — serve as the place of origin for a likewise lengthwise-fused double-limb, which runs out into four equally long toes. More exact symmetry-relations are not statable.

**No. 244d.** *B. taurus*, Lesbre and Jarricot (1908, p. 253, Fig. 5) bring, among other things, the skeleton of a head of cattle quite similar to the previous Guinardian case, from the collection of the Veterinary School at Lyon. Here, however, it is recognizable that the original right shoulder-blade was injured at its free cutting-edge and that the accessory ones adjoin this one more closely. (Perhaps it even refers to the same specimen?)

Several further "Notomelia" cases made known in cattle — i.e. limbs on the back (Geoffroy St. Hilaire, 1832–1837, Gurlt, 1832, 1877, Zundel, 1865, Lesbre and Jarricot, 1908) — are not without further ado traceable to breaks, since these are mostly tumours without skeletal connection, out of which then double or single legs have sprouted, about which more will be said later. As an example let No. 244ᵇ serve (Lesbre and Jarricot, 1908, p. 249, Fig. 1).

No. 245. *Capra hircus*, Blanc (1891, p. 13, Figs. 7–9) gives a detailed depiction of the skeleton of a triple goat foreleg from the veterinary school at Lyon. I have already earlier (Przibram, Exp. Zool., 2nd ed., 1909, p. 211, Pl. XII, Fig. 22)¹ used the figure (Fig. 9) as evidence for a break-triple-formation and must even now again refer myself to Blanc's figures for a correct description, since, as will be set out later, Blanc's text contains a partly contradictory interpretation.
