The right scapula bears, on its neck-margin, in front of the normal articular facet for the right humerus, two further articular facets in the same plane and arranged one behind the other in such a way that the middle one is reversed-symmetrical to the two others, which is especially clearly recognizable in the inner view (Fig. 8). Corresponding to the three facets, three bony crests run over the outer surface of the scapula (Fig. 7), whereas otherwise only one should be present, opposite which stand three coracoid apophyses. Each of the articular surfaces articulates with a humeral head. Here too the head of the middle humerus forms a perfect mirror-image of the two others. If we proceed from the normal leg — that is, the one standing furthest rearward — then the head of the humerus shows at its posterior margin (Fig. 9) the spherical part, which would permit unrestricted rotation in all directions, were a pointed process not laid before it at the anterior margin. If we now proceed from the normal right leg to the next component, then at the posterior margin we encounter the analogous pointed process and only then the articular ball, which for its part is fused with the corresponding one of the third humerus, while only then, furthest forward, does the pointed process of this third upper-arm attach. The elbow-joint of the two accessory forelegs is ankylosed, in that the two ulnae fuse in the uppermost part into a flattened pyramid; to the right and left of this lie the radii, which, as always in goats (and other ruminants), are the stronger. At the distal end of the forearm bones fusion again occurs, but all carpal, metacarpal and finger bones are present in correct number for each component. The two forefeet stand fully parallel side by side and turn the backs of the hands outward, instead of forward as the normal foreleg does; yet the hooves of the foremost component are clearly turned forward.

> ¹ By an error the object is there given as being from a sheep, *Ovis aries*; I herewith correct this. The species designation changes nothing as regards its use as evidence for break-triple-formation.

From this description of the figures it follows with certainty that the middle component of the tripled foreleg possessed a symmetry mirror-image-opposite to that of the right foreleg, while the foremost possessed the symmetry of the right side.

In contrast to this, however, Blanc now writes in the text (p. 15; translated from the French):

"The two supernumerary limbs present a remarkable arrangement. First, there is only one right limb — the more rearward one — and the other is symmetrical to the former, i.e. it is shaped like a left limb. Secondly, each of these two limbs seems to have turned about itself and, from inside outward, to have described an angle of 180°, in such a way that above it is the posterior surface of the humerus which is found directed outward, in the middle the ulna, and below the auricular [outer] surface of the phalanges. In reality the two limbs have simply retained their embryonic position.¹"

Blanc thus interprets the accessory component standing nearest to the normal right foreleg, the posterior one, as a right limb turned 180° against the definitive normal position and, accordingly, the mirror-image limb standing in front of it, separated from the normal one by it, as a left one. Now since the bones do not agree with this without further assumption, he lets the torsion remain undone, whereby admittedly the contact-position of the ulnae could then be explained, likewise the outward-turning of the backs of the hands. But in the embryonic rotation it is not the whole extremity as such that is turned around in the scapula; rather a spiral twisting takes place both of the upper-arm and of the forearm bones. If our case had retained an unperformed rotation, then, in comparison with the normal, upper-arm and forearm would have to appear twisted against one another. But that is by no means so; rather, all parts of each component stand to one another in the correct orientations. In particular let it be pointed out that the pointed process at the (upper) humeral head belongs in all [components] to the radial side, the round articular ball to the ulnar side. Within each component, accordingly, the normal torsion has taken place during embryonic development. A single exception I find in the toes of the middle component, which, as expected, ought to be turned rearward to the same degree as those of the foremost are turned forward. Here there is a quite small disturbance of the mirror-image symmetry, which, however, Blanc does not mention in the text at all and which is either only feigned by the perspective of the drawing or may be caused by the fusion of the toe-segments. Just as we cannot make an unperformed torsion within the individual limb responsible for the symmetry, so such an interpretation also does not agree with the nature of the articular surfaces at the scapula. In the normal rotation of the foreleg through 180°, the torsion of the forearm and upper-arm takes the chief share beforehand: for the rotation of the uppermost humeral piece in the facet, and hence of this facet itself, there remains only a very small angle. Quite the contrary to this, however, we see a mirror-image form of the middle facet against the normal, which would have to correspond to a torsion-omission of fully 180°!

In place of Blanc's two hypotheses — first, omission of the normal torsion; second, mirror-image sprouting of a left extremity with likewise omitted torsion — we must therefore [assume] the applicability of all our

> ¹ In Blanc it reads "portion" (part), which, however, should without doubt mean "Tracht" [bearing/orientation] or as much as "position" (Stellung). For in embryonic development there in fact occurs, as is well known (cf. e.g. Bruns, Presentation of the development of the extremities in O. Hertwig's Handbook of comparative developmental history III/2, 1906), such a rotation. The word "part" would give no sense at all.

hitherto observed symmetry-rules for break-triple-formation. The break-site lay at the anterior margin of the scapular neck, so that from the downward-facing distal wound-surface the foremost component had grown, a direction-correct right one, but from the upward-facing proximal wound-surface the middle one, a reversed-directed, hence left one. The moment of wounding must have lain before the onset of the normal torsion, for otherwise remnants of the not co-performed rotations would have been to be expected.

No. 246ᵃ. *Ovis aries*, Joly (1868, without fig.) makes a brief statement about a lamb born at François Laborde's in M[…] near d'Auch (Gers), in which to the right of the neck a leg with a forked foot stood. Only by feeling had the author convinced himself that the accessory extremity, crossing the neck goat-fashion, sprang from the 4th or 5th vertebra and not from the scapula. It is to be regretted that the skeleton could not be examined; probably the case would have turned out analogous to No. 244ᶜ and ᵈ in cattle.

No. 246ᵇ. *O. aries*, Joly (1869, without fig.; the photographs appended to the treatise seem never to have been reproduced).

"Two scapulae united into one and grown onto the scapula of the principal animal present two *cavitates glenoidales*, of which each receives the corresponding humeral head." Nothing further about symmetry.

No. 247ᶜ. *O. aries*, Joly (1869, the same) saw a second similar monstrosity, which differed from the previous one only by the affected side and the degree of fusion of the accessory forelegs.

No. 247ᵈ. *O. aries*, Geoffroy St. Hilaire (1832–1836, III, p. 198, without fig.) mentions that in a wether a normal foreleg was bent up rearward and immediately in front of it two shorter accessory legs were articulated, whose humeral heads ankylosed just like that of the normal leg.

No. 247ᵉ. *O. aries*, *Garratoni (1745) described, according to Pol (1913, p. 117), "two accessory anterior extremities beside the typical one of the one side" in the sheep.

No. 247ᶠ. *O. aries*, *Regnault (1808; repeated with fig. in Gurlt, 1832) figured a lamb, before whose breast an accessory leg-pair hung down; according to Pol (1913, p. 118) "by the position of the two accessory limbs it makes the impression as if a second shoulder-girdle were placed in front of the typical one," and he conceives the case as a rudimentary double-formation. I myself have a similar case, but in which the accessory leg-pair was displaced further bellyward, [which I] saw in Omaha (N. Am.), and the free dangling about of the whole accessory complex made, in the living animal, indeed also the impression of a parasite. Nonetheless, after Tornier's experiments on *Alytes* (see above No. 216ᵃ⁻ⁿ), it must remain questionable whether we do not have to make a break-triple-formation with total breaking-off of the fragment responsible.

No. 247ᵍ. *O. aries*, Cramer (1900, p. 26, Pl. IV; Pol [1913, p. 117, with reduced Fig. 17]) observed "a case of polydactyly … in the lamb. In this one there is, behind the right elbow-joint, yet another right foreleg, which is well developed. The plantar surface is somewhat turned outward. This third extremity behaves at the carpal joint in a flexed position, so that the metacarpus is directed roughly vertically upward. From the first finger-segment downward we see … the third and fourth toe repeat themselves in mirror-image." "This mirror-image of the third and fourth toe is … a pure malformation, which has arisen through splitting." To this description I have to remark that the supernumerary extremity is to be designated a second right one really only insofar as it likewise stands on the right, but by symmetry, owing to the mirror-image arrangement of the toes, it is to be regarded as one composed of a left and a right. Here the left part is the one that comes to lie nearest to the normal right foreleg, for from Cramer's figure it can be recognized that the fused accessories are fused by their inner margins. The inner toe is in the lamb namely somewhat longer than the outer, which holds both for the normal and for the accessory feet. We would thus have before us the typical symmetry of the break-triple-formation. Its point of origin lies probably at the posterior side of the right scapula, for the accessory, fused fore-thigh, ascending almost parallel to the normal right fore-thigh, can hardly still open into the normal one, but should rather — similarly as in the typical cases of cattle and goat and the preceding ones of the sheep — insert with ankylotic humeral heads at a break-wound of the scapula.

## II. Hind Extremity.

No. 248. *Bos taurus*, ♀, *Vallot (1845–46) cited (with Fig. 8) after Lesbre and Jarricot (1908, p. 256); one-year-old cow with four hind legs. "This animal had in the lumbo-pelvic region 'the remnants of another individual, of which nothing but the hindquarter had remained', i.e. two emaciated and immovable extremities of unequal development, a part of the pelvis and four teats. The extremities hung over the right flank, the one in front almost complete but misshapen and more or less ankylosed, ending in three well-developed toes without counting the chestnut; the other rearward incomplete, deprived of the femur and attached to the accessory pelvis only by muscles; this extremity seemed to consist chiefly of a long and thin cannon-bone, followed by a single toe, behind which one nevertheless glimpsed a small chestnut. The vertebral column was in the lumbar region so bent toward the left that it left free a depression, where the union of the accessory with the principal pelvis took place. Four characteristic teats were observed on the right side of the accessory pelvis, on the croup of the principal animal. This [animal] showed, moreover, at the normal place an udder with eight teats, which raised the number of the same on auto- and parasite together to twelve (Fig. 9)."

Since the original was not accessible to me, I have translated word for word from the French the passage cited by Lesbre and Jarricot. In my opinion the description as auto- and parasite by no means does justice to the facts, which incidentally is also the view of the two named researchers for the Notomelia cases in general. In the present animal there remains, among much else, unexplained in this way: why at the normal place eight instead of four teats are present; how it is that the one accessory leg has one toe too many, the other one too few, since the two must originally have been separated by the vertebral column, so that no exchange of a ray could have taken place; how it is that no trace of an accessory tail was to be seen, although the same, by its position, would project out of the principal animal and therefore could hardly have been melted in; why, finally, a sinking-in of the vertebral column should have been specially arranged for the reception of the accessory pelvis. All these difficulties fall away if we regard the case as a break-triple-formation. Then the accessory pelvic part is not [to be regarded] as a remnant of the parasite, but as the fusion of two half-pelves, which have arisen super-regeneratively from the two wound-surfaces of a break of the pelvic anlage. The wounding at the same time caused the bending and depression of the vertebral column and, in consequence of the simultaneous penetration into the udder-anlage, tore the latter open as well. In later growth a transport of the distal udder-parts newly forming super-regeneratively from the wound-margins, i.e. of the teats, took place, so that these set out in opposite directions. The one set met at the normal site with the normal four teats and formed an eight-teated udder; the others reached the left croup-side. In consequence of the slight gaping of the break-margins at the pelvic anlage, the super-regenerates soon abutted one another, and thus it could happen that the one lower-leg bone, when its upper leg [femur] atrophied, could secondarily fuse lengthwise with its partner into a three-toed leg, while the other lower-leg bone became free and formed for itself a single-toed extremity. In accordance with the rule always observed in the break-triple-formations, here too, moreover, the hind leg hanging nearest to the normal left hind leg is a right one, the one hanging further forward (three-toed) a right one, as follows from the position of the chestnuts. I recall that the accessory pelvic parts were inserted on the left and that the one accessory udder too lies on the left croup-side, while the accessory legs hang down across the spine to the right; hence, for the purpose of establishing their topographical relations, they are to be thought of as folded up. It is to be regretted that the skeletal finding is unknown. The following cases supplement our knowledge in this respect.

No. 249. *B. taurus*, London College of Surgeons Museum No. 163 (Catalogue p. 46; translated from the English; fig. drawn by Przibram, 26 July 1907). "The skeleton of a calf with an accessory pair of pubic bones and of hind limbs."

*"This skeleton seems at first glance to belong to a dipygous calf, but there are no sufficient signs of a dichotomy of the vertebral column present. It differs from the parasitic hypogastrodidymus in the respect that the pelves are not united. It is possibly an example of pyopagus, in which the second embryo, with the exception of the hind limbs and the pelvic girdle, has undergone complete atrophy."

My examination of the skeleton itself showed that it is undoubtedly a question of a break of the vertebral column, through which the left pelvic half was co-affected and super-regeneratively tripled. The vertebral column is — as in the previous animal — bent toward the left and depressed at the curve-apex for the reception of the supernumerary pelves. The break-site is recognizable by the splintering of the spinous processes. Behind the 21st vertebra a half vertebral segment is inserted on the right, the following horizontal vertebral processes are shortened on the left and blunted; the 26th vertebra is formed only on the left, its spinous process attaches on the right obliquely to the preceding one. Evidently the right halves of all vertebrae from this 26th to the 22nd have been displaced rightward forward, so that then the right half of the 22nd lies wedged in just between this and the 21st.

At the break-site of the spine there attach, in front of the normal left pelvic half, a pair of pelvic halves fused by their inner sutures, and indeed — as the description in the catalogue of the museum already pointed out — without leaving room in between for the entry of a vertebral row. Regarded as a whole, the pelvic-half-pair with its hind-leg-pair stands opposed to the normal hindquarter in such a way that the like-symmetrical sides face one another crosswise. It is that arrangement which we have also encountered in frogs (e.g. No. 225, *Rana esculenta*, Van Deen, 1838) and in birds (e.g. No. 232ᵉ, *Fulica atra*, Otto, 1841) and which agrees with the typical symmetry-rules of the break-triple-formation: if one thinks of the supernumerary structures, displaced out of their anatomical plane by spatial restriction or lack of support, as folded back again into the same. In our calf the correctness of this representation follows from the connection of the right accessory pelvic half with the left normal one, while the left accessory one again attaches to the vertebral column. The right supernumerary one is thus to be regarded as the one nearer to the normal one and, correspondingly, as the inversely oriented one. The left supernumerary one, however, in consequence of the downward bending of the spine, has grown out of the break-site topographically downward instead of forward, so that in the figure the left accessory hind leg apparently stands nearer to the left normal one. But if one thinks of the accessory leg-pair as raised forward into the height and at the same time of the bent-off part of the vertebral column as straightened, then this left accessory leg describes a rotation away from the left normal one, and the right accessory one, after the rotation has reached roughly 90°, sets itself up as an exact mirror-image to the normal left one, opposite the latter. The findings at the vertebral column and at the extremities make the assumption of a parasitic embryo superfluous — which anyhow would not agree with any of the known categories — whereas they make the origin as break-triple-formation very probable.

No. 249ᵃ. *B. taurus*, *Siedamgrotzky (1877, p. 53) is likely (according to Ercolani's abstract, 1882, p. 798) to have had a similar case in the calf before him: with a simple vertebral column there are said to have been present two pelves with four extremities.

No. 250. *B. taurus*, Ercolani (1882, p. 791, Pl. I, Figs. 1–6; Bologna, No. 3373) reproduces detailed anatomical investigations by Dr. F. Peli on a calf, which in the living state carried the right hind leg with five toes at a right angle, spread away from the body and without touching the ground with it. The following details about the skeleton of the abnormality suffice for us; the soft parts bring nothing essentially new (p. 792):

"The coxal [bone] of the same results from an ilium, in which the portion participating in the formation of the cotyloid cavity shows itself enlarged (Pl. 2, Fig. 1ᵃ). The pubic bone gives no noticeable anomaly to be seen. The very voluminous ischium shows the traces of a union out of two ischium bones, which, fusing with one another, lose parts of their form-characters (Pl. 2, Fig. 1ᵇ, ᵇ')."

"Besides the cotyloid cavity destined for the reception of the head of the normal femur … there is the remnant of a second cavity, which serves for the reception of the rudimentary head of the accessory femur." "The hip-bones [thigh-bones] consist of a normal femur (Pl. 2, Fig. 1ᶜ), on whose outer side another femur is encountered … " "In the lower-leg region there is found a tibia and a kneecap normally articulated with the upper leg, and inside of it two fibrous bands, which above attach to a fibro-cartilaginous mass that is connected with the inner end of the supernumerary femur by means of three bands and below end at the astragalus. The fibro-cartilaginous core and the bands which follow upon it can be regarded as representatives of the bones of the supernumerary leg (Pl. 2, Fig. 1ᶠ, ᵍ, ᵍ') … " "The region of the heel of this supernumerary leg is composed of 2 calcanei, which with their inner sides enclose a bone that represents two astragali fused with one another. With each of the calcanei articulates a bone which, according to the position it occupies, is to be regarded as a dentiforme … (Pl. 2, Figs. 1ʰ, ʰ', k; l, l')." (p. 793):

"Under the 2 calcanei there protrude two bones, the one situated on the outer margin, the other on the inner margin of the heel and embracing a prolongation of the astragalus, bones which represent the two cuboids and the two scaffoids [scaphoids], looking toward the two tarsi. Finally, on consideration of the front side of the foot, a little bone becomes visible, situated between the two cuboid-scaffoids, which can be nothing other than the representative of the large cuneiformi (Pl. 2, Fig. 1 m, m', n)."

"The principal metatarsus, which follows upon this region, shows two grooves, which permit the conclusion of a fusion of two metatarsi … "

"The toe-region of the supernumerary limb is tripled and each toe consists of three phalanges provided with the corresponding sesamoids."

"The three phalanges of which the outer and the inner [toe] consist are so formed that they could be regarded as belonging to an outer or inner toe of a left or right limb. The middle toe, on the other hand, shows the phalanges arranged in such a way that it cannot be decided to which toe they correspond, in that their margins, the outer and the inner, exhibit the form peculiar to the inner or central margin of the normal phalanges, from which it is to be surmised that such an arrangement comes from the fusion of two inner halves of two phalanges." (p. 797):

"Of the two limbs added to the normal one, the anterior one is to be counted to a left hind leg, the rearward one to a right hind leg."

If we recall that the monstrosity belongs to the right side and that the supernumerary, mutually fused legs lie at the posterior side of the original leg, then we see again the middle component assume the opposite symmetry. The almost parallel position of all components and the far-reaching fusion of the accessories allows the conclusion of a slight gaping of the wound in the pelvis.

The complete disappearance of the accessory lower legs shows us that in the earlier-mentioned case, No. 248, the atrophy of leg-bones can also have set in with melomelia, so that a parasite need not be assumed, whose far-reaching atrophy would only then have led to the remaining-over of the remnants of a hind-leg-pair.

No. 251. *B. taurus*, Gurlt (1832, II, with fig. reproduced from Pol, 1913, p. 120, Fig. 18) "states … of a calf, that only its skeleton is preserved in the collection of the Berlin Veterinary College …, yet brings a figure of the outer forms of a calf (Fig. 18), which corresponds to this skeletal finding: Under and in front of the normal right pelvic half lies an ilium provided with an incomplete articular socket, which is connected by fibro-cartilage with the typical pubic bone, by a ligament with the typical ilium. Of the typical right hind extremity no abnormality is reported. The accessory one consisted of a femur — patella was lacking —, a bone (tibia) ending below in a point and not articulating with the adjoining foot, a foot which, in contrast to the proximal elements, showed an increase of its components: besides a talus there were present two calcanei, two cuboids, a boat-shaped bone (*Os cuneif. tert.*)," furthermore three metatarsalia, which were separated only in their distal part, otherwise fused into a bone exhibiting two longitudinal grooves, finally three complete toes. Evidently typical and accessory hind leg had a common soft-tissue covering up to the foot-root … From the talus onward the two legs are also externally visibly separated; the accessory three-clawed one runs forward, the typical one … is bent dorsalward at a strong angle, so that it looks upward. Gurlt states that with the accessory, hence more headward-situated, socket the typical femur articulated; that is, in the first place, a priori improbable; secondly, this contradicts the already-mentioned figure." Pol's abstract lets it be clearly recognized that it is a question of a case of break-triple-formation similar to the previous one. To his criticism of Gurlt's statement of the connection of the (accessory) articular cavity lying further forward and lower with the simple femur I am unable to subscribe. It may very well be that the smaller piece, appearing as accessory, of the pelvis represents the remnant of a broken-off proximal pelvic part, while the distal part remaining behind and above it wanted to produce, proximalward and distalward, one pelvic half each, which then together, as the larger mass, simulated a normal half. The proximal-directed half, growing down- and forward, let a leg of reversed symmetry — the middle of the three components — sprout out of itself and, during growth, in consequence of the reversed position, came together lengthwise with the component growing down- and rearward out of the distalward-directed end of the distal piece, whereby the toes approaching one another fused into an inner or central toe. Here too, then, our conception resolves apparent contradictions.

[com]plete toes. Evidently the typical and the accessory hind leg had, up to the tarsus, a soft-tissue covering.... From the talus onward the two legs are also externally visibly separated; the accessory three-clawed one runs forward, the typical.. is bent dorsalward at a strong angle, so that it looks upward. Gurlt states that the typical femur articulated with the accessory — that is, the more head-ward situated — socket; this is, on the one hand, a priori improbable, and, on the other, contradicts the figure already mentioned. Pol's report allows one to recognize clearly that this is a case of break-triple-formation similar to the preceding one. I am unable to subscribe to his criticism of Gurlt's statement regarding the connection of the (accessory) articular cavity, lying farther forward and below, with the single femur. It may very well be that the smaller piece of pelvis appearing as accessory represents the remnant of a sundered-off proximal pelvic portion, while the distal part that remained behind and above it sought to produce, proximalward and distalward respectively, one pelvic half each, which then together, as the larger mass, simulated one normal half. The proximally directed half, growing downward and forward, let a leg with reversed symmetry — the middle of the three components — sprout out of itself, and during growth, owing to the reversed position, ascended lengthwise together with the component growing downward and backward out of the distally directed end of the distal piece, whereby the toes approaching one another fused into an inner or central toe. Here too, then, our interpretation resolves the apparent contradictions.

**Nr. 251a.** *B. taurus*, Lesbre and Forgeot (1905, repeated in) Lesbre and Jarricot (1908, p. 270, with Fig. 11). The left hind leg of a calf shows traces of doubling in the presence of a second, smaller hip-bone behind the normal one, in the strengthening of the distal femoral epiphysis, in the appearance of a separate posterior fibula, upon which follows a calcaneus fused with the normal one, further a cuboid, a single metatarsal bone with one toe and one hoof. But precisely the single number of the toe and its indeterminable symmetry make it likely that, as in the frogs Nr. 220, 220a, 221, this is a pair fused from two rudimentarily small ones, hence not a doubling but a triple-formation.

**Nr. 252.** *Ovis aries*, Gurlt (1877, Pl. XVII, Fig. 9?) in a later publication gives the figure of a sheep from which, on the left beside the tail, there hung down a pair of legs completely fused at the thigh and lower leg. The presence of a second pudendum, however, allows one to recognize that the duplicity also continues up into the pelvic region. From the skeletal sketch of the supernumerary double leg (Fig. 9?) the entirely mirror-image arrangement of the two united legs can be recognized, namely the opposed position of the two calcanei. The two central toes brought close to one another seem, as in the preceding cases, to have fused into one. The accessory limb standing nearest to the normal left hind leg shows the symmetry of a right one, as is at once to be gathered from the mirror-image heel-angle; hence the more distantly standing, hindmost one is again a right one.

**Nr. 252a.** *O. aries*, Ercolani (1882, p. 790, Pl. II, Fig. 12; Catalogue-Nr. 2823 of the Bologna Museum) found, on a two-headed lamb without traces of doubling of the pelvis, parts of two accessory legs on the left hind leg. The thigh was normal, the lower leg doubled; to this attaches, parallel to the normal left metatarsus and, like the latter, fastened to a few tarsal bones, a second, somewhat shorter bone, which bears three toes, hence parts of two feet. According to the description, the two inner ones were of normal shape (of which side?); the toe standing nearest to the normal foot was smaller. According to the figure, this one could be mirror-image to the innermost toe, while the one standing between them could be bilaterally symmetrical in itself, so that one half of it would belong to the adjoining one as the second toe of a foot. The two-headedness of the animal and the absence of a break-formation in the pelvis make it appear possible to assign this case not to the break-triple-formations but to the fusion of two embryos.

**Nr. 252b.** *O. aries*, Otto (1841, p. 256, Nr. CCCCXIII) described a sheep which, on the left, at the lumbar region open in the form of a spina bifida, bore an accessory pelvis without sacrum or vertebral column. Joined to this were two rudimentary limbs, which lacked the lower legs but not the feet. The anus was situated below the accessory pelvis and was missing, just like caudal vertebrae, from the normal place, that is, between the terminally placed legs. There were four testicles and two penes present. Unfortunately nothing can be said about the symmetry; the doubling of certain inner parts does not, as discussed several times, argue against origin by break.

**Nr. 253.** *Canis familiaris*, Ercolani (1882, p. 751, Pl. II, Fig. 14). Skeleton of a little dog: On the left side, touching the anterior border of the normal left pelvic half, two fused pelvic halves with mirror-image projecting hind legs are laid against the transverse processes of the vertebral column. The latter itself shows no signs of doubling. The symmetry is the typical one.

**Nr. 254.** *C. familiaris*, Parona (1883, p. 298, Nr. 10 with figure and skeleton). In a newborn little dog the end portion of the vertebral column is deflected to the left; on the right side of the posterior pelvic border a supernumerary pair of legs fused lengthwise along the tibiae, together with its pelvic portion, is wedged in. Viscera normal. The symmetry corresponds to the general scheme.

**Nr. 254a.** *C. familiaris*, *Santi-Sirena (1878) has, according to Parona (1882, p. 298, Nr. 10), described a similar case, but with two ani and two penes. Symmetry not given.

**Nr. 255.** *Felis*? "Catellus de Molossorum genere" Haller (1768, III, p. 52, IXa and b). Vertebral column deflected to the left. To the inner border of the right pelvic half is appended a pair of legs completely fused up to the hollow of the knee, together with their pelvic bones. The tibiae are likewise fused lengthwise, whereas the fibulae are placed freely to the right and left of this tibial bone, and the toes too are entirely free. Of the metatarsus this cannot be seen exactly. With regard to the symmetry, it can be clearly recognized in all bones that the row lying nearest to the normal right hind leg has the opposite symmetry, that is, the left, whereas the more distant one has the symmetry mirror-image to the latter, that is, again the right.

**Nr. 255a.** *Felis catus*, Otto (1841, p. 257, Nr. CCCCXVI), Ercolani (1882, p. 798). A cat bore, on the right side of the body at the lower border of the sacrum, connected by means of ligaments and an embedded rudimentary little pelvic bonelet, a pair of legs fused up to the knee, which was smaller than the normal one. Between the two tibiae lay a single fibula; the feet were entirely free and complete. For our symmetry question the case holds a particular interest in that here the accessory limbs, ordered in front of the normal hind limbs, are fused at their posterior borders, namely at the fibula, whereas in the accessories of our Nr. 254 and 255, appended backward, the anterior borders, namely the tibiae, had flowed together. This again agrees with the symmetry law, according to which the middle component of the tripled limb stands mirror-image to the two others, hence the position of the accessories relative to one another depends on their arrangement in front of or behind the normal extremity.

**Nr. 256.** *Homo sapiens*, Foerster (1861, p. 42, Pl. VIII, Figs. 13—15) figures a three-legged boy in whom the right leg is widened at the pelvis and, above the knee, splits into two branches, of which the inner one is turned vertically downward, the outer one, however, is turned upward against the posterior side of the thigh. This lower leg bears a foot with six toes, the longest of which stands in the middle and projects beside the projection of the foot which divides the latter into two halves, of which the one, besides the mentioned longest toe, has three more of decreasing size arranged outward, while on the other side of the projection two toes stand, of which the one standing farther away seems, through the configuration of the nail, to give itself to be recognized as a double one. According to the figure, the downward-directed lower leg further had two toes, which appear, together with the foot, turned outward and backward; since, however, not all toes are drawn even on the normal left foot, perhaps no weight is to be placed on the absence of the two toes. The symmetry of the foot equipped with six (or seven) toes forms, together with the normal foot, a typical break-triple-formation, in which two components had fused up to the toes by their outer borders, whereby a few in the middle disappeared.

**Nr. 256a.** *H. sapiens*, Foerster (1861) further cites a case described after Haller in the "Journal de Médicine, II. Nr. 4 as a "puer tripes, pede superfluo supra genu exeunte, digitis octonis... duo medii majores." This case would differ from the preceding one merely in the regular formation of the double foot of the accessory limb, since it is expressly stated that of the eight toes the two middle ones were the longest. A regular mirror-image arrangement of the feet fused at their inner borders is therefore probable, so that only the two large 1st toes disappeared at the fusion as the ones meeting one another nearest.

**Nr. 256b.** *H. sapiens*, 12-year-old girl; *Stopnitzky (1901, p. 71, Pl. III, Fig. 4); Stieda (1902, p. 695), Pol (1913, p. 155). The left foot, broadening immoderately distally, shows on the tibial border a two-phalanged, hence 1st, toe, which is dorsally flexed and possesses no metatarsal of its own. To this attach, in a plane inclined to the plantar plane, three three-phalanged toes, each with a metatarsal and cuneiform, which in turn sit upon a navicular that attaches to the tibial half of a broadened astragalus shown by a furrow to be a fusion-product of at least two bonelets. To the fibular half of this talus there are attached, by means of a second navicular, a cuneiform, upon which sit five toes arranged parallel to the plantar plane, in that the middle, two-phalanged one — shortened, however, compared with a normal 1st toe — possesses no metatarsal of its own, but shares that of its tibial neighbour, which, like all the toes still to be discussed, is three-phalanged. The fibular border of the foot is normally built; upon a single calcaneus follows the likewise single cuboid with the 5th and 4th toes. The 3rd and 2nd toes too follow normally. The slight size of the now-following 1st toe and the absence of a cuneiform of its own allow one to conclude a splitting of the anlage, which is also suggested by the doubling of the navicular and astragalus. The tibial split-part we find again in the dorsally flexed 1st toe of the tibial border. Each of these split-parts has tried to regenerate at its break-margin the adjoining three-phalanged toes, and indeed the tibial split-part the original tibial half of the 1st toe in the fibular direction, whereby it has managed to regenerate the 2nd to 4th toes, whereas the fibular split-part the original fibular half of the 1st toe in the tibial direction, whereby only the 2nd and 3rd toes were reproduced in mirror-image. We thus find the essential features of a break-triple-formation present if we regard the tibial border as the more proximal and the fibular as the more distal, with which we also manage in all analogous cases (cf. Nr. 256c, *Macacus*, and further below on Hyperdactyly).

**Nr. 256c.** *Macacus* (sp.?), *"Catalogue of the Teratological Series Museum College Surgeons London" (1872; "Hunterian specimen" Nr. 307); Bateson (1894, p. 340, Nr. 504, Figs. 97—98); Pol (1913, p. 160, Figs. 37—39).

A nineteen-toed left hind extremity of an ape, whose right one was normally formed, has received in the three cited places of the literature just as many different interpretations. Before I therefore give my own, again divergent, explanation, a description on the basis of the figures must be attempted, one which in itself still contains no hypothesis. Since only the lower limb members of the ape have been preserved, it cannot be said whether outside of these too there were signs of multiple-formations present.

At the left pelvic half such signs already make themselves noticeable: behind the normal ilium and ischium there attach, almost parallel to the normal ones, supernumerary bones of the same form. In the accessory ischium a foramen obturatorium smaller in comparison with the normal one is to be perceived, which is placed not parallel to the normal one but at almost a right angle to it, while the wall appears more powerfully formed than in the normal one. Since nothing is mentioned of an accessory pubis — indeed Bateson expressly makes the statement that the accessory ischium extends around the foramen — the latter is therefore bordered on both sides by an ischial portion, which must accordingly be designated as mirror-image doubled. This ischium, together with the accessory ilium, which seems to have a greater breadth than the normal one, borders only a very small acetabulum.

The otherwise single thigh, but about one half broader than the normal right one, bears, besides the normal inner condyles, two further protuberances that stand farther outward. The one has the structure of a large condyle, but a symmetrical form, so that it may be thought of as composed of two outer halves of a normal left condyle. Still farther outward stands a second, smaller elevation, which is laid down broader than the normal trochanter but likewise appears as a trochanter. With the distal, broadened femoral end three bones are connected, of which the innermost represents a typical tibia, only somewhat too thin and bent inward; the middle one appears as a weak tendon, fibrous in its upper part, stretched over the concavity of the former; while the third, outermost, roughly resembles a normal fibula, yet articulates directly with the femoral end. The two lean lower-leg bones insert at their distal end side by side at the proximal anterior border of a stout, broadened astragalus, while the typical tibia reaches over behind the inner border of the same. To the astragalus there attaches, as normally, a single calcaneus outward and backward, which distally bears a single, normal cuboid, which for its part likewise, as normal, sends out the metatarsals to the 4th and 5th toes. On the other hand, to the distal astragalar border there attach, instead of a single navicular, two bones, of which the tibial one has entirely the form of the normal navicular, while the one situated more fibularly, though it too looks similar to a navicular, is of bilaterally symmetrical build, so that it may be thought of as composed of two fibular navicular halves. To the typical navicular there attach, as usual, two cuneiform little foot-bones, which send out the metatarsals to the 1st through 3rd toes, whereby the attachment of the 2nd metatarsal is not entirely in conformity with the norm. To the atypical navicular there attach four cuneiform bonelets, which each send out a metatarsal. The most tibial of these four cuneiforms is twice as large as the others and extends in between the two naviculars; through this process it acquires a particularly pronounced bilateral symmetry, which the others lack. Each two of these metatarsals form an analogous but mirror-image opposed group, in that these groups appear separated by a larger interspace than between the tibial metatarsals, and the toe standing immediately to both sides of this separation is, in each group, the shorter, the more distant one the longer; the corresponding metatarsals and cuneiforms also show corresponding differences in size. With regard to the absolute length of the toes, the longer ones can be compared with the normal third or fourth, the shorter ones with the normal second or fifth. Finally, in the descriptive presentation it must not be forgotten that the most tibial toe, as normal, exhibits only two phalanges, whereas all the others exhibit three.

Now as concerns the interpretation of the case, I can pretty much agree with the other, earlier workers as regards the pelvis, but hold the supernumerary ischium, for the reasons brought forward in the description, to be mirror-image doubled with the falling-away of the accessory pubis. The analogous is to be said of the thigh with its proximal tuberosities. In the assessment of the three lower-leg bones all authors agree in identifying the innermost bone as the typical tibia; on the other hand, the Catalogue of the Museum (1872) addresses the middle lower-leg bone as a second tibia, Bateson as the first fibula; correspondingly the Catalogue regards the outermost bone as the single fibula, while Bateson wishes to designate it as the second fibula. The author of the Catalogue assumes, just as Bateson does, that the matter concerns two left-symmetrical legs, pushed one into the other with the disappearance of the 1st toe of the outer foot. Conversely, Pol regards the leg as composed of two halves mirror-image symmetrical to one another, which, with the disappearance of both 1st toes, are fused at their inner borders. In accordance with this view, the lower-leg bones offer him new difficulties, since the typical inner tibia, as a left one, would find its continuation in a right foot; the 1st toe he regards as accessory, not as the typical thumb [hallux]. He probably for this reason does not express himself clearly at all about the nature of the bones, yet he seems to regard the outermost as a mirror-image repetition of the tibia, since he stresses the contrast to the mirror-image union of two ulnae known in Diplochiria.

In my view the middle, thin bone must — because of its proximal articulation at the outer side of the proximal condyle of the typical tibia, just as because of its distal articulation at the anterior, not inner, border of the astragalus — be regarded decidedly as a fibula, one which for some reason to be explained later has not attained full strength. The analogous articulation at the astragalus and the general form make it likely that the outermost lower-leg bone too should be ascribed fibular character, although then it still remains to be answered why the proximal articulation occurs at the femur alone, and not at a tibia. The form of this condyle, which does not, as in the normal fibula, appear flattened against the tibia but rounded all around, allows one to think of it as composed of two outer, mirror-image repeated heads fused at their inner borders, and accordingly to regard the bone in question as a lengthwise-fused, doubled fibula, whose inner length-half would be inversely symmetrical, that is, corresponding to a right limb.

The over-great breadth of the astragalus likewise permits one to think of it as composed of two to three talus-bones; the calcaneus,¹ rather too small, is certainly single, as is the cuboid and the typical navicular. Between the latter two, however, lies the bone already conceived of in the description as composed of two navicular halves, which the earlier authors, however, addressed merely as a second, single navicular.

> ¹ Pol (p. 160) erroneously states that, according to Bateson, the calcaneus exceeds the normal bone in size.

To the row of apparently six cuneiforms attaching to the two naviculars, the various workers have again ascribed a different meaning.

The "Catalogue" assumes that between the cuneiforms of the first and second navicular there is a second cuboid; Bateson points out, however (p. 342 top), that there is then a cuneiform missing inward of this cuboid, since in all only six bonelets form the row, so that the three cuneiforms which lie before the typical cuboid and the alleged second cuboid leave only two more cuneiforms inward of it. He thinks that the bone interpreted as a second cuboid was counted once more in the Catalogue as a cuneiform. Bateson himself designates the most tibial bonelet as the internal cuneiform (normal 1), the next as questionable (normal 2?), the third as the external cuneiform (normal 3), the fourth and fifth he compares to renewed middle (normal 2) cuneiforms, the sixth again to an external (normal 3) cuneiform.

Pol, in connection with Bateson, likewise speaks of six tarsal bones of the second row: "With the exception of the somewhat smaller first one, the others are approximately equal to one another in size, similar in form to a normal cuneiform" (p. 160). This summary seems to me somewhat too summary, since the third cuneiform is certainly twice as large as the next largest, the sixth. In my opinion the "Catalogue" is right with its counting insofar as this bonelet must be thought of as fused from two; not, however, from a 3rd cuneiform and (2nd) cuboid, but from two mirror-image fused 3rd cuneiforms, which attach quite symmetrically to the nearest-lying borders of the naviculars, but have left no trace of a suture at their own fused outer borders. According to its form, the sixth bonelet, directly adjoining the cuboid, corresponds entirely to a left 3rd cuneiform, hence also to the left or inner half of our doubled third cuneiform bonelet. The two still remaining cuneiforms (which we had, purely descriptively, designated as C. 4 and C. 5) can, according to their trapezoidal form, count merely as middle cuneiforms; with this their position relative to the supernumerary double navicular also agrees. They present a mirror-image pair, and indeed C. 5 has the symmetry of a left foot, C. 4 that of a right foot. Of the correctness of my interpretation one can convince oneself most quickly if, in Bateson's Fig. 98 II, which has been repeated by Pol as Fig. 39, one lays from the angle of the 3rd bonelet of the 2nd tarsal row, which springs in between the naviculars, a line through the middle of the 4th toe, and a second one transversely through the accessory navicular, so that it cuts between C. 4 and C. 5. To the left and right of this latter dividing-line there then lie, arranged in bilateral symmetry, first C. 4 and C. 5, and on both sides the right half of C. 3 and C. 4.

The toes are interpreted in the "Catalogue" as if, from the outer border, a second [foot] had been pushed into the normal left foot in such a way that its 1st toe had disappeared in the region of the 5th normal one; accordingly the toes, reckoned from the tibial border, would be designated as I., II., III., IV., V. — II., III., IV., V. Bateson, albeit with some doubt, subscribes to this interpretation and declares the case in this respect to be unique (p. 342). Pol will not let this exception stand and, as with the double hands to be discussed later (cf. further below under Polydactyly), assumes a symmetrical double-formation, whereby the toes would be designated as ? V., IV., III., II. — II., III., IV., V.; the question-mark is to indicate the questionable nature of the "accessory" toe, as which Pol wishes to regard the two-phalanged innermost toe. This view of Pol's rests upon the mirror-image doublings in the tarsus, sufficiently emphasized in our presentation.

That it too, however, does not quite hit the right mark is already shown by the embarrassment over the 1st toe. According to its number of phalanges, its articulation with the 1st cuneiform, and its position relative to the tibia, there is not the slightest ground to regard it as anything other than the typical 1st toe. The two following toes would, as V. and IV., have to articulate with a cuboid, but lie, according to Pol's own statements, against cuneiforms; they can therefore, as three-phalanged and of different length, represent only the II. and III. toe. Since in the ape's foot the differences in length correspond to those of human hands, the shorter toe is, corresponding to an index finger, to be addressed as II., the longer one, corresponding to a middle finger, as III. Since the toes adjoining the cuboid must, on all interpretations, count as the IV. and V. toe, there remains for us still to clear up the nature of the four toes attached by means of cuneiforms to the second "double" navicular. If our identification of these cuneiforms is correct, we will expect that to the two middle ones a 2nd toe each should attach, and to those flanking them a 3rd toe each. In this too we are not deceived, for the latter have the absolute measurements of the 3rd toe analogous to the middle finger, the former those of the 2nd toe. None of these can represent a 1st toe, for that should exhibit only two phalanges, nor a 4th or 5th, for these normally stand at a cuboid.

In order to gain a survey of the four different interpretations of the nineteen-toed macaque leg, I have set up the accompanying Table E, in which there stands, side by side, for each leg-piece a designation by the authors. To these I have, for the distinction of the like-named pieces, added capital Latin letters A to C, of which A and B are to express pieces with the symmetry of a left leg, that is, correctly oriented ones, but C those with the symmetry of a right leg, that is, inversely oriented ones. It emerges from this compilation that, according to the "Catalogue" and Bateson, only left-symmetrical pieces of two legs would be present; according to Pol, besides left-symmetrical ones of one leg (and the 1st toe of a second?), also homologous ones of a right-symmetrical leg arranged in mirror-image; according to my own view, however, [pieces] of three legs, of which the middle one possesses right symmetry, while both others exhibit left.

My interpretation thus agrees not only piece for piece with the figures, without anywhere running into difficulties, but it ranges the case under the symmetry rules of break-triple-formation, since between two correctly oriented components there lies, in one plane, an inversely oriented one. We now wish to see whether it does not succeed in finding further indications that we really have to do with a hyperregenerative triple-formation following break of the hind-leg anlage. How must such a break have affected the anlage? The whole tibial border of the leg is, as Bateson already pointed out, single, beginning from the pubis up to the ending of the 1st toe. From the norm there deviates only the stronger inward-curvature of the tibia, which permits one to conclude a force pressing it away from the outside. With this we have gained a point of reference for the course of the break-line, which we now try to follow proximalward and distalward.

With this we have gained a foothold for the course of the fracture-line, which we now seek to trace proximally and distally. Through the thigh anlage the fracture must run near the tibial margin in such a way that, leaving the lesser trochanter untouched, it had separated off outward the outer half of the greater trochanter as well as of the capitulum femoris, and, penetrating further into the pelvic anlage, had split the ischium and ilium. Passing between tibia and fibula, the fracture, distal to the tibia, must have grazed the astragalus anlage, severed the outer half of the navicula, passed through between the 1st and 2nd cuneiform, and had its first point of attack between the 1st and 2nd toe.

Fibular to the fracture-line, the fibular portion was originally likewise single, as Bateson still now emphasizes for the fibular margin. Lying against it are the severed-off ilium-, ischium-, capitulum femoris-, trochanter majus-half, the outer femoral margin, the fibula, the outer astragalus-half, the calcaneus, the cuboid with the 5th and 4th toe, as well as, inward of these, the outer navicula-half (of the accessory scaphoid bone) with the 3rd, 2nd cuneiform and the 3rd, 2nd toe. Abnormal is the direct connection of the fibula with the femur, which, however, would follow of itself from the splitting-off of the tibia.

The tibial and fibular longitudinal halves of a leg adduced so far contain all the pieces of a left leg, in single number and correct orientation. What lies between them are supernumerary pieces, which therefore come into consideration as regenerates. Beginning with the tibial split-half of the original leg anlage, we find the outer halves of the ilium, ischium, and of the trochanter-heads regenerated, the femur broadened, on the outer tibial side a not fully ossified, very feeble fibula added, the astragalus and navicula supplemented, a 2nd and 3rd cuneiform together with toes appended. These 2nd and 3rd toes are too feeble for normal ones — Bateson even compares the 2nd with a 5th — and thus still betray a regenerative character. The abnormal attachment of the 2nd metatarsus also to the 1st cuneiform likewise points to the abnormal course of this toe-formation. The 3rd toe shows this same displacement, in that it articulates not only with the inner half of the 3rd doubled cuneiform, but also with the 2nd. With this we have exhausted the correctly oriented regenerates, which have grown fibularward. What still remains are the inversely directed, right-symmetrical pieces, which supplement the fibular split-half in mirror-image — those designated C in the table.

The regenerative origin of the mirror-image supplementation explains the seamless mutual abutment of all the C + B pieces, which only then appear separated when, distally, several elements must be appended side by side — in the cuneiforms, metatarsals and toes. As mirror-images the toes of C and B diverge from one another and form the larger intermetatarsal space that struck all observers. From the same cause, however, the cuneiform B must, during the growth of the inverse 2nd and 3rd toes of C, have been pressed toward the tibial margin. Here it seems first to have brought about a tibial displacement of the regenerating 2nd and 3rd toe of A, but then to have fused with the regenerating cuneiform 3 of A into the double-sized bilaterally symmetrical cuneiform. As in all triple-formations at a break, a second plane of mirroring is present, which runs through this fused cuneiform. The two planes of mirroring here form a very acute angle with one another, which at first glance seems to contradict Bateson's rule, according to which the planes of mirroring should *(Page 184 (printed page 388) is occupied entirely by the wide landscape-format Table E, which spans pages 184–185. The running text continuation of the p.183 paragraph resumes on p.186. Table E is printed sideways and is reproduced in full below.)*

Yet if we look up as far as the proximal end of the humerus and the pelvis, it turns out that here the planes of mirroring do in fact form right angles with one another, as may be seen from the position of the femoral heads and of the foramina obturatoria. Only as a consequence of the fusion of all the femoral split-pieces has the originally greater gaping of the fracture-margins been effaced.

Far from constituting an exception to Bateson's rules, this case is precisely one of the finest confirmations of them and of their reducibility to fractures with super-regeneration. As in all typical triple-formations at a break on the pelvis, here too we have, between the originally distally growing, partly split-off component and its re-generation at the proximal fracture-margin, interposed a proximally directed component which shows inverse symmetry. It is also not without significance — as we shall see in the following discussion of polydactyly — that the tibial split-component merely simply regenerates the radial defect, whereas the fibular one has produced, besides the normal fibular margin, also a mirror-image one directed tibialward. The fibular side is accordingly to be regarded as the more distal, the tibial side as the more proximal, if we hold to our hitherto confirmed views, according to which, under limitation of potency, the more distal part, in inverse direction, is no longer able to regenerate the more proximal one, but merely again the parts lying distal to it.

## c) Cervid Antler.

No. 257. *Cervus capreolus*, Rörig (1901, p. 271, Fig. 78), "Antler of a roebuck with a 'secondary beam' developed on the left side of the frontal bone . . . from Karl Brandt, Ill. Jagd-Ztg. IX, 198."

The secondary beam is a piece of antler anlage split off forward from the left frontal pedicle. One recognizes, from the broadened and shortened formation of the terminal fork of the left primary beam, that a traumatic case is present. The brow tine standing forward on the primary beam is well developed; immediately bordering on the place of the burr corresponding to it is the burr of the secondary beam: this is curved forward and upward and is divided lengthwise by a groove into a front and a rear half, each of which ends in a point, these points standing apart, so that the shorter rear one turns toward the brow tine of the primary beam. This secondary beam can therefore be regarded as a fused pair of brow tines, which together with the normal brow tine represents a triple-formation at a break.

No. 258. *C. capreolus*, Rörig (1901, p. 291, Fig. 79), "Antler of a roebuck with 'secondary beams' developed on both sides of the frontal bone, from the same journal, p. 209."

The right-side secondary beam is only simple; thus represents merely a splitting; but the left-side one bears, near the burr toward the front, a two-pronged conical outgrowth. The matter here is therefore probably a triple-formation at a break on a split-off brow tine; the brow tine is thus represented four times on this side, six times on both together.

No. 259. *C. capreolus*, Rörig (1901, p. 275, Fig. 80), "abnormal antler of a roebuck; abnormal as a consequence of a break of the right-side beam during the time of velvet . . ., from the Deutsche Jägerzeitung, IV, 1885, p. 513."

The right beam is broken at half-height outward and rearward, so that the otherwise — instead of forked — merely simply pointed terminal portion hangs downward. From the kink-point there grow three narrow, almost parallel tines, which is probably to be interpreted as a tripling of the brow tine.

## Regenerative Potencies of the Mammalian Appendages and Polydactyly.

The deer antler is the only appendage of a mammal that, by reason of its normal annual shedding with rapid growth in every year, still postembryonally affords occasion for triple-formations at a break. All the others we must trace back to embryonic splittings.

The particular difficulties that stand in the way of an experimental intervention in the embryos of mammals have prevented us from being able to furnish direct proofs for the origin of double- and multiple-formation through division of a single egg or embryo of a mammal. By analogy with all other animals, however, this hypothesis must be designated the most probable. Formerly one wished to trace all majority-formations within a single amnion back to a fusion of two eggs (Geoffroy St. Hilaire and others); but on the basis of developmental mechanics most more recent authors have rejected this assumption (cf. Schwalbe, 1907, p. 72) and derive the double-formations from monozygotic twins, which were either wholly separated and then partially fused together again, or else never wholly separated, but arose from incomplete separation of the first blastomeres, whereby super-regeneration set in at the surfaces of separation. This mode of consideration allows us to pass, without the application of new principles, from the complete double monstrosities, via partial doublings, to those multiple-formations of the appendages which, as triple-formations at a break, might arise in somewhat later developmental stages.

For these triple- and double-formations too it had formerly been assumed, on the fusion-hypothesis, that originally two separate eggs and embryos had been present, of which then all but the still demonstrable limbs (parasites) had perished, or rather had been taken up by the other embryo (autosite). According to the experiments on the fusion of eggs and embryos in other animals (cf. *Embryogenese* 1907) we should then, to be sure, have to expect to see the autosite swell up to almost its double size. That, however, is now not the case; rather, the many-legged mammals tend to be somewhat smaller than normal ones, which would agree with origin through splitting of a single egg and the use of egg-material for super-regenerates. With double-formations, on the other hand, such enlargements do occur (cf. Schwalbe, 1907, p. 12), so that here we perhaps have to distinguish the second type — fusion from two eggs, or at least from one egg with two nuclei; in general, however, here too the common parts of the partners are not twice as large as would correspond to the norm of a single animal.

If one conceives the presence of a supernumerary leg-pair as the remnant of a parasite, then, on considering the symmetry-relations, one comes upon a new difficulty: namely, with Notomelia it would have to be assumed that the two embryos had fused with one another back to back. But how can a fusion of the viscera then take place? Moreover, with the Pygomeles, in which at the end of the body a symmetrical leg-pair appears in analogous position, doublings of inner organs as well as of penis and anus frequently occur, these however lying symmetrically right and left of the median, not — as the dorsal union would require — above and below the spinal column of the autosite. To avoid this difficulty, Lesbre and Jarricot (1908) conceive the Pygomelie (p. 272, Fig. 12) and the Notomelie (p. 274, Fig. 13) as a lateral union of twins which are completely fused except for the doubled portions. There then belongs to each of the original embryos, at the place of division, one outer normal extremity and one symmetrical to it, grown together with the analogous one of the counter-animal. This conception does justice to the symmetry, but does not explain why the inner limbs are excluded from the spinal column, whereas the outer ones are normally attached to it. We should expect that two spinal-column-ends would maintain themselves side by side, to which the legs adhere right and left, as is realized in the Duplicitates posteriores.

But if we make the slight modification of the theory that no two embryos at all had been present, but rather that, during the formation of the pelvic anlagen, respectively of the shoulder girdle, a median splitting had occurred, then we shall be able, by the super-regenerative route, to attain the same symmetry, without the position relative to the already-formed spinal column causing us difficulty. The presence of doubled soft parts likewise offers, as repeatedly mentioned, no difficulty, since their anlage can be separated together with the skeletal anlage (or perhaps also formed in correlation to it?).

If we now think of the splitting as shifted from the median toward one side, then the double-formation passes over into a triple-formation at a break of the affected limb of that side; if the splitting shifts from the side of the limb-anlage up into the longitudinal axis of the limb, then we again obtain a double-formation, but this time in place of a limb of one side, so that only one supernumerary one is present. If the splitting now shifts in the other direction beyond the limb's longitudinal axis, then it again comes to a triple-formation at a break, which shows a different arrangement of the components, while however the inverse symmetry of the middle one relative to the two others is maintained upright. I have hitherto tacitly presupposed that the splitting in the longitudinal axis of the limb, just as with the body's principal axis, had gone through the median, whereby therefore two equal-sized halves and mirror-image extremities would arise. This presupposition, however, is not a necessary one; rather, we may conceive of the longitudinal splitting as engaging parallel to the median of the limb, but at a lesser or greater distance from it. If, as is mostly the case with the vertebrates, the limb broadens again toward the distal end, then it will be possible only near the median to carry a regular splitting up into the proximal portions, whereas, with further outward shifting of the longitudinal splitting toward one of the limb-margins, either the outward- or inward-lying narrower branch is wholly severed, or the splitting must stop before this complete severance.

Indeed, we see double-formations of the arms or legs in mammals continue chiefly up into the shoulder, respectively pelvic, region precisely when the limb is divided lengthwise into two equal-sized halves; whereas the splitting remains all the more restricted to distal parts the further toward the margin the doubling-mirror-plane falls.

This behavior is clearly to be traced in polydactyly, yet I have found it nowhere emphasized in any compilation. Not only this peculiarity of polydactyly, but also the hitherto unexplained details of the finger- and toe-symmetry and -anomaly appearing in it, turn out, according to the principles hitherto employed by me, to be a single series of various degrees of one and the same mirror-image splitting — which is all the more satisfying inasmuch as even Bateson (1894, p. 395) wished to despair of subordinating all cases under a common rule: "Do these phenomena not suggest to us that we are seeking for an order which does not exist? Is it not as if we wished to name the branches of a tree in their serial order?"

In the following Table F let the documentations for my assertions about the polydactyly-series be presented, at first separated according to mammalian species. I begin with man, because here the normal five-toedness still prevails, as in the amphibians, and no rudimentary rays are present which, on their occasional re-awakening to full development, could feign an increase of the rays. To be sure, here too one has attempted to relate the supernumerary structures to far-removed ancestors¹), but the shape of these structures, completely resembling the present-day human fingers, makes such assumptions impossible.

> ¹ Darwin had adduced as an argument for this the alleged regeneration of amputated supernumerary thumbs and little fingers, but then withdrew the argument (1878), since this regeneration does not seem to go beyond the measure of normal bone-growth. The latter holds presumably also of the case made known later by Fackenheim (1888). Yet experiments would still be desirable.

From the compilation set out below it again emerges, from what Gruber, Taruffi and Pol have adduced (see there 1913, p. 126), namely that all the certain cases of double-hands in man exhibit the uniting-site of the two components at the radial margin.

The illustrations of double-hands occasionally adduced as counter-evidence, [taken] from Aldrovandi from the year 1642 (p. 497) and Rueff as early as 1587 (p. 41), have, as I have shown elsewhere, found their way into the literature in the company of winged people, animal-headed forms and the like; they are by no means to be drawn upon as scientific evidence.¹

> ¹ The illustrations are certainly not drawn from nature, but rather designed after the hearsay of the draughtsman's fancy. In the text, moreover, the particular arrangement of the hands or feet is not pointed out, only their number; thus it reads, with Aldrovandi among others: "Infans geminati digiti," with Rueff: "Nati sunt etiam quidam abundantibus membrum partibus, ut alii membris non rite formatis, alter quidam singulis pedibus manibusque digitos habens duodecim, alter, extrosus manibus in obliquum transversis pedibus, recto alias habentem." Above it stands, on the left, the illustration of the last-named malformation with twisted limbs, on the right a boy with doubled, correctly tibially-united feet whose great toes are fused, and with doubled hands, which are each drawn as composed of two five-fingered [hands]. They would here actually not stand ulnarly united, but rather show a direct connection of the [parts] facing one another at the front. I should also remark that, according to the Latin text, this illustration relates to the malformation first cited there, namely [a child] equipped with abundant limb-parts; the single hands and feet equipped with twelve members could relate to the injured [individual] equipped with single hands and feet bearing the next-to-last [number], "digitos habens duodecim."

In analogous manner, double-feet always show the union at the tibial margin, and the limbs of doubled nature likewise behave like double-sided ones [i.e., like mirror-images].

If we consider the duplications as consequences of splittings and other injuries, then the rule results that, while ulnar parts can indeed arise from the radial margin and radial ones [arise] in analogous manner from the tibial fibular [margin], the reverse does not [hold].

If the radial margin is lacking, we further see appearing in its place the second ulnar [margin] attached to the ulnar one in mirror-image (and analogous to the fibular ones).

It thus behaves exactly as if the radial (resp. tibial) margin were to be regarded as a more proximal part, and the ulnar (resp. fibular) as the more distal part of the limb belonging to it.

If we take into account the topographical position of the said margins in the human limbs, then — at least from the beginning of the development of their five-rayed form onward — we have to regard the radial (resp. tibial) margin as the cranial or anterior one, the ulnar one as the caudal or posterior one (Lewis, 1902, resp. Bardeen, 1905). We thus find the anterior part capable of regenerating the posterior one anew, but not the posterior one of supplementing an anterior one again. In quite corresponding manner, as is well known, the median trunk-parts of the body in postembryonally regeneration-capable vertebrates also behave: namely, while from the trunk vertebral column the tail can indeed be produced, from the tail, however, nothing other than again a tail can be produced, whereby this latter is oriented in mirror-image at a cephalad-directed fracture-surface (cf. above in the case of the break-triple-formations of the salamanders and lizards).

Hyperdactyly and diplochirie thus confirm our way of consideration in a surprising manner, since in setting up the theory no special regard whatever was paid to them.

The particular position of the human thumb, which is opposable to the remaining fingers and is therefore separated from the nearest one, the index finger, by a larger intervening space, has led to also addressing three-phalanged fingers — which appear in similar manner attached at the radial margin of the hand — as thumbs, and to speaking of "hyperphalangy" of the same.

After the foregoing explanations there will probably no longer be any doubt that the three-phalanged finger appearing in place of the always two-phalanged thumb is to be addressed not as a thumb, but as an index finger with the symmetry of the opposite side. The confusion comes about because the thumb of the same side, owing to its position of opposition, appears to assume the same mirror-image position relative to the normal index finger as does the inversely oriented supernumerary index finger grown out from the proximal — i.e., here cephalad-directed — split-surface.

If transitional forms toward the two-phalanged thumb and rudiments of the latter are found radial or ulnar of the accessory index finger, then this is attributable to incomplete carrying-through of the rearrangement into the mirror-image as a consequence of a splitting that has not penetrated far enough.

For the most part, the thumbs of the original as well as of the radialward-attached accessory hand have completely fallen away, which is explained on the one hand by the irreplaceability of the once-completely-lost radial margin, but on the other hand also by the mutually-directed
