| Phalanx count | Bateson | Page | No. | Fig. | Observer | Split extends up to and including | Interpretation (Przibram) |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| — /030 | normal [oder atav.:] | — | — | — | — | — | |
| /330 | A. (1) a | 363 | 535 | 107 | Marsh | Tarsus | |
| /333 | » (3) | 368 | 543 | — | Franck | Metatarsus | |
| /303 | » (4) | » | 544 | — | Wehenkel | Tarsus | |
| m] 03/30 | [Bateson unbekannt I] | — | — | — | Schmalz n. Cramer | 1. Phalange | longitudinal split of the middle toe with mirror-image. Displacement of the ulnar parts | [*The first paragraph on this page continues a sentence from the preceding chunk (…"which can be explained by a differing) growth of the two thumbs." It is therefore not translated here.*]

If we imagine, namely, a longitudinal split laid through the anlage of the radial margin, then the same will gape most at the most distal part — which later produces the ulnar margin — but less at the proximal part; and just here the thumbs, emerging almost perpendicular to the split-plane, will at once be taken up by the surrounding tissue and suppressed.

That with this latter view we hit upon the correct one is shown by the relations at the foot, where no growth of the great toes perpendicular to the split-direction takes place, because these too normally do not have to assume any opposition-position. Here the two great toes meet one another almost in parallel, and they therefore fuse into a strictly bilaterally-symmetrical, broadened great toe.

In the case of the human foot a confusion of an inversely-positioned second toe with a »three-phalanged« great toe is possible, since the great toe exhibits no distinguished position relative to the other toes; but here too we find no indication of a hyperphalangy of the great toe (cf. Pol, 1913, S. 171, 174)!

Hand and foot of the human follow indeed not different but the same laws, and the apparent deviations are merely the applications [adaptations] to the special form-relations. As a thumb there is always only a two-phalanged finger to be designated, just as every great toe is two-phalanged.

Are there, besides the thumb, any two-phalanged full-grown fingers at all? Such [fingers] would have to be attached either at the ulnar margin of the hand or to other metacarpals, without their own metacarpal bone, in such a way that they would assume an opposition-position to the other fingers.

Thumb-like fingers at the ulnar margin do not occur at all, as has been discussed at length, for the union of supernumerary hand-parts always takes place with the radial margins; a thumb at the ulnar margin is not even simulated.

(Nr. 260.) Pol (1913), on the other hand, describes a hypermelia in which a two-phalanged finger is said to have been grown onto the middle-finger of an accessory hand (his figures are reproduced under our Fig. 260).

On a well-developed girl, Anastasia Tsch., Pol observed a »shorter accessory extremity situated anterior and medial to the typical left arm« (S. 138, Abb. 28). This was removed operatively. »According to the operation report, there is found on the front side of the neck of the typical left scapula, immediately next to the typical shoulder-joint — but without connection with it — and immediately below the origin of the Processus coracoides, an accessory glenoid cavity lined with cartilage … With this … is … the head of the one long tubular bone of the accessory *[The sentence continues onto p. 204, which lies outside the owned range (197–203); the remainder of the Anastasia-Tsch. quotation and Pol's discussion are not translated here.]*

---

**Translator notes on the table (pages 197–202):** This is a single multi-page tabular catalogue continued from page 196; the column header row appears on p. 196 and reads: *Phalangenzahl* (Phalanx count) | *Bateson* | *Seite* (Page) | *Nr.* (No.) | *Abb.* (Fig.) | *Beobachter* (Observer) | *Spaltung reicht bis einschließlich* (Split extends up to and including) | *Deutung Przibram* (Interpretation [by] Przibram). The lowercase letters (g, i, e, f, m, k, l, n, etc.) in the leftmost margin are Przibram's case-row labels; a "—" in that margin marks the normal/atavistic baseline row, which usually carries no specimen data. In the *Phalanx count* column, overlines/diacritics on individual digits (rendered here as 3̃) mark inverse (mirror-image) segments as printed; "z. T." = "zum Teil" (in part), Θ is Przibram's printed symbol (here left as Θ). German fragments left untranslated in cells (e.g. "nach Bateson normal", "[B. unbekannt … 381]", "[Bateson unbekannt I]", "normal [oder atav.:]", "» teilw. atav.?") reproduce the printed Bateson-classification shorthand. Abbreviations in the *Interpretation* column reproduce the printed shortenings (Verlag. = Verlagerung/displacement; spiegelb., spiegelbildl. = mirror-image; Längsh., Längst. = Längshälfte/Längsteil; Wiederverschm. = Wiederverschmelzung/re-fusion; Inv. = Inversion; rad. = radial; uln. = ulnar; tib. = tibial; Th. = thumb/Daumen or its abbreviation as printed; St. = ray/Strahl; Lt. = Längst.; d. d. = durch den/durch die, "through the"). Taxon names (*Cervus dama*, *Cervus capreolus*, *Cervus elaphus*, *Equus mulus*) are left as printed.

Pol now asks himself: »how are the reported findings to be interpreted?« and continues: »The two-phalangeal finger, which at its joint-origin is purely membranously attached to the capitulum metacarpi III, cannot be conceived of as an equivalent of a thumb; the lack of a corresponding joint, of a proper strong metacarpus, corresponds rather quite to the behavior of an accessory rudimentary finger« (p. 146). He finally comes to the conclusion that he is dealing with a hand split as a consequence of the intrusion of amnion-threads, which at the split-site would have produced this supernumerary finger« (p. 166).

The arm-bone, [he says,] has the symmetry of a left one, thus of the same side on which it sits (p. 153): »If we examine the whole accessory extremity at a glance, to see whether in its relation to the typical left one the operation of the symmetry-law expresses itself, then from the flexion-posture of the hand and fingers, from the position of caput and tuberculum majus of the arm-bone in its relation to the radial and ulnar arm-bone process, from all the foregoing expositions it emerges that the accessory arm appended to the typical left shoulder-girdle half likewise has the character of a left one, that it therefore does not follow the symmetry-law.«

By the symmetry-law Pol understands the mirror-image symmetry, mostly present in accessory limbs, with respect to typical limbs of the same side; in his case it would therefore have been to be expected that the arm-bone be formed as »right«.

Let us not content ourselves with Pol's summary consideration, but rather go through the parts of the arm-bone piece by piece with respect to their symmetry-affiliation to a left or right extremity.

According to his own illustrations and descriptions the caput humeri faces laterally, the nasiform projection interpreted as the tuberculum majus medialward. In a normal left humerus, however, the caput faces medialward, the tuberculum lateralward. Should therefore the proximal humeral end of the arm-bone likewise correspond to a left one, then it would have to be inserted the other way round and turn the volar side, instead of the dorsal side, toward [the beholder], if it hung down in a relaxed, half-pronation posture. That, however, is not the case, for in the view of the girl from the front the nails, which characterize the dorsal side, clearly show themselves on the finger-sides turned toward the beholder. The arm-bone is therefore to be interpreted as right-symmetric, and the medial-facing border is an ulnar one. The flexion of the fingers is indeed in fact opposite to that of the left hand, which could scarcely be founded in the case of an accessory left-symmetric hand. On the other hand, the two-phalangeal finger interpreted by Pol as an accessory finger-rudiment is opposed to the three-phalangeal one, on whose metacarpus it sits, exactly as a thumb of a right-symmetric hand would be. I find no cause not to see a thumb in it, for the peculiar attachment of a two-phalangeal thumb at the radial border of the metacarpus of a three-phalangeal, supernumerary finger does also occur otherwise (for example in the case which Windle described, cf. Bateson, 1894, p. 327, No. 485, Abb. 88 left; the illustrations are erroneously reversed in mirror-image). If we now consider further the fingers of the »three-group«, then the middle one is the longest, and so can very well represent a middle-finger, and the one which stands between it and the two-phalangeal one is somewhat shorter and can therefore be an index-finger. There remains of the »three-group« still the most-median one to be interpreted, which Pol interprets as the three-phalangeal thumb. This interpretation I consider, for many reasons, unfortunate: firstly it would be longer than most of the other fingers, which never occurs even at the fingers interpreted as three-phalangeal thumbs, and is the case at no developmental stage; secondly it would not stand in opposition to the remaining fingers; thirdly it would, in consequence of the fixed pronation-posture of the hand, come to stand at the ulnar border, where [a thumb] never occurs; fourthly the presence of two nail-rudiments at its terminal phalanx is not explained; fifthly the presence of a »three-phalangeal« thumb, when on the other hand-border a »two-phalangeal« finger exists, is otherwise not known.

Since we have up to now diagnosed at the »three-group« one two-phalangeal thumb, one index-finger and one middle-finger, there remain to us for the complete hand the ring- and little-fingers. Since the most-median finger is three-phalangeal and somewhat shorter than the next, the one interpretable as the middle-finger, it would come into consideration as the ring-finger. For that, however, it seems too broad. Now, however, it bears two nails: it is therefore possible to regard it as a fusionproduct of two fingers, the ring- and little-fingers. In my opinion the »three-group« can therefore for itself be regarded as a five-fingered hand, and indeed as one with right-sided symmetry; it thus springs, without the adduction of the »two-group«, from the symmetry-law expected by Pol, which he could not find realized in his interpretation. The two fingers of the »two-group«, three-phalangeal, with just such a curvature as the three-phalangeal fingers of the »three-« (better five-)group, bear the nails on the same side as these, thus likewise belong to a right-symmetric accessory arm. Pol regards the one standing nearest the »three-group« as the ring-, the more distant one as the little-finger. With this the length- and thickness-relations again do not at all agree, for the nearer-standing one is, after the two-phalangeal one, the shortest and altogether the most slender, whereas the other one hardly falls short of the longest and shows an especially broad metacarpal base. The former comes into consideration as the little-finger, the latter rather as the ring-finger. Its broad base would perhaps again permit the interpretation as a fusion of two fingers, yet it bears only one nail. (Pol himself holds a fusion to be possible, p. 147.) If we leave the hard-to-decide question in suspension for now, then we can in any case designate the »two-group« as the fingers of the ulnar hand-border, just as Pol does, only that the position of the two fingers is reversed, and these here once more repeat that [which is] at the ulnar border of a hand which, at the »three-group«, has developed itself rudimentarily in the two-nailed finger.

As different as my interpretation now seems from Pol's, I nevertheless hold his representation right in one essential part, namely in the relation of the splitting into his »three-« and »two-group« to an ulnar defect.

According to Pol (p. 150) Kümmel's second type of the ulnar defect fits in the following points, and indeed in this case: a) »The radius is ankylosed with the humerus at a more or less obtuse angle.« b) »The arm, but especially the forearm, shortened, the forearm in mid-position between pronation and supination.« c) »In the presence of the distal part of the ulna the hand shows no defect« (in total ulnar defect the ulnar fingers are lacking). d) »4th and 5th finger in clawed position.«

Pol perceives »therefore in the first arm-bone insert a radius-rudiment, in the second arm-bone insert a rudimentary ulna.« »Two differences exist with respect to the elbow-joint capsule at the ulnar defect: 1. ordinarily there exists there an angular ankylosis, though the angle amounts to 100—110° and can also be greater; 2. the distal humero-epicondyles are present there, although deformed.« »These two differences appear … not too considerable, especially if one makes vivid to oneself that there, at an ulnar defect, it concerns a typical extremity, here formations of an accessory extremity.«

If one draws on pictures of the normal arm-development of man, as Lewis (1902) has given them, then one sees that here too the five finger-rays are formed out before it comes to a distinct separation of the lower-arm-cartilage from the upper-arm (l.c. p. 161, Abb. 9, five-week-old embryo). Thereby there shows itself an angular out-drawing of the hind-border of the ulnar anlage in proximal direction, which could correspond to the analogous picture of the second arm-bone insert, in case the ulnar anlage had remained behind at too early a developmental stage. My interpretation differs from Pol's in this, that I assume a subsequent rotation of the lower-arm bones, which are after all at first laid down in supination, into (half) pronation, whereby a seeming interchange of the ulnar and radial border would have to take place. Such a one I find only at each of the both finger-groups for itself, not however at the hand as a whole, as Pol construed it. This behavior explains itself from the absence of the firm connex of the wrist-bones at the both finger-groups: instead of the wrist-bones attached to the radius turning, in pronation, around the ulnar ones, and the latter in connection therewith turning themselves over, a separate rotation of the radial group has taken place, while the ulnar one beside it has betaken itself into the pronation-position.

By this conception we are placed in a position to lead the sequence of the fingers at the accessory hand, as we have sought to diagnose it merely after their anatomical features, back to a unitary genesis.

The arm-bone owes its origin to a longitudinal splitting of the left arm-anlage, whereby the shoulder-anlage was co-affected. The medially split-off piece had begun to form itself into a mirror-image, that is, right-symmetric extremity, but, probably for lack of sufficient formative-material, did not bring it, in the developmental time at its disposal, beyond the stage at which upper- and lower-arm fully separate. It was therefore not possible for the ulna to develop itself further in conformity with the norm and to gain the correct connections. The radius likewise did not bring it to a complete formation; in particular it lacked an anlage for the typical thumb-ray, and since this cannot be regenerated anew out of other, more ulnar anlagen, it remained at a rudiment. The radius had attached to itself, besides the thumb, index- and middle-finger, which also came to development; while a doubling-anlage was created in the middle-finger-border freed by separation from the ulnar group, which gave origin to the two-phalangeal fusion-product. The original ulnar group of the ring- and little-finger, on the other hand, remained connected with the ulna and, after the separation and pronation, possessed a free, outward-turned surface. It is not excluded that here too an approach to regeneration was made, which would have announced itself in the unusual thickness of the metacarpal bone.

In order to gain a better overview of the rather complicated relations, I assemble for it a small Table G, which leads through, from left to right, the fingers of the accessory hand in their arrangement from the body-median toward outside, and compares Pol's with my interpretation.

**Tabelle G.** [Table G — columns I–IV fall under "Pol's three-group" (Pols Dreiergruppe), columns V–VI under "Two-group" (Zweiergruppe)]

|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Number of phalanges | 3 | 3 | 3 | 2 | 3 | 3 |
| Number of nails | 2 | 1 | 1 | 1 | 1 | 1 |
| Proximal metacarpal breadth (2 = doubled) | 2 | 1 | 1 | 0* | 1 | 2 |
| Length (rising from 1 to 5) | 4 | 5 | 3 | 1 | 2 | 4 |
| Diagnosis according to Pol | I | II | III | III* | IV | V |

|---|---|---|---|---|---|

this sequence in length already holds also for very early stages, cf. Lewis 1902, p. 161, Abb. 9 (5 weeks).

## III. General Considerations.

We have, in the tracing of the triple- and double-formations from the plant-animals [zoophytes] up to man, found the same rules confirmed, and have been able, by analogy with the cases experimentally analyzed, or at least observed in their formal genesis, to lead all the others too back to regeneration, or at least regulation, following determined injuries.

Before we seek to disentangle the causal genesis further, I wish still to point out some diversities which the individual animal groups show with respect to the appearance of multiple-formations.

Some groups seem to show a special inclination hereto, as already Gerstaecker (1868, p. 202) remarked:

> »It is certainly not accidental that the numerous malformations of this category observed among the insects belong almost exclusively to the Coleoptera; evidently the strong chitinization of the integumentary skeleton which distinguishes this order inclines toward such an excrescence as recurs also at the corresponding forms of the Crustacea. Here it is, as is well known, the Decapoda which present similar malformations at feelers and legs by no means seldom. Hypertrophically malformed claws of the river-crayfish and lobster, distinguished by accessory fingers and side-branches, are even a frequently-to-be-observed appearance; but also at other forms of this order similar malformations have repeatedly been brought up for discussion, whereas at the soft-skinned crabs they have hardly become known.«

We can, in the causal consideration, go a step further and uncover the cause why precisely strong chitinization will lead to accessory excrescences: this lies in the persisting gaping of wounded hard-parts, which then produce multiple-formations out of every wound-surface. Therefore, conversely, at the »soft-animals« multiple-formations are very seldom, whereas at the vertebrates with hard skeleton they again step up quite often.

A further diversity finds itself in the age-stage at which the various animal groups find the multiple-formations most frequently. At the ten-footed crabs such are found mostly at rather large specimens, whence it may at first be concluded that these malformations are not congenital, but step forth only in the course of postembryonal life. That stands in accord with the regeneration-capacity of these animals, which persists beyond sexual maturity. By direct observations on malformed lobster-claws I have (Przibram, 1907, Abwurf) demonstrated that such malformations pass with difficulty through the molts and mostly lead, in the same case, to autotomy of the whole limb. The probability of their being found at crabs therefore grows with the time which the latter, after the acquisition of the multiple-formation, does not need to molt, and it is greatest at the oldest specimens, which would no longer have to shed the skin at all, or only at year-long intervals. Often such monsters are found overgrown with sea-plants and sea-animals as proof for it that they had not molted for a long time.

Still more distinctly there comes to view at the insects the appearance that the acquisition falls into a relatively late developmental stage; for indeed here triple- and double-formations of the extremities have hardly been observed at any other than imaginal stages. The correctness of my interpretation is proved by the observations on mantid-larvae, of which one acquired a triple-formation at an early stage as a consequence of a fore-leg break, but in the course of further molts lost it again, while the second one was injured shortly before the transformation and was able to preserve the picture of triple-formation up to the imago (cf. above No. 198 and 197). At the middle- and hind-legs of the praying mantises no multiple-formations could be obtained; these legs autotomize, namely, in contrast to the autotomy-incapable catching-legs [Fangbeine], very easily (cf. Przibram, 1919, Fangbeine).

That the apparently inherited multiple-formations at the fruit-fly Drosophila (cf. above No. 199) must likewise arise, at least in part, only at the larval stage, I have already set forth. The action of the cold upon the already-hatched larvae could otherwise not, with increasing cold-duration, increase the frequency of the malformed specimens, as the experimenter found.

In contrast to the still postembryonally regenerating arthropods and anamniotes, we find at the amniotes typical multiple-formations always congenital, with the single, already-mentioned exception of the deer-antler, which after all also regenerates normally postembryonally. Although the postembryonally extinguished regeneration-capacity for limb-formation in the amniotes seems at first to speak against the assumption of regenerative origin of break-triple-formations, the transposition of their genesis to embryonal stages nevertheless agrees very well with the facts. The »being-congenital« is not at all identical with the »being-inherited«, but separated from it by the embryonal period.

I have had repeated occasion to point out that these monstrosities are only seldom transmitted quite symmetrically, and that, as other symmetric characters are not inherited localized either, but only the inclination to malformation, Tornier's »plasma-weakness« is inherited (cf. above fish-fins, No. 201a. Polydactyly of birds). The strongest multiple-formations at birds and mammals have hitherto not been found heritable (for example also not after pairing of two malformed parents — Bos taurus; Geoffroy St. Hilaire); they would therefore presumably rest exclusively on an accidental wounding, which would not have needed first a plasmatic variation for its operation.

Perhaps it is permitted to regard the »plasma-weakness« of the monsters as too great brittleness and rigidity of their embryonal stages, so that these variants behave similarly to the normal postembryonal Coleoptera and crabs?

The numerous experiments lying before us, on the most diverse animal groups, show that we do not need especially predisposed specimens in order to achieve hyper-regenerations at all, although those forms will be usable with better success which also show the desired malformation in the free state (cf. Przibram, Homoeosis I). All animals therefore have in themselves that property which, at injury upon a suitable, that is, still sufficiently regeneration-capable stage, leads to the multiple-formations following quite determined rules, and in particular also to break-triple-formation.

Can we now make this property more precise, so that we derive this reaction-type from otherwise known processes at the living organism?

I have repeatedly indicated how I picture to myself the coming-about of break-triple-formation and similar monsters from the turning of the cells (1906; Chap. 3, Abb. 8; 1909, Reg., p. 231, § 10, Taf. XV), in that I apply Zur Strassen's cell-position-rule for normal eggs or cell-aggregates to the accidental alterations.

According to this rule the centrosome of each cell comes to stand under the middle of the free cell-surface, that is, that part of the surface which is not pressed by adjacent cells or other parts of the organism. Now since by the position of the centrosome the division- and thereby the growth-direction is given, the growth-direction will be reversed if the wandering of the centrosome takes place in the direction reversed from the usual, hence in general at proximally-turned wound-surfaces.

Thereby, to be sure, the reversed growth-direction of the middle component of break-triple-formations is explained, but not yet, without further ado, their mirror-image behavior with respect to the distal part.

component of break-triple-formations, but not yet without further ado their mirror-image behaviour with respect to the distal part.

The following five possibilities still remained open:

1. The proximally-turned wound-surface produces the proximal part lacking to it roughly in the way that a planarian cut in two through the middle is able to produce, from the front-piece rearward the tail, from the hind-piece forward the head. This case could only occur if the wound-surface were totipotent with respect to the parts to be replaced and, in case this were to happen from every cross-section, also equipotent.

Actually such triple-formations, in which the middle component replaces the right end, that is, is not heteromorphic, occur only in the lower, largely fort- and equipotent animals. In ambulacrarians, arthropods, molluscs, and vertebrates such behaviour is no longer found.

2. The proximally-turned wound-surface produces the distal part in reversed growth-direction, yet not with a symmetry mirror-imaged to the normal, but with the symmetry of this distal end itself.

Such a growth would have as its precondition that either a spiral twisting of the regenerate through 180° takes place, such as we nowhere see occurring in the middle component, or that out of the upper side, in its prolongation, the under side, and vice versa, could grow. I have already pointed out earlier (Przibram, 1910, Roux-Festschrift, p. 410; Exp. Zool., IV, Vitality, Chap. III, Polarity) that in no dorsi-ventrally built animal is such a capacity known, that upper and under side are unable to replace one another.

In a single Metazoon-example this growth-mode is possibly nevertheless realized, namely in the double-head observed by Wilson on the sponge Renilla (cf. Exp. Zool. II, Pl. II, Fig. 4h), a form in which precisely upper and under side cannot be spoken of.

3. The proximally-turned wound-surface produces no shaped regenerate at all, but merely a regeneration-stump of indeterminate quality. This came to observation in a few cases, in which a mechanical hindrance through counter-pressing tissue could be recognized.

In the by far preponderant number of cases regeneration does not fail to appear even at the proximally-turned break-surface, provided it occurs at all at the distally-turned one. With the extinction of the distal regeneration the proximal too is always extinguished. The lesser regeneration-velocity from the proximally-turned wound-surface can, however, lead initially to stages in which, despite distinct regenerates on the distally-turned surfaces, merely regeneration-buds are to be seen on the proximally-turned ones.

4. The proximally-turned wound-surface produces the distal part in reversed growth-direction and indeed as an exact mirror-image. This behaviour, which we find realized again and again throughout the entire Metazoon-realm, has as its precondition (a) that the upper side continues directly into a reverse-outgrowing upper side, and likewise the under side, in its prolongation proximalward, again produces only an under side, which indeed [according to 2.] is the case; (b) that outer and inner margin at the component do not alter their determination; (c) that the quality of the body-side affected is preserved, even when it is distinguished from the opposite side by other than symmetry-marks.

The necessity of such a behaviour is evident to us in those cases in which the one margin no longer possesses the regenerative potency to produce the other, as we were able to ascertain analytically in the human extremities. Here the hind-margin cannot produce the fore-margin formations and must therefore also content itself, at the middle component of a triple-formation, with the repetition of itself, whereas the totipotent fore-margin is able to make good what is lacking. (The richer potency of the fore-margin has its counterpart in the richer potency of the trunk-part as against the tail, in that the trunk-vertebral-column is often still able to produce the tail, but this no longer the trunk-vertebral-column.)

5. The proximally-turned wound-surface produces the distal part in reversed growth-direction and indeed as a mirror-image, yet not as an exact one, but with the characters which belong to the opposite side. As we have seen in the treatment of the heterochelous crabs, not a single case can be adduced in which one could establish with certainty such a behaviour. We have therefore no occasion, even there where the body-sides cannot be distinguished otherwise than by their symmetry, to assume an appearance of a bud of the opposite side in the middle component of their break-triple-formations.

With this we clear out of the way the difficulty upon which Bateson (1894, p. 558) had struck when he wished to compare the accessory extremity-pairs with a bud laying itself apart into its two halves:

»In a bud the various organs always present the same surface to each other, or in other words, the planes of division always pass between similar surfaces. In Secondary Symmetry this is not the case . . the extra parts may present to each other, or remain compounded by any of their surfaces, whether anterior, posterior or otherwise. This seems to be altogether unlike anything ever met with in animals and plants. It is as if on a bud of a plant two leaves on opposite sides of the axis could in their origin indifferently present any of their surfaces to each other.«

»In einer Knospe kehren einander die verschiedenen Organe stets dieselbe Fläche zu, oder in anderen Worten: die Teilungsebenen gehen immer zwischen ähnlichen Flächen hindurch. Bei der sekundären Symmetrie ist dies nicht der Fall ... die überzähligen Teile mögen einander irgendeine ihrer Flächen zukehren oder mittelst derselben vereinigt bleiben, sei es die vordere, die hintere oder sonstwie. Dies scheint ganz und gar unähnlich irgend etwas zu sein, was man in Tieren und Pflanzen begegnet. Es ist so, als ob an der Knospe einer Pflanze zwei Blätter an entgegengesetzter Seite der Achse einander bei ihrer Entstehung eine beliebige Fläche zuwenden könnten.«

A further difference as against the vegetable leaf-buds lies in the fact that between the two components of an accessory extremity-pair no axial organs are present nor can be formed.

With this we have again reached the starting-point of the present investigation and have found our views throughout confirmed, whereas Bateson's interpretation cannot be maintained.

Besides Bateson, Della Valle (1913) and Driesch (1908, On the Theory of Organic Symmetry) have also expressed themselves on my account of the break-triple-formation.

Della Valle makes use in large part of my trains of thought, but rejects the cytological insertion as little probable (p. 142) and believes he can manage without it. Let us then leave aside the rotation of the cells at the proximally-turned wound-surfaces and test the sufficiency of Della Valle's explanations, who says:

»L'unico tentativo serio di analisi dei fenomeni di polarità resta così quello di Sachs con l'ipotesi delle sostanze organo-formative, poiché l'interpretazione del valore morfogenetico della posizione relativa non può essere accettata per l'esistenza dei fenomeni di spontanea inversione di polarità. Ma giustamente, domandava Morgan . . . quale è la causa della migrazione di queste sostanze organo-formative in una determinata direzione o della inversione di tale direzione nei casi di inversione di polarità?«

Della Valle's answer can be summarized as follows:

»The only serious attempt at an analysis of the polarity-phenomena thus remains that of Sachs with the hypothesis of organ-forming substances, for the derivations of the morphogenetic value from the relative position cannot be assumed for the existence of the phenomena of spontaneous polarity-inversion. But, Morgan asked with right: what is the cause of the migration of these organ-forming substances in a definite direction or of the reversal of such a direction in the cases of polarity-inversion?« (p. 142.)

6. The proximally-turned wound-surface produces, by means of the equipotentiality of the cross-section, the same as the distally-turned one produces (p. 155); the nature of the structure is independent of the growth-direction (p. 150) and rests on the relation of the place to external factors, e.g. the oxygen-supply (p. 145); the mirror-image arrangement is only a consequence of unchanged regeneration of every point of the cross-section (p. 150); polarity is not an inherent character of the organic substance (p. 139), as it is, for instance, in the case of crystals.

Let us go through each assertion individually:

The equipotentiality of the wounding-cross-section is supposed to enable the proximal surface to form the same shape as the distal one. In the concept of equipotency this is not contained; equipotent are surely cross-sections through a crystal, but nevertheless a crystal cut in two with, e.g., electrically distinguished poles is able to regenerate merely the form of the one or of the other, according to the direction of the wound-surface. Equipotency alone, then, does not suffice to explain the growth in reversed direction.

The independence of the form from the growth-direction is, as Della Valle himself states, restricted to the cases to be explained, whereas otherwise the direction often goes precisely together with a definite form, as for instance in a planarian cut in two through the middle. This independence in the particular case therefore requires a closer explanation.

Della Valle gives the following image, which is supposed to satisfy the requirements of the break-triple-formation:

In a tube closed below, the combustion of carbon proceeds, and the CO that arises is, through the access of oxygen at the open end, further burned to CO₂. There then exist, from the closed toward the open tube-end, gradations of the mixture of C, CO, and CO₂. If now, through the laying-on of a cross-section, access to oxygen is procured at this place too, then there establishes itself here again automatically that gradation of CO–CO₂ which previously existed at the open end. The same phase-stratification will, however, arise also at the cut-margin facing toward the closed tube-end, if the tube had been cut through only to half its circumference. Out of the system C–CO–CO₂ there has thus arisen the system C–CO–CO₂, CO₂–CO–CO, which, in the arrangement of distal and proximal parts, does indeed correspond to the repeatedly used schemata for break-triple-formations.

The simile springs from the circle of thought which I had already expressed, regarding the conception of animal regeneration as an automatic restoration of the physical and chemical equilibrium disturbed through the falling-away of existing phases, in the pertinent volume of the Experimental Zoology (2. Regeneration, 1909, p. 222) and had further developed in a lecture before the Senckenberg Society (Frankfurt 1911). Only, the complete homogeneity of the substances in the tube around the long-axis does not permit Della Valle's planned application to the break-tripleformation to be used as a sufficient explanation for its formation. There can, besides the entering-in of regeneration, at most the striking-in of the inverse direction, but not the symmetry of the regenerates, be derived.

Such a derivation he has, moreover, not at all attempted, and contents himself with the explanation that the mirror-image arrangement is only a consequence of unchanged regeneration of every point of the cross-section. We will, however, try to carry the simile further. In order to keep both of Della Valle's propositions both in accord, we should have to assume that just as many different processes run in proximo-distal direction as there are different side-developments at the form in question. With this, however, there would also still stand open the arrangement discussed earlier as the (2.) possibility, in which it would come not to the mirror-image but to the equi-symmetrical regeneration. In order to exclude this alternative, the directions of the processes must be thought of as unalterable in their mutual parallel position, so that their reversal would have to take place for itself in every length-section of the tube. This again is only possible if a polarity, which determines the mutual coordination of the »direction-tracks«, were to belong to the organism as an intimate property. Such a one, however, Della Valle rejects: for polarity is supposed to be no inherent character of the organized substance (in contrast to that of the crystals¹)).

The circulation-theory, as Sachs, Loeb, and others with them had set it up for the polarity-phenomena in the lower, open forms of living beings, plants and plant-animals, was at first not in the position of striking upon these difficulties at the polarity-reversal, because it was a matter of radial-symmetrical forms, for which a single circulating substance each could be answerable for the development into the one or the other terminal form. If, e.g., in a polyp the hydranth-formation is conditioned by the more oxygen-rich termination, then the circulating hydranth-forming substance will, at a wound-site in consequence of the oxygen-access, again form a hydranth. The direction of the circulation here plays no role at all: we can picture the stream of the hydranth-forming substance ascending in the interior and descending over the whole periphery, or just con-

> ¹) Since I intend to continue the discussion of the crystal-analogies in a further work, I do not wish at this place to enter particularly upon Della Valle's attacks against my comparison of the break-triple-formation with phenomena of the mineral kingdom. I have by no means given it up, as the said critic (p. 143, note 1) concludes from its omission in my writings that have appeared in the meantime, which precisely were not supposed to deal with crystal-analogies.

versely; or also one may visualize it as ascending on one single side, descending on another, and so forth. The circulation-streams may, however, no longer run arbitrarily if the organs to be produced are to be shaped bilaterally-symmetrically, unless a moment lying outside the stream is to be taken into consideration as the cause of the bilateral arrangement, such as a preformed polarity. At any rate, it is still possible, at the poles of the main axis of lower bilateral animals — for example in planarias — to conceive the circulation-stream for the carbon-substance as flowing in the interior head-ward and outwardly on both sides flowing off tail-ward, so that at every incision this stream can be struck and caused to bend over prematurely.

In the case of the appendages of the higher animals, which are neither radially- nor in themselves bilaterally-symmetric, however, this mode of arrangement too is insufficient to do justice to the facts. Were we to conceive, in accordance with the asymmetry, the organ-forming material-stream for a hand as running on the fore-margin finger-ward, on the hind-margin shoulder-ward, so that from it first the thumb, then the index finger, and so forth up to the last finger were formed, then, at an interruption of this stream, first again the thumb and then in turn the remaining fingers would be formed, if one imagines the stream bending over at the injury-site. But the break-triple-formations show precisely the opposite behaviour: in the direction of the demanded stream returning at the hind-margin, there appears next to the bending-off site, from the little finger, a little finger, and so forth.

These various contradictions and difficulties of the circulation-theory can now be avoided, without letting fall the theory of organ-forming substances itself, if one assumes a polarity of living beings as their most proper character, independent of external factors.

Della Valle also errs when he (p. 139, note 1) cites Driesch (1901) as a guarantor for the absence of an inherent polarity, for the latter had since then published several works in the Arch. f. Entw.-Mech. (1906, Studies on the Developmental Physiology of Bilaterality, p. 772; 1907, Remarks on Przibram's Crystal-Analogies, p. 174; 1908, On the Theory of Organic Symmetry, p. 143), in which precisely an intimate structure of the organisms is inferred from the symmetry-conditions. Several things, according to Driesch (l. c.), speak

»very much in favour of a mode of action of external factors which only orders equi-directedly that which is unequi-directedly ordered, but intimately directed; or rather, transforms an equi-directed ordering into another equi-directed ordering. Even without the external factors, equi-directed ordering of the intimate structure seems always to be guaranteed to a more or less high degree. With this, however, all symmetry becomes inherent. It is general considerations which, more than anything else, plead for this conception: Nature does not, in the realm of the organic, generally leave the most important of that which it is to accomplish to chance. And further: were symmetry not in the last instance inherent, then there would have to be far more disturbances of it than are actually known. Whence the intimate structure of the matter of living organisms, handed over from generation to generation, derives, wholly eludes our knowledge. Enough — it is, as it were, the coordinate-system into which the vital shaping-factor, the entelechy, works. If, for secondary reasons, it is no longer capable of compensating disturbances, if it remains, after disturbances, ›right‹ or ›left‹, then there results also a ›right‹ or ›left‹ organism. The relation of external factors to the creation of a definitively visible symmetry must also not be imagined in such a way that the external factor — thus, for instance, the light — simply exerted a rotating action upon the nuclear spindle, and that upon the nuclear division so directed everything further depended.… We also may not imagine that external factors let some ›substance‹ accumulate in a simple way — be it photochemically, or on the basis of differing specific weight, or otherwise. This assumption may be possible for a theory of phototropism;… it becomes impossible where it is a matter of the orientation of the so infinitely minute, and just as infinitely complicated, morphogenetic processes within the frame of a symmetry, as it were within a coordinate-system. For this the existence or non-existence of a substance does not suffice. We need something more intimate, we need something marking, as it were, each individual place, although only marking the place within a directed whole. We must therefore refer all symmetry, and also all actions which proceed from external factors and relate to symmetry, to preformed, directed elements of the ›protoplasm‹, and can see in those actions only directing and ordering occurrences.«

Apart from the introduction of the »entelechy«, which accomplishes nothing for me in the explanation of the phenomena, I can fully concur with these expositions of Driesch. In the same treatise this author says (1908, p. 142) with reference to the secondary symmetry of Bateson:

»Most probably there are present here cases of superfluous regeneration. The remarkable symmetry-conditions of the structures do not seem to me to be explained by Przibram's discussion.«

This discussion was contained in the Crystal-Analogies (Arch. f. Entw.-Mech., XX, 1906, p. 252) and therefore brought no detailed presentation of my view, which has, moreover, since then deepened essentially through the working-up of the entire factual material hitherto available. I hope that now an explanation can be given that is sufficiently satisfying also for Driesch. Just as with him, it was also a matter, for me, of the intimate structure of the organisms, from which the various polarity- and symmetry-conditions must be derivable. The arrangement of the ›preformed, directed elements of the protoplasm‹ demanded by Driesch I do not, however, now have attended to by an entelechy, but rather by the differently-directed attraction of the localized particles themselves, after the manner of a »space-lattice«.

Since I here again deliberately set the crystal-comparison aside, I should like only to sketch briefly how I picture to myself, in broad terms, the introduction of an organic spatial-lattice.

If we consider a regeneration-capable cross-section through an animal, then we have before us an arrangement of points which can be distinguished according to their constitution and which, according to their reciprocal position, can be designated as dorsal, ventral, left and right. If we let the animal grow on and then lay a cross-section through the regenerate, we find points analogous to the earlier ones placed before them, which now in their turn will display just such a process again. Since this process, in totipotent forms, is independent of the position of the cross-section, we can picture these point-planes to ourselves as arranged one behind another in uninterrupted succession throughout the whole animal. If we now wished, after the manner of plate-reconstruction, to represent such an animal to ourselves by laying all the so-designated point-planes one in front of another, then it turns out that we should not do justice to the form of the animal. Since all such planes consist of identical points, the cross-section could nowhere change; there could be no difference between middle, front-end and hind-end.

If we lay through the same animal a median longitudinal section, then we have before us an arrangement of points which are distinguished as dorsal, ventral, anterior and posterior. In analogous manner to the cross-section, we can picture the totipotent animal to ourselves as composed of point-planes which run parallel to one another and to the longitudinal section. Here too, however, the form does not emerge straightforwardly from such an arrangement, because the right and left sides differ in opposite direction from the constitution of the median. A plate-model would here indeed make graphic for us the different form from front and behind, but not from right and left.

If we now imagine the two plate-systems united in such a way that the dorsal and ventral points common to them coincide in each case, then I obtain a form in which front and behind, right and left will be able to be distinguished. But this model does not do justice to the regeneration-capacity, toward front and behind, of qualitatively different organs. For if I lay through it any cross-section whatever, I strike only upon one quality of the points strung one after another along the longitudinal axis. The same point would then also have to regenerate one and the same thing only, which in the totipotent forms is by no means the case. On the contrary, from one and the same cross-section a head-end is again produced toward the front, a tail-end toward the rear.

In every cross-section, then, there must be an arrangement of the points encountered such that the point leading to the head-form toward the front, and the point leading to the tail-form toward the rear, is able to come into activity.

Since now, however, several heads, or several tails, also stand side by side in longitudinal splitting, left and right points too must be represented not merely once each in every cross-section, but several times.

We must picture the point-arrangement front-back and left-right repeated again and again throughout the whole totipotent body, so that a three-dimensional lattice comes into being, whose lattice-points alternate with one another in regular manner. Every three-dimensional lattice-mesh contains every kind of point.

We can lay parallel lattice-planes through the body which, as a consequence of the arrangement set forth, will contain one kind of point at regular intervals and will run at equal spacings. (It is, however, not thereby excluded that other kinds of point too may be encountered in these same planes.) If we consider all the lattice-planes of one kind as a lattice-system belonging together, then the various lattice-systems will be set one into another in regular manner.

If two parallel point-systems are oriented toward each other in such a way that behind every point of the one, anterior point-set there lies one such of another lattice, so that in the cross-section it is inserted at equal distance between two further points of the first (anterior) kind, then with uniform continued growth of the two lattices we obtain a constant maintenance of the difference of the anterior boundary as against the posterior, in that the former will correspond to the points of the first (anterior) kind, the latter to the points of the second (posterior) kind. In this mode of growth nothing will change even if we remove, by a transverse section, a part of the already formed lattice-combination, for the acceleration of growth occurring upon regeneration need never touch the relative ratio of the appending of the two kinds of point.

If, on the contrary, we imagine the appending-velocity in the forward direction raised for the foremost point-set of the first kind, without the second kind of point at the same time partaking in this raising, then the first spatial-lattice will advance forward parallel to itself and leave the second behind. If we now lay through this advanced part a transverse section, then it will be able to strike only upon points of the first kind. The combined lattice-system is thus then divided into two parts, of which the rear one still contains both kinds of point, while the front one is composed only of point-sets of the first kind. Upon continued growth, therefore, the form corresponding to the points of the second (posterior) kind will no longer be able to come to expression.

Quite analogously the case lets itself be construed in which, as a consequence of more rapid growth of the hindmost point-sets toward the rear, a part is created which consists only of points of the second (posterior) kind, and which will thus not be in a position to form any such of the first (anterior) kind. In such a way an originally totipotent lattice-combination will be able to be divided into three zones lying one behind another, of which the middle one still retains totipotency, with growth of the head-end toward the front and of the tail-end toward the rear. The foremost zone, however, can form toward front and rear only what is of like kind, corresponding to the head-end; in analogous manner the hindmost zone only what corresponds to the tail.

We see this case realized in practice in two ways; namely either as the favoring of a particular terminal form through an external factor, e.g. oxygen for the little heads of *Tubularia*, or as a spontaneous differentiation of the terminations of the animal-body with advancing development of the individual animal, e.g. in *Planaria* or *Lumbricus*.

The part separated off from the foremost or hindmost zone is not capable of regenerating the other zonal quality, but rather, upon continued growth, appends even at the "wrong" end the point-sets corresponding to its zonal quality. We therefore obtain the "heteromorphic" double-heads, or double-tails respectively.

An incomplete severance in the foremost or hindmost zone must consistently lead to break-triple-formation with three like-poled components, while in the middle, still totipotent region it will regenerate the two different poles in correct orientation at the accessory body-terminations.

The further the differentiation of the parts increases in the animal series with increasing height of organization, the further we see the spontaneous drawing-apart of the point-systems proceed before us. One of the most instructive cases is formed by the conditions described at the human extremities. Here we have to picture to ourselves that the anterior margin still encompasses both point-systems, of which, owing to their anterior position, normally only the form of the radial, or tibial, zone comes to expression, while the posterior margin forms the ulnar, or fibular, zone. The analysis of the malformations carried out by us shows that the anterior margin is also in a position to regenerate the posterior margin; both lattice-systems are therefore still to be ascribed to it. The posterior margin, on the contrary, no longer forms the anterior margin; here, then, a drawing-apart has taken place, and upon regeneration the sole remaining latticesystem of the second kind forms the ulnar part again even at the anterior margin. Some cases (e.g. Johnson's case for the foot) seem, however, to show that under circumstances too, probably with injury occurring very close to the surface of the anterior margin, only the point-kind of the anterior margin is present and proceeds to continued production of its own peculiar point-sets.

On the other hand, one must not picture to oneself as absolute the disappearance of the one kind of point upon the moving-out, e.g., of the head of the planarian from the totipotent middle zone. It is possible, as P. Lang's (1913) experiments on *Planaria polychroa* have shown, for tails to intercalate themselves once more later, after completion of the heteromorphic double-head, out of the separated-off head-zone. This result is entirely compatible with our exposition, if we recall that for the origination of the "head-zone" we made the greater velocity of the appending of "head"-points at the anterior body-end responsible, so that the "tail"-points perhaps still carried along would receive their increment more slowly than the "head"-points.

The appending of the "head"-points toward the rear, too, tends moreover to occur more slowly than toward the front, as we saw in the experiments, in which the inverse component mostly forms more slowly than the other components of the break-triple-formation.

According to the consideration of our point-systems hitherto followed, the assumption of a turning of cells would not be at all necessary. The heteromorphic parts would arise even without such a thing, if we imagine the individual spatial-lattices pushed apart in a straight line. We must, however, now consider in particular those break-triple-formations in which the break concerns an appendage which itself not only possesses a different anterior and posterior margin, but is besides built differently from left to right, since the one side represents a more proximal, the other a more distal part. If we wished here, by pushing apart more proximal and more distal lattice-nets, to make graphic for ourselves the regeneration setting in at the proximally-turned break-surface, then we should obtain the appearing qualities in proximo-distal succession, and also in antero-posterior succession, in the actually occurring abutment, but nonetheless the mirror-image arrangement to the normal form would not be co-represented. Let us assume, e.g., that at the form to be built the posterior margin is to be drawn out farther distally toward the right than the anterior margin; then, upon drawing apart, the more distal spatial-lattice now emerging at the proximally-turned break-surface would, with uniform pull, let the anterior margin protrude more strongly on the left than the posterior margin. In order to obtain the mirror-image, the more distal spatial-lattice of the posterior margin would have to be drawn more toward the left than that of the anterior margin. Or, upon regeneration, the parts would have to grow the more rapidly the farther toward the posterior margin they lay. There would then have to be established a gradual transition, in that, in the prolongation of each antero-posterior layer, that layer would be most elongated which lies at the outermost posterior margin, and the others correspondingly less.

Such an arrangement, however, still does not correspond to the actual pictures of the break-triple-formation at extremities. For the corresponding layers are found not pushed forward gradually in a straight-line manner, but rather bent off at one and the same angle against the normal direction. The growth-velocity of the posterior margin relative to that of the anterior margin is not greater than at the normally-growing extremity. The assumption of the mirror-image form therefore rests not on an altered relative growth-velocity, but on an abrupt re-ordering that has set in at the break-surface, which I had just tried to explain from the turning of the cells in Zur Strassen's sense.

To the mirror-image arrangement, upon the immediate abutting of the two mirror-images in the mirror-plane, an oblique-angled spatial-lattice does justice, if the boundary-meshes of the original lattice-arrangement are imagined flipped over by 180° in their plane. Such a flipping-over could take place either through turning of the whole boundary-plane in one piece, or through turning of the individual lattice-points. For bilaterally-symmetric lattice-forms these alternatives could not be distinguished from one another; with non-bilaterally-symmetric point-combinations, on the contrary, this decision is possible. Our concrete example of the extremities lets this be recognized at once: had we to do with a turning of the whole boundary-layer in one piece, that is, with a flipping-over of it out of its plane and, turned again by 180°, back into the same, then the symmetry-form would have to have remained the same, i.e. a right extremity would have to produce a right one even at the proximally-turned surface, and the upper and under sides to be newly formed would have to not continue the old ones, but rather be exactly interchanged with one another. Since both of these never hold, we must assume a turning of the individual lattice-points, whose position relative to those of the same mesh indeed changes, namely is carried over into the mirror-image one, yet without leaving their planes, so that these remain unchanged from top to bottom, hence the determination of the upper and under side does not change.

I should like to draw particular attention to the fact that, in the turnings to be assumed, the angle-values of the lattice-meshes do not change, but only exchange their place. They form the constant in the form, while the absolute and relative size of the various components of a break-triple-formation can fluctuate within wide limits, and hand in hand with this their developmental height as well.

In order that the angle-values of the form-shaping be guaranteed, we must ascribe to the points of the spatial-lattice definitely-directed attractive forces, and since the bodies of the Metazoa are composed of cells, we will let the attractive forces act through these. Since, however, we do not get by with one spatial-lattice for the explanation of the form-unfolding of a metazoan species, we must assume in one and the same cell various attraction-spheres, which unfold the effects of the various spatial-lattices still united at the body-part concerned. So we come, by another path, to a view similar to the one Zur Strassen (1903) expressed "for the mechanics of epithelium-formation": that the cells adhere to one another by means of analogous attraction-zones, and that the arrangement of such various attraction-zones is decisive for the form-shaping.

Zur Strassen (p. 109) writes, with reference to his *Ascaris*-observations:

"What we need are cells of the following constitution. Besides the 'sides,' the greatest part, or the whole basal cap, must be attractively active, so that the cell is enabled to build up both flat epithelium-pieces and strongly curved, or solid, blastulae respectively. Secondly, however, it must be provided for that the cells, in spite of their extended contact-capability, do not glide irregularly, but rather are held fast in the same manner with every arbitrary kind of joining-together. To comply with this demand is not at all so difficult. Let us imagine that the attraction-domain encompassing the whole basal cap is not homogeneous, but divided into parallel zones of unequal activity (Fig. 5), be it that strips of qualitatively different irritability really lie one above another, be it that only the intensity of the same changes continuously from the basal pole outward; and further, that always the like-named or equally-strong zones strive after mutual contact; then cells so constituted would, as it seems to me, accomplish that which was demanded above. They would, according to circumstances, join together with their basal regions into a solid tetrahedron or into a regular eight-cell group, or, when there are many of them, by contact of their equatorial zones and with release of the basal caps, compose a curved or even flat free epithelium, without ever a cell being in a position to glide out of the level of the others. This conception, that the cells of all stages possess one and the same preformed building-mode, and that according to the form of the epithelium now more basal, now more equatorial regions come to action, leads us to a necessary consequence: the cells must turn. In what degree and in what direction the cell has to turn in the individual case depends on the one hand on the curvature of the epithelium, on the other hand on that positional relation which exists, at the birth of the cell, between its zones and the spindle-direction." (p. 110): "Let us assume … that the preformed zones run transverse to the spindle-direction, i.e. symmetrical to the inner axis of the young cell (Fig. 6)." "This … assumption now possesses, as I believe, by far the greatest probability. First of all it permits us to bring the symmetrical, zone-wise 'building' of the cell-surface demanded by the theory into relation with the known inner symmetry of the cell … But above all the circumstance carries weight that in all cells of *Ascaris* something like a turning of the original cell-axis — exactly in the sense and to the extent that our assumption requires — is in fact to be recognized."

Zur Strassen's ideas agree with my theory not only in the discussion of contact-effects and turnings, but permit besides yet a further point in the behavior of the break-triple-formations to be elucidated, namely that the components of the same can be united with one another at an arbitrary angle.

What matters thereby is chiefly the gaping of the break-surface. The obliqueness of the break-planes to the longitudinal axis of the formation concerned plays a role only at first, in that the regenerate, consisting of strongly swollen, plastic embryonal cells, following the surface-tension, forms perpendicular to the break-surface ("Barfurth's rule") a roundish dome. Later, however, a straightening mostly sets in (cf. Przibram 1906, Crystal-analogies, pp. 249–250, Exp. Zool., 2. Regeneration, 1909, p. 232, II.).

Della Valle (1913, p. 136, note 2) finds fault that this explanation of Barfurth's rule must restrict itself only to the first hemispherical stages of the regenerates. In view of the later straightening, which has proved to be not functional, this seems to me to be directly an advantage. When Della Valle (p. 136) now even wishes to make the better nourishment at the center of the wound-surface responsible for the more significant growth-increase perpendicularly above the same, then several objections are to be raised. First, this increase could just as well lie in the prolongation of the old longitudinal axis, provided the form of the emerging drop were not decisive. Secondly, it is certainly not in general the nourishing blood-vessel that lies in the middle of the cross-section, hence the best nourishment of the center is not assured. Thirdly, the growth-velocity in regenerates is in general, at one and the same organ, the greater the more proximally the same had been removed (cf. Przibram, 1917; 1918, *Growth-inhibitions*). With an oblique section the more proximal point-of-passage would thus grow more rapidly than the middle one, if special form-building forces did not influence the initial stages. Fourthly, finally, the experiments on two-lobed fish-fins (Morgan, Lit. cf. Prz., Exp. Zool., 2. Reg.) show most distinctly that the perpendicular is maintained only so long as roundish blastema prevails, but then gradually, through the more rapid forward-growth of the parts cut back farther, the original form forms itself again in straight prolongation.

prevailed, but then, gradually, through the more rapid forward-growth of the parts that were cut back further, the original form once again forms itself in straight prolongation.

If the analysis of the break-triple-formation has thus led us to the construction of the organic spatial lattices, which for their part will contribute to the elucidation of many an obscure problem of organic form-development, then I should like to reject Della Valle's reproach (1913, p. 131), as though, in generalizing the Batesonian rules, I had forgotten the frequency of the upholding of animal polarity.

My exposition referred to cases in which there are proximally- and distally-turned wound-surfaces. In all such cases the proximally-turned portion does indeed, under unaltered external factors, actually bring forth the distal part again in inverse fashion.

Apart from these cases, however, there are then indeed also those in which, out of the two wound-surfaces of one and the same break, there grow not the same formations, but those which normally lie in the direction toward which the wound-surface in question looks. This, however, applies only to the totipotent animal-pieces which make up the middle portion of the body; what is here proximal, what distal? If, for example, one bisects a planarian transversely, does the tail-ward-turned wound-surface of the fore-piece look proximal or distal? The same question arises when the severance is not a complete one, and when the cut has been placed somewhat more or somewhat less head-ward.

The discussion may perhaps appear all too formal or dialectical, but it acquires its objectivity through the consideration of totipotency as the coexistence of various spatial lattices, whose stepping-apart first leads to the unfolding of distal parts. So long as all spatial lattices are active, only their resultant ever comes into effect — in the particular example, the formation of a tail toward the rear and of a head toward the front. But as soon as the body has been laid apart into a more proximal and a more distal portion, of which the latter is impoverished in spatial lattices, then in these the action of the eliminated spatial lattices falls away in the "proximal" direction, and only the more distal ones are stamped into form.

For this stamping-into-form the Batesonian symmetry-rules now hold quite generally; they thus represent the more general case, whereas the regular regeneration which Della Valle designates as the more usual behavior first arises out of a combined action of several simple lattices.

The mutual arrangement of the lattices would correspond to what I earlier designated as "stratification-polarity" (1910, zool.-bot.

Society; repeated: Exp. Zool., 4, Vitality, 1913, p. 35). Distinct from this is the polarity of the isolated lattices, which earlier had been distinguished as "directional-polarity," and which can be made equally-poled through the folding-over of the boundary-surface with rotation of the individual points.

How we are to conceive the organic spatial lattices in the individual animal-species and in their organs in particular, on that I can for the present make no statements yet.

Most probably the animal-groups characterized in the "proximo-physiological overview" by their symmetry-relations correspond to classes of lattice-combinations. Since, however, as repeatedly emphasized, I do not wish to enter here upon the ground of fact, the understanding of which is in any case difficult to convey, let the further treatment of the theme be reserved for the future chemistry of the crystal-analogues.

By way of conclusion to the present work, let attention be drawn to various indications which make a lattice-structure appear more probable as the foundation of organic form-developments than some other arrangement of distinctly different substances:

1. While various species are distinguished by various chemisms, and the organs too, e.g. in the anterior region, are found to differ from the ventral region, this cannot be asserted of the proximo-distal direction, e.g. of an arthropod- or vertebrate-limb; the difference must therefore rest upon something other than the stringing-together of various substances.

2. With regeneration the distal parts are reformed, and one can begin from various rejection-heights, so that the regeneration would have to be expected differently according to the surface; whether at first the most proximal, and only quite at the end the most distal, parts should unfold; whether, on the contrary, in each direction the same end-forms are driven forth out of the various spatial lattices.

3. With bilaterally-asymmetrical forms, e.g. of snails, there can, through a break, also be effected a mechanical reversal, the symmetry-reversal. In the case where, despite the structure now offered as possible, the otherwise guaranteed mirror-image reproduction — that is, despite the spatial-lattice-combination from one of the two outermost lattices with a central lattice — the symmetry is to be effected through reversal; for, with the germ-cells, the break does not act before but after, and one obtains, under restoration of the original lattice-combination, again only the normal asymmetry (facts, cf. Conklin in Przibram, lecture, Graz, zool. Congr., 1910).

### Summary.

1. Abnormal triplications of body-parts can be demonstrated, according to definite symmetry-rules, in all groups of the Metazoa.

2. Their origin at injuries, namely at breaks, can occasionally be observed in nature and repeated in experiments.

3. Even on finished natural forms, injury-traces and the unequal formation of the components of a "break-triple-formation" often point, according to Tornier's biotechnical mode of consideration, to the regenerative origin of the supernumerary formations.

4. Even with the appearance of break-triple-formations in several generations (e.g. in fruit-culture, *Drosophila*, after Hoge, cf. Tab. C) it is not a question of inborn hereditary mutations, but of an inclination to breaks, which extends to various body-parts and can be intensified by external factors (e.g. cold) even under post-embryonic action.

5. Within the various classes the break-triple-formation occurs in the wild in heightened degree among the beetles covered with hard chitin ("horn-wings" or "*Coleoptera*"), the lime-armored ten-footed crabs ("*Crustacea*"), and the four-footed vertebrates equipped with hard limb-skeletons (*Vertebrata tetrapoda*).

6. This distribution rests upon the stronger and lasting gaping of the wound-margins in the case of hard tissues, so that the growing regenerates can grow out separately, whereas otherwise they easily grow together.

7. The origin of the break-triple-formations is always traceable to a time at which regenerative potencies of the affected parts can still be demonstrated.

8. They therefore arise, in lower animals, even still at sexually-mature age; in the highest arthropods (crabs) and the highest amphibians (frogs), however, only after injury in the larval state; in the highest vertebrates (Amniota) their appearance falls within the embryonic period.

9. An exception is formed by the stag's antler, whose yearly renewal offers occasion for the appearance of supernumerary regenerates after breaks as well.

10. Since, with the moltings of the arthropods, the parts broadened through triplication easily tear off, break-triple-formations mostly come to observation only on the oldest specimens, no longer (imagines of the insects)

or only very rarely (Decapoda, Crustacea) molting.

11. This behavior agrees with their derivation from regeneration, not however from mutation; for according to the latter we should have to expect inborn triplications on a greater number of younger or smaller specimens.

12. The three components of the break-triple-formation are wont to lie in one plane, because the regenerate growing out of the break-surfaces retains the direction of the longitudinal growth, provided no further secondary distortion takes place — which, however, is never able to alter anything in the originally assumed symmetry-relations (cf. critique of Tornier's *Algira*-experiments, Tab. B).

13. Just as with simple regeneration, so too with the triplication only those parts are reproduced which are to stand distal to the wounding-site, not however proximal forms (cf. Melolontha-feeler No. 145, Tab. C, merely apparent assumption).

14. Of the three components of the break-triple-formation, the middle one grows, in contrast to the two others, out of a proximally-turned break-surface, and produces in this way an inversely-directed form.

15. If the break-triple-formation concerns the main axis of the animal-body, then it cannot be distinguished whether this oppositely-directed form is to be regarded as a mirror-image or as an inversely-placed repetition of the normal one, since through rotation the bilaterally-symmetrical parts can be brought into coincidence.

16. If the triplication concerns an appendage, then it can often be recognized, from the mutual position of the fore- and hind-edge and of the upper- and under-surface, that in the case of the middle component there is a mirror-image, not coincidable through rotation, of the normal form of the same body-side.

17. This holds in particular for the limbs of the arthropods and vertebrates, so that the middle components can then be confused with appendages of the opposite body-side.

18. Further, it can be securely established, on the crabs with normal difference of the two outer body-sides ("heterochely"), that in the case of the middle, mirror-image component it is not a matter of an adaptation to the opposite side, but of a mirror-image repetition of the formation of the same body-side: in not a single case does the middle component cause the special characteristics of the opposite side to come forth, as against the normal components (cf. Tab. Aa, and analogously with double-formations Tab. Ab).

19. In particular this holds also for the lobster figured by van Beneden, as is indeed directly stated in the description ; the apparent similarity of the middle component in the figure with one of the other claw-forms rests partly on a twisting of the cutting-edge, partly on too slight an accuracy of the drawing (cf. No. 87).

20. The inverted orientation of the form on the middle components of the break-triple-formations arises through the retention of the upper- and under-surface of the limbs produced, even in the regenerates, and likewise mostly of the fore- and the hind-edge.

21. The growth of the regenerates emerging from the two break-surfaces is a consequence of the equipotence of the two wound-surfaces with the distal end-formation, and of the extinction of totipotence at these cross-sections; for otherwise proximal parts too would be to be expected.

22. It can be demonstrated, through comparison with double-formations of regenerative origin and with egg-experiments, that precisely the potencies necessary for the production of the break-triple-formation are in each case really present.

23. Thereby there confirms itself everywhere the non-representability of upper- and under-surface, the partial representability of fore- and hind-edge.

24. On the double-hands and double-feet of man there occur merely radial, respectively tibial, not however ulnar, respectively fibular, unions, from which is inferred the incapacity of the hind-edge for the bringing-forth of the forms of the fore-edge of human limbs.

25. An analogous behavior shows itself in the human limbs with break-triple-formation.

26. Under application of the propositions summarized above, many hitherto only-difficultly interpretable cases of polydactyly (cf. Tab. F, I–VIII) can be solved in satisfying manner (e.g. the triple-formation of a *Macacus*-hind-foot in the Museum of the College of Surgeons in London, cf. Tab. F, and Pol's "arm-bone" in *Homo*, Tab. G).

27. The appearances of the break-triple-formation allow the inference of combinations of foldable-over organic spatial lattices, whose closer precising (in analogy to those of the crystals) remains reserved for further communications.

## Explanation of the Plate Figures.

### Plates III–XVII.

All the figures on Plates III–XVII are drawings made after illustrations by other authors or by the author himself from the natural objects.

(The numbers refer to the same numbers cited in the text under the corresponding cases, which is why a special explanation is dispensed with. The numbers not to be found on the drawn plates are to be sought on the photographic plates, and indeed in the column "No." of the plate-explanation, in so far as figures are at all named in the text for the case in question. The drawings are distributed in the following way over the various animal groups and body-parts:)

| Plate | Fig. No. to No. | (Triple-formations at) |
|---|---|---|
| III | 1 to 8a* | Triple-formations at a break and other triple-formations) |
| III | 9 » 12/13 | on the main axis of the Metazoa; on parameres; on mollusc tentacles; |
| III | 13 » 13g | on crayfish antennae and on clawless crab legs; |
| IV | 14 » 17a | on claw-bearing crab legs and claws of equal-clawed crabs; |
| IV | 18 » 22 | in particular on the claws of the river crayfishes (*Astacus* = *Potamobius*); |
| IV | 23 » 29a | on the claws of equal-clawed crabs (*Brachyura*), |
| V | 30 » 34* | in particular on the claws of the edible crab (*Cancer*) and |
| V | 35 » 37 | of the spider crab (*Maja*); on unequal-clawed crabs; |
| V | 38 » 45 | in particular basal and palm joints of the lobster (*Homarus*); |

### List of the tables inserted in the text.

A — p. 279 — Right- and left-handedness in *Eriphia spinifrons*.

Aa — » 286 — Triple-formations on the claws of the first pair of legs of heterochelous crabs.

Ab — » 287 — Double-formations on the claws of the first pair of legs of heterochelous crabs.

B — » 298 — Triple-formation at a break on the antenna of the cockchafer, *Melolontha vulgaris*, (after Mocquerys, critique of Bateson).

Ca — » 322 — *Drosophila ampelophila*, fruit fly; triple-formations (after Hoge).

Cb — » 323 — *Drosophila ampelophila*, fruit fly; double-formations (?; after Hoge).

D — » 350 — *Pelobates fuscus*, hind-leg increase. (Critique of Tornier).

E — » 388 — *Macacus*, abnormal hind-leg (various critiques).

F — » 396 — Polydactyly in mammals, I. *Homo*, a) *Manus*, β) *Pes*; II. *Felis*; III. *Canis*; IV. *Sus*; V. *Cervus*; VI. *Ovis*; VII. *Bos*; VIII. *Equus*.

G — » 411 — "arm-leg" in humans, *Homo* (critique of Pol).

---

*Translator's note.* Przibram's large comparative treatise on reduplication and bilateral symmetry.
